The third chapter of the procedural framework sets out, in elaborate form, the multitude of duties that various government agencies and officials must discharge in order to secure a smooth, credible, and peaceful electoral process throughout the nation. At the core of this arrangement lies the Planning Division of the Election Commission of India, an analytical unit charged with a comprehensive assessment of the nation’s security resources. Its first task is to evaluate the total pool of central and state police and armed forces that are available for deployment, a process that involves gathering data on the strength, readiness, and geographic distribution of each force. In parallel, the division must estimate the specific national requirements for the Central Armed Police Forces, known collectively as the CAPF, which include such agencies as the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, Central Industrial Security Force and others. Once these needs have been quantified, the Planning Division proceeds to allocate the appropriate portions of the CAPF to the individual states, ensuring that each state receives a share proportionate to its security demands and logistical capacity. The division also drafts a national movement plan for the CAPF, a detailed schedule that maps out the routes, timings, and staging points for the forces as they travel from their home bases to the election‑bearing states. In doing so, it seeks to optimize the utilization of state police forces, making certain that the deployment of central units complements rather than duplicates the work of local law‑enforcement agencies. To support these operations, the division maintains a systematic data repository on force multipliers—specialized units, equipment, and capabilities that amplify the effectiveness of ordinary troops, such as surveillance drones, riot‑control gear, and communication suites. Close coordination with the Ministry of Home Affairs and the heads of each security agency is essential, as is the issuance of precise instructions to the Ministry of Railways, which must arrange the transport of personnel and material. The Planning Division also works hand‑in‑hand with the Ministry of Telecommunications to secure the necessary bandwidth for webcasting, internet connectivity, and other electronic services that underpin modern election monitoring. It issues directions concerning the deployment of force multipliers and other ancillary resources, formulates a national deployment plan that integrates all these elements, and supervises the implementation of the plan to guarantee that the intended security posture is achieved on the ground.

The Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA), which heads the internal security portfolio of the Union Government, bears the responsibility of making the CAPF available to the election commission in accordance with the requisitions that arise from the Planning Division’s assessment. After confirming both the requirement and the availability of forces, the ministry must ensure that at least one‑third of the sanctioned strength of each CAPF component is positioned in the relevant states a minimum of thirty days before the polling dates, a measure intended to foster confidence among voters and political stakeholders. To oversee this complex deployment, the MHA appoints a Force Coordinator, a senior officer tasked with synchronizing the movement, induction, and eventual demobilization of the forces across the election‑going states. The ministry also coordinates the provision of auxiliary assets such as helicopters, Rapid Action Forces, and the National Disaster Response Force (NDRF) whenever a state reports a need for aerial transport, rapid response units, or disaster‑relief capabilities. Inter‑state security issues—such as the movement of personnel across state borders, the sharing of intelligence, or the handling of cross‑border threats—are managed through a dedicated inter‑state coordination mechanism that the MHA operates. The ministry further informs state election officials of any intelligence inputs that might affect the security environment, ranging from communal tensions to potential infiltration by extremist elements. In addition, the MHA conducts diplomatic and security talks with neighboring states and countries that share borders with India, to mitigate any cross‑border risks that could impinge upon the electoral atmosphere.

The Ministry of Railways, as the backbone of the nation’s mass transport system, assumes a pivotal role in ensuring that the security forces can be moved swiftly and efficiently to the required locations. It must provide a dedicated and time‑bound rail network for the mobility of troops, meaning that special trains are scheduled exclusively for the purpose of moving police and armed personnel, with minimal interference from regular passenger services. The forces are required to inform the office of the Force Coordinator and the designated Nodal Officer before commencing any journey, thereby enabling the rail authorities to prepare the requisite train composition and schedule. The movement plan, which is prepared well in advance by the Ministry of Home Affairs, includes details of the origin, destination, and timing of each train, allowing the railway authorities to arrange for special trains and “splinter” coaches—small, detached coaches that can be attached to regular services when necessary. Provisions are also made for catering through the Indian Railway Catering and Tourism Corporation (IRCTC); the anticipated number of onboard meals is projected based on the number of troops and the length of the journey, ensuring that logistical support for food is not an afterthought. Train bookings are organized according to the precise points of embarkation and disembarkation, with each company (coy) of troops allocated at least two sleeper‑class coaches, and the tactical‑air‑command (TAC) headquarters receiving an additional coach. Moreover, each special train must contain a minimum of two air‑conditioned third‑tier coaches, providing a higher standard of comfort for senior officers and command staff. Coordination between the railways and IRCTC is especially critical when special trains face delays due to unforeseen circumstances, as timely meal delivery must be synchronized with the troops’ location to avoid hardship. Since railway stations have limited capacity to handle multiple special trains on a single day, the planning process must include provisions for diverting some trains to nearby satellite stations, a decision that must be finalized before the train departs. The railways therefore play a crucial monitoring role, tracking the progress of each train and ensuring that the overall movement schedule remains smooth, particularly during the de‑induction phase when troops return to their home bases.

The Ministry of Telecommunications, responsible for the nation’s communication infrastructure, must begin its preparatory work at least six months before the election schedule is announced. During this period, the Bharat Sanchar Nigam Limited (BSNL) and other service providers are tasked with establishing connectivity for polling stations that currently lack reliable telephone or internet links. This lead time is essential for the procurement of necessary equipment, the erection of towers or satellite dishes, and the coordination of field teams that will install and test the infrastructure. Historically, last‑minute directives have caused service providers to claim shortages of funds or manpower, resulting in incomplete coverage. To avoid this, the framework recommends that, especially in remote or difficult‑to‑reach areas, the responsibility be shared among multiple operators, including private players such as Reliance Jio, Vodafone Idea, Airtel, and others. By distributing the workload, the strain on any single provider—particularly the state‑run BSNL—can be reduced, thereby increasing the likelihood that all polling stations will be equipped with the communications needed for real‑time reporting, voter assistance, and the secure transmission of election data.

The Ministry of Defence contributes to election security by furnishing aerial assets that are crucial for both operational mobility and emergency response. Helicopters, often supplied by the Indian Air Force or the Defence Ministry’s own fleet, are used to transport polling teams and security personnel to locations that are otherwise inaccessible, such as hill stations, islands, or regions with poor road connectivity. In addition, the ministry ensures that air‑ambulance services are stationed at strategic points across the country, ready to evacuate injured personnel or respond to medical emergencies that may arise during the electoral process, thereby enhancing the overall safety net for both voters and officials.

The Ministry of Shipping, though less frequently highlighted, plays an indispensable part in facilitating the movement of personnel and equipment to and from the island territories that form part of the Indian Union. By coordinating the allocation of vessels, ensuring berth availability at ports, and managing the logistics of loading and unloading, the ministry guarantees that security forces and election materials reach islands such as the Andamans, Lakshadweep, and other remote archipelagos in a timely manner, thus integrating these regions fully into the national electoral timetable.

The Ministry of Civil Aviation is charged with providing rotary‑wing and fixed‑wing aircraft services that complement the capabilities of the Ministry of Defence. This includes the deployment of helicopters—sometimes referred to by the colloquial term “Pawanhans”—to reach remote villages, mountainous terrain, or flood‑affected areas where ground transport is impractical. The ministry also arranges for chartered aircraft to ferry election officials, security personnel, and essential supplies on an urgent basis, especially when deadlines are tight or when unforeseen circumstances demand rapid redeployment. In addition, the ministry is responsible for the maintenance and operational readiness of airports, heliports, and landing strips located in distant or under‑developed regions, ensuring that these facilities can support the increased traffic that elections generate.

The Ministry of Surface Transport, which oversees the national highway network, has the duty of maintaining the condition of roads that serve as the arteries for the movement of troops, equipment, and voters alike. By keeping highways in good repair, the ministry helps to prevent delays, accidents, and logistical bottlenecks that could otherwise disrupt the timely arrival of security forces at polling stations or impede voters from reaching the ballot box.

Finally, the role of the General Observer—a neutral, independent individual appointed to monitor the entire electoral process—is to ensure that every facet of the election is conducted in a free, fair, and peaceful manner. Observers are tasked with scrutinizing the preparedness of the election administration, examining the nomination and scrutiny procedures, and evaluating the security arrangements that underpin the voting process. They conduct on‑site inspections of polling stations, verify the integrity of electoral rolls, examine polling materials such as ballot boxes and electronic voting machines (EVMs), and assess the adequacy of facilities for vulnerable groups. Observers also review the force deployment plan, consulting with security officials to confirm that the allocation of personnel is appropriate and that any modifications are justified. The selection of counting centres is made in consultation with observers, who verify that the venues meet the standards required for a transparent tallying of votes. Moreover, observers monitor compliance with the Model Code of Conduct issued by the Election Commission, ensuring that political parties and candidates adhere to the prescribed standards of behavior. In addition to overseeing the randomization of polling staff and counting personnel—a safeguard against manipulation—observers witness the second randomization of EVMs to confirm that the machines have not been tampered with. Throughout the election cycle, the General Observer’s presence in the force deployment plan serves as an additional layer of accountability, reinforcing the democratic principle that elections must be conducted with the utmost integrity, free from coercion or undue influence.

The plan for deploying security personnel at each polling station and throughout every constituency during an election is a detailed document that must be examined and approved by the most senior officer who is acting as a General or Police Observer for the district in question. This senior observer, who is the highest‑ranking official assigned to oversee the conduct of the election in that district, must receive a draft of the entire election plan, which includes the specific allocation of forces such as the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF), the regular state police, and the sector‑level arrangements that are managed by the officer designated as the Sector Officer (often abbreviated as SO). The draft is handed over to the senior observer as soon as the observer arrives in the state, or at the earliest possible moment after the plan has been prepared by the election authorities. Once the observer has the draft, he or she convenes a series of review meetings with the District Electoral Officer (the official responsible for administering the election in the district), the Superintendent of Police (the senior police officer in charge of maintaining law and order in the district), and the Returning Officer (the official who is legally empowered to receive, count and declare the votes). During these meetings the observer examines the proposed deployment of personnel, evaluates whether the suggested numbers and locations are realistic given the local conditions, and assesses the ground realities such as terrain, past incidents of violence, the presence of vulnerable groups, and the security requirements of particular polling stations that have been identified as critical. If the observer finds that any part of the draft does not correspond to the actual security needs on the ground, he or she may direct changes to the numbers of officers, the composition of the forces, or the timing of deployment. After the necessary modifications have been incorporated, the senior General or Police Observer gives his or her final approval to the plan. Once approved, the plan becomes binding on the officials who are charged with executing it on the ground, namely the District Electoral Officer and the Superintendent of Police. They must implement the deployment exactly as it has been sanctioned, and they are not permitted to deviate from the approved arrangement without first obtaining explicit permission from the senior observer. If, for any reason—such as an unexpected escalation of tension, a sudden natural disaster, or the discovery of a new security vulnerability—a change to the deployment becomes necessary after the plan has been set in motion, the officials must promptly inform the senior observer and must secure his or her written approval before making any adjustments. This safeguard ensures that any alteration to the security posture is carefully considered and that the overall coordination of election security remains under the tight supervision of the most senior authority.

The Police Observer, who occupies a pivotal position in the election machinery, is charged with the overarching responsibility of maintaining a peaceful environment throughout the entire constituency during the voting process. The observer’s principal task is to guarantee that every citizen can exercise the franchise—the right to vote—without being subjected to intimidation, coercion, violence, or any form of manipulation that could distort the free expression of the electorate’s will. To fulfil this mandate, the Police Observer must ensure that all instructions issued by the Election Commission of India (ECI), the constitutional body that oversees the conduct of elections, are faithfully followed by the police and other security agencies. These instructions cover a broad spectrum of law‑and‑order measures, including the preparation and execution of the district‑level security deployment plan, which outlines how police forces will be distributed across the district, and the identification of critical polling stations—those locations that, because of their size, location, or past history, require heightened security. Moreover, the observer must verify that a comprehensive vulnerability mapping exercise has been completed; this mapping involves a systematic assessment of areas that are at higher risk of electoral violence, communal tension, or other disturbances, and it forms the basis for allocating additional resources where they are most needed. While the Police Observer’s remit is extensive, it does not extend to the operational details of poll management, such as the actual conduct of voting, the handling of ballot boxes, or the counting of votes. Those responsibilities are vested in the General Observer, who is the senior official tasked with overseeing the entire polling process. By limiting the Police Observer’s duties to law‑and‑order oversight, the system ensures a clear division of labor, preventing overlap and potential conflicts between security functions and the administrative management of the election.

The National Force Coordinator occupies a senior role at the centre of India’s election security architecture. Appointed by the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA), which is the central government department responsible for internal security, the coordinator’s chief function is to serve as the central point of contact for all state‑level inputs concerning the deployment of security forces. On a day‑to‑day basis, the coordinator maintains continuous communication with the State Level Force Coordinators, the officials who manage the planning and allocation of forces within each state. By gathering information on the evolving security situation, the availability of personnel, and any emergent requirements from each state, the National Force Coordinator is able to synthesize a comprehensive picture of the nation‑wide security posture and to advise the Ministry of Home Affairs on adjustments that may be required to meet the needs of the election.

The Chief Electoral Officer (CEO) of a state bears a wide‑ranging set of duties that together ensure that the election is conducted in a secure, orderly and legally compliant manner. First, the CEO continuously reviews the daily law‑and‑order situation across the state, analysing reports from police, intelligence agencies, and local administrators to identify any emerging threats. Based on this assessment, the CEO must ensure that timely and adequate measures are taken to address specific concerns, taking into account the unique security challenges that each state or union territory faces during an election. A crucial element of the CEO’s work is to arrange inter‑border talks with the authorities of neighboring states. These discussions aim to coordinate security measures across state lines, seal borders where necessary, and prevent the movement of potential trouble‑makers or illicit weapons that could jeopardise the election. The CEO is also responsible for establishing and supervising a range of communication and grievance‑redress mechanisms at the state level and within each district. These include a control‑room that monitors real‑time information, a help‑line for voters and officials to raise concerns, a dedicated complaint cell to investigate allegations of misconduct, and, where required, a call‑centre that can field large volumes of calls during peak periods. In line with the Election Commission’s directives, the CEO must oversee the preparation of vulnerability maps, a detailed analytical exercise carried out by the District Electoral Officers in close consultation with police officers, sector officers and field staff. These maps pinpoint areas that are susceptible to violence, intimidation, or other forms of disruption, thereby guiding the allocation of additional security resources. Alongside vulnerability mapping, the CEO must ensure that critical polling stations—those identified as needing special protection due to their size, location, or past history of trouble—are clearly identified and that appropriate security measures are put in place.

Another key responsibility of the CEO is to assess the availability of the state police force and to calculate the total number of personnel required for election duty. This assessment involves a careful balancing act: the CEO must take into account the routine policing responsibilities that the state police must continue to fulfil while also dedicating sufficient numbers of officers to protect the election process. Once the requirement has been quantified, the CEO communicates both the availability of existing forces and the additional requirements to the Election Commission of India. This communication forms the basis for the planning of the deployment of security forces, which may include both the regular state police and para‑military units drawn from the Central Armed Police Forces. When drawing up the deployment plans, the CEO refers to the deployment patterns used in previous elections, adapting them to the current context and any new intelligence. The CEO also oversees the preparation of both the District Security Deployment Plan and the State Security Deployment Plan, ensuring that both are in strict conformity with the Election Commission’s directions. The State Deployment Plan is prepared by the State Police Nodal Officer—an officer of senior rank designated as the point person for security matters—working in close consultation with the CEO. This plan integrates the forces that are available from the state police, the State Armed Police Force (SAPF), and the Central Armed Police Forces that have been earmarked by the Ministry of Home Affairs for election duty.

Optimising the use of state security forces is a further task of the CEO. The officer must strive to deploy personnel in a manner that maximises coverage while avoiding unnecessary duplication. To ensure that the plan is being executed effectively, the CEO conducts regular review meetings, often using video conferencing technology, with the District Magistrates, Additional Directors General of Police, Inspector Generals of Police, Superintendents of Police, and the chief police officials of each district. In these meetings the CEO gathers proposals from the districts for conferring executive magistrate powers on other officers, and subsequently secures the necessary orders from the Home Department of the state. The CEO also arranges for the issuance of a notification under Section 28A of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, which authorises the deputation of police officers to assist the Election Commission in carrying out its functions. Periodic coordination meetings are held with the State Police Nodal Officer, the State CAPF Nodal Officer, and other senior officials to review the state security plan and the detailed force deployment. After the election dates have been announced, the CEO convenes a daily review meeting on law and order and other security issues with the State Police Nodal Officer to monitor the situation as it evolves. The CEO also plans for and implements various “force multipliers” – tools and strategies that enhance the effectiveness of the security apparatus. These may include live web‑casting of the voting process for transparency, the deployment of videographers to record incidents, the assignment of micro‑observers to monitor specific high‑risk areas, and the placement of static magistrates at polling stations to ensure that any breaches of law are dealt with swiftly. In remote or “shadow” areas where communication infrastructure is weak, the CEO ensures that suitable communication equipment is installed, often in partnership with the police department, so that real‑time information can flow to the control‑room. The CEO also oversees the preparation of the state‑level plan for the movement and deployment of forces, ensuring that logistical arrangements such as transport, accommodation, and supply lines are in place. Once the plan is finalised, the CEO is tasked with its effective implementation, making sure that every district follows the prescribed deployment schedule.

An important aspect of the CEO’s role is the formulation of a randomisation plan for the posting of state police personnel. This plan, prepared in consultation with the State Police Nodal Officer, determines how police officers are rotated across different districts and polling stations so that no single officer becomes overly familiar with a particular area, thereby reducing the risk of bias or undue influence. The randomisation plan must be executed in all districts in accordance with the specific instruction numbered 464/INST/2022/EPS dated 15 March 2023, issued by the Election Commission. Finally, the CEO ensures that an air ambulance service is made available from the state government, providing a rapid medical response capability in case of emergencies that may arise during the election period.

The senior officials of the state government, namely the State Chief Secretary, the Home Secretary, and the Director General of Police (DGP), also have clearly defined responsibilities that complement those of the Chief Electoral Officer. Together they engage in inter‑border talks with the authorities of neighboring states to coordinate security measures, share intelligence, and seal any borders that might be vulnerable to infiltration by disruptive elements. In preparation for the announcement of elections, they must appoint a State Level Nodal Police Officer who is not below the rank of Inspector General of Police. This appointment, made at least four months before the election is announced, ensures that there is a senior police officer with the authority and experience to oversee the state’s security preparations. The trio also carries out an assessment of both the availability of existing police forces and the additional requirements that will be needed for the election, communicating these figures to the Election Commission and contributing to the overall planning of security force deployment. It should be noted that every state’s police hierarchy includes a senior position designated as the Additional Director General or Inspector General, a rank that is essential for the effective coordination of the large‑scale security operations that elections demand. By performing these duties, the State Chief Secretary, Home Secretary, and DGP ensure that the state’s administrative and police machinery operate in concert, thereby creating a secure environment in which the democratic process can be carried out without fear or favour.

The responsibility for procuring and managing the communication system that serves the state police rests upon a single officer who is often given the formal title of Nodal Officer Communication. In practice this officer is the central point of contact for all matters that involve the acquisition, installation, maintenance, and operational oversight of the communication equipment that enables police units to exchange information quickly and reliably during both routine policing and special events such as elections. The designation of Nodal Officer Communication is usually made through an official appointment by the state government, and the officer who receives this appointment is expected to act as the primary liaison not only with internal police departments but also with external agencies that supply the technical hardware, software, and services required for a modern police communications network. The duties of the Nodal Officer extend to assessing the specific communication needs of each police station, commonly abbreviated as PS, and to ensuring that the appropriate systems—ranging from radio transceivers and satellite links to secure digital data networks—are provided in a timely manner. Moreover, the officer must coordinate with the neighbouring states and with the central government, because procurement of large‑scale communication equipment often involves inter‑state contracts, shared funding arrangements, and compliance with national standards. By working closely with the central procurement authorities, the Nodal Officer can secure better prices, guarantee compatibility with other law‑enforcement networks, and avoid duplication of effort.

Because elections are a time of heightened security requirements, the appointment of the Nodal Officer Communication is scheduled well in advance of the electoral calendar. The guidelines stipulate that the officer should be named at least six to eight months before the due date of the elections. This lead time is crucial: it gives the officer sufficient opportunity to evaluate the existing communication infrastructure, identify gaps, and issue purchase orders for any new equipment that may be needed. The procurement cycle for sophisticated communication gear can be lengthy, often involving multiple rounds of technical evaluation, tendering, and testing before the devices are finally delivered and commissioned. By starting the process early, the Nodal Officer can avoid the risk of equipment shortages that could hamper the coordination of police forces during the critical period of voting, counting, and post‑election security.

In addition to the communication responsibilities, the same officer or a related official may be tasked with obtaining proposals for the conferment of Executive Magistrate powers on other police officers. Executive Magistrate powers are special legal authorities that enable certain officers to take immediate preventive action, such as imposing curfews, ordering the seizure of property, or directing the removal of unlawful assemblies, without having to go through the ordinary judicial process. The officer’s role here is to solicit written proposals from senior police officials who seek such powers, evaluate the merits of each proposal in accordance with the statutory criteria, and ensure that the appropriate powers are formally granted by the competent authority. This process helps to create a flexible legal framework that can be deployed swiftly in the event of law‑and‑order disturbances during the election period.

The Home Department of the state government also has a set of responsibilities that are closely linked to the overall electoral security framework. One of its core tasks is to prepare a consolidated report on the status of the entire state’s law‑and‑order preparedness, using a standard format known as LOR II. This report, which stands for “Law and Order Report,” is transmitted to the Chief Electoral Officer (CEO) on a daily basis from the moment the election is announced until the final result is declared. The daily report includes data on the deployment of police personnel, the status of communication systems, the readiness of auxiliary forces, and any incidents that may affect the conduct of the election. By feeding this information to the CEO, the Home Department ensures that the election administration has a real‑time picture of the security environment and can make informed decisions about resource allocation.

Another critical duty of the Home Department is the issuance of a notification under section 28A of the Representation of the People Act, 1951. This legal provision allows for the temporary deputation of police officers from the state police to the Election Commission of India (ECI) for the purpose of election management. The notification formally authorises the transfer of designated officers to the ECI, granting them the authority to act under the commission’s direction while retaining their original rank and status within the state police hierarchy. This mechanism ensures that the ECI can draw on the expertise of experienced police personnel without permanently removing them from their regular duties.

The assessment of the availability and requirement of police forces for the election is another essential function. The Home Department must evaluate how many police officers are needed in each constituency, taking into account factors such as population density, historical incidence of electoral violence, and the presence of vulnerable communities. After completing this assessment, the department prepares a demand draft that is sent to the CEO, who in turn forwards it to the ECI. The demand draft specifies the exact number of officers required, the categories of personnel (such as constables, inspectors, and senior officers), and any special skills that may be needed, such as crowd‑control expertise or expertise in dealing with electronic voting machines.

When the state’s own police force is insufficient to meet the projected demand, the Home Department also prepares requisitions for additional forces, such as the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) or police personnel from other states. These requisitions are again sent to the CEO for onward submission to the ECI, which then coordinates with the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA) to allocate the necessary resources. The ability to draw on CAPF units—including the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, and Indo‑Tibetan Border Police—provides the election authorities with a robust reserve of trained personnel who can be rapidly deployed to hotspots across the state.

The mobilization of all types of forces within the state, whether they are regular police units, state armed police battalions, home guards, or volunteer auxiliary forces, is a coordinated effort that falls under the umbrella of the Home Department’s responsibilities. Mobilization entails not only the physical movement of personnel to strategic locations but also the establishment of command and control structures, the allocation of logistical support such as food, water, and medical supplies, and the creation of communication channels that allow for rapid reporting of incidents. By ensuring that these forces are ready and correctly positioned, the department helps to create a conducive environment for a peaceful conduct of the election, thereby reducing the likelihood of violence or intimidation at polling stations.

Logistical arrangements extend beyond the deployment of human resources. The Home Department must also arrange for specialized units that are essential in high‑risk scenarios. These include bomb disposal squads, which are trained to neutralize explosive devices that could be used to disrupt the election process; mounted police units, which are useful for crowd management in large open spaces; and water‑borne units such as patrol boats, which can be deployed along rivers and lakes to secure voting stations located near water bodies. Additionally, the department must ensure the availability of ammunition, vehicles ranging from jeeps to heavy‑duty trucks, helicopters for aerial surveillance and rapid transport, and air ambulances for medical emergencies. All of these assets are placed at the disposal of the CEO, who can direct their use based on real‑time intelligence and operational needs.

Making all necessary logistic arrangements for security forces also involves the provision of basic equipment such as helmets, batons, protective vests, night‑vision devices, and personal protective equipment. The Home Department must maintain an inventory of these items, monitor their condition, and replenish stocks as needed. The logistical planning must also consider the maintenance schedules of vehicles and aircraft, ensuring that they are in good working order on the day they are required. In parallel, the department is tasked with planning and providing the communication equipment that is vital for coordinated action. This includes the distribution of handheld radios, secure mobile phones, and data terminals that enable police units to transmit real‑time information to command centers.

Training forms a cornerstone of the election‑security apparatus. The Home Department is charged with imparting comprehensive training to all forces that will be involved in the conduct of the election. Training programs cover a wide range of topics, including the legal framework governing elections, the rights and responsibilities of voters, protocols for handling electoral materials, crowd‑control techniques, and procedures for responding to emergencies such as fires, medical crises, or acts of terrorism. The training also emphasizes the importance of maintaining neutrality and avoiding any appearance of bias, which is essential to uphold the credibility of the electoral process. By equipping officers with the necessary knowledge and skills, the department enhances both the safety of the electorate and the integrity of the voting process.

The Chief Secretary or the Home Secretary of the state, together with the Commissioner of Police (CS and DGP), must also ensure the movement and mobilization of the State Armed Police Forces to other states when directed by the Ministry of Home Affairs. Such inter‑state deployment usually occurs when the central government seeks to reinforce security in regions facing heightened threats, and the state’s armed police units must be ready to respond to such directives swiftly. This requires careful coordination of transportation, accommodation, and sustainment arrangements, as well as the legal formalities that govern the temporary transfer of forces across state boundaries.

Budget planning for both the state police and the Central Armed Police Forces is another critical element that must be addressed well in advance of the election. The Home Department, in collaboration with the finance ministry of the state, prepares a detailed budget that accounts for all anticipated expenditures, including the procurement of communication equipment, the procurement of vehicles and ammunition, the remuneration of additional personnel, and the costs associated with training and logistics. The budget is then disbursed to the district administrations sufficiently early so that funds are available before the arrival of CAPF units in the state. Timely disbursement prevents bottlenecks that could otherwise delay the deployment of essential resources.

The Commission responsible for overseeing elections has issued a separate set of detailed instructions concerning the appointment, roles, and responsibilities of the State Police Nodal Officer, often abbreviated as SPNO. The SPNO serves as the senior police official who acts as the primary point of contact between the state police apparatus and the Chief Electoral Officer. The process for selecting an SPNO begins with a formal request from the CEO to the Chief Secretary or Home Secretary of the state or union territory. Within fifteen days of receiving this request, the Chief Secretary must provide a panel of at least three experienced and competent police officers who are currently serving in the State Police Headquarters. Each candidate on the panel must be accompanied by a vigilance clearance, which is an official verification that the officer has no pending disciplinary or criminal matters, and an executive record sheet that details the officer’s service history, performance evaluations, and prior assignments.

The CEO then evaluates the panel and seeks the concurrence of the Election Commission of India (ECI) on the selection of a particular officer to be designated as the SPNO. Once the Commission renders its decision, the Chief Secretary formally appoints the chosen officer as the State Police Nodal Officer. The appointment is made in addition to the officer’s existing duties, and the initial tenure is set for three years. This tenure can be extended for an additional three years, but only after receiving explicit concurrence from the Election Commission, ensuring that the officer’s performance remains satisfactory and that the Commission retains confidence in his or her ability to manage election‑related police operations.

The guidelines that govern the composition of the panel of police officers are quite specific. Candidates must hold the rank of Inspector General of Police or higher, reflecting the seniority required to coordinate large‑scale security operations. They must also have prior experience of elections, whether as a police observer, a deployment officer, or in any other capacity that involved direct interaction with the electoral process. This experience ensures that the officer understands the unique challenges that elections present, such as the need for impartiality, the management of large crowds, and the handling of election‑related disputes. Moreover, the candidate must not have been barred by the Election Commission from deployment on election duty, which could happen if the officer had previously been found to have acted in a partisan manner or had violated electoral codes of conduct. Finally, the officer must have a clean record with respect to past election duties, meaning that he or she should not have been transferred or disciplined for dereliction of duty in any previous election.

The appointment process is designed to guarantee that a police officer is designated as the SPNO not only during the election period but also throughout the non‑election period. This continuity allows the officer to develop long‑term strategies, maintain institutional memory, and build relationships with other agencies that are essential for effective election security. The SPNO’s responsibilities, carried out in close coordination with the CEO, are extensive. One of the principal duties is the preparation of a State or Union Territory Force Deployment Plan. This plan outlines how the various police forces, including the state police, the CAPF, and auxiliary forces, will be allocated across constituencies, districts, and specific polling stations. The draft plan must be approved by a committee that includes the CEO, the SPNO, and the State CAPF Nodal Officer, and it is subsequently vetted and, if necessary, modified by the General and Police Observers appointed by the Election Commission. The manual on Force Deployment provides a detailed procedural framework for this process, specifying how data on voter density, terrain, historical incidents, and threat assessments are incorporated into the final deployment matrix.

In addition to internal force allocation, the SPNO must prepare a multilateral augmentation plan that addresses any shortfall in the state’s police strength. This plan involves negotiations with neighbouring states or union territories to obtain temporary assistance from their police forces, home guards, or other law‑enforcement entities on mutually acceptable terms. Such augmentation may be required when the state experiences large‑scale retirements of senior officers, delayed recruitment, or when the regular force is otherwise insufficient to meet the heightened security demands of the election. The plan must detail the number of officers to be borrowed, the duration of the augmentation, the logistics of transportation and accommodation, and the legal framework governing the temporary deployment.

The SPNO also conducts a daily review and assessment of the law‑and‑order situation across the state. This involves collecting data on incidents such as communal tensions, political rallies, demonstrations, and any reports of intimidation or violence. The officer must ensure that timely and proportionate measures are taken to address these concerns, tailoring the response to the specific characteristics of each district or union territory. For instance, if a particular area has a history of election‑related clashes, the SPNO may order additional patrols, set up temporary police outposts, or coordinate with intelligence agencies to monitor potential flashpoints. The overall aim is to maintain public order while preserving the democratic right of citizens to express their political views peacefully.

Coordination with district-level senior police officials, namely the District Superintendents of Police or the Commissioners of Police, is another essential facet of the SPNO’s role. The SPNO must maintain an open line of communication with these district heads, providing them with guidance on election‑related issues, sharing intelligence, and receiving feedback on ground realities. This two‑way communication ensures that policies formulated at the state level are effectively implemented at the district level, and that any challenges encountered on the ground are promptly escalated to the state apparatus for remedial action. Through regular meetings, written directives, and real‑time updates via secure communication channels, the SPNO and the district officers work together to create a seamless security environment that supports the conduct of free and fair elections.

In sum, the intricate network of responsibilities—from the procurement of communication systems and the issuance of legal notifications, through the detailed planning of force deployment and the management of inter‑state augmentations, to the day‑to‑day monitoring of law‑and‑order conditions—forms the backbone of the state’s preparedness for elections. By adhering to the prescribed timelines, following the procedural safeguards, and maintaining close coordination among the Chief Electoral Officer, the Home Department, the State Police Nodal Officer, and the various auxiliary forces, the state ensures that the electoral process proceeds without disruption, that voters can exercise their franchise in safety, and that the democratic principles enshrined in the Constitution are upheld.

The preparation of training materials and the systematic instruction of every police officer constitute an essential element of the overall strategy for safeguarding the integrity of the electoral process. In this regard, the authorities are required to develop comprehensive curricula that cover not only the legal framework governing elections but also the operational procedures that will be required on the ground during the conduct of the polls. Such curricula must be tailored to the varying levels of responsibility among the police hierarchy, ranging from junior constables who are tasked with crowd control and the inspection of polling stations to senior officers who will supervise the deployment of forces, coordinate with other agencies, and make real‑time decisions in response to emerging threats. The training programmes should therefore combine classroom instruction with practical drills, simulations of potential disturbances, and familiarisation with the specific equipment that will be used, such as communication devices, body‑worn cameras, and non‑lethal crowd‑control tools. By ensuring that every member of the police service is adequately prepared, the likelihood of procedural lapses and procedural breaches is markedly reduced, thereby contributing to a smoother, more transparent election.

In addition to the internal police training, the commission responsible for overseeing the elections has prescribed a series of specific measures designed to preserve law and order throughout the electoral cycle. One of the foremost of these measures involves the systematic monitoring of past electoral offences, a process that requires the collation of historical data on incidents such as voter intimidation, ballot stuffing, and the use of illicit cash or liquor to influence voters. By analysing patterns that emerge from this data, authorities can identify hotspots where electoral malpractice has previously occurred and can allocate resources accordingly. Complementary to this monitoring function are preventive actions and measures, which entail the proactive deployment of personnel to deter potential violations before they materialise. These preventive actions are informed by a technique known as vulnerability mapping, wherein the authorities produce detailed geographic representations of areas that are particularly susceptible to intimidation, undue influence, or other forms of electoral interference. The mapping exercise takes into account factors such as the presence of political strongholds, the proximity of liquor outlets, the density of armed groups, and the historical incidence of violence. Once the vulnerabilities have been identified, corrective measures are put in place to mitigate the risks; for example, additional patrols might be scheduled in a village that has a reputation for being a venue for vote‑buying, or temporary barriers might be erected around a polling station that lies near a contested border.

A further critical responsibility concerns the execution of non‑bailable warrants that have been issued by competent courts. Non‑bailable warrants are legal orders that require the arrest and detention of individuals who are accused of serious offences and for whom the court has determined that bail should not be granted. The timely and efficient execution of these warrants is essential because it demonstrates the commitment of the law‑enforcement agencies to upholding the rule of law and to preventing individuals who may pose a threat to the electoral environment from evading justice. The authorities must therefore establish clear timelines and operational protocols to ensure that every warrant is acted upon within the prescribed period, thereby avoiding any perception of selective enforcement.

Special drives aimed at curbing the circulation of illegal arms, illicit liquor, and other contraband items form another pillar of the comprehensive approach to election security. Such drives typically involve coordinated raids on suspected storage facilities, thorough inspections of transport vehicles, and the use of forensic analysis to trace the origins of prohibited substances. By seizing illegal weapons, the state reduces the likelihood of armed confrontations at polling stations, while the removal of illicit liquor helps to prevent the manipulation of voters through intoxication or the inducement of votes with alcohol. Parallel to these efforts is the need to check the illegal transportation and storage of cash and other inducements that could be used to buy votes. The authorities must monitor financial flows, examine the movement of large sums of cash across district and state lines, and investigate any suspicious deposits that could indicate the financing of corrupt practices.

Beyond the aforementioned specific actions, the commission also calls for the adoption of any additional and enhanced measures that may be required to maintain and improve law and order as the election progresses. This clause provides the flexibility to introduce novel strategies in response to emerging threats, such as the deployment of cyber‑security teams to guard against electronic tampering or the use of community liaison officers to engage with local leaders and pre‑empt unrest. To ensure transparency and accountability, the agencies involved must prepare and provide consolidated reports for the State or Union Territory in the formats designated as LOR‑I and LOR‑II. These reports, which are prescribed by the commission, must be submitted to the Chief Electoral Officer (CEO) for review and subsequently forwarded to the commission. The reporting period begins with the formal announcement of the elections and continues through to the completion of the voting process, thereby furnishing a continuous record of actions taken, challenges encountered, and outcomes achieved.

An integral component of the overall security architecture is the assessment of the requirement for Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) from the Government of India. The CAPF, which includes organizations such as the Central Reserve Police Force (CRPF), the Border Security Force (BSF), and the Indo‑Tibetan Border Police (ITBP), provides a national level of reinforcement that can be called upon when the scale of the security challenge exceeds the capacities of the state police. The assessment process must involve a careful analysis of the size of the electorate, the geographical spread of polling stations, the presence of insurgent or extremist groups, and any historical patterns of violence. Once the need is quantified, a detailed justification must be prepared and communicated to the Chief Electoral Officer, who will in turn forward it to the relevant ministries for approval.

Following the approval, a comprehensive induction and de‑induction plan for the CAPF must be drafted in consultation with the State Level Force Coordinator of the Ministry of Home Affairs. The induction plan outlines the procedures for bringing the central forces into the state, including the logistics of transportation, the provision of temporary accommodation, the arrangement of meals and rations, and the security arrangements that will safeguard the forces while they are on the move or stationed at static locations. The de‑induction plan, by contrast, details the orderly withdrawal of these forces once their mission is completed, ensuring that there is no disruption to local law‑enforcement activities and that the transition back to normal policing is seamless.

The planning of force movements must be phased and meticulously monitored. A phase‑wise movement plan delineates the sequence in which different units will arrive, the routes they will take, the time schedules they must adhere to, and the checkpoints where they will be verified. This systematic approach mitigates the risk of congestion on critical roadways, reduces the chance of ambushes or sabotage, and facilitates the coordination with local authorities who must allocate resources such as police escorts or temporary road closures. In conjunction with the movement plan, a contingency plan must be prepared to address emergencies that could arise during the deployment. Such contingencies include rescue operations for injured personnel, evacuation procedures for personnel stranded due to natural disasters, arrangements for hospitalization, and provisions for air or water transportation in case ground routes become impassable. The development of these plans requires close coordination with a range of departments and agencies, including health services, disaster management authorities, and transport ministries.

A critical logistical element is the creation of a comprehensive database of helipads and airfields within the jurisdiction. This database enables rapid deployment of air assets for reinforcement, medical evacuation, or supply delivery. In tandem with the database, a mechanism for inbound and outbound frisking must be established, ensuring that all personnel, equipment, and cargo are thoroughly screened to prevent the introduction of weapons, explosives, or contraband into secure zones. Close coordination with bordering states is also mandated, particularly for the sealing of inter‑state borders, the conduct of joint operations, and the sharing of intelligence. Such cooperation is vital in regions where electoral disputes or insurgent activity may spill across state lines, necessitating a harmonised response that transcends administrative boundaries.

Communication infrastructure is another pillar of the security framework. The preparation of a communication plan involves augmenting the police wireless system to eliminate “shadow zones,” which are areas where signal coverage is weak or nonexistent. By extending the reach of radio networks, authorities ensure that field personnel can maintain constant contact with command centres, thereby enabling swift response to incidents. The security plan must also cover the protection of critical election infrastructure, such as dispatch centres where ballot boxes are received and sent, receiving centres where votes are counted, training centres where officers are briefed, strong rooms where sensitive documents are stored, and warehouses where election materials are stocked. Each of these sites requires tailored security arrangements, ranging from physical barriers and surveillance cameras to the deployment of dedicated guard units.

Special security provisions must be devised for areas that are classified as Left‑Wing Extremism (LWE) zones, insurgency‑prone regions, or locations that share an international border. In such contexts, the threat profile is markedly different, often involving guerrilla tactics, sabotage, or cross‑border infiltration. Consequently, the security plan for these zones may incorporate additional layers of defence, such as the establishment of forward operating bases, the use of mine‑clearing teams, and the integration of intelligence from specialised agencies. Throughout this extensive preparation, close coordination with police observers—individuals appointed to monitor the conduct of police activities during elections—remains essential to ensure that law‑enforcement actions are transparent, impartial, and in line with democratic principles.

The role and responsibility of the State Force Coordinator, as designated by the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA), is a cornerstone of the entire operation. The coordinator is an officer from one of the Central Armed Police Forces, holding a rank not lower than Inspector General, who is tasked with harmonising, liaising, and coordinating the activities of all CAPF units that may be deployed, including the CRPF, BSF, ITBP, and others. This appointment is made at least six to four months before the commencement of the election cycle, providing ample time for detailed planning. One of the first duties of the State Force Coordinator is the appointment of a nodal or sub‑nodal officer for each district, a point person whose primary function is to facilitate the smooth induction of forces into the district’s operational environment. The coordinator also undertakes advance planning for the accommodation of CAPF personnel, consulting with district administrations to identify suitable lodging facilities, whether in government guest houses, schools repurposed as temporary barracks, or purpose‑built camps. This planning is then compiled at the state level to generate a unified accommodation strategy.

Ensuring a coordinated reception at de‑boarding points is another vital responsibility. As forces arrive by rail or road, the coordinator must work with district authorities to arrange vehicles for transport to the designated lodging sites, thereby preventing bottlenecks and confusion. Pre‑induction training for all incoming CAPF personnel is also mandated; this training familiarises the troops with the local terrain, the specific legal provisions governing elections, the standard operating procedures for crowd management, and the cultural sensitivities of the communities they will serve. In regions where improvised explosive devices (IEDs) pose a threat, the coordinator must identify probable IED‑prone routes and circulate this intelligence to the arriving forces, allowing them to avoid high‑risk corridors or to take appropriate protective measures.

The preparation of route charts for both induction and de‑induction is a further element of the coordinator’s duties. These charts detail the safest and most efficient pathways for the movement of troops, equipment, and supplies, taking into account factors such as road conditions, weather forecasts, and intelligence on potential insurgent activity. To facilitate real‑time coordination, the State Force Coordinator is responsible for establishing a State Control Room, a centralised hub where information from various CAPF units is received, analysed, and disseminated to relevant stakeholders. The control room also serves as the nerve centre for the communication plan, ensuring seamless interaction among the state police, CAPF units, the State Election Commission, hospitals, and other election‑related officials.

Optimal utilisation of the CAPF is pursued through confidence‑building measures, which may include joint patrolling with local police, community engagement programmes, and transparent reporting of activities to the public. The coordinator circulates checklists, do’s and don’ts, election manuals, and other relevant materials to all incoming forces, thereby standardising procedures and reducing the likelihood of misunderstandings. In areas afflicted by Left‑Wing Extremism, the coordinator must also oversee anti‑LWE operations, regular de‑mining activities, and the deployment of foot or motorcycle patrols, which are better suited to the rugged terrain often encountered in such zones. Coordination with the State Police Nodal Officer and the Chief Electoral Officer is essential to ensure that the deployment of CAPF aligns with the broader electoral schedule and that resources are allocated where they are most needed.

The District Election Officer, who simultaneously holds the position of District Magistrate, carries a pivotal set of responsibilities that intersect both administrative and law‑enforcement functions. The officer must secure regular reporting from the police on preventive actions undertaken, such as seizures of cash, illicit liquor, or other contraband, and must also gather comparable information from bordering police ranges in neighbouring states. This information is reviewed and, where necessary, corrective measures are taken in consultation with the Senior Superintendent of Police (SSP) or the Superintendent of Police (SP). The district officer compiles this data into comprehensive submissions that are sent to the Chief Electoral Officer, the Director General of Police, the Home Secretary, and the Chief Secretary, thereby ensuring that higher authorities are apprised of the ground realities.

A further duty of the District Election Officer is the preparation of a proper force deployment plan. While the plan is standardised at the state level to maintain consistency across districts, it must retain sufficient flexibility to accommodate districts or regions with special concerns, such as those with a history of communal tension or with difficult topography. The officer oversees the logistical needs of both the state police and any CAPF units deployed, including the provision of vehicles, accommodation, and essential supplies. In the event of an unexpected deterioration in law and order, the District Election Officer must be prepared to mobilise additional forces from reserve units at short notice, ensuring that any escalation can be contained promptly.

The Superintendent of Police assists the District Magistrate in assessing the need for additional forces, drawing on the deployment pattern prepared at the state level. This assessment involves a detailed examination of the crime statistics, the vulnerability map, and the projected voter turnout for the district. Once the need is quantified, the superintendent arranges the requisite logistical support for the incoming forces, which may involve coordinating with transport departments for the movement of troops, securing temporary housing, and ensuring the availability of food and medical facilities. The superintendent also prepares a detailed deployment plan in collaboration with the District Magistrate, specifying the exact locations where forces will be posted, the rotation schedules, and the command hierarchy that will be employed. Coordination with bordering districts, both within the same state and in adjacent states, is essential to prevent spill‑over effects and to maintain a coherent security posture across administrative boundaries. Finally, the superintendent participates in vulnerability mapping, a process that systematically identifies areas at heightened risk of electoral malpractice, violence, or intimidation, thereby informing the allocation of resources and the design of targeted preventive measures.

The District Election Officer, commonly abbreviated as DEO, works in close cooperation with the Superintendent of Police, or SP, and draws upon additional assistance from other officials whenever the situation requires it. Together, these officials are responsible for a wide range of activities designed to prevent disorder, enforce the Model Code of Conduct, and ensure that elections are conducted in a free and fair manner. From the moment an election is announced, a series of special drives are launched with the aim of identifying individuals who have committed electoral offences in the two most recent elections. These drives involve the careful compilation of a list that names persons who have been found guilty of violating election law, as well as the continual updating of records that include individuals known as history sheeters—people with a long record of criminal activity—along with those who have been declared absconders or are classified as fugitive criminals. In cases where such individuals possess firearms, the drives also involve checking whether any weapons have been deposited by them, and, if necessary, initiating the cancellation of their arms licences in accordance with existing statutes and the directions issued by the Election Commission of India.

In parallel with the compilation of these criminal and electoral offence lists, the DEO and SP are tasked with ensuring that any outstanding legal instruments such as warrants or challans are served promptly. A challan, in this context, refers to a formal charge sheet that initiates criminal proceedings. The officers also keep an up‑to‑date register of all pending warrants so that law‑enforcement agencies can act quickly when a suspect is identified. Speeding up the investigation and prosecution of electoral offences that remain unresolved is another key priority, as delayed justice can undermine public confidence in the electoral process. The special drives extend beyond purely political crimes; they also aim to uncover clandestine liquor distilleries that operate outside the law and to seize illegal weapons and ammunition that might be used to intimidate voters or disrupt polling activities.

To maintain a clear picture of the progress of these drives, the DEO must prepare constituency‑wise updates on a daily basis. These updates contain detailed information on the status of each drive within every electoral constituency and are subsequently forwarded to the Chief Electoral Officer, or CEO, on a weekly schedule. In order to prevent the use of firearms for intimidation, the DEO may issue prohibitory orders under Section 144 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, a legal provision that empowers authorities to issue orders in the interest of public safety. Such orders specifically ban the carrying of licensed arms in designated areas, and they are accompanied by a comprehensive verification of all arms licences as well as inspections of arm‑shops to ensure full compliance with the law.

Another set of directives deals with the use of loudspeakers, a common tool for political campaigning that can become a source of disturbance if not regulated. Immediately after an election is announced, the DEO conducts a systematic review of all individuals and entities that hold firearms licences, and this review is followed by a physical verification of the weapons that are actually present within the district. If any discrepancies are discovered, the authorities may order the deposition of the arms and impose a ban on the issuance of new licences for the duration of the election, a measure that is taken in line with the Election Commission’s guidelines. Vehicles traveling through the district are subject to rigorous scrutiny to ensure that no undesirable persons, illicit arms, or ammunition are being moved from outside the area. When a source of potential trouble is identified, the police may invoke Sections 107, 116, or 109 of the Criminal Procedure Code, which give them the power to take preventive action, seize property, and conduct searches to avert any disturbance of public order.

In order to strengthen the administrative framework for election security, proposals are sent to the State Government requesting the delegation of special powers to executive magistrates, as well as the appointment of such magistrates. These magistrates, often referred to as special executive magistrates, are vested with the authority to take swift action in case of any electoral irregularities. The daily law and order situation in each district is recorded in a standardized format known as LOR‑1. This report is transmitted to the Home Department rather than directly to the Election Commission, ensuring that the state’s internal security apparatus remains informed. The State Nodal Police Officer, who serves as the primary liaison for election‑related policing matters, compiles the LOR‑1 reports received from all districts and presents a consolidated briefing to the Chief Electoral Officer. Throughout the election period, the DEO also monitors expenditure in accordance with the financial guidelines issued for election administration, and takes every necessary measure—both preventive and reactive—to maintain law and order throughout the district.

Beyond these preventive and enforcement actions, a central component of the election‑security strategy is the establishment of an effective single‑window system, sometimes referred to by the Hindi term “Suvidha,” which translates to convenience. The primary purpose of this system is to streamline the process by which political parties and candidates obtain the various permissions they need in order to conduct rallies, campaign events, and other election‑related activities within the limited time frame allocated for campaigning. By consolidating all applications, clearances, and approvals into a single, well‑coordinated channel, the single‑window system reduces bureaucratic delays and helps ensure that the local police have sufficient notice to plan and deploy resources for any planned rally or public meeting. A well‑functioning single‑window mechanism therefore not only facilitates a smoother campaign for the political actors but also makes a substantial contribution to the overall security of the election by allowing law‑enforcement agencies to anticipate and prepare for potential crowd‑control situations.

The single‑window system should not be confined merely to the headquarters of a district; rather, it must also be operational at the level of each Assembly Constituency, which is often abbreviated as AC. To guarantee transparency and accountability, the contact details of every official responsible for processing requests at each level must be published on the official website of the Chief Electoral Officer. In addition, the procedures governing the landing of helicopters—an increasingly common mode of transport for high‑profile political figures—must be clearly defined. The website should also list the names and contact information of the nodal officers who are authorized to grant permission for such landings, thereby providing a clear point of reference for campaign teams and ensuring that any aerial arrivals are coordinated with local security arrangements.

Identifying polling stations that might be vulnerable to disturbance is another critical task that falls under the jurisdiction of the DEO and the police. A polling station is designated as “critical” when it meets one or more of several criteria. First, stations located in areas that have historically shown signs of vulnerability—such as neighborhoods with a high concentration of marginalized communities or places that have experienced prior unrest—are flagged. Second, stations where the voter turnout in the previous election exceeded ninety percent, and where more than seventy‑five percent of the votes were cast for a single candidate, are considered at risk because such overwhelming majorities can sometimes provoke opposition activity or claims of bias. Conversely, stations where voter participation was abnormally low—less than ten percent—may also be deemed critical because unusually low turnout can indicate intimidation, coercion, or logistical failures that could affect the legitimacy of the vote.

Further, any polling station that required a re‑poll in the past five years due to a vitiated poll process—meaning the original voting was compromised—must be scrutinized. Vitiation may result from offences such as “booth capturing,” where an armed group forcibly takes control of a polling station to cast fraudulent votes, or other forms of electoral malpractice. Stations where incidents of violence were reported on polling day, leading to the filing of a First Information Report (FIR) with the police, are also classified as critical. In addition, polling stations that have a higher proportion of absentee, shifted, or deceased voters—collectively referred to by the acronym ASD—than the average for the constituency are flagged for close monitoring. The calculation for this metric involves dividing the total number of ASD voters in a specific polling station by the total number of voters assigned to that station, and then comparing the result to the constituency‑wide average. Any abnormal law‑and‑order situation—such as a surge in criminal activity or communal tension—in a particular area surrounding the polling station also triggers a critical designation. Finally, an election observer, who serves as an independent monitor, is consulted before a final list of critical stations is prepared, ensuring that expert judgment complements statistical analysis.

Once a polling station has been identified as critical, a suite of security measures is put in place to safeguard the voting process. One of the most important of these measures is the deployment of personnel from the Central Armed Police Forces, or CAPF, which includes elite units such as the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, and others. These forces are assigned to protect the critical polling station to the maximum extent possible, providing a visible deterrent against any attempt to disrupt the vote. The list of critical stations is communicated to the commanding officers and assistant commanding officers of the CAPF, enabling them to monitor these locations closely and to coordinate any additional reinforcement if the situation warrants it.

In addition to armed security, civil‑level monitoring technologies are employed to increase transparency and to create a record of events that can be reviewed if any dispute arises. Webcasting, which involves live video streaming of the polling station, and videography are installed in each critical station, as well as in polling stations situated in vulnerable areas. The policy stipulates that webcasting must be carried out in all critical stations, and that at least half of the total number of polling stations—including any auxiliary stations set up to accommodate overflow voters—must be equipped with live streaming capabilities, with the higher of the two numbers determining the final coverage target. The purpose of these visual monitoring systems is twofold: they act as a deterrent to potential misbehavior by providing real‑time oversight, and they create an immutable audiovisual record that can be examined in the event of allegations of irregularities. Any significant incident occurring in or around a polling station—such as a clash between supporters, attempts to tamper with voting equipment, or the presence of unauthorized individuals—is also recorded by these cameras to ensure that the full context is captured.

Taken together, the combination of preventive legal actions, rigorous verification of arms licences, systematic tracking of warrants, swift prosecution of electoral offences, the establishment of a streamlined single‑window system, and the deployment of both armed forces and advanced monitoring technologies at critical polling stations forms a comprehensive framework designed to protect the integrity of the electoral process. By integrating these varied components—each grounded in statutory authority, operational guidelines, and practical experience—the District Election Officer, the Superintendent of Police, and their supporting teams work in concert to create an environment where voters can exercise their franchise without fear, and where the outcome of the election reflects the genuine will of the people. This layered approach to election security reflects a deep understanding of the multiple dimensions—legal, administrative, logistical, and technological—that must be addressed in order to uphold democratic principles in a complex and diverse society.

A Micro Observer may be positioned inside a polling station that has been identified as critical, serving as an additional civil safeguard designed to strengthen the overall monitoring of the electoral process. The presence of such an observer is intended to provide a non‑partisan, on‑the‑ground perspective that can verify that the procedures at the voting location are being carried out in strict accordance with the law and that any irregularities are promptly reported. In a similar vein, the Presiding Officer who is in charge of a critical polling station must receive a specific and thorough briefing before the polling day. This briefing is meant to ensure that the officer is fully aware of the requirement to verify any EPIC (Electors Photo Identity Card) or any other identification document that has been approved by the Election Commission of India, and to record the outcome of that verification in the “Remarks” column of Form 17A, the official register that logs the identities of voters who have cast their ballots. The notation in the remarks column serves as an audit trail, confirming that each voter’s identity has been checked against the official record and that any discrepancies have been duly noted.

In order to anticipate and mitigate the risk of intimidation directed at voters, it is necessary to conduct a systematic mapping of vulnerability throughout the constituency. This mapping exercise begins with the sector officers, who are senior officials assigned to particular geographic zones within the electoral area. These officers must travel to the locations that have been identified as potentially vulnerable, accompanied by a police officer, to assess the situation on the ground. Their assessment should be carried out in consultation with local administrative officers, whose knowledge of community dynamics and past incidents can help refine the list of areas that require special attention. In addition to the inputs from the administrators, the list of “worry” areas compiled by the candidates—often referred to as the candidates’ “Worry List”—must also be taken into account, because political participants frequently have detailed awareness of hotspots where intimidation or undue influence might be attempted.

The first step of the vulnerability mapping is to identify the source of any potential threat or intimidation. This involves naming individuals or groups who are likely to lead efforts to exert undue influence over voters, based on both past incidents of electoral misconduct and current apprehensions expressed by the community. Once the potential perpetrators have been identified, the mapping must also pinpoint the contact points within the habitats or communities that could be used to spread intimidation.
