The result of this work is a comprehensive, polling‑station‑wise map of vulnerability for the entire constituency, which is recorded in a format that is annexed to the operational guidelines.

Preventive measures are then formulated jointly by the District Election Officer (DEO) and the Senior Police (SP). These measures include confidence‑building activities that aim to assure voters that the poll will be free and fair. Such activities may involve frequent visits by senior officials and Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) to the identified vulnerable locations, thereby demonstrating a visible security presence. Regular feedback on the security situation must be provided by district intelligence agencies, and the DEO or the Returning Officer (RO) should maintain a dialogue with the candidates and political parties to keep them informed of the steps being taken. When an observer arrives at a polling station, he must be furnished with the details of the vulnerability mapping for that specific station, allowing him to focus his monitoring efforts where they are most needed. The DEO and SP are required to develop an action plan that outlines how they will address any potential threats, drawing upon the powers granted under sections 107, 116, and 151 of the Code of Criminal Procedure (CRPC). These sections empower the authorities to compel alleged trouble‑makers to appear at a police station, to establish police pickets at strategic points, and to conduct regular patrols and visits by officers.

On polling day, special attention must be paid to any sections of the electorate that appear conspicuously absent from the voting process. In such cases, a specially designated squad is dispatched to investigate whether the absence is the result of intimidation, logistical obstacles, or other hindrances, and to ensure that no voter is prevented from exercising his or her franchise. At the conclusion of the poll, the senior officer responsible for the area (the Senior Officer, or SO) must submit a special report to the Returning Officer detailing whether voters from the identified vulnerable habitats were able to cast their votes without obstruction. Political parties must also be briefed on the locations that have been classified as vulnerable, with these sections clearly marked on electoral maps of the area. The Central Armed Police Forces are supplied with a list of such vulnerable locations and are instructed to make routine visits as a means of building confidence among the electorate. If the Election Commission of India (ECI) issues a specific order, the observer may be required to read aloud the entries in Form 17A and to examine the marked copy of the electoral roll that was used at the polling station, thereby providing an additional layer of verification.

The preparation and implementation of a District Security Deployment Plan is another essential component of the overall security strategy. Full and optimal utilization of the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPFs) and State Police Forces (SPFs) must be ensured, meaning that all available personnel and resources are deployed in a manner that maximizes coverage and effectiveness. The District Deployment Plan is to be drafted jointly by the DEO and the Senior Police officer, after which it is examined and approved by the most senior General officer and the most senior Police observer, both of whom bring extensive experience in managing large‑scale security operations. In addition to the district‑level plan, a more detailed local deployment plan must be prepared, which aligns with the broader state‑wide plan but incorporates micro‑level details that reflect the specific conditions and requirements of each locality. No deviations from the approved plan are permitted unless explicit, written permission is obtained from the most senior General officer or Police observer, thereby preserving the integrity of the operational blueprint.

Observers are to receive a draft version of the force deployment plan upon their arrival, allowing them to familiarize themselves with the intended distribution of security personnel. The guidelines stipulate that the CAPF must not be deployed in any critical polling booth with fewer than half of a section’s strength in ordinary areas; a section generally comprises thirty to fifty soldiers, so a half‑section would still be a substantial presence. In regions affected by Naxalite activity or other forms of civil disturbance, the minimum strength at a critical polling booth must not fall below a full section, ensuring a robust deterrent against any attempts at violent disruption. The deployment plan must be illustrated with a sketch map for each sector, clearly identifying vulnerable pockets and the polling stations that are deemed critical. Security provision for contesting candidates should be based solely on a thorough threat assessment rather than being provided as a routine measure, thereby preventing unnecessary escalation and preserving the principle of a level playing field. Observers themselves must also receive appropriate security, commensurate with the risk profile of their assignment.

A three‑tier cordoning system is to be established in counting centers, creating successive layers of security that separate the ballot‑handling areas from public access zones, thereby safeguarding the integrity of the counting process. Inter‑state and inter‑district borders must be sealed well in advance of the poll, preventing the movement of illicit materials or individuals who might seek to interfere with the election. After the poll, the CAPF will be tasked with guarding the strong rooms—secure storage areas where ballot papers and election equipment are kept—until the final declaration of results. The order of deployment for forces at polling stations is to follow a hierarchy of sensitivity, beginning with the Central Armed Police Forces, followed by State Armed Police (SAP), and finally District Armed Police (DAP). Additional measures, known as force multipliers, such as mobile units, rapid response teams, and specialized surveillance equipment, must be allocated to the most critical polling stations to enhance their security posture. Logistical arrangements—including accommodation, transport, food, and medical support—must be meticulously organized for all security forces to ensure that they can operate effectively throughout the election period.

Training and briefing of every single personnel member are mandatory, guaranteeing that 100 percent of the deployed staff are aware of their duties, the legal framework governing elections, and the procedures for handling any incidents that may arise. In advance of the election, day‑to‑day plans must be prepared that outline how the central forces will be utilized for area domination from the moment they arrive on the ground. These schedules specify which neighborhoods will be covered on each day, thereby ensuring systematic coverage and minimizing gaps in security. The district deployment plans must be fully implemented without deviation, providing a coherent and coordinated security environment across the entire constituency.

Finally, a special management plan for the last forty‑eight hours before the polls close is required. The campaign period officially ends forty‑eight hours prior to the closing time of the polls, and this interval demands an intensified presence of security forces as a visible show of strength. The purpose of this heightened presence is twofold: first, it serves to deter illegal activities such as voter intimidation, bribery, the purchase of votes, or other forms of electoral malpractice; second, it acts as an important confidence‑building measure, reassuring voters that the election will be conducted fairly and without coercion. During this critical window, both central and state police forces are deployed jointly, combining their resources and expertise to create a seamless security network that covers the entire electoral landscape. This joint deployment ensures that any last‑minute attempts to disrupt the voting process are swiftly identified and neutralized, and that the electorate can approach the polling stations with confidence that their right to vote will be respected and protected.

The effective conduct of an election in any district is not merely a matter of setting up voting booths and printing ballot papers; it is a complex, multi‑layered operation that demands meticulous planning, constant vigilance, and the coordinated effort of a wide range of public officials. At the heart of this operation are the district authorities, whose responsibilities extend far beyond the simple administrative tasks of logistics. In order to guarantee that each polling station area—commonly referred to as a “PS area”—remains free from intimidation, coercion, or any form of undue influence over voters, these authorities must engage in a sustained process of strategic thinking and practical application. This means that the district administration must be alert to any attempts by local power brokers, criminal elements, or partisan groups to sway the electorate by threats or inducements, and must intervene swiftly and decisively to neutralize such attempts. The goal is to create a level playing field in which every citizen can express a genuine choice without fear of retaliation or the perception that the outcome is being predetermined. The notion of a “level playing field” in this context refers to an environment where no candidate or party enjoys an unfair advantage because of the use of state resources, intimidation tactics, or manipulation of the voting process. The district officials must therefore monitor the political climate closely, coordinate with law‑enforcement agencies, and ensure that the mechanisms of the election are transparent, impartial, and secure.

One of the most critical components of the overall election management framework is the preparation of a special, time‑sensitive operational document that is commonly known as the “Last 48‑Hours Management Plan.” This plan is not a generic checklist but a detailed, action‑oriented guide that is activated in the final two days before the polls open. Its purpose is to delineate the precise duties of all essential election functionaries, ranging from senior district officers to the lowest‑ranking police constable who is stationed at a polling station. The plan must also spell out the responsibilities of the security forces at every level—state police, district police, and any auxiliary or paramilitary units that have been called upon to provide additional support. Within the same document, there must be a clear set of do‑and‑don’t directives that are tailored for each stakeholder group, ensuring that everyone knows exactly what actions are permissible and which behaviors are prohibited. The plan should also outline the methods for publicizing its contents, so that the electorate and civil‑society observers are aware of the measures being taken to safeguard the election, thereby enhancing confidence in the process. Furthermore, the plan must incorporate a rapid‑response mechanism for the handling of complaints, grievances, or incidents that may arise in the critical final hours. This mechanism should specify the channels through which reports can be lodged, the timeline for investigation, and the escalation procedures for serious breaches. By providing a comprehensive, pre‑emptive blueprint, the Last 48‑Hours Management Plan acts as a safeguard against last‑minute disruptions and helps maintain the smooth flow of the electoral process.

The responsibility for drafting this indispensable plan does not rest on a single individual. Rather, it is a collaborative effort that must involve the District Magistrate—commonly abbreviated as DM—and the Superintendent of Police, known as the SP. The DM is the chief administrative officer of the district, tasked with overseeing the execution of government policies and ensuring that law and order are maintained. The SP, on the other hand, is the senior police officer responsible for all policing matters within the district, including the deployment of personnel and the coordination of security operations. By working together, the DM and the SP combine their expertise in civil administration and law enforcement to produce a plan that is both legally sound and practically enforceable. Their joint preparation ensures that the plan reflects a balanced perspective, integrating the administrative imperatives of the district with the operational realities of maintaining public safety during a high‑stakes democratic exercise.

Within the broader hierarchy of law‑enforcement officials, the Sub‑Divisional Officer (Police), often referred to as the SDPO, and the Thana Level Officer, known as the Station House Officer or SHO, play pivotal roles in both the maintenance of general law and order and in the specific duties related to the conduct of polls. Their responsibilities are multifaceted and demand a proactive approach. In terms of general law and order, the SDPO and SHO must first compile comprehensive lists of individuals who have been identified, on a thana‑by‑thana basis, as having participated in subversive activities during previous elections. Subversive activities might include acts such as voter intimidation, ballot box theft, or organized disruption of the polling process. Once these individuals are identified, the officers are required to order preventive actions against them, which may involve close monitoring, temporary detention, or other lawful measures designed to neutralize any potential threat they pose. In parallel, the officers must keep updated records of known anti‑social elements and criminals, again organized by each police station or “thana,” and direct preventive action against these persons to ensure that they do not exploit the election period to further illegal activities.

Another essential duty is the diligent follow‑up on arrest warrants that have been issued against individuals who have fled or are otherwise in hiding, referred to as “absconders.” The SDPO and SHO must review the status of these warrants, assess why any delays might have occurred, and take decisive steps to accelerate the process, thereby preventing absconders from influencing the election environment. Intelligence gathering forms a further cornerstone of their responsibilities. The officers must actively seek out information on illegal manufacturing or transport of firearms, ammunition, and illicit liquor—activities that can be used to fund or fuel electoral violence. Upon acquiring such intelligence, the officers are mandated to take swift, effective action, which could range from raids and seizures to the initiation of criminal prosecutions. In order to curb the proliferation of weapons during the sensitive electoral period, the officers are also required to conduct systematic checks of all existing arms licences. Should any licence holder be deemed a risk, the officers must recommend to the SP, who in turn advises the DM, that the licence be suspended for the duration of the election.

The legal framework governing elections includes provisions for the enforcement of Section 144 of the Criminal Procedure Code, commonly abbreviated as “S.144 CrPC.” This section empowers a magistrate to issue an order prohibiting the assembly of people in a specific area for reasons of public safety. Whenever such an order is promulgated during the election, the SDPO and SHO must ensure its strict enforcement, thereby preventing large gatherings that could be used for intimidation or violence. Coordination does not stop at the district boundary; the officers must also maintain communication channels with their counterparts in neighboring or border states. This inter‑state coordination is vital for checking the illegal movement of money, arms, liquor, and other contraband that could be used to destabilize the election process. In addition, the officers must enforce statutes that protect private property and regulate the use of loudspeakers, which are often employed for campaign propaganda but can become sources of disturbance if not properly controlled.

On the ground, the police must conduct intensive patrolling and checks in public spaces that naturally attract crowds, such as bus stands, railway stations, hotels, lodges, and weekly markets. These patrols serve both as a deterrent to potential wrongdoers and as a means of rapid response should any incident arise. Moreover, the officers are required to submit daily law‑and‑order reports from the thana level to the SP, ensuring that the senior police leadership remains fully apprised of the evolving security landscape throughout the election period.

Beyond these general duties, the officers have a suite of specific poll‑related responsibilities that must be fulfilled in close coordination with the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate (SDM). First among these is the finalization of a list of critical polling stations and vulnerable hamlets—small settlements that have historically been prone to electoral disturbances. This list, created after consulting the SDM, is then submitted to the SP for further action. The officers must also evaluate the number and type of security forces required at each polling station, determining the appropriate mix of static guard posts and mobile patrol units needed to safeguard the voting process and to maintain overall public order. When forces are drawn from outside the district or even from other states, the officers are tasked with arranging their logistical support, which includes accommodation, transport, food, and medical facilities, ensuring that these external contingents can operate effectively without undue hardship.

A central component of the election infrastructure is the establishment of a fully equipped control room. This facility serves as the nerve centre for real‑time monitoring of the election day, allowing the police to receive updates from field units, dispatch resources where needed, and coordinate responses to any emerging incidents. In conjunction with the control room, the officers must enforce the Model Code of Conduct, a set of guidelines issued by the election commission that governs the behaviour of political parties and candidates during the election period. The Model Code of Conduct seeks to create a fair environment by restricting activities such as the use of government resources for campaign purposes, the distribution of inducements, and inflammatory rhetoric. To ensure uniform understanding, the SDPO and the SDM must jointly brief all subordinate officers on the code’s provisions, emphasizing the legal consequences of violations. Training sessions for police personnel and the subordinate police officers (SPOs) stationed at the thana level are also required, focusing on the specific duties they will perform on election day, the handling of electoral offenses, and the protocols for interacting with voters and candidates.

Physical verification of polling stations is another vital task. The officers must personally inspect each polling station to confirm that it meets the required standards for safety, accessibility, and logistical readiness. While a full verification of every station is ideal, the guidelines stipulate that at least thirty percent of the polling stations must undergo a thorough physical check, ensuring a representative sample of the entire electoral landscape is scrutinized. In addition, the officers must work with the SDM to chart safe and efficient routes for the transportation of ballot boxes and election personnel to each polling station, securing these routes against potential threats. They must also develop detailed local‑level plans for patrolling, setting up fixed pickets, and establishing check points that will deter any unauthorized movement of people or materials that could disrupt the voting process.

In regions that experience left‑wing extremism, insurgency, or militancy, the officers have an added layer of responsibility. They must gather intelligence specific to these volatile environments, assessing the likelihood of attacks or sabotage. Any operational plan designed for such high‑risk areas must first obtain approval from the SP, ensuring that the strategy aligns with broader district security policies. Security arrangements for the candidates themselves also fall under the purview of the police. The officers must evaluate any threats faced by individual candidates, coordinate with their personal security details, and, where necessary, provide additional protection. This must be communicated to the SP in a timely manner so that appropriate resources can be allocated.

Overall, the intricate web of duties assigned to the Sub‑Divisional Officer (Police) and the Station House Officer reflects the comprehensive nature of election management in a democratic system. Their work spans the maintenance of everyday law and order, the prevention of electoral malpractices, the enforcement of legal provisions, and the proactive safeguarding of both the electorate and the candidates. By executing these responsibilities with diligence, coordination, and a clear understanding of the legal and procedural framework, they help ensure that elections are conducted in a free, fair, and peaceful manner, thereby upholding the fundamental principles of democratic governance.

The process of sealing a state’s land frontiers, whether they are domestic borders shared with neighboring states or the seaward limits that touch the Arabian Sea, the Bay of Bengal or the Indian Ocean, is a complex and coordinated activity that is undertaken as part of the overall strategy to preserve law and order during the election cycle. The first step in this undertaking is the convening of border meetings, which are formal gatherings of officials from each of the states that share a common boundary. These meetings are not merely ceremonial; they are designed to establish a systematic approach for the physical sealing of interstate land borders, to review any existing arrangements for sealing international frontiers, and to assess the adequacy of coastal security in those states that possess a coastline along the major maritime zones of the Indian subcontinent. In practice, the state’s Nodal Officer for Law and Order, who is the senior official charged with the overall maintenance of public peace, initiates the border meetings and ensures that they are conducted at two distinct levels of administration: a higher level at the state capital where senior policymakers and senior police officials deliberate, and a more operational level at the district headquarters where the day‑to‑day implementation is planned and monitored. The expectation placed upon each state that is directly involved in these border interactions is that it must render complete cooperation in sealing the border before any election is called, because an open or poorly monitored boundary can become a conduit for the movement of individuals and contraband that could jeopardise the fairness of the poll.

In order to make the sealing effective, the authorities must carry out stringent checks at the interstate Naka, which are the official crossing points where roads, railways or highways intersect state boundaries. At these points, the focus of inspection is threefold: first, to prevent anti‑social persons—individuals with a history of criminal activity or disruptive behavior—from entering the election‑going state; second, to stop the smuggling of alcoholic beverages, which is a major source of illicit revenue and a potential source of disorder; and third, to intercept illegal firearms, which could be used to intimidate voters or political candidates. The Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF), which include the Border Security Force, the Central Reserve Police Force and other centrally administered police agencies, may assign a senior commander to take part in these interactions, thereby bringing additional manpower and expertise to the border checks. Beyond the immediate border, the states must also work to neutralise the major suppliers of liquor and narcotics that are targeting the election‑going states. This is a nuanced task because sometimes the states that serve as transit points are not themselves the source of the contraband; for instance, a state that does not share a direct border with the poll‑hosting state may nevertheless be the origin of a drug trafficking route that passes through a neighboring “border” state. Consequently, the cooperation required extends to tracking the supply chain back to its source and disrupting it at every stage.

Control over the manufacture and distribution of illegal arms is another critical component of the border‑sealing agenda. The states are required to prevent the production of unauthorised weapons and to intercept the flow of such arms from bordering states into the poll‑going state. If circumstances demand, the border states must also assist in the arrest of individuals who have absconded or who have a history of criminal offences, particularly those who are wanted for election‑related violations in the state that is holding the election. This cooperation includes tracking and apprehending “muscle power” operatives—men who are hired for intimidation or violence—who may be recruited from one state and transported to another to influence the outcome of the vote. The border states, therefore, have a duty to monitor the movement of people with a criminal background who might be hired as hired thugs, and to intervene if they attempt to cross into the poll‑going state. Likewise, individuals wanted for offences under the liquor prohibition laws, if they manage to slip into a bordering state, must be arrested by the authorities of that state. In order to respond swiftly to any emergent situation, each bordering police station must be equipped with dedicated forces that can be deployed at short notice. The Excise Department, which is responsible for regulating the production and distribution of alcoholic beverages, must maintain a strict vigil over distilleries located near the border to ensure that no illegal proliferation of liquor occurs that could affect the election. Modern surveillance technology also plays a role; closed‑circuit television cameras installed on National Highways and State Highways at the Naka points provide continuous visual monitoring, helping to identify and deter illegal activity.

To guarantee that the entire system of border sealing and inter‑state cooperation functions smoothly, a separate but closely linked framework of inter‑state coordination is established. At the highest level, the senior officials of each bordering state—the Chief Secretary, who is the top civil servant; the Home Secretary, who oversees internal security; and the Director General of Police, the head of the state police—must meet to plan the pre‑poll, poll‑day and post‑poll phases. These meetings focus on a range of issues such as the deployment pattern of security personnel in the border areas, preventive actions against potential disturbances, and common concerns that include the regulation of the inter‑state movement of cash, liquor and other items that could be used to influence voters. To keep the coordination dynamic and responsive, each state must designate a nodal officer for law and order who holds a rank no lower than that of an Inspector General of Police. These officers are required to maintain daily contact with their counterparts in the neighbouring state, sharing intelligence, reviewing the effectiveness of checkpoints, and discussing any incidents that could affect law and order. At the district level, a similar pattern of interaction is replicated: the senior officers of the bordering districts, including the Deputy Inspector General of Police, the District Magistrate and the Senior Superintendent of Police, hold regular meetings to plan field‑level operations and coordinate resources. The district administration appoints its own nodal officers—often the Additional District Magistrate who also serves as the Law and Order Nodal Officer, together with an Additional Superintendent of Police—who are tasked with ensuring that the coordination mechanisms operate without delay or confusion.

The successful deployment of security forces for election security also depends on careful planning of resources and budgeting, a process that must begin well before the announcement of the poll dates. The Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA) at the central level is responsible for conducting advance coordination meetings with the state administrations to evaluate the number of companies—military‑style units—of Central Armed Police Forces that will be required based on the perceived threat level in the area slated for deployment. Representatives from the various intelligence agencies, such as the Research and Analysis Wing and the Intelligence Bureau, are also brought into these deliberations so that the assessment of force requirements is grounded in a realistic appraisal of the security environment. Once a preliminary estimate is agreed upon, a further coordination meeting is held between the MHA, the CAPF leadership and the state officials to decide the exact composition of the forces, taking into account the existing availability of companies within the central forces and ensuring that their primary duties—such as guarding critical infrastructure and responding to insurgency threats—are not unduly compromised.

Before any election can be held, the central forces must review their own inventory of resources and equipment, and plan for the necessary accommodation and logistical support that will be needed to sustain the deployment. This review includes confirming the availability of manpower, the stock of arms and ammunition, the appropriate uniforms and related gear suited to the climate and terrain of the deployment zone, communication devices that will allow secure and reliable contact between units, and sufficient transport assets such as vehicles and fuel. The forces must also arrange for mess facilities to feed the personnel, basic medical supplies and first‑aid equipment, and protective gear such as bullet‑proof jackets for those who may be operating in areas that have a history of militancy or insurgency. On the state side, the Director General of Police is expected to initiate an early and thorough assessment of the budgetary requirements for both the state police and any central forces that will be called in. This financial planning must be comprehensive, covering everything from the cost of extra uniforms to the fuel needed for additional vehicles, and must be completed well before the election commission announces the date of the poll. By undertaking these steps—coordinated border sealing, inter‑state intelligence sharing, and meticulous resource planning—the authorities aim to create an environment in which the electoral process can proceed without the undue influence of illicit arms, smuggled liquor, illegal cash flows, or organized criminal elements, thereby safeguarding the integrity of the democratic exercise.

The responsibility for ensuring that the police forces of a state are adequately staffed and equipped for the conduct of an election rests primarily with the Home Department of the state government, the Chief Electoral Officer (often abbreviated as CEO) and the Director General of Police (DGP). These three officials must first carry out a systematic assessment of the existing manpower available within the state police establishment. This assessment is not a simple head‑count; it involves a detailed review of the number of constables, inspectors, senior officers, specialized units, and any reserve forces that may be called upon. Once the internal capacity has been measured, the Home Department, together with the CEO and the DGP, must formulate a careful projection of how many additional personnel are needed from the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF). The term CAPF refers to the eight paramilitary forces that are under the administrative control of the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA) and include such organisations as the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, Sashastra Seema Bal, and others. The projected requirement for these central forces is then communicated to the Election Commission of India (ECI), which acts as the central authority for the conduct of elections, and not directly to the Ministry of Home Affairs. In other words, under no circumstances should the state officials correspond directly with the MHA regarding the demand for central forces; the channel of communication must be routed through the ECI to preserve the procedural integrity of the election management system.

At the same time, the state must prepare a comprehensive budgetary estimate that captures all logistical and ancillary requirements of the security forces that will be deployed for the election. This budgetary exercise must be undertaken in consultation with a wide range of agencies that have a stake in election security, ranging from the district level officials who will be responsible for on‑the‑ground coordination, to the finance department that will allocate the necessary funds, and the various technical departments that manage equipment and transportation. The budget must cover not only the direct costs of salaries and allowances for the police and central forces, but also the costs of arms and ammunition, uniforms, communication devices, vehicles, temporary accommodation, mess facilities, and any other material support that is needed to sustain a large contingent of security personnel for the duration of the electoral process.

A key element of the planning process is the coordination meeting that must be convened by the Home Department and the DGP together with the District Administration. This meeting must be held at least four months before the scheduled election date, providing sufficient lead time for a thorough analysis of the local situation. During this meeting, the officials must assess the current availability of police personnel in the district, as well as the anticipated requirement for central forces based on the perceived level of threat in the area where the forces will be deployed. Threat perception is a technical term that refers to an assessment of the likelihood and potential impact of violent incidents, disturbances, or any form of law‑and‑order challenges that could arise during the election. This assessment should be informed by intelligence inputs, historical patterns of election‑related violence, and any specific intelligence alerts that have been received in the run‑up to the election.

The meeting must also include representatives from both the state and central intelligence agencies, such as the state’s Director of Intelligence, the central Intelligence Bureau, and other agencies that have a mandate to gather and analyse security‑related information. In addition, the Additional Director Generals of Police (ADGPs) who head the various armed units of the state police must be present, as they bring expertise on the operational capabilities and limitations of the forces under their command. By bringing together this broad spectrum of stakeholders, the deliberations can arrive at a realistic estimate of the number of central force companies – a ‘company’ being a sub‑unit of a central force, typically consisting of about one hundred personnel – that will be needed to maintain law and order, protect polling stations, and ensure the safety of voters and election officials.

After this multi‑agency discussion, the state must move to the next step of consulting the Chief Electoral Officer. The CEO, who is the senior-most election official in the state, is required to gather inputs from the District Electoral Officers (DEOs) – the officers who oversee election operations at the district level – and incorporate these inputs into a final demand letter. This demand letter must clearly articulate the number of central force companies that the state believes are necessary, and it must provide a justification for each request. The justification may include references to specific threat assessments, the size and geography of the polling area, the expected voter turnout, and any special circumstances such as the presence of large public gatherings or ongoing civil unrest.

Once the CEO has compiled the demand, the state projects its request through the Election Commission of India to the Ministry of Home Affairs. This projection is not merely a numerical request; it must be accompanied by a detailed explanatory memorandum that outlines why each particular central force has been requested, what role it is expected to play, and how the force will be deployed in the field. The ECI then organizes a coordination meeting that brings together representatives of the Ministry of Home Affairs, the central forces themselves, and the state administration. This meeting serves as a precursor to the final decision on how many central force companies will actually be deployed, taking into account the availability of those companies within the central forces, the operational commitments of those forces elsewhere in the country, and the need to preserve their primary roles, which may include border security, anti‑terrorist operations, or other national duties.

To ensure that all of these steps are followed in a timely and orderly fashion, the state must designate a State Level Force Coordinator. This officer is charged with tracking the progress of each element of the planning and procurement process, ensuring that the timelines are met, that the budgetary allocations are correctly made, and that the coordination meetings are held as prescribed. The coordinator must also act as a liaison between the various departments and agencies, facilitating the flow of information and resolving any inter‑departmental issues that arise.

In the period leading up to the election, the state government is required to conduct a thorough review of the availability of a wide range of resources. This review includes a check on the stock of arms and ammunition that will be needed by both the state police and the central forces, ensuring that there are sufficient quantities of service rifles, pistols, ammunition cartridges, and any special weapons that might be required for crowd control, such as non‑lethal launchers. The review also extends to uniforms and uniform items, which must be in good condition, properly sized, and available in sufficient numbers to clothe all personnel who will be on duty. Communication equipment, such as two‑way radios, satellite phones, and mobile data terminals, must be inspected and tested to guarantee reliable contact between field units, command posts, and control rooms.

Vehicles form another critical component of election logistics. The state must verify that there are enough well‑maintained vehicles, including patrol cars, vans, and larger transport trucks, to move personnel and equipment to and from polling stations across the state. In addition, the vehicles must be equipped with appropriate safety features, such as run‑flat tires, communication kits, and, where required, armor plating. The mapping of police stations (commonly abbreviated as PS) must be updated to reflect the current jurisdictional boundaries, road networks, and route maps that will be used by the forces to navigate during the election period. Proper mapping ensures that each police station knows its area of responsibility and can coordinate effectively with neighbouring stations.

Control rooms, which serve as the nerve centres for real‑time monitoring of election‑related activities, must be fully equipped with large display screens, video feeds from CCTV cameras installed at polling stations, and secure communication lines. These control rooms should have backup power supplies, redundant internet connections, and sufficient staffing to analyse data and respond to incidents as they occur. The budgeting process must allocate funds not only for the procurement of new equipment but also for the maintenance and operational costs associated with these resources. Districts must receive the necessary budgetary allocations to cover both the personnel expenses for state police officers and the expenses incurred by the central forces stationed in the district.

Accommodation and related amenities for the security forces must meet the standards set by the Election Commission of India. This includes provision of adequate tents or barracks for the central force companies, with considerations for sanitary facilities, mess arrangements, and recreational spaces. The accommodation must be designed to house a full company, typically about a hundred personnel, and must include separate spaces for officers, non‑commissioned officers, and rank‑and‑file troops. The accommodation plans should also account for special categories of personnel, such as women troops, by providing dedicated toilets and private changing areas for every five or six female personnel to ensure comfort and safety.

The mess facilities must be capable of preparing and serving meals to large numbers of troops, with a reliable supply chain for ration items such as rice, wheat, pulses, cooking oil, and fresh produce. On polling day, the state must arrange for packed lunches or a system of remuneration for meals for both state police and central forces, ensuring that all personnel remain nourished and can perform their duties without interruption.

Logistic planning for the election involves several distinct phases, beginning with procurement. The procurement function is tasked with acquiring all the material goods that are necessary for the successful deployment of security forces. This includes communication equipment such as radios and signal jammers, which are used to prevent the misuse of mobile phones for illicit coordination during the election. In areas where the risk of land‑mine or improvised explosive device (IED) attacks is higher, the state may need to procure mine‑protected vehicles for the central forces, which are specially designed to withstand blasts. Uniforms must be ordered in the required sizes, and any specialized gear such as helmets, body‑armor vests, and riot shields must be procured in accordance with the standards set by the ECI.

Accommodation logistics for the central force companies extend beyond merely providing a place to sleep. The state must arrange for kitchen facilities, dining halls, and sanitation facilities that can handle the daily needs of the troops. For instance, a standard company may require a barrack that can accommodate eighteen personnel per unit, a room set aside for an officer with attached basic facilities, a kitchen and dining hall equipped with basic amenities, storage rooms for equipment and supplies, and a certain number of bathrooms and toilets. In normal circumstances, a company would need six such barracks, one officer’s room, one kitchen, three storage rooms, and ten bathrooms. During periods of heightened health concerns, such as a pandemic, the accommodation requirements increase, demanding additional barracks, extra bathrooms, and enhanced sanitation facilities to ensure compliance with public health guidelines.

Transportation planning is another essential component. When central forces are brought in from other states, the responsibility for arranging rail transport lies with the central forces in coordination with the Ministry of Railways, while the state government is responsible for the subsequent movement of these forces from the railhead to the final deployment sites. For interstate and intrastate movements of the central forces, the state government must arrange road transport, which includes scheduling buses, trucks, and other vehicles to move personnel and equipment to the designated locations. Similarly, the state government must handle the transportation of its own police personnel, special armed police (SAP), and any other law‑enforcement units that will be deployed within the state for the duration of the election. This includes transportation for activities such as monitoring Model Code of Conduct compliance, supervising election officials, providing security for political campaigns and rallies, and maintaining general law and order. All transportation arrangements must be made well in advance, with vehicles inspected for roadworthiness, fuelled, and equipped with necessary safety gear.

When planning the deployment of central force companies, the state administration must guarantee that there is adequate accommodation and transport from the point where the troops disembark to the sites where they will be stationed. The accommodation requirement for a single company of a central force is detailed: during normal periods, the company would need six standard barracks, each capable of housing eighteen personnel, a single officer’s room, a kitchen and dining hall, three storage rooms, and ten bathroom facilities. In a health‑crisis scenario, such as a pandemic, these numbers increase to ten barracks, additional officer accommodations, more kitchen and dining facilities, more storage space, and fifteen bathrooms. For the tactical headquarters (TAC) of the company, the requirements include three officer rooms, one additional barrack, three storage rooms, a kitchen and dining hall, six bathrooms, and six toilets. For female troops, special provisions must be made: separate toilets for every five or six women and private changing spaces must be earmarked to respect privacy and comfort.

The maintenance aspect of election logistics ensures that all the equipment, vehicles, and facilities remain functional throughout the election period. A systematic maintenance schedule must be established, covering regular checks of arms, ammunition, communication devices, vehicles, and accommodation structures. Preventive maintenance is essential to avoid breakdowns that could jeopardise security operations. All equipment must be inspected and tested well before the election date, and any defects must be rectified promptly. The state must also ensure that the procurement of vehicles and other assets complies with the guidelines issued by the Election Commission, which may stipulate specifications regarding vehicle type, capacity, and safety features.

Finally, the budgeting process must be transparent and comprehensive. The state must allocate funds for the procurement of new items, the maintenance of existing assets, the payment of salaries and allowances to both state and central personnel, and the logistical support needed for accommodation, mess, transportation, and communication. The budget must be communicated to the district authorities in a timely manner so that they can disburse the necessary resources to the ground level. In summary, the entire process of preparing for an election involves a coordinated, multi‑layered effort that blends strategic assessment, inter‑agency cooperation, detailed logistical planning, and rigorous financial management. By following the prescribed procedures—starting from the assessment of state police manpower, through the projection of central force requirements, the preparation of budget estimates, the holding of coordination meetings, the procurement and maintenance of equipment, and the provision of accommodation, transportation, and mess facilities—the state can ensure that the election is conducted in a secure, orderly, and peaceful manner, upholding the democratic principles enshrined in the constitution.

The operational planning for a single company of the Central Armed Police Forces, which may also be referred to as a State Armed Police unit or an Integrated Reserve Battalion, requires a detailed inventory of transport and support assets in order to ensure the smooth execution of its mandate. In the case of one company, the prescribed fleet consists of a fifty‑two‑seat bus that is to be allocated for the movement of personnel and equipment, supplemented by two additional minibuses each having a capacity of ten seats, which are intended for intra‑company shuttling and rapid redeployment of small groups. For senior officers a dedicated truck is to be provided, together with a personal vehicle for the commanding officer, thereby allowing the senior leadership to travel independently of the main convoy; the same arrangement of one truck and one private vehicle is repeated for the movement of other key officials within the unit. The medium‑weight vehicle category is represented by a single unit, while the light‑weight vehicle category also includes one unit, each of which will be used for patrols, liaison duties and the transport of limited supplies. An additional contingency is built into the deployment plan: if the duration of a particular operation is expected to be shorter than one second—a theoretical extreme used to illustrate the need for immediate supervisory capacity—three extra light vehicles, one per platoon, are to be made available to guarantee effective oversight of the troops on the ground.

When the command headquarters is established for a tactical operation, the transport requirements shift slightly. The headquarters requires a twenty‑five‑seat bus to accommodate the headquarters staff, and the allocation includes two such buses to provide redundancy and to enable simultaneous movement of distinct functional groups. A medium‑weight vehicle is again required, and three light‑weight vehicles are added to the roster, ensuring that the headquarters can maintain communication links, conduct reconnaissance, and respond swiftly to any emerging situation.

Special consideration is given to units composed primarily of women, often referred to as Mahila Troops, whose operational effectiveness is linked to the preservation of dignity and safety. Accordingly, vehicles assigned to these troops must meet standards that ensure privacy, appropriate seating arrangements, and security features that respect cultural norms, thereby allowing the women personnel to perform their duties without compromising personal comfort or safety. In addition to the provision of the vehicles themselves, it is essential to secure an adequate supply of fuel for the entire fleet, because the vehicles will remain in the custody of the company until the personnel are formally de‑inducted at the conclusion of the assignment. The schedule, mode of movement, and specific routes to be taken by the Central Armed Police Forces units must be kept confidential; public disclosure of these operational details could jeopardise the safety of the forces and undermine the integrity of the electoral process they are tasked to protect.

The financial planning for these logistical arrangements is governed by a set of budgeting procedures that involve the highest levels of state police administration and the state’s civil service. The Director General of Police, who serves as the chief law‑enforcement officer of the state, is required to commence the planning and assessment of budgetary needs well in advance of any election or major security operation. This assessment must take into account not only the day‑to‑day logistical requirements of both the state police and the Central Armed Police Forces—such as vehicle maintenance, fuel consumption, and spare parts—but also the honoraria that are payable to the personnel for the additional duties they perform during the election period. Once the Director General has compiled the comprehensive estimate, this information must be communicated to the Home Secretary of the state, who is responsible for incorporating the security requirements into the regular annual budget. Because the timetable for general elections is fixed and publicly known, the state government is obligated to ensure that the necessary funds are earmarked in the budget for the fiscal year in which the election will take place. The Home Secretary, acting in concert with the Director General, finalises the budgetary allocation for all security actors, thereby guaranteeing that the resources are available when needed. In the event of an unscheduled electoral event such as a bye‑election or a mid‑term poll, the moment the date is announced the Director General must promptly begin a fresh assessment of the financial requirements and transmit the revised estimate to the Home Secretary for swift inclusion in the fiscal plan. The Finance Secretary of the state bears the ultimate responsibility for making sure that the budget includes sufficient provisions not only for routine election‑related expenditures but also for contingency funds that can be deployed in case of unforeseen emergencies during both general and bye‑elections. Payments of honoraria to members of the Central Armed Police Forces must be processed without delay, in accordance with the separate instructions issued by the Election Commission, to ensure that the personnel are duly compensated for their extra service.

Vulnerability mapping, a technical term used by the Election Commission, refers to the systematic identification of voters or groups of voters who are at heightened risk of being prevented from exercising their franchise or being coerced into voting in a particular manner. In the context of elections, vulnerability is defined as the susceptibility of any individual voter or a segment of the electorate to wrongful exclusion, intimidation, or inducement, whether through monetary bribes, offers of goods, or any other form of undue pressure that could distort the free and fair expression of the vote. The purpose of conducting a vulnerability mapping exercise is to anticipate, before the polling day, which voters may be exposed to such risks, to understand the sources of that vulnerability—be they political actors, criminal elements, or socio‑economic pressures—and to design targeted remedial measures that mitigate those risks in advance.

The legal framework that underpins the fight against electoral malpractice includes several provisions of the Indian Penal Code and the Representation of the People Act, 1951. Section one hundred seventy‑one‑B, subsection one, of the Indian Penal Code criminalises the act of offering any gratification, whether in cash or kind, with the intention of inducing a person to exercise any electoral right, as well as the acceptance of such gratification as a reward for exercising that right or for attempting to influence another voter; this offense is commonly termed bribery, although a mere declaration of public policy or a promise of future governmental action does not fall within the scope of the provision. Section one hundred seventy‑one‑C of the same code addresses the offence of undue influence, defining it as any voluntary interference, or an attempt thereof, with the free exercise of any electoral right, thereby rendering such conduct a punishable crime. Further, section one hundred twenty‑three, clause two, of the Representation of the People Act, 1951 categorises any direct or indirect interference by a candidate, the candidate’s agent, or any person acting with the candidate’s consent, with the free exercise of electoral rights as a corrupt practice; this provision, together with the relevant sections of the Indian Penal Code, provides a broad legal arsenal that enforcement agencies can invoke when confronting acts that create voter vulnerability.

The Commission, recognising the powerful role that physical muscle power can play in influencing electoral outcomes, especially in regions where socio‑economic conditions create a fertile ground for coercion, introduced a formal mechanism for vulnerability mapping in the year two thousand and seven. This mechanism was designed to identify, at a granular level, those polling stations or localities where voters might be exposed to threats, intimidation, or inducement, and to prescribe corrective actions that could be implemented well before the election day. A series of detailed instructions followed the introduction of this mechanism, directing election officials, police authorities and other stakeholders to conduct systematic surveys, to analyse socio‑political data, and to coordinate the deployment of security personnel and awareness campaigns in those identified hotspots. The overarching aim of these instructions is to neutralise the menace of electoral intimidation by ensuring that the identified vulnerable locations receive heightened supervision, targeted voter education, and, where necessary, the presence of additional security forces capable of deterring and responding to any attempts at undue influence. By embedding vulnerability mapping into the overall election management plan, the Commission seeks to safeguard the democratic process and to uphold the principle that every eligible voter should be able to cast a ballot free from fear, coercion, or manipulation.

However, in recent times, along with muscle power, money power has also become an important factor unduly influencing voters on account of prevailing socio-economic scenario. Thus, there can be pockets of economically backward sections with low awareness about ethical voting which may be observed keenly during vulnerability Mapping. In this regard, the Commission has issued a consolidated instruction No. 464/L&O/2023/EPS(VM) dated 21.06.2023 on Vulnerability Mapping, Critical Polling Stations and preventive measures. The tool of VM has been effectively utilized since its inception and depending upon the emerging requirements, it has been modified/adapted from time to time. After over fifteen years of experience of utilizing this tool in all General Elections, Parliamentary and Assembly, the mechanism of VM has now become institutionalized. Drawing from these experiences and learning from the best practices from across the country, the series of instructions issued by the ECI on VM have now been consolidated and augmented further in a systematic framework in this monograph for more effective application in the field. 5.4. Three Stages of Vulnerability Mapping (VM) Exercise In all the Constituencies going to poll, the Vulnerability Mapping (VM) exercise shall be carried out without exception. The VM exercise takes place in three stages:

(i) Identification of the voter’s/voter segments, village/hamlets/ area wise vulnerable to threat or undue influence

(ii) Identification of the persons causing such vulnerability using muscle/ money power

(iii) Initiating preventive measures against the persons responsible for causing vulnerability. 5.5. Parameters of Vulnerability

Every DEO needs to collect input based on present and past records concerning the elements of vulnerability in his/her district/constituency. VM-1(Annexure-I) provides a set of parameters to collect the input on vulnerability. The DEO should collect such information at least six months before the date of poll fixed for the last held General Election from RO/SDM of each Assembly Segment and continue updating it subsequently. After appointment of Sector Officer (four months before the date of poll of last held General election) for the exercise of Vulnerability Mapping, this input needs to be provided to him/her. Suitable training shall be imparted to the Sector officer and Sector Police Officer on their roles, responsibilities and timelines of activities for Vulnerability Mapping. Other important parameters that are also to be taken into consideration by DEO while carrying out vulnerability mapping exercise and while giving written briefs to Sector Officers include the following:

a) Pre-poll complaints: - Pre-poll complaints may be considered as an important input of Vulnerability Mapping. Complaints are important on many counts. It is a live phenomenon showing depth of political competitiveness much before the poll day. Complaints are not always genuine. However, number and nature of complaints verified true by election machinery may be taken as an indicator of vulnerability. Pre-Poll complaints have usually two dimensions. One relates to MCC including the misuse of money power to bribe voters in cash/kind, and the second relates to political rivalry of diverse nature primarily concerned with maintaining/consolidating/losing of political ground by leaders of political parties. Indeed, the second variety of activity starts taking place much before the Poll Day. Complaints are a manifestation of tremors felt by political parties in the run-up to the elections. Hence, number and nature of complaints need to be considered. b) Act of political parties: - Number of complaints related to violation of MCC cases, prior to poll, are also important. Experience suggests that political parties are reluctant to take permission of household owners for wall-writing on private property. In rural areas, it is almost taken as granted. The voters may not muster courage to raise voices against the much-organized body called political party, in particular, ruling political party. This creates sense of vulnerability in voters. In this context, the propensity of MCC violations has a strong bearing upon vulnerability of the Polling Station and shall, therefore, be taken into consideration in mapping of vulnerability. c) Voting percentage in the previous elections: The cases of low/high voter turnout should be examined and analyzed to understand the factors leading to vulnerability, if any. The presence of minorities, scheduled castes, scheduled tribes, other vulnerable sections and domination of anti-social elements should be taken into account for assessing the issue of vulnerability in those areas. The Sector Officer will use the proforma VM-2 (Annexure-II) for checking and determining vulnerable areas, particularly in non-LWE areas during the visit to the Polling Station(s). Besides the Sector Officer’s VM reports, the DEO must also ensure the following:

a) Cross-verification of vulnerability report: - There must be some additional mechanism of collecting, cross verifying and collating information at a relatively higher level of the Sub-Divisional Magistrate (SDM). Some more inputs may be added thereon, from SDPOs/other sources/ district intelligence inputs, making vulnerability plan rationalized and realistic.

During the conduct of General Assembly Elections, the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate, commonly abbreviated as SDM, plays a pivotal coordinating role. The SDM is the senior administrative official responsible for a sub‑division, which is an administrative unit that lies below the district level and typically comprises several assembly constituencies. Within each of these constituencies, there is a Returning Officer, known as the RO, whose chief duty is to oversee the entire electoral process for that constituency, ensuring that voting takes place smoothly, that the counting of votes is accurate, and that the overall integrity of the election is maintained. The SDM’s task, therefore, is to work closely with each of these Returning Officers, aligning the sub‑divisional administration with the requirements and timelines set by the election authorities. This coordination involves sharing information about local resources, security arrangements, and any logistical challenges that may affect polling stations, as well as ensuring that any directives issued by higher electoral bodies are implemented uniformly across the sub‑division.

In addition to the direct interaction between the SDM and the Returning Officers, the election commission has established several supplementary channels through which relevant information and concerns can be gathered. One such channel involves the so‑called “worry lists” that political parties and individual candidates submit to the District Election Officer, or DEO. A worry list is a compilation of specific issues, grievances, or potential problems that a party or candidate anticipates in the course of the election. These might include concerns about the availability of polling staff, the condition of election equipment, the safety of particular voting locations, or any other factor that could compromise the fairness or smooth conduct of the poll. The commission has issued explicit instructions that the DEO must take these worry lists into account when undertaking Vulnerability Mapping, a systematic exercise that aims to identify and assess areas or aspects of the electoral process that are particularly susceptible to disruption, fraud, or violence. By incorporating the perspectives of the parties and candidates, the mapping exercise gains a more holistic view, reflecting the on‑the‑ground realities that might not be visible to the central electoral machinery.

The information supplied by political parties and candidates does not move directly to the highest level of decision‑making without a preliminary check. Before the DEO receives the worry lists, the data are subject to validation at the level of the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate. This means that the SDM reviews the submissions to confirm their authenticity, relevance, and completeness, but the validation does not extend to any administrative units below the sub‑division. By restricting the verification process to the SDM level, the system seeks to balance thorough scrutiny with administrative efficiency, ensuring that only credible concerns ascend the chain of command to the District Election Officer for further action.

Recognizing that voters themselves constitute the largest and most directly affected group of stakeholders in any election, the commission has also mandated the creation of a dedicated communication channel that leverages call centres and control rooms. These facilities are staffed by trained operators who can receive reports, complaints, and suggestions from members of the public. The idea is to provide a convenient, accessible avenue for citizens to convey any observations or grievances they might have regarding the conduct of the election, whether it concerns the availability of polling booths, the behavior of officials, or any form of intimidation they might encounter. By aggregating this grassroots feedback, the electoral authorities can develop a richer, more nuanced picture of the election environment, complementing the inputs that come from political actors.

In parallel, the District Intelligence Branch of the district police also contributes information to the vulnerability assessment. This branch is tasked with gathering intelligence on potential threats, such as the planning of disruptive activities, the presence of extremist groups, or any other security concerns that could affect the polling process. Their contributions are vital because they bring to bear the specialized knowledge and investigative capabilities of the police, allowing for a more accurate identification of risk zones and the formulation of appropriate mitigation strategies.

To ensure that the reporting of vulnerabilities is both authentic and reliable, the entire process of collecting, cross‑verifying, validating, and collating information must be conducted at a relatively higher administrative or police level. By “higher level” the guidelines mean that the verification should occur at an authority that has sufficient jurisdiction and oversight, such as the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate or the District Election Officer, rather than at lower, potentially fragmented levels of the administration where consistency may be harder to guarantee. This hierarchical approach to verification helps to prevent the propagation of erroneous or spurious data and ensures that any actions taken in response to identified vulnerabilities are based on a sound evidentiary foundation.

The section of the guidelines dealing with actions to be taken for vulnerability mapping lays out a structured plan for appointing suitable sector officers and delineating their responsibilities. The appointment process begins with an assessment of the local terrain and the availability of human resources. In regions where the geography is challenging—such as mountainous areas, flood‑prone zones, or places with poor transportation infrastructure—the election authorities must carefully consider how many polling stations a single officer can realistically supervise within a reasonable travel time. The guideline specifies that each sector officer, abbreviated as SO, is to be responsible for overseeing ten to twelve polling stations, and that the total area covered should be traversable in one to two hours. This ensures that the officer can respond promptly to any issues that arise at any of the polling sites under his or her jurisdiction.

The timing of the appointment is also clearly defined. Sector officers are to be appointed four months before the scheduled date of polling for the most recent general election. This advance period allows sufficient time for training, familiarisation with the specific polling stations, and the development of contingency plans. Because the role of sector officer is described as one of the most responsible positions within the election apparatus, the selection process is intended to identify the most capable and experienced officers. The guidelines stress that the “best officers”—those with a proven track record of administrative competence, integrity, and the ability to handle pressure—should be chosen. In circumstances where the local pool of qualified personnel is insufficient, the central government may be called upon to deploy officers from other regions, ensuring that the required expertise is available.

It is important to note that the term “Sector Officer” does not refer to the physical locations of the polling stations themselves; rather, it denotes the officer’s responsibility for the vulnerability‑mapping work related to the specific sections that fall within the ten to twelve polling stations assigned to them. In other words, the officer’s duties are tied to the demographic and geographic characteristics of the area, not to the mere count of polling station sites.

Once the election schedule has been announced and the polling process is set in motion, the sector officers are elevated to the status of Sector Magistrates for the same area, with this designation taking effect at least seven days before the actual polling day. This elevation confers upon them the powers of a Special Executive Magistrate, a legal authority that enables them to take immediate, executive action to maintain law and order, enforce compliance with election rules, and address any emergent security concerns within their sector. The Chief Electoral Officer, abbreviated as CEO, is responsible for coordinating with the state government to facilitate the formal conferment of these special magistrate powers. This may involve issuing official orders, arranging training on the specific legal provisions that apply to Special Executive Magistrates, and ensuring that the officers are equipped with the necessary tools and resources to exercise their authority effectively.

The responsibilities of a sector officer before the poll are extensive and multifaceted. First and foremost, the officer must conduct a comprehensive assessment of the vulnerability landscape within their assigned area. This involves reviewing the worry lists submitted by parties and candidates, analysing the intelligence reports supplied by the district police, and incorporating feedback received through the voter call centres. The officer must then synthesise this information into a coherent vulnerability map that highlights potential flashpoints, such as areas with a history of electoral violence, locations where the physical infrastructure is weak, or zones where logistical bottlenecks are likely. The mapping exercise also requires the officer to identify the resources needed to mitigate each identified risk, ranging from additional security personnel to alternative voting arrangements.

In addition to mapping, the sector officer is tasked with coordinating with local law enforcement agencies, ensuring that the police are aware of the identified vulnerabilities and that they have adequate deployment plans. This coordination includes joint briefings, the sharing of intelligence, and the development of rapid response protocols in case of an incident. The officer also works closely with the Returning Officer for each constituency to align the operational plans, ensuring that any special arrangements—such as the use of auxiliary polling booths, the provision of transport for voters in remote areas, or the installation of additional security equipment—are properly documented and implemented.

Furthermore, the sector officer must oversee the training of election staff within their sector. This training covers not only the technical aspects of handling voting machines and ballot papers but also the legal obligations of election personnel, the procedures for reporting irregularities, and the protocols for maintaining a neutral and secure polling environment. The officer ensures that each polling station under their supervision has a clear chain of command and that staff members are familiar with the emergency contact numbers and reporting mechanisms established by the call centre and control room system.

Finally, the sector officer is responsible for establishing a system of continuous monitoring as the election day approaches. This includes regular field visits to polling stations to verify that preparations are on schedule, the deployment of spot checks to assess compliance with security measures, and the maintenance of a real‑time log of any incidents or anomalies reported by voters, candidates, or officials. By maintaining this vigilant oversight, the sector officer helps to ensure that the vulnerability mapping exercise translates into concrete actions that safeguard the integrity of the electoral process, thereby reinforcing public confidence in the democratic system.

The task of preparing for an election in a democratic society involves more than simply setting up polling stations and printing ballots; it requires a careful assessment of the social and physical environment in which the vote will be cast. One of the most critical components of this preparation is the exercise known as Vulnerability Mapping, a systematic process that identifies those voters and communities that are most at risk of intimidation, coercion, or other forms of electoral malpractice. The individuals charged with carrying out this exercise are the Sector Officers, senior officials who are assigned a defined geographic portion of an assembly constituency. Their duties before the poll, on the poll day, and in the coordination with police personnel are laid out in great detail so that the election authority can ensure that the ballot is cast freely and fairly.

Before the actual day of voting, the Sector Officer is required to initiate the Vulnerability Mapping exercise in accordance with the timetable that has been fixed by the election administration. This initiation involves launching a series of field visits, community meetings, and data‑gathering activities that are designed to create a clear picture of which areas, villages, and hamlets are likely to experience pressure on voters. The officer must make at least three separate trips to the assigned area; the repeated visits serve two complementary purposes. First, they help to build confidence among the local population, signalling that the election machinery is present, attentive, and prepared to protect the integrity of the vote. Second, the successive visits allow the officer to fine‑tune the mapping by incorporating new information that may emerge after each encounter with community members, local leaders, and other stakeholders.

During these visits the officer’s primary analytical task is the identification of specific villages, hamlets, and even small segments of the electorate that are vulnerable to threats or intimidation. Vulnerability may arise from a variety of sources, such as the presence of powerful local landlords, criminal gangs, or political actors who have a vested interest in influencing the outcome. The mapping exercise therefore requires a careful cataloguing of not only the geographic locations but also the particular individuals or groups who create the conditions of vulnerability. The emphasis is placed on names rather than on sheer numbers; the election officials must know precisely who is exerting undue influence so that appropriate safeguards can be put in place. All of this information is to be recorded in a prescribed format that is submitted to the Returning Officer or the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate without revealing the source of the intelligence, thereby protecting informants and maintaining the confidentiality of the data.

Ensuring that every eligible voter can reach the polling station without obstruction is another core responsibility of the Sector Officer. This accountability extends to the provision of logistical support, the removal of physical barriers, and the monitoring of any attempts to block or discourage voters from exercising their franchise. To facilitate rapid communication in case of an emergency, the officer must compile a list of contact points within the vulnerable community, complete with telephone and mobile numbers, so that any incident can be reported and addressed promptly. In the event that the Sector Officer is designated, after a formal notification, to act as a Sector Magistrate—a quasi‑judicial role that carries the authority to make immediate decisions to protect the election process—he or she must be accompanied at all times by a police officer who holds the rank of Sector Police Officer. This accompaniment is essential because the magistrate’s powers are exercised in conjunction with law‑enforcement authority, ensuring that any orders issued can be enforced without delay.

Because the Sector Officer, when acting as a magistrate, will be exercising a combination of administrative and judicial functions, he or she is required to draft a Sector Management Plan. This plan is a comprehensive document that includes a sketch map of the polling stations within the sector, a list of telephone numbers for the officials responsible for each polling station, and the names of election‑related officers and police stations that will be called upon for assistance. The plan also enumerates the individuals who are identified as responsible for maintaining peace, as well as a catalog of known anti‑social elements—people with a history of violence, intimidation, or other disruptive behavior who could threaten a peaceful poll. The creation of such a plan ensures that every stakeholder knows exactly who to contact and what actions to take in the event that an incident arises.

In addition to the internal coordination required to produce the management plan, the Sector Officer must also engage in a series of external meetings with various local authorities and community representatives. Meetings with labour inspectors and officers responsible for food and supply are important because they often have detailed knowledge of workers’ living conditions and can point out workplaces or residential colonies where employees might be vulnerable to coercion. Likewise, discussions with heads of educational institutions—particularly residential schools, boarding houses, and hostels—allow the officer to identify student populations that might be susceptible to pressure from external actors.
