Interactions with owners of factories, warehouses, and other commercial premises are equally essential, as these premises frequently house large numbers of workers who may lack the freedom to vote independently. The officer also meets with representatives of resident welfare associations, village heads, and the elected heads of local self‑government bodies such as the Gram Pradhan or Sarpanch, in order to build trust, explain the purpose of the vulnerability mapping, and enlist their cooperation in protecting the electorate.

The flow of information does not end with these community meetings. The Sector Officer is required to share relevant intelligence with the beat constable, the police officer who is permanently stationed in the local neighbourhood, so that the constable’s routine patrols can be adjusted to focus on identified hotspots. A sound understanding of the geography of the polling stations and the surrounding area is indispensable; the officer must be able to navigate the terrain, recognize the location of each polling booth, and anticipate potential logistical challenges such as difficult access routes or natural barriers. Moreover, the officer must be familiar with the demographic distribution of the electorate, taking into account the composition of voters by caste, creed, and religion. This knowledge helps to anticipate where communal tensions might arise and to plan preventive measures accordingly. Finally, the officer must identify any persons who operate printing presses, as these facilities could be used to produce counterfeit electoral materials, propaganda, or other items that might influence the voting process.

When the actual day of voting arrives, the Sector Officer—who may also be serving as the Sector Magistrate—has a set of additional responsibilities that complement the general duties expected of any election official on poll day. The officer must continuously monitor whether the vulnerable sections that were identified during the pre‑poll phase are actually turning up at the polling stations. This monitoring is not a one‑time check but an ongoing observation that may involve direct contact with polling staff, observation of voter queues, and liaison with the police. If the officer detects any sign of intimidation, harassment, or a sudden drop in turnout among the vulnerable groups, he or she must immediately alert the Returning Officer and the district administration so that rapid remedial action can be taken. In many jurisdictions, the officer may also deploy specially constituted “flying squads”—mobile police units that can be dispatched quickly to any location where a problem is reported. These squads operate under the direction of the Sector Officer and the Sector Police Officer, ensuring that any threat is addressed in real time. The activities outlined here are illustrative; depending on the particular sensitivities of the locality—such as the presence of a history of electoral violence, communal tensions, or organized crime—higher‑level election officials such as the District Electoral Officer or the Returning Officer may add further duties to the list in order to strengthen the vulnerability mapping exercise.

The successful execution of Vulnerability Mapping also depends on the proper appointment of police personnel at both the sector and the police‑station levels. Each police station covers a defined portion of an assembly constituency, and for the purposes of the mapping exercise the district superintendent of police must designate a police officer to act as the Sector Police Officer. This officer accompanies the Sector Officer in the field and jointly conducts the mapping activities. The rank of the designated officer cannot be below that of an Assistant Sub‑Inspector or a Head Constable, ensuring that the officer has sufficient authority and experience to intervene when necessary. In addition to the sector‑level officer, a specific police officer is assigned at each individual police station to track the activities of known trouble‑makers and to keep them under close surveillance. This officer is formally titled the Designated Police Officer for Vulnerability Mapping. The Returning Officer and the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate for each assembly segment coordinate closely with both the Sector Officers and the Designated Police Officers, creating a chain of communication that extends from the grassroots level all the way up to the district administration.

Training is a further essential element of the system, because neither the Sector Officers nor the Designated Police Officers can be expected to perform their duties effectively without a thorough understanding of the election process, the local demography, and the legal framework that governs their actions. Consequently, both categories of officials undergo joint training sessions that cover a broad array of subjects. During these sessions, participants receive a copy of the electoral roll for each polling station within their jurisdiction, complete with the names of hamlets and villages, so that they can identify every voter in the area. They are also briefed on the Polling Station Wise Assured Minimum Facilities, which are the basic services—such as drinking water, sanitation, and shelter—that must be provided at each polling booth. Historical data on voter turnout for the last two general elections at both the assembly‑constituency level and the individual polling‑station level are reviewed, giving the officers a sense of typical participation patterns and highlighting any areas where turnout has historically been low. Gender ratio statistics are examined to understand the balance between male and female voters, which can be a key indicator of where targeted outreach may be needed. The officers also study records of Model Code of Conduct violations—referred to as MCC violations—in their sector for the previous two elections, learning from past transgressions to prevent recurrence. A route map of the sector, which may be a simple sketch rather than a precise scale drawing, is provided so that the officers can visualize the relative positions of polling stations and major thoroughfares. Additional information required under the official Vulnerability Mapping form, known as VM‑1, is also supplied, ensuring that every relevant data point is captured in a standardized manner.

To translate this training into action, a joint visit programme is drafted during the training period. This programme outlines the schedule of field visits, the specific objectives for each visit, and the responsibilities of each participant. The Returning Officer, the Sub‑Divisional Police Officer, the District Electoral Officer, and the district superintendent of police are all tasked with conducting periodic joint reviews of the work performed by the Sector Officers and the Designated Police Officers. These reviews assess how well the vulnerability mapping has been carried out, evaluate the effectiveness of the corrective measures that have been taken, and identify any new issues that may have arisen during the course of the exercise.

The identification of vulnerable areas, segments, villages, and hamlets begins with the first stage of the vulnerability‑mapping process, which is the series of frequent visits that the Sector Officer makes immediately after being appointed. The officer is expected to make at least three trips to the assigned area, and each visit is conducted together with the police officer—either an Assistant Sub‑Inspector or a Head Constable—who has been designated to accompany him or her. The itinerary for these visits is not decided unilaterally; rather, it is formulated jointly by the Returning Officer, who is the chief election official for the constituency, and the Deputy Superintendent of Police or the Senior Deputy Police Officer, who bring in the law‑enforcement perspective. By working together from the outset, the election officials and the police can combine their respective expertise in community outreach and security, creating a comprehensive picture of where vulnerabilities exist and what steps must be taken to mitigate them. This collaborative approach is intended to ensure that the electoral process is conducted in a manner that respects the democratic rights of every citizen, especially those who might otherwise be marginalized or coerced, and to uphold the fundamental principle that a free and fair election is the cornerstone of a healthy democracy.

The official tasked with overseeing a particular electoral sector, known as the Sector Officer, is required to undertake a comprehensive field mission that reaches every locality and every small cluster of habitations—often referred to as pockets—within the geographic perimeter of each polling station that lies in his or her jurisdiction. This mission is not a cursory inspection; it demands that the officer move from one community to another, engaging in open‑ended, widespread discussions with the residents, listening to their concerns, and gathering on‑the‑ground intelligence that can reveal the social and economic conditions that render certain households or families especially vulnerable during an election. Vulnerability, in this context, may arise from a variety of causes such as extreme poverty, dependence on a particular employer or landlord, low levels of education, caste or communal marginalisation, or any other circumstance that could make an individual or a group susceptible to coercion, intimidation, or undue influence. In addition to identifying those who are vulnerable, the officer must also identify the persons, groups, or other factors that are responsible for creating or exacerbating that vulnerability. These could be local strongmen, criminal elements, influential politicians, or even informal networks that exercise control over a community through economic pressure or social ostracism. By documenting both sides of the equation—the at‑risk individuals and the sources of risk—the Sector Officer lays the groundwork for a targeted response that can protect the integrity of the electoral process.

While undertaking this extensive outreach, the officer must keep in mind two critical temporal dimensions. First, the officer should review any incidents that have occurred in the past, such as previous episodes of electoral violence, intimidation, or fraud that may have taken place in the same locality. Historical patterns often provide clues about where trouble is likely to recur. Second, the officer must also be sensitive to current apprehensions that may be circulating among the electorate, for instance rumors of ballot‑box stuffing, threats of violence on election day, or recent arrests of local leaders. These present‑day fears can be just as telling as historical data, because they may indicate new dynamics or emerging threats that require immediate attention. By integrating both the historical record and the contemporary mood, the officer can produce a more nuanced assessment of the security and vulnerability landscape.

The moment the election commission formally announces that an election will be held, the Sector Officer must act without delay to formalise the information gathered during the field visits. This formalisation takes place on two prescribed forms, labelled as Proforma VM‑2 and Proforma VM‑3, which appear as annexures II and III in the official procedural handbook. The officer must travel to each polling station, verify the data collected earlier, and record it in these standardized templates. The purpose of these templates is to ensure that every sector captures the same categories of information, such as the names of vulnerable households, the nature of the threats they face, and the identity of any individuals or groups that might be responsible for creating those threats. The use of a standard format also facilitates comparison across different sectors and enables higher‑level officials to compile a consolidated view of vulnerability across the entire district or state.

In addition to the information supplied by the Sector Officer, the process mandates consultation with local law‑enforcement and civil administration officials, such as the local police officer, the Tehsildar (often abbreviated as TDO), the Mamlatdar, or the Police Inspector. These officials possess detailed knowledge of local law‑order conditions, pending criminal cases, and the presence of any illegal weapons caches. Their inputs are therefore essential for refining the list of vulnerable households and for verifying the identity of potential troublemakers. The officer must incorporate these insights before finalising the forms, thereby ensuring that the final vulnerability mapping reflects a consensus view that balances community intelligence with official law‑enforcement perspectives.

It is important to understand that the template designated as VM‑2 is not intended to be a rigid checklist that limits the scope of data collection. Rather, it is an enabling tool that guides the officer in gathering the most relevant information, but the officer retains the discretion to record additional details that may have a direct bearing on the vulnerability of a polling station. For instance, if the officer learns during a conversation that a particular village has a history of caste‑based intimidation that is not captured by the standard fields, that information can be added in an annex or a free‑text section. The overarching principle is that no pertinent data should be omitted simply because it does not fit neatly into the prescribed categories.

When the General Observer, the Police Observer, and the Expenditure Observer arrive in the constituency to commence their oversight duties, the District Election Officer (DEO) and the Returning Officer (RO) are required to transfer all of the compiled, polling‑station‑wise vulnerability data to these observers. This transfer is carried out using two further forms—VM‑4 and VM‑5, which appear as annexures IV and V. In addition to these documents, the DEO and RO must also provide the observers with a curated list of “critical” polling stations, meaning those stations that have been identified as having a high concentration of vulnerable voters or a high probability of disruption. The final version of this list, as well as the accompanying vulnerability maps, must receive the approval of the senior most General Observer and the senior most Police Observer, ensuring that the highest‑ranking officials in the observation hierarchy endorse the data before any operational decisions are made based on it.

The observers themselves are not passive recipients of this information; they are mandated to visit the identified locations, engage directly with voters, and maintain a continuous watch over any developments that might affect the conduct of the election. Their role is to act as a bridge between the ground realities captured by the Sector Officer and the higher‑level administrative mechanisms that can intervene, thereby providing real‑time feedback that can trigger corrective action if needed.

Identifying the individuals or groups that cause vulnerability is a separate but closely linked task. The DEO provides an initial set of inputs in Annexure‑I, often referred to as VM‑I, which outlines the broad categories of vulnerability observed in the sector. Building on this foundation, the Sector Officer, together with the Sector Police Officer—a senior police official assigned to the same geographic area—conducts regular field visits. During these visits, they update the vulnerability mapping and specifically pinpoint the names of voters or voter segments who are at risk of being threatened, intimidated, or subjected to undue influence. This identification process is performed on a polling‑station‑by‑polling‑station basis, meaning that for each station the officer records the name of the village or hamlet, the names and addresses of potential troublemakers, and any other distinguishing details that might help law‑enforcement agencies locate and monitor them. The confidentiality of the sources of this intelligence is of paramount importance; if the informants request anonymity, the officer must protect their identities to preserve the flow of information and avoid endangering those who provide it.

Having compiled a list of persons responsible for creating or amplifying vulnerability, the next phase involves taking decisive action against those individuals. This action is coordinated jointly by the District Election Officer and the Superintendent of Police of the district. The two officials hold a joint review meeting in which they examine the compiled data and formulate a focused action plan designed to neutralise the identified threats. The plan may encompass a wide range of law‑enforcement measures, such as invoking specific sections of the criminal code to arrest or detain troublemakers, conducting raids to seize illegal firearms, or, where legally permissible, applying preventive detention to keep high‑risk individuals in custody for a short period before the election. The plan may also stipulate that troublemakers be required to present themselves periodically at the local police station to demonstrate good behaviour, or that police pickets—temporary outposts of police personnel—be established at strategic points near vulnerable polling stations. In addition, the plan could involve the voluntary deposit of licensed arms by individuals who possess them, thereby reducing the likelihood of violence on election day.

A special emphasis is placed on the period of the final seventy‑two hours before the polls open. This window is considered critical because it is often when attempts at intimidation or obstruction are intensified. Accordingly, the action plan must allocate additional resources and heightened vigilance during this period to ensure that any last‑minute attempts to disrupt voting are thwarted. Throughout the implementation of these measures, it is essential that all actions be carried out in a strictly non‑partisan manner; the law‑enforcement response must not favour any political party, nor should it be perceived as being directed against a particular political group. Maintaining impartiality helps to preserve public confidence in the electoral process and prevents accusations of bias that could undermine the legitimacy of the election.

In parallel with the identification of troublemakers, the election administration also seeks to create a network of contact persons within the vulnerable groups themselves. The names and mobile phone numbers of these contact persons are recorded so that the authorities can maintain direct communication channels, disseminate information about safety measures, and receive timely updates from the community. To ensure continuous monitoring of the identified troublemakers, each police station—referred to as a “Thana”—must appoint a Designated Police Officer specifically responsible for overseeing the vulnerability mapping. This officer’s duties include tracking the movements and activities of the troublemakers, ensuring that any breaches of the law are promptly addressed, and coordinating with other police units as needed.

One particularly insidious form of voter suppression that the guidelines address is the illegal appropriation of the Electoral Photo Identity Card (EPIC), which serves as the official voter identification document in India. In some cases, vulnerable voters have been coerced into handing over their EPICs to local strongmen in exchange for money, with the intent of preventing them from exercising their franchise. The directives require that such incidents be stopped immediately. District Election Officers, Sub‑Divisional Police Superintendents (SPs), and Returning Officers must monitor the situation closely, investigate any reports of EPIC theft, and take swift corrective action, which may include filing criminal complaints, recovering the stolen cards, and providing replacement documents to the affected voters.

To reinforce the deterrent effect, the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate (SDM) or the Deputy Superintendent of Police (Dy. SP) is instructed to issue formal warnings to identified troublemakers, informing them that they are being individually tracked and that any further attempts to interfere with the election will be met with decisive law‑enforcement action. This warning serves both as a psychological deterrent and as a legal notice that the authorities are vigilant.

Beyond the punitive and preventive measures, the guidelines also outline a suite of confidence‑building activities aimed at reassuring vulnerable communities that the electoral process will be safe and fair. The Sector Officer is expected to make regular return visits to the identified areas, maintaining ongoing contact with local residents and updating them on the steps being taken to protect them. Focused activities under the Systematic Voter Education and Electoral Participation (SVEEP) program are to be organised, which may include awareness camps that explain voting rights, the importance of participating in elections, and the mechanisms in place to safeguard the vote. Modern information‑technology tools such as the c‑VIGIL mobile application—a platform that allows citizens to report threats or irregularities in real time—are to be promoted, alongside the publicisation of the Voter Helpline Number 1950, which provides a direct line for voters to seek assistance or lodge complaints.

Higher‑ranking officials, including the District Election Officer, the Superintendent of Police, and the various observers, are also encouraged to make personal visits to the vulnerable pockets. Their presence is intended to convey a clear message that the administration is attentive and committed to ensuring a peaceful electoral environment. In addition, the District Election Officer and the Returning Officer must maintain an open dialogue with the candidates contesting the election and with representatives of political parties, soliciting feedback on any concerns they may have regarding security or voter intimidation. This feedback loop helps to fine‑tune the security arrangements and to address any emerging issues promptly.

Regular intelligence inputs from the District Intelligence Branch (LIB) are also to be incorporated into the overall assessment. The LIB provides analytical reports that can identify emerging trends, such as the formation of new criminal alliances or the procurement of weapons, which might affect electoral security. When multiple vulnerable pockets are clustered together geographically, the District Election Officer is instructed to organise dedicated police squads that are stationed at convenient locations near the cluster. These squads are to be placed on standby so that they can be deployed instantly on the day of polling without any delay, thereby forming an integral part of the broader District Security Plan that outlines the allocation of security resources for the entire district.

In sum, the comprehensive framework described above is designed to create a multi‑layered protective mechanism for the electorate. It begins with grassroots intelligence gathering by the Sector Officer, proceeds through systematic documentation and verification, incorporates collaborative inputs from local law‑enforcement and administrative officials, and culminates in a coordinated set of preventive, corrective, and confidence‑building actions. By ensuring that vulnerable voters are identified, that the sources of their vulnerability are pinpointed, and that both punitive and supportive measures are deployed in a non‑partisan and transparent manner, the procedure seeks to uphold the fundamental democratic principle that every eligible citizen should be able to cast a free and fair vote without fear of intimidation, coercion, or undue influence.

In order to guarantee that the electoral process is free from intimidation and undue influence, particular attention must be paid to groups that are historically more vulnerable to coercion. Female voters and young voters, for example, have been shown through numerous studies and field observations to be especially susceptible to pressure not only from external actors such as political operatives or criminal elements, but also from members of their own families who may seek to steer their electoral choices. Consequently, it is essential to launch a comprehensive programme of awareness‑generation that educates these segments of the electorate about their right to cast a ballot without fear. Such a programme must explain, in simple language, what constitutes intimidation, how to recognise it, and the avenues of recourse that are available when a voter feels threatened. The aim is to empower women and young people to reject any attempts at coercion and to report incidents promptly, thereby reducing the likelihood that they will become soft targets for bribery, threats, or other forms of undue influence.

To give this awareness effort a concrete structure at the grassroots level, the election administration should establish Village Level Awareness Groups (VAGs) and Ward Level Awareness Groups (WAGs). These are community‑based collectives composed of respected local citizens, teachers, health workers, and other trusted figures who are trained to act as eyes and ears on the ground. Their principal function is to gather reliable information about any intimidation or coercive activity that may be taking place within their jurisdiction. By doing so, they create a network of informants who can alert the authorities to emerging problems before they become widespread. Moreover, the presence of an organized group that is visibly engaged in protecting the integrity of the vote serves as a confidence‑building measure for the local population, reassuring them that the election machinery is actively monitoring the environment and is prepared to intervene if necessary.

In addition to the formation of these local groups, the administration should design and implement a targeted ethical voting awareness campaign that focuses specifically on those geographic pockets that have been identified as vulnerable. Vulnerable locations are typically areas where historical data, intelligence reports, or recent complaints suggest that voters may be offered cash or material gifts in exchange for their support of a particular candidate or party. The campaign must be tailored to the local cultural context, using language and media that resonate with the community, and it should emphasize the legal prohibitions against bribery, the moral importance of voting according to one’s conscience, and the personal and collective consequences of participating in corrupt practices. By concentrating resources on these high‑risk zones, the election authority can more effectively deter the distribution of cash or kind, and can reduce the overall incidence of vote‑buying.

A further layer of protection is provided by continuous surveillance carried out by specialised teams. The Female Security Teams (FSTs), the Special Surveillance Teams (SSTs), and the Vigilance and Verification Teams (VVTs) are tasked with maintaining a round‑the‑clock watch over the identified vulnerable sites. Their duties include patrolling the area, monitoring communications, documenting any suspicious activity, and promptly relaying information to the central command centre. By ensuring that there is always at least one observer on duty, the probability that intimidation attempts will go undetected is dramatically lowered.

The Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF), which comprise the nation’s elite paramilitary units such as the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, and Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, are assigned a specific “area domination” plan for poll day. The senior officers of the CAPF—namely the Commanders and Assistant Commanders—receive a detailed list of vulnerable locations from the District Election Officer (DEO). This list is compiled based on intelligence inputs, past incident reports, and demographic analyses that highlight where electoral malpractice is most likely to occur. When CAPF units are deployed to a constituency ahead of the polling date, they are instructed to give special attention to these flagged sites, conducting thorough sweeps and establishing a visible presence that deters potential disruptors.

Particular care is taken in the days leading up to the election, especially six to seven days before voting, because this is the period when political actors often attempt to distribute cash or material incentives to sway the electorate—a practice known as “expenditure‑sensitive” bribery. The CAPF’s advance presence serves as a deterrent to such illicit activity. On the actual polling day, the Commanders and Assistant Commanders are required to physically visit each vulnerable pocket, not merely to patrol but to engage with the local community, thereby reinforcing confidence that the security forces are actively safeguarding the vote. Should any obstruction, such as road blockades, intimidation by local strongmen, or attempts to tamper with voting equipment, be encountered, the officers must immediately record the incident, note the precise time, and inform the appropriate electoral officials. These officials can include the Returning Officer (the official responsible for overseeing the conduct of the election in a constituency), the District Election Officer, the Senior Police (SP), election observers, or the Sector Officer who supervises a cluster of polling stations. Prompt communication ensures that corrective measures can be taken without delay.

In circumstances where there is a history of tampering with Electronic Voting Machines (EVMs)—for instance, if an EVM has been previously broken into or looted—a pre‑emptive deployment of CAPF personnel may be ordered to secure the machines and to monitor the polling station throughout the voting process. This proactive stance reduces the risk of technical sabotage that could compromise the integrity of the electronic vote count.

On the day of the poll, a coordinated set of actions is required to guarantee that any vulnerable individuals are able to cast their ballots without fear. At the moment the polling parties—teams that transport ballot boxes and voting equipment from the central dispatch centre to individual polling stations—are dispatched, the Returning Officer must brief the Presiding Officer (the official who manages the day‑to‑day operations at each polling station) about the specific vulnerable locations that lie within the station’s jurisdiction. This briefing includes details of any known threats, the identity of at‑risk voters, and the recommended security procedures. Throughout the voting period, the Sector Officers, who oversee a group of polling stations, are tasked with preparing a report that highlights any unusually low voter turnout in sections that correspond to vulnerable areas. Such anomalies can be indicative of intimidation or obstruction and are documented for later scrutiny by the election commission.

The Sector Magistrates, who are judicial officers assigned to oversee law and order during elections, together with mobile police forces, must conduct at least two visits to each identified vulnerable village, hamlet, or dwelling area on the polling day. During these visits, they verify that adequate security cover is in place, assess whether any intimidation is occurring, and, if necessary, augment police presence to protect the electorate. Observers and senior election officers who are touring polling stations are likewise instructed to give special attention to signs of undue influence, such as the presence of large numbers of campaign workers near the ballot box, attempts to coerce voters inside the polling booth, or any form of physical obstruction that prevents voters from reaching the voting venue.

Police patrol units, operating under the direction of the local police command, keep a continuous log of the vulnerable locations they monitor and relay updates to a central control room. When a location appears to be at risk—perhaps due to a sudden influx of people, reports of threats, or physical barriers—police pickets (temporary guard posts) may be established to ensure that voters can access the polling station safely and without hindrance. The Commanders and Assistant Commanders of the CAPF repeat their confidence‑building visits on polling day, reinforcing the message that the security forces are present and vigilant. Any obstruction they encounter, such as blocked roads or attempts to intimidate voters, must be recorded with a timestamp and reported immediately to the Returning Officer, District Election Officer, senior police officials, observers, or the Sector Officer, thereby creating an auditable trail of response.

Adequate deployment of security personnel, both police and CAPF, is essential in these vulnerable zones. The number of officers assigned is calibrated based on the size of the electorate, the level of prior intimidation, and the logistical challenges of the terrain. This ensures that the presence of security forces is proportionate to the risk while avoiding unnecessary militarisation of the voting environment.

After the polling stations close, the responsibilities shift to the post‑poll analysis phase. The Returning Officer and the District Election Officer must incorporate any inputs they received concerning mass intimidation, threats, or obstruction into their official reports. These reports are submitted to the election commission and may influence decisions on whether to order a re‑poll in a particular station or to pursue legal action against perpetrators. Election observers, who are independent monitors tasked with verifying the fairness of the process, are required to give particular attention to the issue of “VM” (voter manipulation) at every stage of the election, from the pre‑poll environment through to the final tally. In their final observation reports, they must include a dedicated section that discusses any incidents of manipulation they observed, the effectiveness of the measures taken, and recommendations for improvement.

Observers also engage in a detailed examination of Form 17A, which is the official record of votes counted at each polling station, and the marked copy of the electoral roll, which lists the names of voters who actually cast their ballots. During the scrutiny process—a formal verification that takes place after voting is complete—observers compare the numbers on Form 17A with the tally on the electoral roll to detect any discrepancies that could indicate fraudulent activity. If the election commission orders a deeper audit, these documents become central to the investigation.

The election commission further mandates strict accountability and confidentiality regarding the entire vulnerability‑mapping exercise. Each civil servant and police official involved in identifying vulnerable polling stations and monitoring them throughout the election cycle is assigned a clear set of responsibilities, documented for each constituency and polling station. Should any official fail to perform these duties—by neglecting to act on intelligence, by providing inaccurate reports, or by compromising the security of vulnerable voters—disciplinary action may be initiated. This could range from formal reprimand to suspension or dismissal, depending on the severity of the dereliction.

Moreover, the commission explicitly prohibits any press conference or public dissemination of details related to critical polling stations, vulnerability mapping results, the locations of security deployments, or any other sensitive operational information. Such disclosures could jeopardise the very security measures designed to protect voters, as they might enable malicious actors to anticipate and circumvent protective actions. All field personnel, from senior commanders to on‑the‑ground officers, are therefore required to be sensitised to this confidentiality requirement. They must understand that the protection of the electoral process relies not only on physical security but also on the careful handling of information, ensuring that the integrity of the vote remains uncompromised from the pre‑poll period through to the final declaration of results.

The responsibility for communicating any information concerning electoral vulnerability to members of the press rests solely with the constitutional authority that oversees elections in the country. In practice this means that whenever a situation arises that might require a statement to be made to the media about a weakness in the electoral process—whether it be a report of intimidation, a threat to a particular community, or a vulnerability identified in a specific polling station—such communication may be undertaken only by the Election Commission of India, which is the apex body charged with the conduct of free and fair elections, or by the Chief Electoral Officers of the individual states and union territories. The Chief Electoral Officer, who acts as the senior electoral administrator for a state, may issue a media release only after receiving explicit instructions from the Commission, thereby ensuring that all public statements are coordinated, consistent, and in line with the overall policy of the election machinery. This chain of command prevents the inadvertent spread of unverified or fragmented information and reinforces the principle that the integrity of the electoral process must be protected through a single, authoritative voice.

In order to systematically identify, record, and address areas where the electorate might be susceptible to coercion, intimidation, or other forms of undue influence, the election administration has devised a set of detailed reporting formats that must be completed by officials at various levels of the hierarchy. The first tier of this reporting system involves the Sector Officer, an officer designated to focus on a specific geographic or administrative segment, often corresponding to a group of polling stations within a district. The Sector Officer is tasked with the careful and methodical completion of two principal documents. The first of these is known as VM‑2 (SO), a form that requires the officer to list, on a polling‑station‑by‑polling‑station basis, all localities that have been identified as vulnerable, the particular pockets of voters who may be at risk, and the specific voter segments—such as caste groups, religious minorities, or economically disadvantaged sections—that could be targeted. In addition, the officer must enumerate any individuals or groups who have been observed or reported to be exerting intimidation or pressure on the electorate. This document, annexed as Annexure II to the procedural manual, serves as the raw data collection tool that captures the granular details of vulnerability at the micro‑level.

The second document that the Sector Officer must fill out is designated VM‑3 (SO). This form is essentially a summary of the information recorded in VM‑2, but it aggregates the data so that each polling station’s vulnerabilities and the list of intimidators are presented in a concise, tabulated format. This summary, which appears as Annexure III, is intended to facilitate quick reference and analysis by higher‑level officials who need an overview of the situation across the entire sector. Both VM‑2 and VM‑3 must be completed for each identified vulnerable locality or pocket while the Sector Officer is physically present in the area, thereby ensuring that the information is as current and accurate as possible. The on‑site verification process also allows the officer to engage directly with local officials, community leaders, and law‑enforcement personnel to corroborate the data.

Once the forms have been completed, the Sector Officer is required to retain copies of the filled‑in documents for his or her own records. This retention is an important safeguard that creates an audit trail, allowing any later inquiries into the accuracy or completeness of the reporting to be addressed. After retaining the copies, the original forms must be forwarded to the Returning Officer of the relevant constituency within three days after the official announcement of the election. The Returning Officer, who is the chief election official for a particular assembly constituency, is responsible for supervising the conduct of the poll within his or her jurisdiction, and the timely receipt of these vulnerability reports is essential for the preparation of security and logistical arrangements.

In those rare instances where a Sector Officer, after conducting a thorough survey of the constituency, finds that no hamlets, villages, or specific voter segments meet the criteria of vulnerability, a formal certification process must still be undertaken. The District Election Officer (DEO), who oversees the entire district’s electoral operations, must obtain written confirmation from field functionaries at multiple administrative levels—including the police station (Thana), the block‑level officers, the sub‑divisional magistrate, and the senior police officer (Superintendent of Police). These officials attest that, based on their knowledge and the on‑ground observations, no vulnerable village, hamlet, or voter segment has been identified within the district. The DEO then compiles these attestations into a single certificate and submits it to the Chief Electoral Officer, thereby formally documenting the absence of vulnerability. This certification must be submitted within five days after the election is announced, ensuring that the Commission has a complete picture of both the presence and absence of risk across all districts.

The next phase of the reporting chain is the compilation of the constituency‑level vulnerability report by the Returning Officer. Using the data received from the Sector Officers, the Returning Officer aggregates the information to produce a document known as VM‑4 (RO). This format presents a summarized view of the vulnerability landscape within the entire constituency and includes a list of individuals or groups identified as causing the vulnerability. The Returning Officer retains a copy of this report for internal use and must make it available to the District Election Officer within five days of the election announcement. This rapid turnaround is designed to give the district‑level officials sufficient lead time to coordinate with police, local administration, and security agencies to mitigate any identified risks before the poll date.

The District Election Officer then follows a similar process at the district level. After receiving the VM‑4 reports from all the Returning Officers within the district, the DEO consolidates them into a single district‑wide document designated VM‑5 (DEO). This format provides a comprehensive report on the identification of vulnerability across the district and outlines the actions that have been taken or are planned to address the issues. The DEO must forward this consolidated report to the Chief Electoral Officer of the state within seven days after the election is announced. The DEO’s role is critical because it bridges the gap between the constituency‑level data and the state‑level strategic planning, ensuring that the state’s electoral machinery has a clear, actionable picture of where security resources need to be deployed.

At the top of the state hierarchy, the Chief Electoral Officer is tasked with producing an overarching state‑wide vulnerability report known as VM‑6 (CEO). The CEO compiles all the district reports (VM‑5) into a single comprehensive book, which serves as the official record of vulnerability mapping for the entire state. In addition to this compilation, the CEO prepares the VM‑6 format, a summary that highlights the key findings, trends, and policy recommendations derived from the district‑level data. Both the compiled book and the VM‑6 summary must be submitted to the Election Commission of India within ten days of the election announcement. The Commission then reviews this material to assess the overall risk environment and to make any necessary high‑level decisions regarding the allocation of central security forces, the issuance of special instructions, or the modification of polling arrangements.

In order to support the entire vulnerability mapping exercise, the Election Commission has mandated the creation of a dedicated Law and Order Portal for each state. This requirement stems from an instruction (No. 464/INST/2023/EPS dated 8 June 2023) which obliges every state to transmit a daily Law and Order Report II (LOR‑II) to the Commission throughout the election period. The LOR‑II is a concise, cumulative synopsis of a range of law‑enforcement activities that are directly relevant to the conduct of elections. These activities include preventive actions taken by the police and other agencies to forestall disturbances, seizure reports that detail the confiscation of illegal weapons, liquor, narcotics, or other contraband, and updates on vulnerable areas, persons, and intimidation actors along with the measures taken to neutralize the threats. The report also enumerates cases under the National Board for Women (NBW), lists identified bootleggers, documents instances of externment—where individuals are barred from a particular area for security reasons—highlights individuals classified as “bad characters” due to a history of criminal or disruptive behavior, and records any media‑related incidents that may affect the election environment. The State is expected to compile these details on the law‑and‑order portal, which is designed to maintain a polling‑station‑wise ledger of persons causing vulnerability and the corresponding administrative or police actions taken against them.

The functional deployment of the law‑and‑order portal is scheduled to occur four months before the date of the poll in the preceding election cycle. This advance implementation allows sufficient time for the portal to be populated with reliable data, for users at various administrative levels to become proficient with its interface, and for any technical glitches to be addressed. The portal thus becomes an essential tool for real‑time monitoring and coordination among electoral officials, law‑enforcement agencies, and the state administration.

The timeline for the entire vulnerability mapping process is anchored to the election calendar and is deliberately structured to commence four months prior to the poll date. This early start ensures that District Magistrates, who are the senior civil administrators in a district, and Superintendents of Police, who head the district police force, are fully apprised of the vulnerability profile well before the Commission conducts its review. Since vulnerability mapping is a pre‑poll activity, all steps—from data collection to final reporting—must be completed within the prescribed schedule, leaving no room for delays that could compromise election security.

The specific activities and their respective time limits are outlined as follows. First, the District Election Officer must gather and compile basic information for each assembly constituency (AC) from the Returning Officers and the Sub‑Divisional Magistrates (SDM). This collection, which adheres to the template set out in Annexure‑I, is to be completed six months before the poll date of the preceding election. The basic information includes demographic details, past election performance, the location of polling stations, and any historical incidents of violence or intimidation. Once this foundational data has been assembled, the DEO is required to update it, still using the Annexure‑I format, before it is handed over to the Sector Officers for further analysis.

The appointment of Sector Officers and their police counterparts—Sector Police Officers—is slated to occur four months before the poll date. This appointment ensures that each geographic sector has a dedicated official responsible for overseeing the vulnerability assessment and for coordinating with local police stations. Similarly, the appointment of police officers at the level of individual police stations, who will act as the eyes and ears on the ground, must also be finalized within the same four‑month window. This dual appointment creates a collaborative framework in which civil election officials and police officers work in tandem to identify and mitigate risks.

Four months prior to the election, the District Election Officer also conducts training sessions for the newly appointed Sector Officers and Sector Police Officers. This training covers the use of the reporting formats, the methodology for identifying vulnerable localities and voter segments, the procedures for engaging with community leaders, and the protocols for documenting intimidation incidents. Following the training, the DEO hands over the basic constituency information—already updated and verified—to the Sector Officers, thereby equipping them with the necessary background to commence their field visits.

Through this meticulously staged process, the election administration builds a layered, data‑driven picture of vulnerability that informs the deployment of security forces, the design of polling logistics, and the formulation of targeted interventions aimed at safeguarding the electorate. By adhering to the prescribed timelines and by ensuring that each level of the hierarchy—from the grassroots Sector Officer to the Chief Electoral Officer and ultimately to the Election Commission—contributes accurate and timely information, the system strives to uphold the fundamental democratic principle that every citizen should be able to cast a vote free from fear, coercion, or undue influence.

The sixth activity listed in the schedule concerns the establishment of a functional Law and Order Portal for the State, a digital platform that will be operated by the Election Commission of India, the constitutional authority responsible for administering free and fair elections across the country. This portal must be fully operational at least four months before the date on which the last election’s polling is to take place. The purpose of the portal is to provide a real‑time interface for the coordination of police, administrative, and election officials, allowing them to monitor law‑and‑order conditions, receive reports of disturbances, and dispatch resources swiftly. By making the portal operational well in advance of the poll, the commission ensures that all stakeholders have adequate time to become familiar with its features, conduct training sessions, and integrate the system into their existing workflow, thereby reducing the likelihood of communication breakdowns on election day.

The seventh activity is divided into two sub‑parts. Sub‑part (a) requires a preliminary identification and submission of Vulnerability Mapping (VM) reports that detail the locations and characteristics of vulnerable areas, vulnerable population segments, individual villages or hamlets that are prone to electoral intimidation, and the persons who are identified as causing or capable of causing such vulnerabilities. These reports must be compiled in the formats specified in Annexure‑II and Annexure‑III, which are standardized templates that capture geographic coordinates, demographic data, historical incidents of violence, and any known criminal or political actors. The preliminary submission is to be made two months before the date of polling for the last election, giving the central authority sufficient lead time to review the data, verify its accuracy, and request any clarifications.

Sub‑part (b) of the same activity demands a final identification of the same vulnerable entities, again recorded in Annexure‑II and Annexure‑III, but this time after the Election Commission has issued its Press Note—a public announcement that typically outlines the overall election schedule and any special measures to be taken—and before the formal gazette notification that legally confirms the election timetable. The interim period between the Press Note and the gazette is crucial because it allows the state election machinery to refine the preliminary list, incorporate field feedback, and ensure that the final vulnerability map reflects the most current intelligence.

The eighth activity mandates the submission of all vulnerability mapping reports, in the prescribed Annexure‑II and Annexure‑III formats, to the Returning Officer (RO). The Returning Officer is the official tasked with overseeing the conduct of the election in a particular constituency, including the counting of votes and the declaration of results. These reports must be handed over within three days after the election announcement, thereby ensuring that the RO has immediate access to the latest vulnerability data as soon as the election timeline becomes public.

The ninth activity is split into two further components, labelled 9A and 9B. In 9A, the Returning Officer is required to compile the reports submitted by all Sector Officers—these are officials who manage election-related tasks in specific geographic zones within the constituency—and forward this compiled dossier to the District Election Officer (DEO) using the template set out in Annexure‑IV. This compilation must be completed within five days of the election announcement. The DEO, who oversees the conduct of elections across the entire district, uses these sector reports to obtain a comprehensive view of any emerging threats or irregularities.

In 9B, the District Election Officer then aggregates the vulnerability mapping reports received from the various Returning Officers, consolidates them into a single document, and submits this to the Chief Election Officer (CEO) in the format of Annexure‑V. The CEO is the senior election official for the state and is responsible for ensuring that the state’s election machinery functions in accordance with the guidelines of the Election Commission of India. This step must be completed within seven days after the election is announced, providing the CEO with a complete picture of the vulnerable locations and actors across the district before any final preparations are made.

The tenth activity requires the District Election Officer to issue a formal certificate stating that there are no vulnerable villages or areas within a particular Assembly Constituency. This certificate, addressed to the Chief Election Officer, serves as an official affirmation that the constituency has been cleared of any identified threats that could affect the fairness of the election. The certificate must be issued within seven days of the election announcement, ensuring that any doubts about the security situation can be addressed promptly.

The eleventh activity involves the Chief Election Officer compiling the vulnerability mapping reports that have been submitted by all District Election Officers across the state, and then forwarding this comprehensive compilation to the Election Commission of India in the format of Annexure‑VI, which is designated as “VM 6(CEO).” This final state‑level report must be submitted within ten days of the election announcement, giving the Election Commission a short but sufficient window to review the aggregated data, identify any gaps, and issue further instructions if needed.

The twelfth activity sets a deadline for the completion of preventive or legitimate actions against those individuals who have been identified as responsible for creating vulnerability. These actions may include legal proceedings, issuing show‑cause notices, or other administrative measures aimed at neutralizing the threat. The requirement is that all such actions be concluded at least seven days before the actual polling day, thereby removing or mitigating the risk of intimidation, violence, or other forms of undue influence on the electorate.

The thirteenth activity calls for a coordinated plan and execution of joint confidence‑building visits by a cadre of senior officials: the District Magistrate or Superintendent of Police (DM/SP), the Sub‑Divisional Magistrate or Deputy Superintendent of Police (SDM/Dy SP), and the Tehsildar or Police Inspector (PI). These officials are to conduct visits to those areas that have been identified as the most vulnerable, with the aim of reassuring the local population, addressing grievances, and demonstrating the presence of law‑enforcement and administrative support. This activity must be carried out within two weeks prior to the polling day, giving enough time for any concerns raised during the visits to be addressed.

The fourteenth activity pertains to pre‑poll domination by the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF). The CAPF, which includes elite paramilitary units such as the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, and Indo‑Taiwan Force, is to be deployed to secure the identified vulnerable polling stations. This deployment must begin at least three to five days before the poll, allowing the forces to establish a visible presence, conduct security sweeps, and deter any attempts at booth capture or intimidation.

The fifteenth and final activity in this schedule requires strict vigilance and monitoring of the vulnerable areas and individuals on the actual polling day itself. This monitoring is to be carried out by Election Observers, the District Election Officer, the Returning Officer, Sector Officers, and the regular police force. Their collective responsibility is to observe the conduct of voting, report any irregularities instantly, and intervene if any form of coercion or violence arises, thereby ensuring that the polling process proceeds smoothly and in accordance with the law.

The text then moves to a criticality assessment, which explains the legal framework underlying the concern about undue influence. Undue influence in elections is defined as an electoral offence under section 171C of the Indian Penal Code, which criminalizes any voluntary interference or attempted interference with the free exercise of any electoral right, such as the right to vote or to contest an election. In parallel, section 123(2) of the Representation of the People Act, 1951 defines a “corrupt practice” as any direct or indirect interference, or attempt to interfere, by a candidate, his election agent, or any other person acting with the consent of the candidate, with the free exercise of any electoral right. By recognizing the role of muscle power—often manifested through intimidation, threats, or physical violence—the commission acknowledges the need for special safeguards, especially for weaker sections of society who are more susceptible to coercion. Consequently, the commission has issued instructions aimed at curbing the menace of threat and intimidation by identifying “critical polling stations,” which are specific polling booths where the risk of undue influence is considered high.

To identify these critical polling stations, the commission has set out a series of objective criteria that must be applied by the District Election Officer and the Returning Officer. First, any polling station that lies within a vulnerable pocket—meaning a geographic cluster where a significant portion of the electorate is socially, economically, or politically disadvantaged—or that is located in a region already flagged as vulnerable, must be considered. Second, polling stations where the voter turnout in the previous election exceeded ninety percent, and where more than seventy‑five percent of the votes were cast for a single candidate, are flagged because such overwhelming majorities may indicate coercion or a lack of genuine competition. Third, stations where turnout was less than ten percent are also deemed critical, as abnormally low participation can signal voter apathy induced by fear or intimidation. Fourth, any polling station where a re‑poll was ordered in the last five years due to vitiation of the poll process—such as evidence of booth capturing, ballot stuffing, or other electoral offences—must be included. Fifth, stations where any form of violence occurred on the polling day, leading to the lodging of a First Information Report (FIR) with the police, in any Lok Sabha (national) or State Legislative Assembly election during the past five years, are also classified as critical. Sixth, polling stations that have a higher than constituency‑average percentage of absentee, shifted, or deceased (ASD) voters—calculated as the total number of such voters in the constituency divided by the total number of polling stations—are flagged because unusually high absenteeism can be a sign of voter disenfranchisement or manipulation. Seventh, any location where intelligence or police reports indicate an abnormal law‑and‑order situation, such as rising communal tensions or organized criminal activity, must also be treated as a critical polling station.

Having identified these stations, the District Election Officer and the Returning Officer must incorporate all of the aforementioned inputs when finalizing the list of critical polling stations that will receive additional protective measures. The Election Commission of India’s observers are to be consulted before the list is finalized, ensuring that the central authority validates the selections and that there is consistency across different districts. For each polling station that meets one or more of the critical criteria, a suite of measures may be deployed. First, every critical polling station must have a deployment of Central Armed Police Forces, providing a maximum possible level of security to deter any attempts at intimidation, violence, or unlawful interference. Second, the complete list of critical stations must be communicated to the commanding or assistant commanding officers of the CAPF units, so that these senior officers can allocate resources, monitor the situation, and respond rapidly if an incident arises. Third, technical monitoring mechanisms such as live webcasting, video recording, or other surveillance systems are to be installed at the polling stations. These civil‑focused monitoring tools serve as a transparent means of documenting the conduct of the poll, allowing any disputes to be resolved with objective evidence, and acting as a deterrent to would‑be offenders who are aware that their actions are being recorded.

In sum, the schedule of activities and the criticality assessment together form a comprehensive framework designed to safeguard the integrity of the electoral process. By setting precise timelines for the preparation of law‑and‑order infrastructure, the systematic collection and submission of vulnerability data, the issuance of certificates confirming the absence of threats, and the deployment of both human and technical resources at high‑risk polling stations, the Election Commission of India seeks to ensure that every eligible voter can cast a ballot free from fear, coercion, or any form of undue influence. The legal provisions under the Indian Penal Code and the Representation of the People Act provide the statutory basis for penalising any attempts to subvert this process, while the operational guidelines translate those statutes into actionable steps on the ground, thereby reinforcing the democratic principle that elections must be conducted in a free, fair, and transparent manner.

Webcasting shall be done in all critical polling stations and all polling Station in vulnerable areas or at least in 50% of total polling stations including auxiliary polling stations, whichever is higher. (iv) Critical events in and around the polling station shall also be videographed. (v) A Micro Observer may be deployed inside the critical Polling Station, as additional civil measure, to enhance monitoring. (vi) The Presiding Officer of critical Polling Station shall be specifically briefed to ensure that the EPIC/ approved identification document, if any, are properly verified and reflected in the ‘Remarks’ column of Form 17 A. [The details of non-CAPF measures have been described in the manual “Force Multiplier and non-CAPF Measures”] The Commission’s direction regarding the incidences of EPICs being snatched away from vulnerable voters and the incidences of people being induced to deposit their EPIC on payment of money in order to prevent them from exercising their franchise are to be monitored. The Commission has directed that a strict vigil shall be kept about such development and DEOs/SPs/ROs shall closely monitor the situation and in case of any such incident happens, the snatched/taken away EPICs be recovered and those EPICs be restored to the actual voter. Delivering Voter information slips to all the vulnerable voters should also be ensured. Special caution should be taken by RO that delivery of Voter information slips must be handed over to voter himself. The Observers shall give their full attention to this issue and verify at every stage (before poll/ on poll day) and submit reports to the Commission from time to time. A special mention shall be made about this in their final report. Apart from this they should make an intelligent reading of the Form 17A and the marked copy of the electoral roll used in the polling stations at the time of Form 17A scrutiny ordered, if any, by the Commission after the poll. CHAPTER 6 FORCE MULTIPLIERS AND NON-CAPF MEASURES

Force multiplication/multiplier is a term commonly used in armed forces parlance and refers to those attributes which when applied to or utilized by a given force, makes its impact much larger and more effective. Latest and effective equipment, training, good transport logistics, etc. are considered force multipliers. During elections, the Central Police Forces (CAPF) are considered the main force multipliers due to their perceived neutrality, superior training and discipline and experience of dealing with challenging situations. With an estimated voter population touching 1.51 billion in 2030, the number of polling stations in the country is likely to cross 12 lakhs as compared to the current number of 11 lakhs. Given the fact that the manpower available with CAPF is likely to remain more or less constant, it is essential that this limitation is appreciated and a number of non-CAPF measures are delineated for becoming effective force multipliers. Based on the past experiences, advancement in technology, and recommendations of the internal deliberations, non-CAPF measures are now classified in the following 10 categories: -

1. Technology – Use of Technology such as Webcasting, CCTV, Still Cameras, Videography, SMS monitoring, c-Vigil App etc. for monitoring and complaint redressal. 2. Media including social media - for supporting free, fair, independent, inclusive, informed, ethical, participative, vigilante, event-free elections. 3. Manpower Management - Advance auditing and timely deployment of the manpower requirements for the various electoral tasks. 4. Capacity Building of Manpower – standardized, high quality and need-based training. 5. Manpower Deployment Planning – Advanced and effective Deployment Planning in complete confidentiality and with elements of surprise (such as randomization). 6. Motivation and Welfare of Manpower Deployed (including Police Force) - For high moral and maximum work satisfaction. 7. Communication Plan – Robust and thoroughly tested communication plan (such as SAT phones, wireless etc.). 8. Route Planning – Advance planning and arrangements for efficient use of transport and effective route planning for smooth and hassle-free mobility during elections. 9. Complaint Management System - Active Call Centres and Control Room dealing with complaints within stipulated timeframe. 10. Inter-State Cluster for Sharing of State Police Manpower - For sharing of State police manpower in a pre-determined manner. There is a separate manual and instruction on Force Multiplier and Non-CAPF measures. For details of Non-CAPF measures, the concerned manual and instruction No.464/INST/2022/EPS dated 19.06.2023 can be referred to. CHAPTER 7 PLANNING FOR FORCE DEPLOYMENT

Planning is the foundation of effective and efficient force deployment during electoral process. The Commission emphasizes the initiation of it much before the announcement of election dates. 7.1 Pre-poll Planning (before announcement of polls)

A. Appointment of Nodal officers and Demarcating Areas of Responsibility.

In preparation for a general election the government is required to name a series of senior officials who will act as the principal points of contact, or “nodal officers,” for the many different agencies that will be called upon to ensure that the electoral process proceeds smoothly and without violence. The designation of these officers must be completed well in advance of the public announcement of the election dates, typically six to eight months before the schedule is released, so that sufficient time is available for planning, coordination and training. The first of these officials is the National Force Coordinator who will function as the nodal officer for all matters that involve the Central Armed Police Forces, the State Armed Police, and the Intelligence Reporting Bureau. The Ministry of Home Affairs, which is the central ministry responsible for internal security and for the overall administration of the armed police forces, will appoint an officer of the rank of Inspector General (IG) from one of the Central Armed Police Forces to this post. The officer’s chief responsibilities will include assessing the total number of personnel that will be required from each of the central forces, allocating those personnel to the various states and union territories, and overseeing their movement and deployment as the election timetable unfolds. In effect, this officer becomes the single, highest‑ranking liaison between the Ministry of Home Affairs and the Election Commission of India for all issues concerning the deployment of central security forces.

The second senior official to be named is the State Police Nodal Officer. At the level of an Inspector General of Police, this officer will be the topmost police authority within a particular state who is charged with the overall maintenance of law and order during the election period. The officer’s remit is broad and includes the duty of ensuring that the atmosphere in the state remains conducive to free and fair voting. To achieve this, the State Police Nodal Officer must keep a close eye on the preventive actions that are being taken by district police headquarters, intervene where necessary, and provide guidance to district commanders on matters ranging from crowd control to the prevention of intimidation of voters. In addition, the officer acts as the single channel through which all incidents that could have an impact on the conduct of the polls are reported to the Election Commission. The officer is also tasked with the establishment of a “law and order portal” for the state—an electronic platform where real‑time information on security arrangements, incidents, and resources can be uploaded and monitored. This portal must be functional well before the election schedule is announced, and it must be kept up‑to‑date throughout the pre‑poll, poll and post‑poll phases. The State Police Nodal Officer is further required to submit daily reports to the Election Commission in a prescribed format known as the LOR‑II (Law and Order Report – Phase II), thereby providing the Commission with a continuous flow of data on the security situation. The officer also prepares the State Force Deployment Plan, which details how both central and state police forces will be positioned across the state’s districts. Finally, the officer has overall responsibility for the management of resources, which includes the planning and allocation of personnel, vehicles, communication equipment and other logistical support that will be needed to secure the election.

The third key appointment is the State Force Coordinator, an officer who holds the rank of Inspector General or Additional Director General within the state police hierarchy. This individual’s principal function is to act as the bridge between the Election Commission, the Ministry of Home Affairs and the central armed forces that have been allocated to the state for election duties. The State Force Coordinator prepares a detailed movement plan that outlines how central armed police units will be moved from their home bases to the districts where they are needed, as well as how state police units may be transferred between districts to meet emerging security demands. In addition to the macro‑level movement plan, the coordinator also oversees the formulation and implementation of a “Local Area Domination Plan” prepared by each district. That plan specifies the deployment of security personnel within a district so that every polling station and its surrounding neighbourhood is adequately covered, thereby reducing the chance of any disruption or intimidation.

The fourth officer to be designated deals specifically with matters of election‑related expenditure and the seizure of illicit assets that might be used to influence voters. The Home Department of the state or union territory may appoint a senior police officer, again at the rank of Inspector General or higher, as the nodal officer for election expenditure. This officer works closely with the State Police Nodal Officer and is responsible for executing the directives of the Election Commission that pertain to the confiscation of cash, liquor, contraband and any other inducements that could be offered to voters in violation of the Model Code of Conduct. The officer’s duties include coordinating with the nodal officers of other enforcement agencies—such as the Income Tax Department, the Enforcement Directorate and the customs authorities—to ensure a unified approach to the detection and seizure of prohibited items. While the appointment of a separate officer for election‑expenditure matters is optional, many states elect to retain the State Police Nodal Officer as the single point of contact for this function. Importantly, the Election Commission does not need to grant any special approval for a state to name a police officer to this role, because the Commission’s statutes give the state governments discretion to organize their own enforcement structures.

The fifth and final nodal appointment relates to training. An officer of the rank of Additional Director General who heads the state’s training department is identified as the nodal officer for election‑related training. This officer is tasked with preparing a comprehensive training plan that will be delivered to all security personnel—both the state police and the members of the Central Armed Police Forces who will be deployed in the state. The training plan covers a wide range of topics, including crowd management, the use of non‑lethal weapons, procedures for handling electronic voting machines, protocols for the protection of polling stations and the rights of voters, and the legal framework that governs election security. By ensuring that every officer and constable who will be on duty during the election has received uniform, high‑quality training, the nodal training officer contributes to a consistent and professional security environment across the entire election landscape.

Having outlined the cadre of nodal officers, it is also necessary to identify the specific forces that may be called upon to provide security. The Central Armed Police Forces, which operate under the direct control of the Ministry of Home Affairs, comprise a number of distinct agencies each with its own area of expertise. The Assam Rifles, historically a paramilitary force with a primary role in the northeastern region of the country, is one such agency. The Border Security Force is charged with guarding India’s extensive land borders and preventing transnational crime. The Central Industrial Security Force provides protection to critical infrastructure, including airports, nuclear installations, and major industrial complexes. The Central Reserve Police Force is the largest of the central forces and is deployed for internal security duties, riot control, and disaster response. The Indo‑Tibetan Border Police is responsible for safeguarding the Himalayan frontier with China and for conducting high‑altitude operations. The National Security Guard, often known as the “Black Cats,” is a specialised counter‑terrorism and hostage‑rescue unit. The Sashastra Seema Bal is tasked with securing the borders with Nepal and Bhutan and with preventing cross‑border smuggling. In addition to these named forces, the Ministry of Home Affairs may designate any other security organization as required to meet the specific needs of a particular election.

The process by which the required strength and composition of forces is assessed is perhaps the most critical element of election planning. This assessment must blend a realistic appraisal of the ground situation with an understanding of the political sensitivities of the various stakeholders, which include the state governments, political parties, the Election Commission and the electorate itself. The very act of announcing a schedule of elections is, in itself, a confidence‑building measure, signalling to all participants that the Commission has taken into account the security requirements and is prepared to provide a safe environment for voting.

To gather the necessary information, the Election Commission begins by convening a high‑level meeting that brings together the chief secretaries of all states and union territories, the directors general of police, the home secretaries, the chief electoral officers, and the police nodal officers. In this forum the participants discuss the prevailing law‑and‑order climate, pinpoint regions within each state that may present particular challenges, and deliberate on the number of personnel from the central armed forces that will be needed to supplement the state police. The meeting also serves as an opportunity to review the overall readiness of the election machinery, including the availability of electronic voting machines, transportation logistics, and communication networks.

Following the national‑level discussion, each state conducts a more detailed set of consultations with its own police leadership, telecommunications regulators, and private service providers that operate cellular networks. These discussions aim to map out “mobile shadow zones,” which are areas where the availability of reliable communication is limited and where the risk of misinformation or intimidation may be heightened. By identifying these zones, the authorities can plan for the deployment of additional resources, such as mobile vans equipped with satellite communication, to ensure that election information reaches every voter and that any incidents can be reported in real time.

The Election Commission has issued comprehensive guidelines that prescribe how to calculate the security requirement for any election. These guidelines break down the duties of security forces into several categories: static surveillance teams that monitor polling stations on an ongoing basis, rapid response or “flying squads” that can be dispatched at short notice to address emerging threats, and area‑domination units that establish a visible security presence during the pre‑poll period to deter violence.
