In addition, the guidelines outline the responsibilities for protecting the polling stations themselves, safeguarding the transport of electronic voting machines, and ensuring the safety of the machines after voting has concluded. The identification of which specific forces will fulfill each of these roles is a joint exercise conducted by the Ministry of Home Affairs and the Election Commission, taking into account the unique security profile of each state and the availability of personnel.

Through the coordinated efforts of the nodal officers, the meticulous identification of the forces, and the thorough assessment process, the election administration seeks to create a secure environment that enables every eligible citizen to cast a vote without fear. The detailed planning and advance designation of senior officials not only provide clear lines of authority but also embed a systematic approach to resource allocation, training, and real‑time monitoring, thereby reinforcing the integrity of the democratic process.

The practice of deploying members of the Central Armed Police Forces, commonly abbreviated as CAPF, to oversee the conduct of elections has become an almost routine element of the electoral machinery in the vast majority of Indian states. Over the past few electoral cycles, political parties representing a wide spectrum of ideological positions have consistently insisted on the presence of these centrally recruited police units, arguing that their involvement is essential to guarantee elections that are free from intimidation, fair in their administration, and peaceful in their overall atmosphere. The underlying logic of this demand rests on the perception that officers drawn from the CAPF, being recruited and posted from regions that are not locally rooted, lack any pre‑existing relationships or loyalties that might compromise their impartiality. In other words, because these officers do not belong to the local community, they are thought to be less susceptible to pressure from local political actors, criminal networks, or other vested interests that could otherwise influence the electoral process. This belief has, in turn, generated a growing strain on the national pool of CAPF personnel, especially during periods when multiple states hold elections simultaneously or when a single state conducts both its legislative assembly (often referred to as the “assembly”) and its parliamentary (Lok Sabha) elections in close succession.

In order to alleviate this acute shortage of centrally posted police officers, a policy of inter‑state sharing of state‑level police forces has been introduced. Under this arrangement, police personnel who belong to the regular state police services of one state are temporarily deputed—meaning they are assigned on a temporary basis—to another state that is in the midst of conducting an election. In effect, these state police officers function as if they were members of the CAPF for the duration of the election, thereby expanding the overall pool of trained law‑enforcement personnel available to maintain order at polling stations, safeguard election officials, and manage crowd control. By drawing on the resources of multiple states, the burden on any single state’s CAPF contingent is reduced, and the overall credibility of the electoral process is enhanced because a larger, more diverse security presence can deter local attempts at manipulation or violence.

A key component of this inter‑state sharing framework is the identification of geographic clusters, known as zones, within which states agree to exchange police forces. The creation of these zones is intended to facilitate the rapid and organized deployment of personnel, taking into account factors such as the relative proximity of the states, the similarity of their administrative procedures, and the historical patterns of cooperation between their police services. The zones have been delineated into five broad groupings. The first, often referred to as the East Zone, comprises the states of Bihar, Odisha, Jharkhand, West Bengal, Sikkim, and Chhattisgarh. The second, the North‑East Zone, includes the eight states that make up the northeastern region of the country: Assam, Nagaland, Meghalaya, Manipur, Mizoram, Tripura, and Arunachal Pradesh. The third, designated the West Zone, brings together Rajasthan, Gujarat, the union territories of Daman & Diu and Dadra and Nagar Haveli, Maharashtra, Madhya Pradesh, and Goa. The fourth, the North Zone, consists of Jammu and Kashmir, Himachal Pradesh, Punjab, Uttarakhand, Uttar Pradesh, Haryana, Delhi, and the union territory of Chandigarh. Finally, the South Zone incorporates Andhra Pradesh, Telangana, Karnataka, Kerala, Tamil Nadu, Puducherry, as well as the island groups of the Andaman & Nicobar Islands and Lakshadweep. Within each of these zones, the participating states are expected to prepare a comprehensive outline that describes how a zonal reserve of state police personnel can be assembled. This outline must take into account the total strength of the police force in each individual state, the proportion of officers that can be spared without jeopardizing routine law‑and‑order duties, and the specific number of officers that can be offered to another state within the same zone for election‑related duties.

When elections are held simultaneously in more than one state within a given zone, the demand for additional police personnel inevitably rises. In such circumstances, the states must negotiate the exact numbers of officers to be exchanged, ensuring that each state’s own security requirements are still met while providing sufficient manpower to the election‑hosting state. It is important to note that, as a general rule, these shared state police forces are to be deployed only in areas that are not classified as “LWE” or “low‑weaponized extremist” zones. LWE is an abbreviation used to describe regions that are affected by left‑wing extremism, commonly associated with Maoist insurgency, as well as other forms of militancy and insurgency that may be present in certain parts of the country. The rationale for restricting deployment to non‑LWE areas is that police officers from a different state may lack the specialized training, local knowledge, and operational experience required to safely operate in high‑risk insurgency environments.

Nevertheless, there are circumstances in which a state’s specialized police unit that has received dedicated training for anti‑Naxal (i.e., anti‑Maoist) operations may be called upon to assist in an LWE‑designated constituency of another state. Such deployment is permissible only after the Election Commission of India (ECI) has granted explicit permission, and only after the receiving state has taken appropriate precautions to ensure that the officers are fully briefed on the local dynamics of the insurgency, the terrain, and the rules of engagement. The deployment of specialized anti‑Naxal units across state lines therefore requires a higher level of oversight and coordination.

In order to prepare state police officers for election duties outside their home state, a comprehensive pre‑induction training program must be conducted. This training is overseen by the designated State Police Nodal Officer, who is the senior official responsible for coordinating police participation in the election, as well as by the State Police Nodal Officer (CAPF), who acts as the liaison between the state police service and the central police forces. The training curriculum covers a range of topics, including the specific role of police officers in the electoral process, the legal framework governing elections, the customs and cultural practices of the host state, a detailed briefing on the geography and demographics of the polling areas to which the officers will be assigned, and a review of standard operating procedures (SOPs) that govern the conduct of election‑related policing. By ensuring that each officer receives a thorough orientation, the authorities aim to minimize confusion, reduce the likelihood of misunderstandings, and promote a uniform standard of conduct across all jurisdictions.

Beyond the training of individual officers, the inter‑state sharing mechanism also calls for the establishment of joint coordination bodies. A Joint Coordination Committee (JCC) is to be constituted for each election, comprising the Chief Election Officer (CEO) or an additional CEO of the election‑conducting state, the State Police Nodal Officer, a State CAPF coordinator, and a force coordinator appointed by the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA). This committee is tasked with overseeing all aspects of the cooperation, ranging from the logistical arrangements required to move personnel and equipment across state boundaries, to the operational planning that determines where officers will be posted, to the welfare considerations that ensure the health, safety, and morale of the deputed officers. The JCC meets at regular intervals—often weekly or bi‑weekly—depending on the timeline of the election, and it develops a detailed plan that outlines the steps involved in mobilizing the police forces, inducting them into the host state’s election apparatus, deploying them to the appropriate polling stations, overseeing their day‑to‑day activities during the voting period, and finally de‑inducting them after the conclusion of the electoral process.

To support the continuous flow of information and to resolve any emergent issues in real time, a Central Communication and Coordination Centre (CCCC) is set up in the capital city of the state or union territory that is holding the elections. This centre operates around the clock, maintaining a 24‑hour communication network that links the various levels of the police hierarchy, the election officials, and the central agencies responsible for security. The centre acts as a nerve centre, receiving reports from field officers, dispatching instructions, coordinating the movement of reinforcements if needed, and ensuring that any problems—such as breakdowns in transport, medical emergencies, or unexpected security incidents—are addressed promptly.

A further essential element of the overall security plan is the profiling of each assembly constituency and the individual polling stations within it. The District Election Officer (DEO) and the State Police (SP) are jointly responsible for compiling a security profile that synthesizes a variety of inputs. These inputs include law‑and‑order assessments that reflect the current crime trends and the intelligence reports gathered by the police, the identification of villages, hamlets, or settlements that are considered vulnerable due to past incidents of violence or communal tension, an evaluation of how critical a particular constituency is based on its history in previous elections and its present political climate, an analysis of the ease or difficulty of communication at the polling station—particularly whether the area is a “communication shadow” where mobile or internet connectivity is limited—and any other specific information that may be relevant, such as recent incidents of political intimidation or threats against candidates.

In parallel with the security profiling, an infrastructure profile of each constituency is compiled. This profile details the topographical characteristics of the area—whether it is dominated by riverine features, hilly terrain, dense forest cover, desert landscape, or a combination thereof. It also records the physical infrastructure that will affect the movement of election personnel and security forces, such as the condition of roads, the presence of bridges, the availability of public buildings that may serve as polling stations, and any logistical challenges that may arise due to the geography. These infrastructure details are crucial for planning the routes that police and election staff will take, for ensuring that there are adequate facilities for the storage of voting equipment, and for anticipating any potential bottlenecks that could impede the smooth conduct of the vote.

When it comes to constituencies that are classified as LWE or insurgency‑affected areas, the profiling process takes on additional layers of complexity. Security profiling in these zones involves categorizing the extent of extremist influence, ranging from “most vulnerable” to “vulnerable,” based on the level of activity of left‑wing extremist groups, the frequency of attacks, and the degree of disruption to normal civil life. The terminology used to describe these categories may differ from state to state, but the underlying purpose remains the same: to provide a clear, data‑driven picture of the security environment so that appropriate police contingents can be allocated. Infrastructure profiling in LWE areas follows the same parameters as in non‑LWE regions, with an added focus on the condition of critical infrastructure such as roads and bridges that may be targeted by insurgents, and on the availability of safe routes for both police and civilian movement.

Having gathered comprehensive security and infrastructure profiles for each district, the next step is to assess the availability of police forces and other departmental personnel who can be designated as special police officers for election duty. This involves taking stock of the total number of police officers stationed in the district, as well as any additional staff from other government departments—such as the forest service, the municipal corporation, or the revenue department—who may have relevant training or who can be temporarily assigned to assist in election security. Once the pool of available personnel is identified, a preliminary force deployment plan is drafted. This plan specifies, for each polling station within the district, the number of police officers and ancillary staff that will be required, the composition of the forces in terms of regular police versus Central Armed Police Force personnel, and any specialized units that may need to be deployed in areas with higher risk. The plan also outlines the timing of deployment, the sequence of movements, and the contingency measures that will be activated if the security situation deteriorates.

Overall, the strategy of pooling state police forces for use in non‑LWE states represents a pragmatic response to the twin challenges of maintaining electoral integrity and managing the limited resources of the central police agencies. By organizing states into logical zones, establishing joint coordination committees, creating dedicated communication hubs, and conducting thorough profiling of both security risks and physical infrastructure, the authorities aim to construct a resilient and adaptable security architecture. This architecture is intended not only to safeguard the immediate conduct of elections but also to foster public confidence in the democratic process, demonstrating that the state possesses the capacity to protect the right of every citizen to vote in a safe and orderly environment, regardless of the geographical or political complexities that may be present.

The preliminary plan for the deployment of security forces during an election is inherently a living document that must be able to adapt to a range of changing circumstances. Because the availability of the Central Armed Police Forces, commonly abbreviated as CAPF, can vary from one moment to the next, the plan cannot be fixed in stone. It must be responsive to unforeseen incidents of law and order disturbance, to unexpected spikes in communal tension, to the emergence of high‑profile or particularly important candidates whose presence may attract additional security requirements, and to public health emergencies such as the COVID‑19 pandemic. In practice, this means that the authorities responsible for the plan must continuously monitor the on‑ground situation and be prepared to revise the allocation of personnel, vehicles, and equipment as new information becomes available.

At the level of individual states, the responsibility for preparing the deployment plan lies with the State Police Nodal Officer, often abbreviated as SPNO, or the State Force Coordinator. These officials must produce two separate schedules: one for areas that are classified as law‑and‑order sensitive, sometimes referred to as LWE (Law and Order/Violence‑prone or Extremist) zones, and another for constituencies that are considered non‑LWE. The preparation of these schedules is undertaken in close consultation with the Chief Electoral Officer, known as the CEO, who is the senior election official in the state. Moving to the district tier, the District Magistrate, designated as DM, together with the Superintendent of Police, or SP, assumes the same task of drafting distinct deployment plans for LWE and non‑LWE areas within the district, should any such distinction exist. The dual‑track approach ensures that resources are concentrated where they are most needed while avoiding unnecessary concentration in quieter regions.

To coordinate the myriad agencies that will be called upon during the election, a State Level Coordination Group is created by the CEO. This group is composed of a range of officials whose collective expertise spans law enforcement, rural and urban development, education, forestry, and transportation. The CEO nominates an Additional or Joint CEO to serve on the group, and all senior police officers who serve as nodal points for the election are included as well. Representatives from the Panchayat and Rural Development Department, the Urban Development Department, the School Education Department, the Forest Department, and the Transport Department also take seats at the table; the list is intentionally not exhaustive, allowing each state to add further departments that reflect its particular needs and challenges. The purpose of this coordination group is threefold: first, to identify police personnel within each department who can be designated as Special Police Officers for election duties; second, to evaluate the security‑related assets—such as vehicles, communications equipment, and specialized units—that each department can contribute to the election effort; and third, to pool both human and material resources, thereby creating a force multiplier effect and ensuring effective liaison among the participating agencies.

The overarching structure for the final force deployment plan follows a matrix that aligns the state, district, assembly constituency, and polling station levels. This matrix takes into account several layers of criticality. Physical infrastructure, including common set‑ups such as police outposts or temporary command centers, is examined alongside topographical considerations like hills, rivers, or forested terrain that may affect movement. The profile of each candidate, especially after the filing of nominations, is also a factor because certain candidates may attract larger crowds or heightened security concerns. From the moment the election is announced until the final counting is completed, a series of coordinated actions must be undertaken. These actions begin with the determination of guiding principles for force deployment, which are set after thorough consultation with a host of officials, including the District Electoral Officer who also serves as the DM, the Commissioner of Police, the Senior Superintendent of Police, the State Police Nodal Officer, the Director General of Police, the Home Secretary, and representatives of the Election Commission of India. Following this strategic framing, a detailed security deployment plan for both state and district levels is drafted based on an assessment of what forces are available and what the anticipated requirements will be. The plan then calls for the requisition of CAPF units or other central forces from the Ministry of Home Affairs and the Election Commission. In practice, the arrival of the CAPF is staged: one third of the allotted companies—each company being a sub‑unit of the force—are expected to reach the designated area roughly a month before polling day to establish a visible presence and carry out area domination, while the remaining two thirds are to be deployed about a week before the election, ensuring that sufficient manpower is on hand for the final stages of the operation.

Regular coordination meetings at the state level are convened in the CEO’s office, bringing together the State Police Nodal Officer, the State Nodal Officer from the Election Commission, and representatives of the CAPF to monitor progress and resolve any emerging issues. In areas where there is a heightened risk of communal violence, a rapid response unit known as the Rapid Action Force may be deputed to provide a swift and specialized reaction capability. Border security considerations also come into play; meetings are held with officials from neighboring states and, where applicable, with the authorities of bordering countries to discuss the sealing of inter‑state borders and the prevention of cross‑border infiltration. The deployment plan must also factor in the logistical challenge of moving forces in phases, the need to sanitize LWE areas in line with health guidelines, and the timing of local festivals or communal events that could affect crowd behavior. Special attention is given to the placement of polling stations in difficult terrain such as riverine islands, hilly districts, or forested zones, where accessibility can be an issue. Additional specialized resources—such as the National Disaster Response Force, the State Disaster Response Force, mounted police units, bomb disposal squads, and dedicated vehicles for the CAPF throughout the election period—are also requisitioned. Financial oversight is ensured by earmarking specific CAPF expenditures, and all personnel undergo pre‑induction training to familiarize themselves with election‑related duties. Air assets, including helicopters, air ambulances, and satellite phones, are secured to provide rapid medical evacuation and reliable communications in remote areas. A strategic communications plan is formulated for regions that fall into “shadow zones,” where normal telecommunications coverage is poor; this involves regular liaison with the Telecom Department, the state-owned Bharat Sanchar Nigam Limited, and other service providers. Photographic documentation of CAPF deployment in front of each polling station, as well as site visits to vulnerable locations, are part of the verification process. Different CAPF units are assigned duties according to their core competencies—for instance, the Central Industrial Security Force or the Railway Protection Force may be tasked with safeguarding strong rooms that hold ballot boxes, while the Border Security Force or the Indo‑Tibetan Border Police could be deployed to highly volatile LWE zones. Ongoing monitoring of law and order includes the enforcement of night‑time curfews that have been in place for more than six months, the seizure of illicit liquor, the collection of illegal firearms, the imposition of fines under the Motor Vehicles Act, the execution of bonds under the Criminal Procedure Code against troublemakers, and the issuance of orders for externalisation or preventive detention of habitual offenders. The state’s administration also issues directives to ensure uniform distribution of essential supplies such as food, dry rations, or cash assistance throughout the election period, and dedicates a fleet of vehicles exclusively for the CAPF’s use.

An additional layer of operational security is the randomization of state police personnel at polling stations on election day. The objective of randomization is to prevent any appearance of bias or collusion by ensuring that officers do not serve in the same police precincts where they are ordinarily stationed. Personnel drawn from outside the district—such as police constables or home guards—can be deployed without the need for randomization because they have no local ties. For officers who are resident within the district, their names are cross‑checked against a computerized human‑resource management system maintained by the Home Department, if such a system exists, to verify the integrity of the deployment lists. When constables or home guards belong to the same district as the polling station, the practice is to assign them to stations that lie outside the jurisdiction of their regular police station, thereby minimizing the risk of familiar relationships influencing the conduct of the poll. Whenever feasible, the randomization process also seeks to place officers in polling stations that fall within the same parliamentary constituency where those officers are registered voters, thus enabling them to exercise their franchise using an electronic voting device, which further reinforces the principle of impartiality.

Finally, the amount of security force required for any election is not a one‑size‑fits‑all figure; it depends on the specific nature of the election, the geographic spread of the constituencies, the prevailing law‑and‑order climate, the presence of high‑profile candidates, and the logistical challenges posed by terrain and infrastructure. By carefully assessing these variables and by integrating the diverse capabilities of central and state agencies, the election administration can construct a flexible, responsive, and well‑coordinated deployment plan that safeguards the integrity of the democratic process while protecting voters, candidates, and election staff.

The magnitude of the security and police deployment that is required for any Indian election is not a fixed figure; rather, it changes according to the type of election being held, the particular geographical area involved, and the prevailing law‑and‑order conditions. When a state is called upon to conduct by‑elections for individual Assembly Constituencies, which are the legislative districts that elect members to the state legislatures, or for individual Parliamentary Constituencies, which send representatives to the lower house of the national Parliament, the number of officers and constables that must be posted to each polling venue is generally lower than the figure that would be required for a full‑scale State Assembly General Election, in which every constituency in the state votes on a single day or over a series of phases. The most extensive requirements arise during a Lok Sabha General Election, the nationwide contest that determines the composition of the lower house of the Parliament of India, because in that scenario the entire country votes, often in multiple phases, and the logistical and security challenges are multiplied many times over. In each of these situations, the state government that is responsible for conducting the poll must devise a State Force Deployment Plan. This plan is a detailed blueprint that takes into account the existing law‑and‑order situation in the state, the number of election phases that have been scheduled, a systematic mapping of areas that are vulnerable to violence or disruption, an assessment of how critical each location is in terms of its potential impact on the overall election process, and the inventory of police and auxiliary forces that are actually available at the time the plan is drawn up.

The guidelines that have been issued for preparing these deployment plans are intended to be suggestive rather than mandatory. In practice, the final determination of how many personnel will be posted where is made by two senior officials: the Chief Electoral Officer (CEO), who is the senior election administrator in the state, and the State Police Nodal Officer (SPNO), who is the senior police officer responsible for coordinating election‑related policing. Both of these officials must act in accordance with the instructions that come from the Election Commission of India, the constitutional authority that supervises all elections in the country. At a minimum, the plan must provide for the posting of at least one un‑armed police constable together with one member of the Home Guard or a Special Police Officer at every location where a polling station is set up. The Home Guard is a volunteer force that assists the regular police in maintaining public order, while a Special Police Officer is a person who has been granted temporary police powers for the specific purpose of the election. In addition, if a polling station has been identified as “critical”—that is, if it is situated in an area that is prone to communal tension, insurgent activity, or any other form of disruption—a second constable who is armed must also be posted. The presence of an armed constable adds a deterrent effect and enables the security team to respond more effectively to any violent incident that might arise. If a polling location houses more than one polling station, which can happen in densely populated urban wards or in large rural panchayats where many voters are assigned to a single site, the number of officers must be increased proportionately so that each polling station has the requisite minimum staffing.

In regions that are not affected by left‑wing extremism (often abbreviated LWE) or by other forms of militancy or insurgency, the deployment of forces from the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) and the State Armed Police (SAP) follows a set of specific recommendations. The CAPF are a group of federal police agencies that include, among others, the Border Security Force, the Central Reserve Police Force, the Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, the Central Industrial Security Force, and the Sashastra Seema Bal. These forces are usually called upon for duties that demand a higher level of training, better equipment, and greater mobility than those of the regular state police. For a polling location that has been classified as a critical polling station, the guidelines advise that at least half of a CAPF section—typically consisting of ten to twelve personnel—should be deployed, if it is feasible to do so. This half‑section is often broken down further into specialized units: Flying Squads, which are mobile teams capable of rapid response and quick movement across the terrain; Static Surveillance Teams, which are tasked with maintaining a constant watch over a particular area; and Quick Reaction Teams, which are small, highly trained groups that can be summoned at a moment’s notice to intervene in emergencies. Moreover, at all locations where the electronic voting machines (EVMs) that have been used to cast votes are stored after the polls close—known as the strong rooms—and at the counting centres where the votes are tabulated, a full platoon of CAPF personnel must be on duty around the clock. A platoon usually comprises roughly thirty to forty soldiers, and in the election context it is organized into three shifts so that there is continuous coverage 24 hours a day, seven days a week.

When the state’s own police forces are unable to meet the demand for armed personnel at critical polling stations, the state may also call upon the State Armed Police or the Indian Reserve Battalion (IRB), which is a component of the state police that has been specially trained and equipped to handle situations that require a higher level of force. The decision regarding how many SAP or IRB officers will be deployed is taken jointly by the SPNO and the CEO, after a careful assessment of the criticality of each location and the resources that are currently available. The same rule of scaling up the numbers applies if a polling site contains more than one polling station. Personnel drawn from the SAP or IRB may be assigned to the same types of specialized tasks as the CAPF: they can be organized into static surveillance teams that keep a watchful eye on vulnerable spots, into flying squads that patrol the surrounding neighbourhoods, into inter‑state border check posts where voters and officials cross from one state to another, into inter‑district check posts that monitor movement within a state, into quick reaction teams that respond instantly to any breach, and even into roles such as police support officers who assist election observers in maintaining order at the polling stations.

The requirements are considerably more demanding in areas that are affected by left‑wing extremism, which are regions where insurgent groups that follow Marxist–Leninist ideologies have a presence and may attempt to disrupt the electoral process. In such zones, the deployment of the Central Armed Police Forces is intensified. A critical polling station in an LWE‑affected area should, wherever possible, be protected by at least one full section of CAPF, which is a larger unit than a half‑section and generally consists of twenty to twenty‑four personnel. As before, this section should be broken down into flying squads, static surveillance teams, and quick reaction teams to ensure that the security presence is both flexible and responsive. In addition, a full company of CAPF—typically made up of one hundred to one‑hundred‑and‑fifty personnel—must be stationed on a continuous 24‑hour basis at every strong room where the polled EVMs are kept and at each counting centre where the results are compiled. The company is organized into three shifts, each shift lasting eight hours, so that there is always a fresh and alert contingent on duty. The state may also augment the CAPF presence with SAP or IRB personnel, following the same procedure of joint decision‑making between the SPNO and the CEO. The number of these additional officers will again be calibrated to the level of criticality identified for each polling location and will be increased if a polling site contains multiple polling stations. The SAP and IRB officers may be tasked with the same range of duties as the CAPF: they can serve in static surveillance teams, in flying squads that patrol the area, at inter‑state and inter‑district border check posts, in quick reaction teams ready to intervene at a moment’s notice, and as police support officers who assist election observers and help maintain order at the polling venues.

Beyond the responsibilities that pertain directly to the conduct of voting on the day of the poll, the deployment plan must also incorporate detailed arrangements for the counting phase of the election, which is the period when the sealed electronic voting machines are opened and the votes recorded on them are tallied. The final deployment plan therefore includes a specific component that deals with the guarding of the EVM strong rooms by CAPF personnel from the moment the machines are collected after the polls close until the completion of the counting operation. This guarding duty involves securing the innermost compartment of the counting centre, often referred to as the “cardon,” where the actual counting takes place, to prevent any unauthorized access or tampering. In parallel, a deployment plan for the state police at the counting centres must be prepared. The state police are charged with preventing entry by any person who does not have official authorization, thereby ensuring that only duly appointed election officials, observers, and accredited personnel are allowed inside. They must also station frisking personnel at the entrances, including women police officers or women Home Guard volunteers, so that any items that could be used to disrupt the counting process can be checked in a gender‑sensitive manner. Additionally, executive magistrates—senior officials appointed by the state government with the authority to maintain public order—must be present to manage crowd movement, to coordinate any necessary evacuations, and to oversee the orderly conduct of the counting. After the results are announced, the same magistrates, together with police personnel, are responsible for regulating victory processions, which are public celebrations that can sometimes turn rowdy if not properly supervised.

To summarize the entire process of formulating both the state‑level and district‑level deployment plans, there is a sequence of steps that must be followed meticulously. First, the district administration, which includes the District Collector, the Superintendent of Police, and other local officials, carries out an assessment of the local situation, evaluating the specific security challenges, the geography, the history of electoral violence, and any other factors that could affect the smooth conduct of the election. Second, the office of the Chief Electoral Officer and the nodal officers who are responsible for law and order engage in a dialogue with the district machinery to exchange information and to fine‑tune the assessment of requirements based on the latest intelligence. Third, comprehensive data are gathered on a wide array of parameters: the vulnerability and criticality of each polling location, the availability of police and auxiliary forces, the presence of communication “shadow areas” where signal coverage is poor, zones where historically voter turnout has been low, especially among women, known trouble spots where past incidents have occurred, and the overall manpower that can be mobilized. This data collection and analysis form the factual foundation on which the final numbers of police constables, armed officers, Home Guard volunteers, and specialized units are decided. By adhering to this structured methodology, the election authorities aim to ensure that each polling station, each strong room, and each counting centre is protected by an appropriate and proportionate security presence, thereby safeguarding the integrity of the democratic process and guaranteeing that the will of the electorate is expressed without intimidation or interference.

The preparation of the election‑related security arrangements begins with a systematic compilation of data at the state level. The first task is to assemble a comprehensive database that draws together all the information gathered from the various districts. This database includes details of the number of police personnel available, the locations of polling stations, the identification of vulnerable areas, and any intelligence on anti‑social elements that may pose a threat to the peaceful conduct of the election. Once the data have been collated, the authorities prepare an indicative deployment plan for the state. This plan is a provisional document that outlines, in a tentative manner, how the state police forces will be allocated across the different regions and types of duties that will be required on election day. The indicative plan serves as a framework for subsequent discussions with the district‑level machinery, which includes district election officers, senior police officials, and other officials responsible for the conduct of the poll in each district. The purpose of these discussions is to refine the provisional allocations, taking into account local conditions, specific security challenges, and the capacity of each district to manage its own responsibilities.

After the consultations are completed, the state deployment plan is firmed up. The final plan is issued in a form that is also indicative for the districts, but it now clearly spells out the minimum numbers of personnel that must be deployed for each category of duty. The categories include static deployment, which refers to personnel who are posted at fixed locations such as polling stations or strong rooms; mobile deployment, which involves officers who are assigned to patrol routes or respond to incidents; police‑station deployment, which ensures that each police station has a requisite number of officers available for election‑related duties; and reserve deployment, which sets aside a small contingent of officers who can be called upon if an unexpected situation arises. In addition to the state police, the plan also incorporates the allocation of central forces, which are the paramilitary and central armed police forces that operate under the authority of the Union Government. These central forces are assigned to the state only after the Election Commission of India has communicated its decision to deploy them, a process that is referred to as intimating by the ECI.

The deployment plan is crafted in such a way that several strategic objectives are achieved. First, it ensures that no central force personnel are kept in reserve; all central troops are put to active use, thereby maximizing the contribution of these forces to the election security effort. Second, the plan is designed so that at least eighty percent of the state police personnel are utilized, which reflects the principle that the primary responsibility for maintaining law and order lies with the state’s own police service. Third, the plan seeks to assign the maximum possible number of central force personnel to static duties at polling stations, because the presence of centrally recruited, highly trained officers at the voting locations adds an extra layer of security and confidence for the electorate. Fourth, the plan guarantees that those police stations that are considered critical—because of their size, location in a sensitive area, or history of trouble—are covered by central forces, thereby providing a stronger deterrent against any attempts at intimidation or violence. Finally, the plan calls for the utilization of Home Guards and related auxiliary forces for the specific purpose of maintaining orderly queues at polling stations, while the state police are tasked with the broader responsibility of maintaining law and order throughout the constituency, district, and state.

To assist officials in preparing the detailed deployment documents, an indicative format has been annexed to the procedural guidelines. This format comprises four tables that serve as templates for both state‑level and district‑level deployment plans that do not involve the deployment of the Left Wing Extremism (LWE) forces. The first table records the location details of each polling station, providing a geographic reference for security planning. The second table enumerates the strength of the state police force, broken down rank‑wise, which allows planners to match the appropriate level of seniority and capability with the tasks at hand. The third table presents the district security deployment plan, outlining how resources will be distributed within each district. The fourth table outlines the overall state security deployment plan, integrating the district plans into a cohesive state‑wide strategy. These tables are referenced in Annexures IX through XII and serve as practical tools for translating the high‑level policy objectives into operational realities on the ground.

The actual deployment of forces proceeds through a series of defined stages, each with its own set of responsibilities and timelines. In the pre‑poll‑day phase, the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) are required to arrive in the area well in advance of the election. Their early arrival enables them to conduct flag marches, which are ceremonial processions designed to demonstrate a visible security presence and thereby dominate the area psychologically. These flag marches are complemented by point patrolling, a method of assigning officers to specific, strategically important points for continuous observation, and other confidence‑building measures that aim to reassure the local population that the election environment is safe. The CAPF units coordinate closely with the local police under the guidance of the District Election Officer (DEO) and the Superintendent of Police (SP). They are supplied with an area‑wise list of anti‑social elements—individuals or groups known to have a propensity for disruptive or violent behavior—along with a list of vulnerable hamlets, which are small settlements that may be at higher risk due to geographic isolation, socioeconomic factors, or a history of political tension. The list also identifies critical polling stations that require heightened security.

In order to execute these tasks, specific teams within the CAPF are constituted. These teams carry out the flag marches, conduct point patrolling, and perform sanitation duties, especially in areas affected by Left Wing Extremism, where the presence of insurgent activities may require additional cleaning and security measures to prevent the spread of misinformation or physical hazards. The CAPF also engages in direct interaction with residents of vulnerable hamlets, often on foot, to build trust and gather local intelligence that can inform further security actions. Moreover, the central forces are deployed to man check‑posts, which are fixed points where vehicles and individuals are inspected, to prevent the illegal movement of cash, liquor, drugs, and any unaccounted election material that political parties or candidates might attempt to smuggle into the polling areas.

The state police, meanwhile, assume a complementary set of duties that are geared toward ensuring comprehensive coverage of security needs. In situations where the CAPF presence is deemed insufficient to dominate the area, the state police step in to fill the gap, undertaking area domination themselves. They are also responsible for guarding the strong rooms where Electronic Voting Machines (EVMs) are stored before the polling, as well as the warehouses that house the machines. The state police conduct vulnerability mapping, a systematic process of identifying locations that are susceptible to disruption or attack, and they take preventive measures based on the findings of this mapping. They enforce the Model Code of Conduct, which is a set of guidelines issued by the Election Commission to regulate the behavior of political parties and candidates during the election period, thereby preventing the misuse of government machinery and ensuring a level playing field. The police establish check‑posts to stop the illegal flow of cash, liquor, drugs, and the movement of anti‑social elements, thereby curbing the potential for election‑related corruption or violence. Their overarching duty is to maintain general law and order in the catchment area of each polling station as well as in the broader constituency, district, and state. The state police also monitor election expenditure to ensure compliance with the prescribed limits and prevent undue influence on voters. They are tasked with handling the movement of Very Important Persons (VIPs) who may travel to the area for campaign or official purposes, ensuring that such movements do not disrupt the electoral process. Finally, the state police implement the deployment plan, coordinating the placement of personnel as outlined, and they provide logistical support to the CAPF, supplying them with essential resources such as fuel, food, and communication equipment.

On the day of the poll and in the immediate period following it, both the CAPF and the state police continue to play distinct yet interlocking roles. The CAPF’s responsibilities on polling day include the guarding of polling stations themselves, the strong rooms that contain the EVMs equipped with Voter‑Verifiable Paper Audit Trails (VVPAT), and the escort of the polled EVMs as they are transported to counting centres. They also guard all poll materials, including the machines, the poll personnel, and the overall polling process, thereby ensuring the integrity of the vote. In addition to static guarding of identified trouble spots—areas that have historically experienced disturbances—the CAPF undertakes patrolling duties on assigned routes that cover a fixed cluster of polling stations, providing a mobile security presence that can respond quickly to any incident. They also operate as “flying squads,” which are rapid response units that patrol a defined area with an element of surprise, ready to intervene at short notice in case of emerging threats. After the polling has concluded, the CAPF remains responsible for the security of the strong rooms that house the polled EVMs, safeguarding them until the counting process is complete.

The state police, during the polling day, maintain general law and order not only in the vicinity of the polling stations but also inside the polling premises and in the surrounding areas. They are charged with ensuring the safety of voters, poll workers, and the poll materials, thereby creating an environment in which citizens can exercise their franchise without fear. The police continue to enforce the Model Code of Conduct, monitoring political activity to prevent violations such as the use of government resources for campaign purposes. They also keep up the Election Expenditure Monitoring Guidelines, checking that political parties adhere to spending limits. The state police provide security for the polled EVM strong rooms, establishing an intermediate and outer cordon that forms a layered protection system around the counting centre, which is often overseen by the district police force. They also oversee the security of the EVM warehouses where machines are stored before being dispatched to the polling stations.

Following the conclusion of the polling, the focus shifts to the counting day. On this day, the CAPF takes on the role of forming the third and innermost cordon around the strong rooms and counting centres, providing the highest level of protection to the place where votes are tallied. The state police, in turn, are responsible for the intermediate and outer cordons, creating a broader security perimeter that deters any attempts at external interference. In addition to these protective duties, the state police are tasked with managing the victory procession, a public celebration that follows the announcement of election results. The procession often involves large crowds and high‑profile political leaders, and it requires careful crowd management, traffic control, and the maintenance of public order to prevent any incidents.

The entire security preparation is guided by a detailed activity and timeline schedule that outlines when each step should be undertaken and who is responsible for it. Six months before the scheduled poll date, the District Election Officer and the Superintendent of Police begin a preliminary vulnerability mapping exercise. This analysis identifies the polling stations that are most at risk and the specific threats that may affect them. Four months before the poll, the officers carry out verification of arms shops, execute a National Banned Weapons (NBW) check, review past electoral offences, and conduct raids on illicit liquor operations, all of which are measures intended to reduce the availability of illegal weapons and substances that could be used to disrupt the election. Between six and four months prior to the polling day, the state officials assess the availability of the state police force and determine the quantity of CAPF personnel required. They then communicate these requirements to the Election Commission of India, which in turn decides on the allocation of central forces. The Chief Electoral Officer (CEO) and the Senior Police Nodal Officer (SPNO) oversee this process, ensuring that the necessary resources are identified and secured well before the election.

All of these measures, taken together, constitute a comprehensive framework for safeguarding the democratic process. By integrating detailed data collection, careful planning, close coordination between central and state agencies, and a phased deployment of forces that addresses both static and mobile security needs, the authorities aim to create an environment in which elections can be conducted peacefully, fairly, and without intimidation. The use of Home Guards for queue management, the emphasis on utilizing a high proportion of state police while reserving central forces for critical tasks, and the systematic timeline that stretches from months before the poll to the counting day all reflect a meticulous approach designed to uphold the integrity of the electoral system and to protect the fundamental right of citizens to cast their votes without fear.

Election DEO/SP in consultation with General/Police observer. I. Intra state/Interstate movement plan in phased elections

Where elections in the state are to be held in phases, or where CAPF and state police has to be deployed in another state in the subsequent phase of elections, movement of CAPF and State police from one phase to the other should be made a sub part of the final deployment plan. This movement plan, finalized by the state force coordinator should be included in the final deployment plan, to facilitate smooth transition of forces from one phase to the other. The CAPF and the state forces should be briefed about the movement plan during their training sessions. This movement plan should inter alia include the following:

a) Proposed date of transportation. b) Number of vehicles required for transportation and strategies for requisition (within the district/ from elsewhere in the state)

c) Details of lodging and messing facilities/halts in the transit route. d) Post transportation, details of relieving of vehicles requisitioned, including payment of hire charges to vehicle owners. e) The above-mentioned sub plan should be submitted by the SP to the state force coordinator and shared with his counterpart in the concerned District/ state. J. State Force Coordinator’s coordination with ECI, MHA, Railways, etc. for CAPF

The Force coordinator shall coordinate actively with the following:

a) For ensuring the following:

(i) Approval of the final state deployment plan from ECI. (ii) Requisition of CAPF forces from MHA, allotted to the state after National level deliberations. (iii) Issuing of state specific instructions from ECI and MHA to CAPF, from outside the state. b) Ministry of Railways, for ensuring the following:

(i) Special trains for movement of CAPF forces into the state. (ii) Allotment of adequate number of Coaches in special trains for safe and comfortable long journey of forces. (iii) Priority needs to be accorded to Election special trains to ensure timely availability of forces in respective States. (iv) Identification of safe and convenient points of disembarkation of forces in the state. (v) Special messing facilities for forces on trains. (vi) Arrangement of special trains by railways & food by IRCTC. (vii) Single window booking system in Railways

c) Ministry of Defence for ensuring the following

(i) Availability of Air Force helicopters, for transportation of polling personnel and security forces in difficult areas/LWE areas. d) Civil Aviation Ministry for ensuring the following

(i) Availability of aircraft for use as air ambulance in medical emergencies during the elections. e) Shipping Ministry for ensuring the following:

(i) Availability of boats/ ships for transportation of polling personnel and security forces in coastal areas/islands. f) State government for ensuring the following:

(i) Preparing force’s movement plan within the state. (ii) Induction and de-induction of forces

(iii) Training of forces, to be conducted by nodal police officer (Training)

K. Role of State Nodal Police Officer

a) Role related to Logistics, Database and Welfare

(i) Creation of database of SPF& other forces with details of registration in the PER, telephone nos. etc. (ii) Earmarking of accommodations and other logistics for troops till final de-induction.

The planning and execution of electoral security in India involve a multitude of inter‑related actions that must be coordinated among central and state agencies, the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF), the state police, and a host of other stakeholders. One of the essential components of this framework is the provision of comprehensive medical support for the personnel who will be deployed in sensitive and often high‑risk environments. This support includes the earmarking of super‑specialty hospitals that are equipped to handle complex medical cases, the arrangement of cashless treatment facilities so that injured officers can receive immediate care without the delay of financial formalities, and the establishment of air‑ambulance services, which may involve both fixed‑wing aircraft and helicopter platforms capable of rapid evacuation from remote or congested areas. By guaranteeing such medical cover, the authorities aim to reduce the physical and psychological stress on the forces, thereby maintaining operational readiness throughout the election period.

Another critical measure is the allocation of a unique identification number to every member of the armed forces and police units that are assigned to election duty. This identifier functions much like a personal badge that can be used to track deployment, verify credentials at checkpoints, and streamline logistical processes such as the distribution of equipment, rationing of supplies, and the recording of incidents. In addition, the timing of the pre‑poll deployment of forces is calibrated to the perceived threat level in each constituency. Intelligence assessments, historical data on communal tensions, and real‑time inputs from local law enforcement help determine when and where troops should be positioned so that they can intervene before any disturbance escalates.

A robust communication setup is equally indispensable. Modern elections require secure, reliable, and redundant channels of information flow between field units, command headquarters, and civilian authorities. This includes the use of encrypted radio networks, satellite links, and dedicated data lines that can support voice, video, and text communications under adverse conditions. The establishment of such infrastructure ensures that commanders receive timely situational reports and can issue directives promptly, thereby enhancing the overall effectiveness of the security apparatus.

The role of the CAPF also extends to pre‑induction training and specialized instruction on counter‑improvised explosive device (IED) techniques. Since the Central Armed Police Forces generally arrive on the ground only after the official election schedule has been announced and the Ministry of Home Affairs has finalized deployment details, it is imperative that they receive an orientation that clarifies their responsibilities in relation to the state police and local law enforcement agencies. This orientation must address the chain of command, the scope of jurisdiction, and the mechanisms for inter‑agency coordination. To build the capacity of the CAPF, a series of concrete actions can be undertaken. First, a comprehensive initial briefing should be delivered by senior state police officers in the designated holding areas where the forces will assemble. This briefing should cover the legal framework governing election security, the operational objectives for the upcoming poll, and the specific duties that the CAPF personnel will be expected to perform.

Training should ideally be conducted at the company (Coy) level, grouped into clusters that correspond to the eventual deployment locations. By conducting the sessions in the same geographic zones where the forces will operate, the trainees can become familiar with the local terrain, the layout of polling stations, and the prevailing sociopolitical dynamics. The curriculum must include practical exercises on patrolling techniques, crowd‑control tactics, and the identification of potential hazards. It should also outline the dos and don’ts for operating in a civilian environment, emphasizing respect for the rights of voters, adherence to the code of conduct, and the importance of maintaining a non‑partisan stance. Local troops—those recruited from the area where the election is taking place—should be actively engaged in the training process, both as mentors and as sources of contextual knowledge about the community.

The state’s nodal officer, who serves as the focal point for all police training activities, must ensure that the training of police personnel is completed within the stipulated time frame, properly documented, and formally reported to higher authorities. Upon successful completion of the training modules, every participant should receive a standardized set of training materials that have been developed specifically for state security forces and a separate set tailored for the CAPF. These documents should contain detailed briefings on the role of security personnel at the polling stations, the responsibilities of the election staff, and the procedures for interacting with each other during the critical phases of voting and counting. In many Western democracies, security officers are issued pocket‑sized cards that summarize common scenarios they may encounter, cite the relevant legal provisions, outline the expected conduct, and list the contact details of senior officials and emergency contacts. Indian states could adopt a uniform version of such pocket cards, thereby providing a quick reference tool that enhances situational awareness and legal compliance among security personnel.

Coordination among the myriad agencies involved in election security is orchestrated primarily through the State Police Nodal Officer and the State Force Coordinator, who together act as the liaison between the state apparatus and a range of national entities. These include the National Force Coordination Centre, the Election Commission of India (ECI) and the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA), the Chief Electoral Officer (CEO) of the state, the Indian Railways, the Directorate General of Civil Aviation, the Ministry of Defence, the District Magistrates (DMs) and Superintendent of Police (SPs), as well as the nodal officers of neighboring states who must be kept informed of cross‑border movements and potential spill‑over effects. By maintaining regular communication channels with each of these bodies, the state officers can synchronize the deployment of forces, share intelligence, and resolve logistical challenges in a timely manner.

Documentation plays a pivotal role in ensuring accountability and facilitating after‑action reviews. The State Nodal Officer must guarantee that every training session, brief, and exercise is properly recorded, with attendance sheets, evaluation reports, and feedback forms stored in a systematic manner. These records serve as evidence of compliance with statutory requirements and provide a basis for future improvements in the training regimen.

A further cornerstone of the election security architecture is the establishment of fully equipped control rooms at both the state and district levels. These control rooms operate under the supervision of the senior police nodal officer at the state level and the Superintendent of Police at the district level, respectively. Their primary functions include tracking the movement of security forces as they transition from one operational phase to another, maintaining continuous communication with units on the ground such as quick reaction teams and patrol parties, and monitoring the overall law and order situation in the lead‑up to the poll. Control rooms also keep a vigilant eye on specific events that have the potential to devolve into disturbances, such as nomination processions, large public rallies, and visits by prominent political leaders or “star” campaigners. They collect reports on the execution of the deployment plan for the polling day and the counting day, and they serve as the hub for emergency reporting, linking election officials, the media, and senior command officers. Instructions issued during crises are relayed swiftly to the District Magistrate, the Superintendent of Police, the Chief Electoral Officer, and the state‑level police nodal officers. In addition to these operational functions, separate control rooms are set up to manage live streaming and webcasting of election‑related content, ensuring that the public can access real‑time information from both state and district perspectives.

On the actual polling day, a set of prescribed dos and don’ts guides the behavior of security forces to maximize both effectiveness and public confidence. The visibility of the forces—whether through mobile patrols, static check posts, or a presence at polling locations—must be sufficient to dominate the environment and deter any would‑be trouble‑makers. All magistrates, zonal officers, and police or CAPF officers stationed at polling stations should have at hand the contact details of the nearest police station (thana) and the control room, including physical addresses and mobile phone numbers, so that they can summon assistance without delay. The control rooms themselves must be staffed around the clock, equipped with reliable communication hardware, and ready to respond instantly to any emergency that may arise. Check posts and barriers are to be positioned strategically along major thoroughfares to intercept the movement of weapons, explosives, or individuals with a history of violent conduct, commonly referred to as “goonda elements.” Border sealing measures—such as the closure of unofficial entry points and the deployment of personnel to monitor highway exits—must be rigorously enforced to prevent the infiltration of disruptive actors.

Election officials require strong mobility support, with sufficient reserves of personnel stationed at polling booths and in surrounding areas to enforce the Model Code of Conduct and to provide assistance wherever it is needed. Reserve forces should be placed in a configuration that allows for rapid deployment to any hotspot, avoiding the concentration of mobile teams in a single area while leaving other zones inadequately covered. The control room must continuously monitor the movement of polling parties as they transport ballot boxes from polling stations to collection centers after voting has concluded, ensuring that these convoys are not ambushed or delayed.

In addition to these general guidelines, there are specific instructions concerning the manner in which the Central Armed Police Forces should conduct static guarding of polling stations. On the eve of the poll, that is, the day before voting begins, CAPF personnel are required to take up their assigned positions at the designated polling stations. Their primary duty is to protect the physical integrity of the polling sites and to regulate entry, ensuring that only authorized election staff, voters, and accredited observers are permitted inside. This static guard duty complements the dynamic patrols and rapid‑response capabilities of other units, creating a layered security environment that safeguards the democratic process from intimidation, violence, and any attempt to tamper with the ballot.

In the conduct of elections the presence of personnel from the Central Armed Police Forces, commonly abbreviated as CAPF, at polling stations is mandated by the Supreme Court of India in order to safeguard the integrity of the voting process. When a polling station is to be guarded, the usual practice is to station a single member of the CAPF at the entrance of that polling place. This guard may be assigned either to remain in a fixed position, which is known as a static duty, or to move periodically between several polling stations, a practice described as an oscillating mode. The purpose of positioning a CAPF officer at the doorway is to provide an unobtrusive yet vigilant observation of all activities that occur inside the voting hall, to deter any attempts at intimidation, tampering, or other irregularities, and to report any unusual or suspicious occurrence to the officer who has overall responsibility for the CAPF contingent or to the election observer designated for that area. The term “static guard duty in an oscillating mode” refers specifically to situations in which a single building houses more than one polling station and the number of CAPF personnel available is insufficient to place a separate guard at each door. In such circumstances the officer who is posted at one entrance may be instructed to move, or oscillate, from one polling station door to another at regular intervals, thereby extending his watchful eye over multiple voting sites. While he is rotating, the officer continues to keep a careful record of the conduct within each polling station and is required to communicate any anomalies directly to his immediate superior, who may be the company commandant of the CAPF unit, or to the designated election observer, ensuring that the chain of command remains clear and that any potential breach can be addressed without delay.

If for any reason a CAPF officer who has been assigned static guard duties fails to arrive at the polling station to which he has been posted, the law is explicit that the election cannot be allowed to begin at that location. The presence of the central armed force is considered a non‑negotiable prerequisite for the opening of the poll, and the absence of that security element invalidates the commencement of voting. This rule reflects the principle that the safety of the electorate and the sanctity of the ballot box must not be compromised, even if it means postponing or canceling voting at a particular station until appropriate security can be assured.

There are polling stations that have been earmarked exclusively for CAPF deployment, meaning that no other police agency is expected to take over the security role at those sites. In such exclusively assigned stations the local state police are expressly prohibited from substituting for the CAPF. Moreover, senior officers of the state police, whether they are accompanied by a contingent of constables or not, are not permitted to place themselves at the entrance of the polling station or to assume any supervisory or command function over the CAPF personnel who are present. This separation of duties is intended to avoid any confusion of authority, to preserve the independence of the central forces, and to ensure that the chain of command remains unambiguous during the delicate phase of voting.

In certain operational contexts, the election authorities may decide to create mixed or composite teams that combine members of the CAPF with officers from the local state police. The rationale behind forming such composite units is to maximise the number of polling stations that can be covered by static guards, especially when the total strength of the CAPF is limited. When a composite team is formed, the CAPF personnel continue to occupy the doorways of the polling stations and they remain answerable to their own hierarchy, specifically the company commandant who leads the CAPF unit. The state police members of the composite team provide auxiliary support but do not interfere with the reporting lines or the command structure of the CAPF, thereby preserving the integrity of the central forces’ operational protocols while still benefiting from the additional manpower supplied by the state police.

Even when the CAPF is performing static guard duties, whether in a purely CAPF‑only arrangement or within a composite team, it is considered prudent to have at least one, and preferably two, unarmed local police personnel or Home Guard members stationed within the precinct of the polling place. These unarmed officers are not there to replace the armed CAPF guard but to serve as an immediate source of reinforcement should a situation arise that requires additional manpower, such as a crowd disturbance or a technical problem with voting equipment. By having local police personnel on site, the election officials create a rapid response mechanism that can summon additional law‑enforcement resources without delay, thereby enhancing the overall security posture of the polling station.

In addition to static guard assignments, the CAPF may also be tasked with mobile patrolling duties. Mobile patrolling involves assigning a contingent of CAPF officers to travel along a predefined route that encompasses a cluster of polling stations, often referred to as an election sector. The mobile unit’s responsibilities include supervising the conduct of the election at each station that lies on its route, providing a visible deterrent against misconduct, and offering assistance to the sector’s election officers and the sector police officers who are responsible for that geographical area. The mobile patrol moves from one polling station to the next, checking that the voting process is proceeding without irregularities, that the ballot boxes are secure, and that the electronic voting machines are functioning properly. To carry out this task effectively, the mobile unit is given a list that identifies which polling stations are considered critical because of a history of disturbances, which stations have not yet received any static guard coverage, and which localities along the route are deemed vulnerable due to factors such as high political tension or previous incidents of electoral violence. While on patrol, the CAPF officers are required to maintain constant and close supervision over these identified stations, staying in frequent contact with the election sector officer, the sector police officer, the micro‑observers who monitor individual stations, and even the presiding officers who are in charge of managing the polling process at each location. This continuous communication ensures that any emerging problem can be reported instantly and that appropriate corrective measures can be taken without delay.

To facilitate the mobile patrolling function, the CAPF is supplied with detailed route maps, often in the form of sketches, that illustrate the geography of each sector under its jurisdiction. These sketches display the precise locations of all polling stations within the sector, indicate the level of sensitivity or risk associated with each station (for example, whether a station is in a high‑tension area or a relatively peaceful zone), and list the contact details of the sector election officer, the returning officer, the observer appointed by the Election Commission, and any other officials who are assigned static duties in the sector. By having this information at hand, the mobile CAPF unit can quickly identify which polling stations require immediate attention, coordinate with the relevant officers, and ensure that the entire sector remains under vigilant surveillance throughout the election day.

Another specialized deployment of the CAPF is the “flying squad” mode. In this arrangement the CAPF contingent is not attached to any specific polling station or electoral sector. Instead, the squad remains fully mobile, moving unpredictably throughout a designated area in order to conduct random, surprise checks at any polling station that falls within its operational zone. The purpose of the flying squad is to create an element of uncertainty for any potential wrongdoer, as they cannot be sure when or where the squad will appear. The squad reports its observations and findings directly to the district control room, which serves as the central hub for monitoring election security across the district. The flying squad is typically accompanied by a zonal magistrate, an officer of the judiciary who provides legal oversight and ensures that the squad’s activities conform to the procedural requirements of the election law. This combination of police mobility and judicial presence is designed to reinforce the rule of law while preserving the impartiality of the electoral process.

After the voting has concluded at a particular polling station, the electronic voting machines (EVMs) that have been used to record the votes must be securely transported to a central receiving center where they will be stored until the day of counting. The CAPF is tasked with escorting both the polled EVMs and the polling personnel from the polling station to the receiving center. This escort service is critical because the machines contain the electronic record of each voter’s choice, and any tampering or loss of the machines could undermine the credibility of the election. By providing an armed escort, the CAPF ensures that the machines are not intercepted, tampered with, or otherwise compromised during the transit.

In addition to escorting the machines, the CAPF is also responsible for guarding the strong room in which the polled EVMs are stored after they arrive at the receiving center. The strong room is a secure vault‑like facility that is designed to resist unauthorized entry and to protect the machines from theft, damage, or sabotage. The CAPF personnel assigned to guard this room maintain a constant watch, control access to the room, and keep detailed logs of any entry or exit, thereby ensuring that the integrity of the stored machines is preserved until the counting process commences.

There are circumstances in which the number of CAPF personnel available for election duty may be insufficient to meet the security requirements of all polling stations. In such cases, the Election Commission may authorize the deployment of local state armed police forces to take over the exclusive security duties at certain polling stations. This substitution is not automatic; it is undertaken only after the Election Commission issues specific instructions, usually through its senior-most general and police observers who are responsible for overseeing the entire election operation. The decision to use state armed police in place of the CAPF is therefore a carefully calibrated measure, taken only when the central forces cannot provide the necessary coverage, and always under the close supervision of the Commission’s top officials to ensure that the standards of security and impartiality are maintained across the entire electoral process.

In circumstances where the integrity of the voting process must be protected, the local police are instructed not to remain directly in view of the entrance to the polling station. Instead, they are to be stationed at a point that is sufficiently removed from the doorway so that the officers who are inside the polling place cannot see them. The distance must be such that there can be no reasonable doubt or apprehension on the part of the poll officials that the police might interfere with the conduct of the election that is taking place behind the door. By keeping the police out of sight, the law seeks to eliminate any perception that the presence of uniformed officers could influence the behaviour of voters, poll workers, or candidates, and thereby preserve the atmosphere of a free and fair vote.

The Central Armed Police Forces, commonly abbreviated as CAPF, must receive a thorough briefing before they are deployed to election duties. This briefing is the responsibility of the officer who is in charge of the unit that is being sent. When the deployment is to take place at a police station, the Station House Officer, who is the senior police officer in charge of that station, is required to conduct the briefing. If the deployment is on a larger scale, such as an entire district, the briefing is carried out by the District Magistrate together with the Superintendent of Police. Their briefings cover a range of topics, including the specific challenges that have been identified in the area, the appropriate tactical responses that the forces should employ, and a clear set of do‑and‑don’t instructions that are intended to guide the behaviour of the troops while they are on election duty.
