In addition to briefing the police, the election officials who are responsible for overseeing the voting process—commonly known as observers and Returning Officers—should also be given an orientation about the role, duties, organisational structure and operating procedures of the security forces that will be present. This orientation helps the election functionaries understand how the forces will be deployed, what their limits of authority are, and how the forces will interact with the polling staff and the electorate. The polling parties, that is, the political parties and their agents, are also advised in a strict manner to travel together with the security forces whenever they need to move in the vicinity of the polling stations, so that their safety can be assured and the possibility of any untoward incident can be reduced.

The planning for the movement of troops across state boundaries, or within a single state, is a matter that the Commission overseeing the elections has directed the chief secretaries and the chief executive officers of all states to manage. The directive requires the state governments to prepare a detailed deployment plan well in advance. This plan must be drawn up in consultation with the designated nodal officers of the various armed forces that will be involved. The purpose of the plan is to guarantee that the forces can be moved smoothly from one location to another, whether the movement is from one district to another within the same state (intra‑state) or from one state to another (inter‑state). By coordinating the movement of troops ahead of time, the authorities hope to avoid delays, bottlenecks and logistical complications that could jeopardise the smooth conduct of the election.

After the day on which the voters cast their ballots, the focus of the election machinery shifts to the counting of the votes. However, before the counting can take place, there are occasions when a re‑poll may be required, for example if an irregularity is discovered at a particular polling station. In such cases the arrangements that were made for the original polling day must be replicated for the re‑poll, because the same security considerations apply. The deployment and subsequent withdrawal—known as de‑induction—of security forces from the counting centres must be handled with equal care. If there is a sufficient interval between the polling and the counting, the authorities need to develop a plan that outlines when and how the forces will be withdrawn, while also specifying which elements of the force must remain in place until the counting is completed. In elections that are conducted in phases, the withdrawal of troops is normally built into the original deployment schedule so that the limited number of security personnel can be used most efficiently. This withdrawal process is usually divided into two stages: the first stage occurs after the polling day, and the second stage takes place after the counting is finished. Because the timing of these stages must be coordinated across multiple agencies, the Election Commission of India and the Ministry of Home Affairs need to receive the relevant details well before the operations commence. Since the number of counting centres is typically far smaller than the number of polling stations—often only one centre per district—the task of providing security for the counting locations is comparatively straightforward.

The Indian Railways plays a vital role in the de‑induction of forces. The railway administration must arrange special trains that will convey the troops from the locations where they have been stationed back to their home bases. The catering division of the railways, known as the Indian Railway Catering and Tourism Corporation, is responsible for providing meals, bedding and other necessary facilities to the personnel during their journey. The railway’s involvement requires meticulous planning because the trains must be scheduled so that they reach the departure points on time, travel safely, and return the forces to their respective states without causing any disruption to the regular railway traffic.

The Director General of Police of each state, who also serves as the State Force Coordinator, bears the responsibility of ensuring that the CAPF units are released in a timely manner so that they can be redeployed to other states if required. This coordination is essential for maintaining a balanced distribution of security resources across the country during the election period.

The state governments are required to provide a range of logistical support to the Central Armed Police Forces and the State Armed Police (SAP) until the forces are finally withdrawn. The first obligation is to arrange suitable accommodation for the troops and to provide all other necessary logistics, such as mess facilities, medical support and sanitation, for the entire period of their deployment. The accommodation and transport arrangements must be verified in advance, and any deficiencies that are discovered must be corrected immediately to prevent inconvenience or operational disruption. Each contingent of forces must be assigned a liaison officer who will travel with the troops and act as a point of contact between the forces and the state authorities until the troops leave the state. Upon arrival, the liaison officer must hand over a comprehensive information booklet that contains all details relevant to the deployment. This booklet should include maps of the routes that will be used, a record of any previous incidents in the area, a list of known extremist or Maoist groups, descriptions of their typical methods of operation, information on communal sensitivities and local political issues, the locations of nearby hospitals—including those that accept cashless treatment—important telephone numbers, and any other data that could assist the forces in performing their duties safely and effectively.

Transportation for the forces must meet the standards set by the Election Commission. Vehicles must be in good working order, and the drivers must be trusted, experienced and familiar with the local terrain. When a large number of contingents are deployed in a single district, the authorities must identify several refuelling stations in advance so that the vehicles do not all converge on a single fuel point, which would cause unnecessary delays and congestion. The accommodation provided must be in accordance with the pay scale and comfort level that the Election Commission has prescribed for security personnel. If a contingent is arriving from a neighbouring state and the road distance is roughly three hundred kilometres, the election‑bound state must arrange road transport for that contingent. In cases where the election is being held on an island or in a location that can be reached only by sea, the state must make advance arrangements with the maritime authorities to provide a steamer or ship for the movement of the forces.

The deployment plan for each district must be communicated well ahead of time to all the stakeholders, including the police, the election officers and the security commanders, to ensure seamless coordination on the ground. Before the forces are inducted, a pre‑induction training programme must be organized for the newly arrived contingents. This training should cover the geography of the area, the socio‑political context, the nature of any security threats, and the specific duties that the forces will perform during the election. For operational efficiency, the forces should be positioned in contiguous blocks rather than being scattered, because this arrangement enhances command and control. The guiding principle of “the right force at the right place” must be observed, meaning that the type and size of the security unit assigned to a particular location should be proportionate to the assessed risk at that location. Areas that are difficult to reach or lack reliable communication infrastructure—referred to as shadow areas—must be identified in advance, and special communication arrangements must be put in place to ensure that those areas remain connected to the command structure. By adhering to these detailed procedures, the election authorities aim to provide a secure environment that enables citizens to exercise their franchise without fear or intimidation.

The local police authority must designate a representative for each contingent of personnel that arrives in the state, and this representative is required to meet the incoming contingent at the point of arrival. The purpose of this meeting is to escort the members of the contingent to the places where they will be accommodated or deployed for duty, thereby ensuring that they are settled quickly and can begin their assignments without unnecessary delay. This practice is intended to provide a smooth transition for the forces and to reduce any confusion that might arise when large numbers of officers are moved into a new jurisdiction.

When it comes to financial matters, any payments that are classified as ex gratia, which are voluntary or goodwill payments made without legal obligation, must be processed and disbursed within a time frame that has been predetermined by the governing regulations. In addition, honoraria, which are fees paid for services rendered, as well as allowances for food, must also be settled promptly according to the same schedule. The timely release of these funds is essential for maintaining morale among the personnel and for avoiding administrative bottlenecks that could interfere with operational readiness.

Information concerning any pre‑existing agreements with super‑specialty and multispecialty hospitals must be communicated to the commander of each contingent. Such agreements typically allow for cash‑less treatment of injuries or illnesses that may be incurred during the course of duty, meaning that the hospitals will provide services without requiring immediate payment from the patients. By sharing the details of these arrangements in advance, the commander can ensure that the contingency plans for medical emergencies are fully operational and that the forces have immediate access to high‑level medical care when needed.

The health authorities must also convey to the contingents any information that is specific to the geographic area in which they will be operating, especially regarding diseases that are endemic or have a heightened risk of occurrence. For example, if malaria is known to be prevalent in the region, the authorities should provide comprehensive guidance on preventive measures, such as the use of insecticide‑treated nets, indoor residual spraying, and prophylactic medication, as well as details about the appropriate therapeutic drugs that should be administered in case of infection. This health‑related briefing is crucial for protecting the well‑being of the personnel and for preventing outbreaks that could compromise the election process.

In situations that demand an urgent medical evacuation, the administration must have provisions for air ambulance services and, where appropriate, the deployment of helicopters. These aerial assets are to be readied for rapid response to any exigency that cannot be addressed adequately by ground transportation, such as severe injuries sustained at remote polling stations or in areas where road access is limited. By ensuring that the necessary air‑lift capabilities are on standby, the authorities can greatly reduce the time required to transport patients to advanced medical facilities, thereby improving survival chances and overall outcomes.

The deployment schedule must also take into account the specific duties for which certain contingents have been earmarked. For example, those groups that have been assigned to provide strong‑room security—that is, the protection of secure storage facilities where valuable electoral materials and cash are kept—must not be diverted to man polling‑station booths. This restriction is imposed because strong‑room security responsibilities typically extend for a period of two days before the polling day, during which the security detail must be fully focused on safeguarding the sensitive materials. Assigning these personnel to other tasks during that critical window would undermine the security of the electoral assets.

Modern operational support increasingly relies on unmanned aerial systems, commonly referred to as drones or unmanned aerial vehicles (UAVs). The authorities therefore need to arrange for the availability of such devices to assist with tasks such as aerial surveillance, crowd monitoring, and rapid transmission of visual information from remote or hard‑to‑reach locations. These platforms can provide real‑time situational awareness to command centers, enabling quicker decision‑making and more effective coordination among the various agencies involved in election security.

To further enhance coordination, joint security control rooms should be established at the district level. These control rooms serve as centralized hubs where representatives from the police, the central armed police forces (CAPF), and other relevant agencies can share information, monitor ongoing activities, and issue directives in a synchronized manner. By co‑locating the command elements, the likelihood of miscommunication is reduced, and the overall efficiency of the security apparatus is improved.

All logistical aspects of accommodation, transportation, and other support services must remain under the responsibility of the forces themselves until the final de‑induction, which is the formal process of relieving the personnel from their election‑related duties. This ensures that the forces retain full control over their operational logistics, thereby preventing any potential disruptions that could arise from external interference or premature handover of resources.

When female contingents, often referred to as Mahila Coys, are deployed, special considerations must be made to address their specific needs. These may include provisions for separate sleeping quarters, appropriate sanitation facilities, and any cultural or safety requirements that are unique to female personnel. By attending to these details, the administration demonstrates respect for gender‑sensitive requirements and helps maintain the comfort and effectiveness of the women serving in the election operation.

The provision of defense material, which encompasses items such as protective gear, communication equipment, and weapons, must be aligned with the specific needs of each contingent. The supply chain should be organized so that the required equipment is delivered in a timely manner and matches the operational profile of the unit, thereby ensuring that the forces are adequately equipped to carry out their assigned tasks.

The large presence of central armed police forces (CAPF) over an extended period creates a demand for a full range of basic amenities that support both comfort and operational efficiency. The local administration bears the responsibility of providing these amenities, which may include clean drinking water, reliable electricity, proper sanitation facilities, mess arrangements for meals, medical assistance, and recreational spaces. Periodic monitoring of these services is essential to ensure they remain at an acceptable standard throughout the election cycle. The overarching goal of this provision is to foster a conducive environment that allows the forces to perform their duties effectively while also safeguarding their health and well‑being. This principle is further elaborated in the relevant chapter of the broader procedural manual.

The communication framework for elections is of paramount importance because the electoral process involves a multitude of activities that take place at the national, state, district, and individual polling‑station levels. Strategic communication planning cannot be overlooked, as it lies at the heart of successful election management. A robust and thoroughly tested communication plan contributes to the smooth execution of tasks ranging from the preparation of state‑level and district‑level election management plans, through the actual conduct of polling, to the counting of votes and the declaration of results. Continuous tracking and monitoring of these diverse activities demand reliable and secure communication links among the numerous stakeholders, including election officials, law‑enforcement agencies, and logistical support teams. Detailed guidance on constructing and operating such communication networks is provided in a separate manual that deals specifically with force multipliers and non‑CAPF measures; interested parties should consult that document for comprehensive instructions.

The police machinery occupies a central role in ensuring that elections are conducted in a peaceful and orderly manner. However, the absence of violence does not automatically guarantee that an election is free and fair. The integrity of the electoral process also depends on the correct and unbiased implementation of election laws and the directions issued by the Election Commission of India. Achieving this level of compliance requires that all police personnel, from senior officers to constables, possess a thorough familiarity with the relevant statutes, guidelines, and procedural instructions. This knowledge can only be acquired through systematic and periodic training programmes.

To this end, the State Nodal Police Officer for Election Training is tasked with preparing a comprehensive training calendar and developing the content of the training modules. The officer must also ensure that the maximum number of training sessions are completed before the formal announcement of the polling dates. The training initiative involves a systematic approach wherein all police officials and constabulary members who are likely to be involved in the election process receive instruction that aligns with the directives issued by the Election Commission.

A typical training plan may be described in narrative form as follows. At the state level, a group of master trainers, drawn from the Institute of Integrated Disaster Management in New Delhi, are engaged approximately four months before the poll to develop the curriculum and to conduct high‑level instructional sessions. Two months prior to the election, senior officers such as range inspectors general, superintendents of police, and additional superintendents are assembled at the state police headquarters for more focused training that addresses operational planning and command responsibilities. One to two months before the polling day, deputy superintendents, inspectors, station house officers, and police sub‑inspectors are gathered at district police lines to receive training that emphasizes field tactics, crowd control, and coordination with other agencies. Finally, one month before the election, police constables, head constables, and any other personnel—including members of the CAPF who are slated for deployment at polling stations or mobile units—undergo training at sub‑division or local police stations, or at the specific areas where they will be deployed, to ensure they are fully prepared for the tasks that await them.

Among the topics that are considered essential for this training, the curriculum covers a broad spectrum of issues. General subjects for state‑level nodal officers, heads of range, superintendents of police, deputy superintendents, and station house officers include vulnerability mapping, which involves identifying areas that are at higher risk of electoral disturbances, and confidence‑building measures designed to foster trust among the electorate. The training also addresses the identification of critical polling stations and clusters that require special attention, the planning and execution of preventive drives, and the detection and prevention of the misuse of money power and illicit election expenditure. Additional components cover the protection of public property from defacement, maintenance of discipline during the nomination phase, promotion of communal harmony, and the specific duties that must be performed on polling day and on counting day. Coordination with the central armed police forces, deployment strategies for these forces, the application of the Model Code of Conduct, an overview of the entire poll process, relevant legal provisions, and other emerging issues are also included in the syllabus.

In summary, the comprehensive approach described above seeks to integrate logistical support, health and safety measures, communication infrastructure, gender‑sensitive arrangements, and extensive training into a cohesive framework that underpins the successful conduct of elections. By attending to each of these elements in detail, the authorities aim to create an environment in which the electoral process is not only peaceful but also transparent, fair, and reflective of the democratic aspirations of the nation.

The responsibilities of an Assistant Sub‑Inspector (ASI) or a Police Inspector (PI) or a Police Sub‑Inspector (PSI) in the context of election management extend far beyond the routine policing duties that are familiar to most officers. One of the central tasks entrusted to these officers is the oversight of the web portal that serves as the digital hub for election‑related data, a system that must be kept current on a daily basis. This includes the compilation of daily reports that capture a wide variety of operational details such as the monitoring of public expenditure associated with election activities, the deployment and movement of flying squads—mobile units that respond rapidly to emerging situations—and static surveillance teams, often abbreviated as SSTs, which are stationed at fixed points to maintain a constant watch over critical areas. In addition, the officers are charged with supervising inter‑state border check post operations, a function that involves the establishment and management of temporary checkpoints on the boundaries between states in order to prevent the illegal transport of weapons, contraband, or other prohibited items that could influence the electoral process. The control room, which functions as the nerve centre of all police activity on election day, also falls under their jurisdiction; here they must ensure that communications are clear, that information flows efficiently, and that any emerging incidents are logged and acted upon without delay. The scope of their duties further embraces the enforcement of prohibition laws and excise regulations, especially in regions where the sale of alcohol and other regulated goods is restricted during the election period. The officers must also manage the election cell, a specialized unit within the police organization that coordinates all law‑enforcement aspects of the electoral process, and they must work closely with executive magistrates to ensure that police patrols are conducted in a systematic manner, providing a visible deterrent to any potential disturbance. Security provision for candidates and election observers is another critical element, requiring detailed risk assessments, the deployment of protective details, and the coordination of movement plans to guarantee the safety of these individuals throughout the campaign and on the day of voting. Moreover, the safeguarding of strong rooms—secure storage facilities that house valuable election equipment such as Electronic Voting Machines (EVMs) and the accompanying Voter Verified Paper Audit Trail (VVPAT) units—demands meticulous planning, especially when these devices are in transit between storage locations, distribution centres, and polling stations. All incidents, whether minor or major, must be recorded in an incident reporting system that captures the nature of the event, the response taken, and any follow‑up actions required. On the actual polling day, the duties of the PI, PSI, ASI, and higher‑ranking officers such as the Head Constable (HC) shift toward direct security management at polling stations. This includes providing guards and security personnel who are stationed at each polling venue, coordinating with the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) or State Armed Police Forces (SAPF), liaising with the presiding officer and the polling staff, and ensuring that security staff are fully aware of the prescribed do’s and don’ts that govern their conduct during the delicate and highly visible electoral process.

The state police headquarters has a pivotal role in building the capacity of both the state and central security forces that are called upon to maintain law and order during elections. One of the first actions required of the headquarters is the preparation of a comprehensive checklist that enumerates the responsibilities of all key officials who will be involved in the conduct of the elections, including officers from the CAPF. This checklist must reflect the latest instructions issued by the Election Commission of India, which periodically updates its guidelines to address emerging challenges. The checklist is used to identify the nodal officers—officials who will act as points of contact and coordination—who must be appointed at the state level for a series of specialized functions. The first nodal officer, whose duties revolve around the security of EVMs and VVPAT units as well as the induction, de‑induction, and welfare of the CAPF personnel, must work in close coordination with the Inspector General of Police (Operations), the central ministries such as the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA) in New Delhi, and the logistical teams that arrange for the movement, accommodation, and sustenance of the forces deployed for the election. This officer is also responsible for ensuring the safety and secure transportation of the electronic voting machines and their paper audit trails, a task that demands stringent checks at every stage of the supply chain. The second nodal officer, designated for training, is required to develop a training calendar in collaboration with the Chief Electoral Officer and the state police’s own nodal officer for training matters. This calendar outlines the schedule for role‑based training programs that target a wide spectrum of personnel, ranging from senior police leadership such as Commissioners of Police and Heads of Range, down to Sub‑Divisional Police Officers, Station House Officers, and commandants of various units. The training programmes must be tailored to the specific responsibilities each group will assume, ensuring that police officers, State Reserve Police Force (SRPF) members, Home Guard personnel, and CAPF units are all adequately prepared for their respective duties. The third nodal officer focuses on the management of postal ballots and electronic payments. This officer maintains a database that contains the details of postal ballot papers assigned to police forces, SRPF units, Home Guard members, and other auxiliary forces, and also oversees the electronic payment of honoraria to these individuals, thereby ensuring that those who contribute to the electoral process receive timely and accurate remuneration. The fourth nodal officer is tasked with overseeing the implementation of the Systematic Voter Education and Electoral Participation (SVEEP) activities, working closely with the Chief Electoral Officer to organize voter education initiatives at both state and district levels, thereby encouraging informed participation and enhancing the overall legitimacy of the election.

In addition to these four functions, a further set of nodal officers is required to address contemporary challenges that arise in the digital age. The fifth nodal officer, responsible for monitoring social media and cybercrime, must keep a vigilant eye on platforms such as SMS messaging services, Facebook, Instagram, Twitter, and other emerging social networks to detect and assess online campaign activities. This officer must also identify any objectionable content that contravenes the Model Code of Conduct, coordinate with district police to initiate appropriate action, and work in partnership with the Indian Computer Emergency Response Team (CERT‑IN) and other cyber‑security agencies to mitigate threats. The sixth nodal officer, dealing with web portal management and daily reporting, has the duty of collecting data from districts related to law and order, compiling it into comprehensive reports known as Law & Order Report I and II, and ensuring that daily updates are submitted in a timely fashion. The seventh nodal officer, charged with developing the communication plan, must mobilise resources, identify areas that lack adequate communication infrastructure—often referred to as black spots or shadow areas—and design a communication strategy in coordination with the state police’s nodal officer to ensure that all stakeholders receive clear, consistent information throughout the election period. The eighth nodal officer, responsible for inter‑state border coordination, must liaise with neighboring states and union territories to set up inter‑state border check posts, facilitate the arrest of criminals who cross state lines, and manage issues related to the deposition of licensed firearms, enforcement of dry‑day prohibitions, and the sharing of intelligence that is critical to maintaining public order during the elections.

Beyond the appointment of these nodal officers, it is essential that both state and central police forces have access to reference material that consolidates the legal framework applicable during elections. This material should clearly outline the sections of the Indian Penal Code, the Representation of the People Act, and the Model Code of Conduct that become relevant in the electoral context, distinguishing between cognizable offences—those that allow police to arrest without a warrant—and non‑cognizable offences, which require a warrant. The reference guide must also specify the appropriate actions to be taken for each type of violation, thereby providing field officials with a ready‑to‑use handbook that can be consulted in real time. To ensure that this information reaches the officers on the ground, the reference material should be distributed widely among all field personnel, including those posted in remote or rural stations.

Effective coordination between the Chief Electoral Officer’s office and the police headquarters is a further cornerstone of successful election management. The Director General of Police (DGP) may designate a senior officer to serve as a dedicated nodal officer who maintains daily contact with the Chief Electoral Officer, thereby ensuring that the Commission’s instructions are communicated promptly and that any deviations are corrected without delay. The Election Commission has issued numerous directives concerning the training of police personnel engaged in election duties, and these directives have been compiled and expanded upon to address additional aspects of preparation. At the state level, a State Nodal Officer for Training must be appointed, preferably an officer of the rank of Additional Director General of Police (ADGP) or Inspector General (IG) who heads the training branch. This officer is responsible for arranging, overseeing, preparing content for, monitoring, and reporting on the training programmes that are delivered to the state police forces. The training programme is organized at two hierarchical levels: at the state police headquarters, where senior officers receive strategic guidance, and at the district level, which includes sub‑district units such as blocks, tehsils, and talukas, ensuring that training cascades down to the grassroots level. In a general election, every member of the state police force, from the highest ranks down to the constables, is required to undergo training that is relevant to the duties they will perform during the election, thereby creating a uniformly prepared police workforce that can effectively safeguard the democratic process.

The training program for personnel involved in the conduct of elections is not limited solely to the officials of the electoral administration; it also extends to members of the State Armed Police and the Home Guard forces, who are required to receive instruction that is specifically tailored to the duties they will perform on election day. These uniformed cadres, being the primary agents of law and order during the polling process, must be familiar not only with the procedural aspects of the election but also with the legal framework that governs their conduct, the handling of crowds, and the protection of polling stations. Consequently, the same comprehensive curriculum that is applied to election officials is also imparted to these security forces, ensuring that every individual who may be called upon to intervene in an electoral situation possesses a uniform standard of knowledge and skill.

In order to achieve a uniformly high level of competence, the training material must be standardized, of superior quality, and responsive to the practical needs that arise in the field. The instructional package therefore includes printed handbooks that set out the essential procedures, as well as supplementary items such as pocket-sized reference guides, informational flyers, and other portable documents that can be consulted quickly during the course of an election. These materials are designed to be both concise and exhaustive, providing clear explanations of the steps to be taken in various scenarios, the legal provisions that apply, and the best‑practice methods that have been distilled from past experience. By making these resources readily available, the election authorities aim to reduce ambiguity and to promote consistent decision‑making across the diverse jurisdictions where elections are held.

A crucial component of the preparation process is the preparation and periodic updating of a detailed checklist that enumerates the duties and responsibilities of the senior officials who are directly involved in the conduct of the poll. This checklist reflects the most recent instructions issued by the Election Commission of India, which may evolve from one election cycle to the next in response to emerging challenges or legislative changes. The checklist serves as a practical tool that guides each official in the performance of tasks such as overseeing voter registration, monitoring polling station set‑up, ensuring the security of ballot boxes, and supervising the counting of votes. By aligning the responsibilities of each official with the latest commission directives, the checklist helps to prevent oversights and to maintain accountability throughout the electoral process.

In addition to the operational checklist, the authorities must also develop and distribute comprehensive reference material that cites the specific sections of the various statutes that become relevant during elections. This reference guide identifies, for each applicable law, whether the offence is classified as cognizable—meaning that police may arrest without a warrant—or non‑cognizable, which requires a warrant for arrest. It also outlines the precise actions that must be taken if a violation is observed, ranging from immediate detention to the filing of a formal complaint, and it clarifies the procedural steps that follow. By making this legal compendium accessible to every participant in the election machinery, the election commission ensures that all actions taken on the ground are firmly grounded in statutory authority, thereby reducing the risk of unlawful or arbitrary enforcement.

Effective coordination between the chief electoral officer’s (CEO’s) office and the headquarters of the state police is another essential requirement. The CEO must convey any instruction that pertains to the maintenance of law and order, the preparation of deployment plans for the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) at the state or district level, or any other guideline that the Election Commission has issued. These instructions must be communicated without delay to the police headquarters so that the police can integrate them into their operational planning, allocate personnel and equipment accordingly, and synchronize their activities with those of the electoral officials. This seamless flow of information helps to create a unified response to any potential disturbance, ensuring that the law‑enforcement agencies and the election officials act as a single, well‑coordinated entity.

At the district level, the district electoral officer, who also serves as the District Collector or District Magistrate, is tasked with conducting a briefing for senior police officials, including the Superintendent of Police and other high‑ranking officers. In this meeting, the officer highlights all the recent pronouncements of the Election Commission that must be incorporated into the training programmes for personnel under their jurisdiction. Particular attention is given to instructions concerning the preservation of law and order during the poll, the execution of non‑bailable warrants against individuals who pose a threat to the electoral process, the seizure of illegal firearms, the surrender of legally owned weapons, the enforcement of excise laws to curb the illegal movement of liquor, the crackdown on bootleggers, restrictions on the use of motor vehicles for campaign purposes, the requirement for prior permission for any campaign vehicle, and the prohibition on using any vehicle to transport voters on the day of polling. By explicitly detailing these points, the district officials ensure that every police officer understands the full spectrum of duties that may be required of them.

The enforcement of the Model Code of Conduct (MCC), which is the set of guidelines that govern the behaviour of candidates, political parties, and their agents during the election period, is a joint responsibility shared by the district electoral administration and the police administration. When the district administration, in cooperation with the Returning Officer, establishes squads or teams to monitor compliance with the MCC, it must assign clear lines of accountability. Each police officer at the level of the police station (Thana) is required to know exactly what actions they are expected to take if they encounter a violation, and they must be able to report such incidents promptly to their superiors. This shared responsibility creates a system of checks and balances that strengthens the overall enforcement of the code.

Another vital component of district‑level training involves the use of a Complaint Monitoring System (CMS). This digital or manual system is designed to capture and track reports of election‑related violence, intimidation, or other offences. Training sessions therefore include instruction on how to log complaints, how to verify their authenticity, and how to prioritize them for investigation. By familiarising police personnel with the CMS, the district authorities aim to improve the speed and accuracy with which incidents are recorded and addressed, thereby reducing the likelihood that serious violations go unnoticed.

The analysis of historical offence data is also emphasized in the training curriculum. The district electoral officer and the Superintendent of Police jointly review the status of cases that were filed in connection with the previous election, identify any pending matters, and expedite follow‑up actions where necessary. Police officers are taught to collect information on past incidents, to analyse patterns—such as recurring hotspots of violence or particular types of electoral fraud—and to use these insights to allocate resources more effectively in the current election cycle. This systematic approach to data‑driven policing helps to anticipate problems before they materialise.

A relatively new concept that has been introduced by the Election Commission is vulnerability mapping. This methodology involves the systematic identification of polling stations and clusters of voters that are considered to be at heightened risk of violence, intimidation, or other forms of disruption. The Commission issued a specific circular—number 464/L&O/2023/EPS(VM) dated 21 June 2023—outlining the procedure for carrying out such mapping. The district electoral officer, together with the Superintendent of Police, must explain the underlying principles of vulnerability mapping to police officials, illustrating how the process generates reliable inputs that guide the allocation of security personnel, the placement of monitoring equipment, and the deployment of rapid response teams. By understanding the rationale behind the identification of critical polling stations and clusters, police officers can act proactively rather than reactively.

The misuse of monetary influence, commonly referred to as “money power,” has become a major challenge to the integrity of the electoral process. Candidates and political parties have been observed to employ a variety of illicit tactics to sway voters, including the direct payment of cash, the distribution of liquor, the handing out of food packets, the organization of meals under the guise of birthdays or anniversaries, and the gifting of items in connection with mass weddings. Each of these practices constitutes a form of inducement that undermines the principle of a free and fair election. To combat this, every police officer at the level of the police station is required to monitor and document such activities within their jurisdiction. Training programmes therefore stress the importance of meticulous record‑keeping, the collection of video evidence, and the creation of a clear documentary trail that can be used in subsequent investigations. The police are also instructed to cooperate closely with the election officials, forming a joint team that can swiftly identify and respond to any breaches of the law concerning election expenditure.

The movement of liquor across state lines for the purpose of influencing voters poses a particular problem. The training curriculum advises police officers to focus on inter‑state border checkpoints and commercial tax posts, where they can intercept consignments of alcohol that are being smuggled into election‑bearing states. When such a seizure occurs, a thorough investigation must be launched to trace the origin of the liquor, to identify the parties responsible for its transport, and to establish any links between the supply chain and the election campaign. In addition, the media should be promptly briefed about the seizure, both to inform the public and to deter future attempts at smuggling. By incorporating these investigative steps into the training, police officials become better equipped to disrupt the financial and material incentives that corrupt the voting process.

In sum, the training modules designed for election officials, police officers, and auxiliary forces are intended to be comprehensive, practical, and firmly anchored in the legal mandates issued by the Election Commission of India. By providing standardized instructional materials, detailed checklists, legal reference guides, and a framework for inter‑agency coordination, the authorities seek to create a cohesive and well‑informed workforce that can safeguard the electoral process from violence, intimidation, and illicit inducements. The emphasis on vulnerability mapping, historical offence analysis, complaint monitoring, and the rigorous enforcement of the Model Code of Conduct reflects a modern, data‑driven approach to election management, while the focus on documenting and investigating the misuse of money power underscores the commitment to upholding the democratic principle that elections must be conducted free of undue influence. This multifaceted training strategy, when fully implemented, is expected to enhance the credibility of elections and to reinforce public confidence in the integrity of the democratic system.

The training curriculum that is to be delivered to police officials must include a thorough exposition of the Election Commission’s directives concerning the ceiling for election‑related expenditure. In particular, the officials need to understand the specific instructions that the Commission issues regarding the maximum amount of money that may be spent during the conduct of an election, the legal rationale behind imposing such a ceiling, and the mechanisms through which compliance is monitored. Equally important is a clear explanation of Section seventy‑seven of the Representation of the People Act, which obliges every recognized political party to submit a list of its “star campaigners” – those individuals whose public appearances and speeches are likely to draw large crowds and therefore have a substantial impact on the election environment. The provision is intended to enable the Commission to keep track of high‑profile campaigners, assess the risk of law‑breaking, and ensure that the financial disclosures related to their activities are transparent. Police officials must be made aware of how the list is prepared, the timeline for its submission, the criteria for designating a star campaigner, and the consequences for parties that fail to comply, which may include penalties or legal action. By grasping these details, the police will be better equipped to enforce the law and to assist the Commission in maintaining a level playing field.

Another critical element that must be incorporated into the training programme is Section one hundred and seventy‑one‑H of the Representation of the People Act, which criminalises the act of making an illegal payment to influence the election of a candidate. This provision addresses a wide range of corrupt practices, from the direct handing over of cash to a candidate or their agent, to the indirect financing of rallies, advertisements, or travel expenses that are not disclosed in the candidate’s account of election expenditure. The training should elucidate what constitutes an “illegal payment,” the statutory thresholds that distinguish permissible contributions from prohibited ones, and the evidentiary standards that the investigating officers must meet to secure a conviction. Moreover, the officials should be informed about the procedural steps for filing a First Information Report, the role of the investigating officer in gathering documentary and testimonial evidence, and the importance of preserving the chain of custody for any financial records. Understanding the gravity of this offence, which is punishable by imprisonment and fine, will reinforce the police’s commitment to safeguarding the integrity of the electoral process.

The curriculum must also devote considerable attention to the prevention of defacement of public property, a problem that frequently arises during heated campaign periods. The Election Commission regularly issues detailed instructions that aim to curb the vandalism of public infrastructure, such as government buildings, schools, street signs, and other communal assets, which may be used as canvassing sites or political slogans. These instructions often include guidelines on the permissible use of public spaces for political messaging, the methods for quickly identifying and removing unauthorised postings, and the penalties for those who damage or misuse public property. Training officers should be instructed on how to interpret these Commission orders within the framework of existing state laws that may address vandalism, public nuisance, or destruction of government property. The session should cover the step‑by‑step process for implementing the Commission’s directives, from notifying local municipal authorities to coordinating with the district’s legal advisers, and should outline the chain of command that ensures a swift response. In addition, the methodology for enforcement must be discussed, emphasizing the importance of proportionality, documentation of incidents, and the use of non‑violent techniques to restore order. By the end of the training, field officials should be able to apply the Commission’s instructions in a manner that respects both the rule of law and the rights of citizens.

Discipline during the nomination process is another area that requires explicit guidance. When a candidate files a nomination, the Representation of the People Act allows the candidate to be accompanied by no more than four other individuals. This restriction is intended to prevent crowding, reduce the potential for intimidation, and maintain an orderly environment within the registration office, commonly known as the Returning Officer’s (RO) room. Although most constituencies adhere to this rule, there are occasional lapses that result in chaotic scenes, with excessive numbers of supporters crowding the premises and disrupting the formalities of nomination filing. To mitigate such disturbances, the training must stress the need to designate a nodal officer – a senior police official appointed by the district superintendent of police (SP) – who will be responsible for “sanitising” the RO’s premises. The term “sanitising” in this context refers to the systematic verification that only the allowed number of persons are present, the removal of any unauthorised individuals, and the maintenance of a calm atmosphere conducive to the smooth processing of nominations. The nodal officer’s duties should be clearly delineated: they must conduct a visual check of the entry points, enforce the limit on accompanying persons, and ensure that the entire procedure is recorded on video for transparency and future reference. The SP must convey these responsibilities during the training session, illustrating the chain of command, the procedural checks, and the legal basis for the limitation. Additionally, the training should address the monitoring of election pamphlets that violate Section one hundred and twenty‑seven‑A of the Representation of the People Act, 1951, which prohibits the distribution of material that contains false statements or incites hatred. Officers should be taught how to identify such illegal pamphlets, the process for seizing them, and the reporting mechanisms that trigger further legal action.

The preservation of communal harmony is a paramount concern for the police administration, especially during the heightened emotions of an election campaign. The training must therefore alert officials to the dangers posed by the circulation of campaign material that could inflame communal tensions. This includes posters, flyers, audio‑visual content, and social media posts that contain religious or caste‑based provocations, or that portray a particular community in a negative light. The instructional session should outline the relevant provisions of electoral law, including the Model Code of Conduct, which is a set of guidelines issued by the Election Commission to ensure that political parties and candidates conduct themselves in a manner that does not aggravate communal sensitivities. By familiarising police personnel with these legal frameworks, the training will empower them to intervene promptly when inflammatory material is detected, to initiate investigations, and to collaborate with intelligence agencies to preempt potential outbreaks of violence.

A comprehensive orientation on poll‑day and counting‑day duties is essential for all officers who will be deployed at polling stations. The training must convey the sequence of events that constitute the polling process, beginning with the opening of the polling station, the verification of voter identities, the handling and safeguarding of the electronic voting machines (EVMs), and the subsequent sealing of the voting compartments. Police officers are tasked with ensuring the security of each polling station, which involves patrolling the surrounding catchment area, monitoring for any suspicious activities, and responding to any threats that may arise. They must also be instructed on how to identify “vulnerable” locations – for instance, areas with a history of electoral violence, regions with dense communal mixes, or polling stations situated near sensitive infrastructure – and to develop contingency plans for rapid deployment of additional forces if needed. The logistics of transporting the polled EVMs to the counting centre must be described in detail, including the use of secure vehicles, the protocol for sealing the machines, and the documentation required to maintain an unbroken chain of custody. Once the voting concludes, the police are responsible for guarding the strong rooms where the EVMs are stored until counting commences, ensuring that only authorised personnel have access, and preventing any tampering. The training should also cover the specific roles of senior officials on counting day, such as supervising the opening of the sealed boxes, overseeing the tabulation of results, and managing any protests or disruptions that may occur when the results are announced.

Effective cooperation between the state police and the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) is vital whenever the latter are deployed in a district or constituency to augment security. The training programme must therefore contain a dedicated segment that clarifies the respective jurisdictions, operational boundaries, and command structures of the CAPF relative to the local police. Officers should be made aware that the CAPF, which includes forces such as the Central Reserve Police Force, Border Security Force, and Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, are often called upon to provide additional manpower in districts with large populations, difficult terrain, or heightened risk of electoral violence. To facilitate seamless collaboration, the district administration must prepare a comprehensive briefing package for the CAPF. This package should contain a list of “vulnerable pockets” – specific villages, towns, or neighbourhoods that are prone to disturbances – as well as detailed information about the district’s terrain, climatic conditions, and any logistical challenges that may affect movement. It should also include a directory of police stations (PS) and their locations, a communication plan that outlines radio frequencies, contact numbers, and reporting protocols, and a set of clear dos and don’ts that guide CAPF personnel on how to interact with local communities and respect the civil administration’s authority. The training must guide the police in preparing this material, emphasising the need for accuracy, timeliness, and clarity, so that the CAPF can be deployed effectively and with minimal confusion.

In order to foster rapid and reliable coordination between civil election officials and police authorities, a directory of key functionaries should be compiled and distributed during the training. This directory must list the names, official designations, landline telephone numbers, and mobile phone numbers of senior officials in the Election Commission’s district office, the state election commission, and the police hierarchy, including both the superintendent of police and the designated nodal officers. By providing this contact information in a single, easily accessible document, the training seeks to eliminate delays caused by unclear lines of communication and to enable swift information exchange during critical moments of the election. The directory should also be handed over to senior CAPF officers so that they can directly contact their police counterparts in case of emergencies or operational queries.

When it comes to the preparation of training material specifically for the CAPF, the content must be carefully curated to address the immediate needs of election security. The state should design a modular training program that spans three to seven days of intensive field instruction, focusing on the practical aspects that CAPF personnel will encounter during low‑level electoral deployment. This instruction should be enriched with district‑specific data, such as maps of polling stations, identification of high‑risk zones, and local cultural sensitivities. The training must also include a clear set of “dos and don’ts” that outline acceptable conduct for CAPF soldiers operating in civilian environments, such as respecting local customs, avoiding the use of excessive force, and maintaining proper documentation of any incidents. To ensure the highest quality of instruction, the state should identify experienced trainers and resource persons – senior police officers, election officials, and legal experts – and brief them well in advance on the training objectives and the expected outcomes. Particular attention must be given to areas with difficult or harsh terrain, such as hilly regions, flood‑prone districts, or places with extreme weather conditions, where specialized training on movement, logistics, and health safety is essential. By incorporating these elements, the training will prepare the CAPF to respond effectively to the unique challenges posed by each electoral environment.

Motivation and morale of the security forces constitute a critical component of the overall success of the electoral process. Conducting elections in a democratic nation of vast size and diversity demands the mobilisation of large numbers of police personnel, CAPF troops, and auxiliary staff, which can be a physically and psychologically taxing endeavour. Pro‑active measures taken by district police and the district administration can significantly alleviate stress and boost the spirits of the forces. Initiatives may include the provision of adequate rest periods, the establishment of well‑equipped temporary accommodation near deployment sites, the recognition of exemplary performance through commendations and awards, and the organisation of brief morale‑building sessions that allow personnel to share experiences and concerns. Additionally, ensuring that logistical support – such as food, water, medical supplies, and reliable transportation – is consistently available helps to prevent fatigue and burnout. By fostering an environment in which the forces feel valued, supported, and clearly informed about their duties, the administration can enhance operational efficiency and reduce the likelihood of errors or lapses in security during the election. This comprehensive approach to motivation, combined with the detailed legal and procedural training outlined above, forms the backbone of a robust framework designed to safeguard the democratic process and uphold the rule of law throughout the electoral cycle.

The central and state governments have recognised that the smooth conduct of elections in a democratic country such as India depends not only on the integrity of the voting process itself but also on the quality of the support services that are extended to the uniformed personnel who are charged with maintaining law and order on the day of the poll. In order to give a coherent and systematic structure to these support services, the Commission has issued a comprehensive set of directives that are intended to be implemented by every state administration without delay. At the apex of this structure the Commission proposes that, in each state, an officer holding the rank of Inspector General of Police – the senior-most police officer in the state hierarchy who is responsible for overall law‑enforcement strategy and coordination – be designated as the Nodal Officer for the entire election exercise. By naming an Inspector General as the point person, the intention is to ensure that the highest level of authority and administrative experience is directly overseeing all matters related to the Central Armed Police Forces, commonly abbreviated as CAPF, which include the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, Sashastra Seema Bal and other central paramilitary units that are regularly deployed for election duty. The Nodal Officer’s responsibilities will cover a broad spectrum of logistical and welfare concerns, ranging from the provision of adequate accommodation for the troops, the arrangement of transport and other essential supplies, to the implementation of welfare measures such as compensation for extra hardship, the supply of packed lunches, and reimbursement for refreshment charges that are incurred during long periods of duty. By appointing the Inspector General in this capacity with immediate effect, the state governments are expected to issue clear instructions to all their departmental heads at the district level, directing them to cooperate fully with the CAPF units and to make available any resources that might be required for the smooth conduct of the polls. This directive underscores the principle that the state’s administrative machinery must act as a partner rather than a passive observer when central forces are brought in to secure the electoral process.

At the district level, the Commission’s guidelines stipulate that each district must install a dedicated District‑level Nodal Officer for welfare purposes, and that this officer must hold a rank no lower than Deputy Superintendent of Police. The Deputy Superintendent is a mid‑senior rank in the state police service, generally entrusted with the supervision of police operations across a sub‑division or a large urban area; by setting this minimum rank, the Commission seeks to guarantee that the officer in charge possesses sufficient authority and experience to address the diverse needs of the deployed forces. The contact details of this district Nodal Officer, including telephone numbers and official email addresses, must be circulated widely to all members of the state police as well as to the officers of the central forces who are assigned to the district for the election period. This ensures that any issues related to accommodation, logistics, medical emergencies, or welfare can be reported and resolved quickly through an established chain of communication.

Medical support for the police and paramilitary personnel engaged in election duty is a matter of paramount importance, and the Commission therefore mandates a comprehensive medical cover that includes earmarking of super‑specialty hospitals for the treatment of serious injuries. Super‑specialty hospitals are tertiary care institutions equipped with advanced medical technologies and specialist doctors capable of handling complex trauma, cardiac care, neurosurgery and other high‑risk conditions. The directive calls for cashless medical treatment, meaning that the cost of treatment will be covered by the government without requiring the individual officer to pay out of pocket and later seek reimbursement. In cases where a wounded officer must be evacuated from a remote or inaccessible location, the plan requires the provision of air ambulance services and, where necessary, helicopter support to transport the patient swiftly to a suitable medical facility. In addition, the state should enhance the overall medical infrastructure in the areas where large numbers of police personnel are deployed, by temporarily deploying additional doctors from other parts of the state to augment local health‑care capacities. Essential medicines, such as analgesics, antibiotics, antipyretics and emergency drugs, must be stocked in sufficient quantities at a centralised holding area within each district, and their distribution must be overseen by the medical officer of the local unit to guarantee that the correct dosage is administered under professional supervision.

Accommodation and logistics for the security forces must be arranged well in advance of the election dates. The Commission calls for a systematic plan that includes the identification of suitable lodging facilities, the provision of clean drinking water, sanitation, and the distribution of food packets that meet nutritional standards. Senior officials, such as the Additional Chief Secretary, the Principal Secretary of Home Affairs, or the Director General of Police, should conduct periodic visits to the accommodation sites to monitor the quality of the facilities and to address any deficiencies promptly. In order to determine the exact quantum of forces required for each phase of the election, a security audit can be undertaken. Such an audit involves a detailed analysis of the security situation in each constituency, the historical incidence of unrest, the geographical spread of polling stations, and the anticipated voter turnout. By quantifying the required strength, the audit helps prevent the situation where an inadequate number of personnel leads to excessively long duty hours, which in turn creates fatigue, loss of morale, and potential lapses in security.

Training is another critical component of the pre‑deployment preparation. The Commission emphasises that all security forces mobilised from outside the state must undergo a proper pre‑induction training programme. This training is designed to familiarise the personnel with the local terrain, climate conditions, cultural practices, and historical background of the region in which they will operate. Understanding the customs and language of the local population reduces the risk of inadvertent offence, while knowledge of local troublemakers, criminal networks, and extremist ideologies equips the officers to anticipate and neutralise potential threats. The training modules should include practical simulations, role‑playing exercises, and briefings on the legal framework governing the conduct of elections, thereby ensuring that the officers can perform their duties without error.

In addition to formal training, the forces must be sensitised and briefed well before deployment on the specific precautionary measures that are required during the election period. Sensitisation involves creating an awareness of the need to respect the rights of voters, to maintain impartiality, and to avoid any conduct that could be perceived as intimidation. Mandatory personal briefings conducted by the District Election Officer, who also holds the position of Deputy Commissioner (DEO‑cum‑DM), together with the Senior Superintendent of Police (SSP) or Superintendent of Police (SP) of the district, have been identified as a powerful motivating factor. During these briefings, officers are instructed on the routes they will patrol, the procedures for securing the periphery of each polling booth, the protocols for providing protection to voters and polling officials, and the procedures for escorting the officials safely after the polls have closed. By receiving clear, written instructions and verbal guidance from senior district officials, the officers are more likely to internalise their responsibilities and to act with confidence.

The preparation of detailed route maps is an essential logistical step that must be completed at least one month before the elections. These maps should indicate the precise locations of all polling stations, the intensity of anti‑social activities reported in the area, and should be marked with a grid reference system that can be easily interpreted by all operational parties, including the CAPF, state police, and local administration. Making these maps available to every unit ensures that all personnel share a common situational awareness and can coordinate movements efficiently. To further reinforce learning, the Commission suggests the use of case‑study methods, supplemented by photographs, video footage, and documentary films that illustrate previous incidents, both positive and negative. By analysing these case studies in group discussions, the forces can identify best practices and avoid repeating past mistakes.

The timing of the various phases of polling must allow for a sufficient gap between them. This interval provides the forces with a period of rest, reduces the likelihood of cumulative fatigue, and creates a window for the replenishment of supplies and the assessment of any emerging security concerns. Decision‑making at the state and district levels should be participatory, involving coordination meetings that bring together representatives of the Election Commission, the state police, the central forces, and other relevant agencies. Such meetings facilitate the sharing of information, the resolution of inter‑agency conflicts, and the joint planning of operations.

Each CAPF team operating in a district should be paired with a representative of the local police from the moment of their induction until the conclusion of the electoral process. The local police officer brings invaluable knowledge of the terrain, the dialects spoken by the community, and the nuances of local customs, all of which help the central forces to perform their duties more effectively and with greater cultural sensitivity. The role of Indian Railways and its catering arm, the Integrated Railway Catering and Tourism Corporation (IRCTC), in providing transport, meals, and accommodation for the forces is also highlighted as a motivational factor. By utilising the extensive rail network and the catering services, the administration can ensure that large numbers of personnel are moved efficiently and fed adequately, even in remote locations.

In particularly inaccessible or mountainous constituencies, the deployment of helicopters can be a game‑changing logistical solution. Helicopter lifts allow security personnel and polling officials to be dropped directly at polling stations that are otherwise unreachable by road, thereby conserving the energy of the officers and ensuring that they arrive on time and in good condition for poll day duties. Modern technology must be harnessed to its full potential. The district administration should convey to the forces that any incident can be reported instantly and that a rapid response team is on standby. To this end, a fully equipped control room should be established at the district headquarters, equipped with Very High Frequency (VHF) radio sets, mobile phones, and conventional telephone lines. This control room must remain operational around the clock, from the pre‑electoral period through to the final counting of votes, so that real‑time information on the ground can be collected, analysed, and acted upon.

Preparation of detailed daily movement plans for the pre‑poll, poll, and post‑poll periods is vital for reducing stress among the deployed forces. These plans outline the routes, timings, rest periods, and contingency measures for each unit, and they must be prepared well in advance. The operational plans should be reviewed and personally supervised by the Superintendent of Police to ensure that they are realistic and that any adjustments can be made quickly. After the polling stations close, a safe and orderly return of the forces to their respective base camps must be guaranteed, using the same logistical channels that brought them to the polling sites.

Exigency plans, which are emergency response procedures, must also be formulated beforehand. Such plans cover scenarios such as the evacuation of a wounded officer in the event of a violent encounter, the replacement of Electronic Voting Machines (EVMs) if they are damaged or destroyed, and the reinforcement of security personnel in case of unexpected escalation of violence. All necessary resources – ambulances, spare EVMs, additional troops – must be pre‑positioned and the details of the plans must be communicated clearly to every member of the security forces, so that in a crisis they can act swiftly and without confusion.

The Election Commission of India (ECI) should recognise and reward units that demonstrate exceptional performance or achieve significant recoveries of lost or damaged equipment. Such recognition can take the form of certificates, commendation letters, or public acknowledgment, which serve to boost morale and encourage a culture of excellence. Finally, the various orders and guidelines that govern compensation for hardship, known as ex‑gratia payments, as well as the uniformity of remuneration across different forces, must be reviewed and updated in a timely manner. This ensures that all personnel receive fair and consistent remuneration for the additional risks and responsibilities they shoulder during the election process, thereby reinforcing the principle of equitable treatment and maintaining the confidence of the forces in the system that supports them.

A stable environment of law and order forms the essential foundation upon which free, fair, participatory and peaceful elections can be conducted. When the public perceives that the streets are safe and that the authorities are capable of preventing violence, intimidation or any form of coercion, voters are more likely to turn out in large numbers and to cast their ballots without fear. For this reason the State Police, which is the primary law‑enforcement agency within each Indian state and union territory, must marshal every available resource at its disposal to safeguard the electoral process. The mobilization does not stop with the regular police personnel; it also incorporates the auxiliary forces that exist to reinforce the police in times of heightened demand. These auxiliary bodies include the Home Guard, a volunteer organization that assists the police in crowd control, patrolling and emergency response; the Village Defence Force, a community‑based group that helps maintain peace in rural hamlets; and the Traffic Brigade, which is tasked with regulating vehicular movement, managing traffic congestion around polling stations and ensuring that transportation for voters and election staff proceeds without obstruction. By integrating these auxiliary units with the regular police, the authorities create a layered security architecture that can respond rapidly to emerging threats and that can provide additional manpower for routine duties such as guarding polling stations, escorting ballot boxes and monitoring public gatherings.

In addition to the state‑level forces, the Central Armed Police Forces, commonly abbreviated as CAPF, are called upon to supplement the state and union territory police when the scale of the election exceeds the capacity of local resources. The CAPF comprises several centrally administered paramilitary organisations such as the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, Central Industrial Security Force and others, each of which possesses specialised training, equipment and experience in dealing with large‑scale public order situations. When the Election Commission of India determines that a particular state or union territory requires additional security personnel, it issues a request to the Ministry of Home Affairs, which in turn issues moving orders to the relevant CAPF units, directing them to deploy to the identified districts and to assume specific responsibilities during the election period. It is crucial that both the CAPF and the State Police share a clear and common understanding of the objectives of the deployment before the operation commences. The moving orders from the Ministry of Home Affairs are typically transmitted in a formal written format, outlining the geographical areas of responsibility, the number of personnel to be deployed, the duration of the assignment and any special instructions regarding coordination with local authorities. To ensure that the directives are interpreted correctly and that the forces are adequately prepared, a comprehensive review of the orders must be undertaken at a senior level. While the minimum level for this review is the rank of Commandant within the CAPF, it is preferable that the review be conducted by a Deputy Inspector General (DIG) who is directly responsible for commanding the deployed troops. This higher‑level review allows the commander to examine both the explicit tasks described in the orders and the implicit expectations regarding the maintenance of public order, the protection of electoral infrastructure and the prevention of any form of electoral malpractice. The commander must then issue clear guidance to the individual officers and soldiers under his command, explaining the purpose of their deployment, the standards of conduct expected of them, the rules of engagement, and the procedures for reporting incidents. In parallel, the State Police operate under the overall strategic direction of the Director General of Police (DGP), the senior most police officer in the state, who is responsible for integrating the efforts of the regular police with those of the auxiliary forces and the CAPF. However, the day‑to‑day operational leadership in each district rests with the Superintendent of Police (SP), who must translate the overall objectives set by the DGP into concrete instructions for the police constables, the Home Guard volunteers, the Village Defence personnel and any other supporting staff assigned to election duties. The SP’s role is therefore critical: he must ensure that every officer and volunteer understands the specific goals of the deployment, the legal framework governing electoral conduct, and the practical measures needed to achieve a peaceful and orderly voting process.

The responsibilities that fall upon the District Superintendent of Police and his supporting staff are extensive and require meticulous planning, coordination and execution. The first phase of preparation, known as the pre‑poll arrangements, begins many months before the actual polling day. Planning for the election should be initiated roughly six months in advance, a time frame that allows for the identification of potential flashpoints, the allocation of resources, the training of personnel and the establishment of communication channels between the various agencies involved. One of the earliest steps is a joint touring exercise undertaken by the District Magistrate (DM) and the Superintendent of Police (SP). The DM, who is the senior civil administrator in the district, and the SP together travel to the more sensitive parts of the district, inspecting schools, community centres, market places and any other locations that might become focal points for political activity. Their joint presence serves a dual purpose: it signals to the public that the civil administration and the police are working in concert to ensure safety, and it provides an opportunity for both officials to assess ground realities, meet local leaders, and gather intelligence on possible sources of tension.

Simultaneously, the administration must ensure that the right officers are placed in the right posts before the election notification is issued. This involves assigning a Station House Officer (SHO), who is the senior police officer in charge of a particular police station, to each station that will be responsible for overseeing security at the associated polling stations. In addition, the officers in charge of outposts—smaller police detachments that may be situated in remote villages—must also be identified and briefed. Where the Electoral Commission of India’s norms require a higher level of oversight, gazetted officers such as the Sub‑Divisional Police Officer (SDPO) or an Additional Superintendent of Police (Additional SP) are posted to supervise larger clusters of polling stations. The criteria for these postings are based on professional competence, experience in handling law‑and‑order situations, and the ability to act impartially, thereby ensuring fairness in the conduct of the election.
