Nevertheless, the law provides a mechanism for removing an officer who is perceived to be acting with bias; such a removal can be effected only after obtaining prior approval from the Election Commission of India, thereby safeguarding the independence of the police while preserving the integrity of the electoral process.

Another critical element of pre‑poll preparation involves senior officers making targeted visits to the most vulnerable polling locations. The Sub‑Divisional Magistrate (SDM), who is the civil administrator of a sub‑division within the district, together with the SDPO, are directed to tour all the critical polling booths and any villages or hamlets that are deemed at risk of disturbance. Likewise, the Additional District Magistrate (ADM) or the Additional SP, as well as the DM and SP themselves, should also conduct extensive visits to a substantial number of these sensitive sites. The presence of senior officials on the ground serves three important purposes. First, it enables them to develop a more nuanced and comprehensive understanding of the local dynamics, including caste relations, communal sensitivities, and political rivalries, and to devise appropriate mitigation strategies. Second, the personal involvement of senior officers helps to reassure voters that the administration is attentive to their safety, thereby reducing fear and encouraging higher voter turnout. Third, these visits allow the senior officials to directly observe the performance of the SHO and other subordinate officers, offering them an opportunity to provide immediate feedback, correct any deficiencies, and ensure that the measures taken at the grassroots level are aligned with the overall security plan.

The Station House Officer, as the operational commander at the police station level, is tasked with visiting every polling station and each village within his area of responsibility. This exhaustive tour serves several interrelated objectives. Primarily, it acts as a confidence‑building measure: by appearing in person, the SHO signals to the electorate that the police are present and prepared to protect them, which in turn encourages citizens to cast their votes without intimidation. Special attention is given to villages where a particular community forms the numerical minority; for example, in a village where Muslims constitute the majority, Hindus would be regarded as the minority and would merit specific reassurance. During these visits, the SHO also educates voters about their constitutional rights, the importance of casting a valid vote, and the need to remain vigilant against fraudulent practices such as impersonation or ballot stuffing. The officer uses the opportunity to identify individuals who have a history of causing trouble during elections—commonly referred to as “goondas” or “history sheeters”—and to develop preventive strategies to neutralize any potential disruption. In addition, the SHO updates the register of firearms licences held by residents in the village, because under Election Commission guidelines, any licensed weapons must be surrendered to the authorities for the duration of the polling period to prevent intimidation. By conducting a demographic assessment, the SHO becomes acquainted with the composition of the electorate in terms of caste, religion and political affiliation, thereby gaining insight into any existing tensions or grievances that could erupt on polling day. Finally, the officer evaluates the suitability of each polling booth from a logistical perspective, examining factors such as accessibility for voters, the ease with which security reinforcements can reach the site, and the availability of basic amenities, all of which are essential for a smooth and orderly voting process. Through this comprehensive approach—combining strategic planning, senior‑level oversight, careful allocation of personnel, and detailed ground‑level assessments—the state and central law‑enforcement agencies create a robust framework designed to uphold the democratic principle that every citizen’s vote can be cast freely, fairly and without fear.

The physical premises chosen for the conduct of the election must be situated in an area that offers ample open space around it, allowing for the free movement of election personnel, security forces, and the public. Adequate illumination is essential not only for the safety of those present but also for the prevention of any covert or illicit activities that could be concealed by darkness. Moreover, the location must be free from any form of intimidation, coercion, or undue influence exerted by any individual, group, or organization. In other words, the building and its surroundings should not be subject to pressure from local strong‑men, political thugs, or any other actors who might attempt to sway the electoral process through fear or violence.

In accordance with the standards laid down by the Election Commission of India, a thorough criticality analysis of each polling booth must be undertaken. This analysis involves assessing the vulnerability of a booth based on a set of predefined parameters, such as its geographic location, the density of the electorate, the presence of any history of communal tension, and the likelihood of disruption. Where a booth is determined to be of high criticality—meaning that it is especially prone to disturbances—the deployment of a “roof‑top morcha” becomes necessary. A roof‑top morcha consists of a team of armed personnel positioned on an elevated platform that provides a 360‑degree field of vision, thereby creating a strong visual and physical deterrent against rowdy elements on the day of polling. The presence of such a fortified observation post is intended to discourage any attempts at intimidation or violence by making it clear that the security forces have a comprehensive view of the area.

Intelligence collection is a cornerstone of election security. The Station House Officer (the senior police officer in charge of the local police station) is required to maintain an up‑to‑date picture of any illegal activities that could threaten the integrity of the election. This includes gathering information on the possession and trafficking of illegal firearms, the illegal distillation of liquor, the storage or dumping of explosives, and any other contraband that could be used to create chaos. The SHO must keep himself constantly informed about developments in the surrounding villages, which may involve making repeated visits to those villages to observe the local situation first‑hand. Greater mobility and easier access to remote areas enhance the ability of the police to collect timely and accurate intelligence, allowing for swift preventive action before any illicit activity can affect the election.

The next stage, known as force assessment, requires a careful synthesis of the intelligence reports, the findings of the criticality analysis, and the guidelines issued by the Election Commission. Based on this synthesis, the police must evaluate what additional resources are required to maintain law and order on election day. This assessment covers the need for extra personnel, specialized weapons, communication equipment such as radios and telephones, anti‑riot gear, and the budgetary provisions necessary to procure these items. Existing manpower and material resources must be taken into account so that the assessment does not double‑count assets that are already available. Following the Election Commission’s directives on the deployment of forces, the Superintendent of Police (the senior officer responsible for policing the entire district) must forecast the demand for Central Armed Police Forces, reserve armed units, specific pieces of equipment, and the financial outlay required to sustain the operation.

Preventive actions are instituted under sections 107 and 116 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, which empower the police to initiate legal proceedings aimed at maintaining public order. These sections allow the authorities to pre‑emptively bind individuals who have a history of involvement in election‑related disturbances, such as aggressive party workers, caste or communal agitators, professional goons, local extortionists, and anyone known to wield illegal arms. The SHO must ensure that each of these persons is required to post a substantial surety—a monetary guarantee—under section 116(3) for a period of six months. This financial bond serves as a deterrent; if the individual breaches the conditions of the surety, the bond is immediately forfeited. Publicizing the seizure of sureties functions as a strong message to the broader community that the administration is serious about preserving peace, while also ensuring that the legal instrument is not misused for harassment or partisan targeting. Continuous supervision and careful monitoring are essential to balance the need for security with the protection of civil liberties.

Special drives are planned to commence approximately six months before the anticipated election date, using the date and month of the most recent general election as a reference point. These drives are comprehensive operations that target a range of illegal and potentially disruptive activities. First, they focus on individuals who are wanted or have been rewarded for criminal conduct, as well as those who hold outstanding warrants or have been previously convicted of offenses related to elections, such as possession of illicit weapons, illegal distillation of liquor, narcotics trafficking, or the handling of explosives. Second, the drives aim to arrest and, where appropriate, detain preventively those who continue to engage in criminal behavior, including political goons, intimidators, and other high‑risk individuals. Third, routine checks are conducted on vehicles, guest houses, and known explosive dump sites to ensure that no contraband is being stored or transported under the cover of election activity. Fourth, the drives seek to resolve lingering disputes of a political nature by invoking Section 110 of the Code of Criminal Procedure and any relevant state special acts, thereby preventing these disputes from spilling over into violence on election day. Fifth, a specific list is compiled of persons who have been reported to have committed offenses under the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes (Prevention of Atrocities) Act of 1989 during previous elections, ensuring that they are monitored closely. Sixth, the drives involve the revocation of firearms licenses for individuals with criminal records, followed by the collection and deposition of any licensed arms in accordance with Election Commission norms. Seventh, inspections of arms dealers and explosive warehouses are carried out to verify compliance with legal standards. Eighth, bail applications for criminals who might influence the election process are opposed, thereby limiting their ability to operate freely. Finally, each police station is tasked with investigating and prosecuting all electoral offenses that were registered during the prior election cycle, ensuring that accountability is pursued relentlessly.

The proclamation of Section 144 of the Code of Criminal Procedure is a legal tool used to impose restrictions on public assembly in order to enforce the Model Code of Conduct. By invoking Section 144, authorities can prohibit gatherings that might lead to unrest, thereby creating a legal environment conducive to the peaceful conduct of the election.

Preventive checking at security barriers constitutes another layer of protection. At each checkpoint, security personnel are mandated to inspect all incoming and outgoing traffic for weapons, liquor, explosives, and individuals who may be deemed anti‑social or prone to inciting violence. This systematic screening helps to prevent the influx of contraband and undesirable elements into the polling area.

Strict compliance with the Model Code of Conduct (MCC) is essential for maintaining a level playing field among political parties and candidates. The District Magistrate (the senior civil administrator of the district) and the Superintendent of Police must maintain regular communication with all political parties and candidates to ensure that the MCC is observed at every stage of the election process. The MCC contains detailed provisions concerning the misuse of vehicles during the election period, the prohibition of liquor sales, the prevention of vandalism to public and private property, regulation of loudspeaker usage, restrictions on the publication and distribution of election‑related brochures and pamphlets, limitations on the carrying of arms, guidelines for video recording of critical events, and rules governing the timing and permission for public rallies. It also requires political parties to bear the cost of security arrangements for any public meeting they organize, and it stipulates that canvassing activities must cease at least forty‑eight hours before polling. On the day of nominations, when parties typically attempt to showcase their numerical strength, the enforcement of the MCC must be particularly rigorous. By applying the code with utmost firmness, the administration demonstrates its resolve and capacity to uphold the rule of law, while simultaneously preparing the police force for the heightened activity that typically follows nomination filing. Adequate security arrangements, commonly referred to as “bandobast,” must be put in place at the offices of Returning Officers—the officials responsible for overseeing the electoral process—at the exact moment nominations are submitted, thereby averting any possibility of untoward incidents.

Any violation of the Election Code of Conduct must be met with the strongest possible punitive action, signaling to all participants that breaches will not be tolerated. This approach reinforces the credibility of the election and deters future infractions.

Finally, comprehensive security arrangements, also termed bandobast, must be provided for all processions, rallies, and public meetings organized by the various political parties. Prior permission from the appropriate authorities is required before any such gathering can take place. The political parties are obliged, under Election Commission guidelines, to finance the security measures required for any visit by a VIP (very important person) or for the protection of their own events. By ensuring that these financial responsibilities are met, the administration can allocate adequate resources for crowd control, traffic management, and emergency response, thereby safeguarding the electoral environment from disorder and ensuring that the democratic process proceeds smoothly and without intimidation.

All election‑related gatherings, whether they are public meetings, political rallies, processions, or any other form of assembly that takes place in the course of the electoral calendar, must be documented thoroughly. The documentation should include both written records and visual recordings, and the visual recordings should be made by professional videographers whenever the gathering is considered important or has the potential to influence the outcome of the election. By preserving a clear and accurate visual record, authorities are able to refer back to the exact sequence of events, the speakers who addressed the crowds, the messages that were conveyed, and any incidents that may have arisen, thereby providing an objective basis for later review or for use in any legal or administrative proceedings that may follow.

The role of the media in a democratic election is indispensable, and it is therefore essential that media outlets be kept fully informed about the measures being taken to ensure that the election is conducted in a free, fair, and transparent manner. Regular briefings should be organized in advance of the poll day so that journalists can publicize the steps that the election administration and the law‑enforcement agencies are taking to safeguard the integrity of the voting process. On the day of the poll itself, special security arrangements must be made to protect those journalists who have been authorized to cover the polling stations, the counting centres and any other critical locations. The presence of a secure environment for the press not only enables reporters to do their work without fear of intimidation or violence, it also sends a clear signal to the electorate that the authorities are committed to openness and accountability. Information about the security arrangements and any other logistical support provided to the media should be released to the public on the same day, so that voters can see that the polling process is being monitored by independent observers.

Intelligence agencies, which comprise a network of officers and analysts trained to gather, process and evaluate information that may affect national security, are called upon to play a specific role in the electoral context. Their task is to identify and collect evidence of any attempts to manipulate the ballot by the introduction of so‑called dummy candidates—individuals who are placed on the ballot not to win but to confuse voters, to split the vote of a particular segment of the electorate, or to act as a cover for illegal financial transactions. When the intelligence reports, corroborated by other reliable indicators such as financial records, communications intercepts or witness statements, provide sufficient grounds to believe that a candidate is being used merely as a placeholder, the authorities should move promptly to declare that individual a dummy candidate and to remove the name from the ballot. This preventive step helps to preserve the integrity of the voting process and to protect genuine competitors from unfair competition.

Creating an atmosphere that encourages peaceful participation in the election is as much a matter of perception as it is of concrete security measures. A conducive environment is one in which voters feel confident that the law‑enforcement agencies are competent, that any attempts at intimidation will be swiftly dealt with, and that the overall tone of the campaign is one of legitimate political competition rather than violence or coercion. To achieve this, several public‑relations initiatives may be employed. For example, organising flag‑bearing marches that demonstrate the presence of disciplined police units, broadcasting media reports that show police officers conducting regular firearms practice, and holding open training camps that are visible to the public all serve to convey the message that the police are prepared and vigilant. Publicising the arrest or detention of individuals who are known to have the capacity to instigate trouble on polling day further reinforces the notion that the authorities are taking decisive action against potential disruptors. By combining visual displays of readiness with transparent communication about enforcement actions, the police can build both morale among their own ranks and confidence among the electorate.

The supervision of police activity during the election is a continuous process that must be carried out with the same rigor as any other public‑service function. Every action taken by the police should be monitored to ensure that it complies with legal standards, that it is impartial, and that any deviations are corrected in a timely manner. The guiding principle of supervision can be expressed in the familiar maxim that an activity that is not overseen is, in effect, not being performed at all. Consequently, police officers should be readily accessible to political parties, to members of the press and to ordinary citizens who wish to raise concerns or lodge complaints. Regular meetings should be convened in which representatives of these groups can present their observations, and the police should verify the validity of any allegations and, where appropriate, implement corrective measures. This open‑door policy helps to maintain public trust and to prevent the perception that the police are acting in a partisan or arbitrary fashion.

Leadership within the police force during an election must be both visible and supportive. Senior officers should make a point of moving among the rank and file, engaging directly with the men and women who are on the front lines of election security. By visiting police posts, speaking with individual officers, and explaining the significance of their duties, senior leaders can reinforce morale and ensure that every member of the force understands the importance of maintaining order, protecting citizens and upholding the law. In addition, leaders should extend the same level of attention to officers who have been transferred from state or central agencies to assist with the election. Sharing meals, offering briefings, and attending to the logistical and personal needs of these external personnel fosters a sense of unity and purpose. The overall style of leadership that emerges should be one of “walk‑the‑talk,” meaning that senior officials not only issue directives but also demonstrate through their own conduct the standards they expect from everyone else.

Training and briefing are critical components of preparedness, especially when the stakes are high and the timetable is compressed. Educational materials such as training films, illustrated booklets, and concise manuals should be distributed to all personnel who will be involved in election duties. These resources should explain, in plain language, the code of conduct that governs the behavior of police officers during elections, as well as the specific duties that each officer is expected to perform. Motivational slogans and short speeches can be used to inspire confidence and to remind officers of the noble nature of their mission. The election code of conduct, which is the set of rules issued by the election commission to regulate the behavior of candidates, political parties and officials, must be explained clearly so that any breach can be identified and addressed quickly. Officers should be instructed on the “do’s and don’ts” of election policing: they must be impartial, treat all voters with courtesy, be punctual in responding to incidents, and understand precisely when the use of force is justified and when it is not. They should also be reassured that they will receive full institutional support for actions taken in good faith, and they should be equipped with the contact numbers of the nearest police stations and mobile phone hotlines to facilitate rapid communication. Particular attention should be paid to the training of personal security details assigned to protect candidates and other high‑profile individuals. These security teams must be briefed that they are not permitted to engage in intimidation of voters or to turn a blind eye to any violent acts committed by the persons they are protecting. If any member of a personal security detail violates these instructions, the law should be applied strictly and the offender should face the severest possible legal consequences.

The provision of security to candidates, election observers, and the electronic voting machines (EVMs) must be carried out in strict accordance with the standards set by the Election Commission of India. These standards prescribe the number of police personnel, the type of equipment, and the level of vigilance required to protect individuals and critical election infrastructure. During the final days of campaigning, many prominent politicians and other dignitaries travel to constituencies to deliver speeches, meet voters and rally support. At the same time, the regular police force is often occupied with routine duties such as patrolling villages, conducting special operations, and verifying voter lists. To avoid overburdening the regular units, a dedicated task force should be formed specifically to handle the security needs of visiting VIPs. This task force should receive a thorough briefing on the procedures and should operate under the command of a senior officer who can coordinate its activities with those of the local police station officer. Whenever a high‑profile individual plans to visit an area that falls under a particular police station’s jurisdiction, the special task force can be dispatched to supplement the local station house officer, thereby ensuring that both the routine responsibilities of the station and the additional security requirements are met without compromising either.

The electronic voting machines, which are sophisticated electronic devices used to record voter selections, must be stored in a secure facility both before they are dispatched to polling stations and after the voting has concluded, until they are taken to the counting centre. This facility, often referred to as a makeshift strong room, must be designed to allow easy ingress and egress of transport vehicles while simultaneously preventing unauthorized access. The strong room should be protected by multiple layers of security. First, an anti‑sabotage inspection must be carried out to detect any attempts to tamper with the machines. Second, both inner and outer security cordons—physical barriers established by police or central armed police forces—should be set up to keep intruders at a distance. Third, round‑the‑clock guards drawn from either the armed police or the Central Armed Police Forces should be stationed at strategic points, and watchtowers equipped with floodlights should provide constant visual monitoring. Additional safeguards include a reliable locking mechanism, fire‑fighting equipment, and a closed‑circuit television system that records every movement inside and around the strong room. Access control measures, such as biometric or password‑based entry, should be employed to ensure that only authorized personnel can open the doors. By integrating these various security elements, the integrity of the voting machines is preserved from the moment they are stored until the final tally is completed.

Finally, a dedicated election cell must be created within the police hierarchy to serve as the nerve centre for all election‑related activities. This cell should be led by an officer of the rank of Deputy Superintendent of Police or Police Inspector, and it must be equipped with the full complement of modern communication and information‑technology tools. Telephones, fax machines, photocopiers, computers, scanners and reliable internet connectivity are essential for rapid information exchange, documentation and reporting. The cell should operate under the overall supervision of a senior police officer who can ensure that its functions are carried out efficiently. Among its principal responsibilities is the continuous monitoring of police actions across the jurisdiction and the preparation of detailed reports that are sent to the Election Commission of India and to any other relevant authorities. By maintaining a constant flow of accurate data, the election cell enables higher‑level decision‑makers to assess the effectiveness of security measures, to identify any irregularities, and to take corrective action when necessary. This systematic approach to oversight, combined with the other measures described, forms a comprehensive framework designed to protect the democratic process, to uphold the rule of law and to ensure that the election is conducted in a manner that is transparent, impartial and respected by all stakeholders.

The task of preparing for a general election in a district is a massive logistical exercise that demands meticulous planning, coordination among multiple agencies, and strict adherence to the guidelines issued by the Election Commission of India (ECI). The ECI, which is the constitutional authority responsible for administering elections to the Lok Sabha, Rajya Sabha, and State Legislative Assemblies, issues detailed instructions on how each element of the electoral process should be organized, especially in respect of the security of the polling stations. In order to meet these requirements, the senior officer who is in charge of the district’s election operations must first compile a comprehensive list of those polling booths that are deemed to be sensitive. Sensitive booths are those where the probability of law‑and‑order disturbances, communal tension, or other forms of intimidation is higher than average, often because of past incidents, the presence of a large number of influential political actors, or the demographic composition of the electorate. The compilation of this list is not a simple matter of looking at a map; it involves consulting past election reports, intelligence inputs, and the observations of local officials, and it must be done under the direct supervision of an Observer appointed by the Election Commission. Once the list is finalized, a deployment plan must be drawn up that aligns with the specific guidelines set forth by the Commission. These guidelines prescribe, for example, the number of police personnel that should be posted at each booth, the type of equipment they should carry, and the manner in which the forces should be rotated to avoid any perception of bias.

With the sensitivity matrix in place, the next major step is to mobilize the human resources that will be required on the day of polling. This mobilization encompasses a wide variety of forces, each of which brings a different set of capabilities to the election environment. The district police, commonly referred to as the local law‑enforcement agency, forms the core of the security contingent, but they are supplemented by personnel drawn from the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF), which include the Border Security Force, Central Reserve Police Force, Indo‑Tibetan Border Police, and other centrally administered paramilitary organisations. In addition, State Armed Reserves—state‑level paramilitary units that are often called upon during emergencies—are incorporated into the plan. The senior officer must also consider forces that are stationed in neighboring districts and can be called in to reinforce the security detail if the situation demands. Rural and semi‑urban areas often rely on traditional local security providers such as village chowkidars, who act as watchmen for their communities, and home guards, who are volunteers trained to assist the police in maintaining order. The National Cadet Corps (NCC), a youth development movement under the Ministry of Defence that trains school and college students in basic military discipline, is another valuable source of manpower, as its cadets can be deployed for crowd management and logistical assistance. Finally, any other voluntary groups that have expressed a willingness to serve, such as local NGOs, student unions, or community service organisations, may be incorporated into the pool of election‑day personnel. The senior officer must keep a detailed account of the numbers, ranks, and special skills of all these forces, noting where each individual is drawn from, in order to be able to allocate them efficiently on the day of the poll.

Having identified and accounted for all the personnel, the district election office must arrange for their reception and accommodation. Separate reception counters need to be established for each external unit that is arriving, because the logistics of checking identification, issuing temporary passes, and directing the arriving contingent to its designated lodging differ from one formation to another. For instance, a CAPF contingent will have different documentation requirements compared to a group of NCC cadets. The reception area must therefore be organised into distinct sections, each staffed by clerks who are familiar with the particular procedures that apply to the respective force. Once the personnel have cleared the reception, they are assigned to temporary housing that is adequate to sustain them for the duration of the election operation. The accommodation plan must guarantee that basic necessities are available: a reliable supply of drinking water, sanitary facilities such as toilets and bathing areas, a mess hall where meals can be prepared and served, and an uninterrupted source of electricity for lighting and the operation of electronic devices. In addition, provisions for medical aid must be made, either through on‑site first‑aid stations staffed by trained medics or by ensuring quick access to a nearby hospital. The comfort of the personnel is not a luxury but a practical requirement; well‑rested and well‑cared‑for staff are far more likely to perform their duties with diligence and composure, thereby reducing the risk of errors that could compromise the integrity of the election.

The deployment of personnel does not stop at the provision of accommodation. A further layer of planning involves assigning specific duties to individual members of the force and ensuring that they are equipped with the correct documentation and tools for the tasks they will perform. Each officer and constable is issued a duty card that contains the details of the polling station to which they have been assigned, the time at which they must report for duty, the name of the supervising officer, and any special instructions that pertain to the particular booth. The duty cards serve as a verifiable record that can be cross‑checked on the day of the election to confirm that each person is where they are supposed to be. In addition to the duty cards, a comprehensive election booklet is prepared for every participant. This booklet outlines the responsibilities that each individual must fulfil, the standard operating procedures for handling election material, the protocol for interacting with voters and election officials, and the chain of command for reporting incidents. By distributing this booklet well in advance, the senior officer ensures that every participant is familiar with the expectations and can act consistently throughout the polling process.

Security equipment must also be allocated in a manner that matches the sensitivity of each booth. Anti‑riot gear, which includes items such as helmets, shields, batons, and chemical agents, is distributed according to the risk assessment carried out earlier. Highly sensitive booths, for example those located in areas with a history of communal clashes, will receive a larger stock of such equipment, while booths with a relatively calm environment may receive only the basic necessities. The movement of this equipment is closely monitored, and a detailed inventory is kept so that any loss or misplacement can be quickly identified and addressed. Alongside the physical gear, the deployment plan must also encompass the logistical support required to move personnel and equipment efficiently. This includes the requisition, allocation, and monitoring of vehicles—both for transporting voting machines, known as electronic voting machines (EVMs), and for ferrying police and security forces to their assigned locations. Communication sets, such as portable radios, satellite phones, and wired telephone lines, are essential for maintaining contact between the various levels of command, especially in remote or difficult‑to‑reach polling stations. The senior officer must ensure that each team has a primary communication channel and a backup (alternate) channel, and that the priority of messages—such as distress calls versus routine status updates—is clearly defined. The distribution of arms and ammunition, which may be required for the protection of sensitive booths, must also be tracked carefully to prevent any unauthorized use.

Financial management is another critical component of the overall plan. The election budget allocated by the central government must be monitored with precision, and funds must be disbursed in a timely manner to cover the honorarium that is paid to police personnel for their extra duty, the food allowance that compensates for meals provided during the election, and any travel and dearness allowances (TA/DA) that are granted for movement and expenses incurred. Advances may be issued to the commanding officers of each unit so that they can procure necessary supplies ahead of time, with the expectation that the expenses will later be reconciled against the official accounts. Inadequate or delayed financial support can lead to demoralization among the ranks, which in turn could affect the quality of policing on election day.

The coordination of the entire operation reaches its climax on the day of voting, when the district office transforms into the central control room for the election. The control room functions as the nerve centre, receiving real‑time information from polling stations, dispatching additional resources where disturbances are reported, and maintaining a constant flow of communication between field units and senior officials. The senior officer, often referred to as the District Election Officer (DEO), oversees the control room and ensures that all information is processed swiftly and accurately. In order to make the deployment of men and equipment as effective as possible, the senior officer must adhere strictly to the sensitivity‑based policy prescribed by the Election Commission. This policy dictates that police personnel should not be posted at polling stations that fall within the jurisdiction of the police station where they are permanently assigned, to avoid any perception of favoritism or undue influence. It also requires that the caste composition of the police contingent at each booth be mixed, reflecting the demographic diversity of the electorate and thereby reducing the risk of communal bias. The deployment plan must be randomly inspected, meaning that unannounced checks are conducted to verify that the policy is being implemented correctly and that no deliberate deviations have occurred. A trustworthy senior officer is entrusted with this responsibility, and his or her integrity is paramount because any lapse could undermine public confidence in the election process.

Another essential element of the deployment plan involves the pooling of polling parties based on the routes they will travel. Election material, such as EVMs, ballot boxes, and other essential supplies, must be moved from central storage locations to the individual polling stations. To accomplish this, each member of a polling party is assigned a specific vehicle number, which is recorded in advance so that on the day of dispatch there is no confusion about which vehicle each person should board. This systematic assignment reduces the likelihood of mix‑ups, prevents delays, and ensures that the material reaches each booth in a timely fashion. In conjunction with the vehicle allocation, the senior officer prepares duty cards and distributes them to the personnel, arranges reception counters for forces arriving from outside the district, and finalizes the accommodation plan for those forces. The distribution of TA/DA and remuneration is coordinated so that the personnel receive their payments without undue bureaucracy, which helps maintain morale. Additionally, the officer oversees the redeployment of vehicles, arms, and other equipment after they have been used, ensuring that they are returned to the appropriate storage points for accounting and future use.

Effective communication planning is a vital, yet often underappreciated, component of the entire operation. The senior officer must decide how many communication sets will be deployed, which frequencies will be used for primary communication, and which will serve as backups. In some remote areas, the primary channel may be a satellite link, while a terrestrial radio network may act as the alternate. The priority of communication must be established; for example, an SOS signal from a polling station under attack must be given higher precedence than routine status reports. By carefully mapping out these communication pathways and ensuring that every team is equipped with the appropriate devices, the district can conserve energy and resources, allowing more focused effort on the actual policing of the poll day.

The dispatch of polling parties is a complex logistical operation that must be executed with precision. At least two days before the scheduled poll, distribution centres are set up where election material—including EVMs, voter lists, and ballot papers—is loaded onto vehicles. These centres are fortified with a sufficient “bandobast,” a term of Indian origin that denotes a strong security presence, typically consisting of police constables and armed personnel who guard the material against tampering or theft. Police escorts and mobile police parties accompany the polling parties as they travel along pre‑designated routes, ensuring that the election material reaches each polling booth well before the voting window opens. In certain cases, the routes may need to be “sanitized,” meaning that they are inspected and cleared of any potential threats such as hidden explosives, roadblocks, or hostile individuals. This pre‑emptive action minimizes the risk of disruptions on the actual day of voting.

On the day of the election, the police have a clearly defined set of responsibilities that are articulated in an action plan aimed at ensuring a smooth and peaceful polling process. One of the primary objectives is to establish “area domination,” a strategy that involves a visible and proactive police presence throughout the constituency to deter any potential violations of the Model Code of Conduct—a set of guidelines issued by the Election Commission that governs the behaviour of political parties, candidates, and officials during the election period. The police must strictly enforce these rules at the polling booths, which includes preventing the erection of party booths within 200 metres of a polling station, limiting the number of chairs and tables at any allowed party booth to two chairs and a single table, and prohibiting the display of election posters or any form of crowding that could intimidate voters. Voters must be allowed to queue orderly, and polling agents, who are appointed by political parties to monitor the voting process, must behave appropriately—sitting properly, refraining from obstructing the flow of voters, and not taking the electoral roll (the official list of registered voters) outside the polling station. Furthermore, no minister, member of parliament, or member of the legislative assembly is permitted to act as a polling agent, as their presence could be perceived as exerting undue influence.

For polling booths that have been identified as sensitive, an additional layer of security is provided by placing armed personnel on rooftops, offering a commanding view of the surrounding area and a rapid response capability in case of any disturbance. Voters may also be subject to frisking, a security measure that involves a quick visual and tactile inspection to ensure that they are not carrying prohibited items, such as weapons or contraband, that could threaten the safety of the election environment. All personnel assigned to bandobast duty must possess a clear understanding of the location of the nearest police stations, telephone exchanges, telegraph offices, and wireless stations, as well as the placement of the nearest striking force—a specialized rapid‑response unit designed to intervene in emergencies. They must also be aware of the locations of Quick Reaction Teams (QRTs) and the headquarters of mobile police parties, so that they can call for reinforcements without delay.

If a disturbance arises that requires additional support, the protocol mandates that the nearest available communication means be used to send an immediate intimation to the appropriate authority, typically the senior officer overseeing the district’s election security. This rapid notification enables a swift mobilization of additional forces to contain and resolve the situation before it escalates. The Election Commission also imposes strict rules regarding the movement of vehicles within the polling area. Only vehicles that possess a valid permit issued specifically for election duties are allowed to operate, and the misuse of such permits—such as unauthorized use for personal errands or commercial transport—is prohibited. The permit holder, often an election agent or another authorized individual with a clean criminal record, may be accompanied by one additional person, also without any criminal background, to ensure accountability and reduce the potential for abuse.

Finally, the overall success of the election day rests upon the careful orchestration of all these elements: the identification and protection of sensitive booths, the mobilization and accommodation of diverse security forces, the precise deployment of men and equipment, the efficient dispatch and safeguarding of election material, and the strict enforcement of electoral conduct codes. By adhering to the comprehensive guidelines laid down by the Election Commission of India and by ensuring that each component of the plan is executed with diligence, transparency, and fairness, the district can facilitate a peaceful, orderly, and credible voting process that upholds the democratic principles upon which the nation is built.

The requirement that photographs of the two individuals who have been granted official permission be prominently displayed on the windscreen of any vehicle used in election‑related activities is intended to serve as a visual deterrent against the transport of “goondas,” a colloquial term for hired thugs or criminal elements, as well as ordinary civilians who might otherwise be used to intimidate voters or interfere with the polling process. By ensuring that the identity of the authorized personnel is clearly visible, election officials hope to reduce the risk that unauthorized persons will be ferried to polling stations for illicit purposes. In addition, the same regulation expressly forbids the transport of voters by any private or public conveyance on the day of the election, a measure designed to prevent the possibility of vote‑buying or undue influence that could arise if voters were moved en masse by parties or interest groups.

Strategic placement of temporary roadblocks, known locally as “naka” barriers, together with permanent checkpoint barriers, is mandated to be carried out with sufficient police force to inspect every vehicle that approaches a polling venue. The purpose of these barriers is to provide a physical line of defence that allows trained personnel to stop, search, and verify the contents of each vehicle, looking specifically for illicit weapons such as firearms, explosives, or homemade bombs, as well as contraband items like arrack or other forms of liquor that could be used to bribe or agitate voters. The same inspections are meant to identify and detain “anti‑social elements,” a phrase that encompasses individuals who have a record of disruptive behaviour, intimidation, or participation in violent acts. Only those drivers who present a valid permit—an official document that confirms they have been cleared for movement on the restricted election day—are permitted to proceed, because the law imposes a blanket ban on vehicular traffic on election day in order to minimise congestion and the opportunity for organized interference.

A further precaution involves the sealing of district borders on the day of polling. By closing off entry points, the authorities aim to prevent any criminal operatives or hostile groups from crossing into the district and attempting to sway the outcome by intimidation, ballot stuffing, or other illegal means. The level of police force deployed at each polling location is calibrated according to the sensitivity of the area, which is assessed based on factors such as previous incidents of violence, the presence of politically volatile communities, and intelligence reports indicating heightened risk. The more sensitive the booth, the greater the number of officers and the higher the readiness of specialized units.

In addition to the duties of the checkpoint personnel, other senior officers have clearly defined responsibilities. The district’s senior officials, including the Sub‑Divisional Police Officer (SDPO) and the Station House Officer (SHO), are expected to maintain high mobility, meaning they must be able to move quickly across the district with a reserve of additional personnel that can be called upon at short notice. Their mobility ensures that they can enforce the election code of conduct not only at the polling booths themselves but also along the roads leading to them, providing immediate support wherever the situation demands. To guarantee continuous coverage, the police structure includes a network of sector and zonal mobile units, each equipped with its own reserve force. The operational plan calls for at least one of these mobile units to reach any given polling booth within a fifteen‑ to twenty‑minute interval, thereby creating a rapid response capability that can deter any attempt at intimidation or violence.

These mobile units are also tasked with logistical support, such as delivering food packets to officers who are posted at booths or manning barriers for extended periods, ensuring that the personnel on duty remain nourished and able to perform their duties without fatigue. In villages where there is a genuine fear that members of weaker social groups or local minorities might be prevented from leaving their homes due to intimidation, specially designated village mobile parties are to be deployed inside those communities. Their presence serves both as a protective measure for vulnerable residents and as a signal that the state is actively safeguarding their right to vote.

To further strengthen monitoring, “tracking teams” equipped with video cameras are assigned to keep an eye on individuals who are considered “difficult,” meaning they have a history of breaching election laws or have been identified by intelligence sources as likely to cause trouble. These teams receive explicit instructions to arrest any such individual the moment they engage in illegal activity or violate the electoral code. Complementing the tracking teams are adequate reserves of anti‑riot units, which are specialized police formations trained to handle large‑scale disturbances. These units are positioned strategically so that, upon receiving real‑time information about a flare‑up, they can rush to the affected area, contain the disturbance, and maintain order until the situation stabilises. Once the crisis is resolved, the anti‑riot units return to their headquarters to remain on standby for any further calls for assistance.

Mobile police teams have a multifaceted role that begins well before polling day and extends through the completion of the voting process. Their first task is to escort the election material—such as ballot boxes and electronic voting machines (EVMs)—as well as polling personnel and any police officers assigned to static security posts, known as “bandobast,” to the designated polling stations. Prior to the actual election, these mobile teams travel along the routes assigned to them in order to become familiar with the terrain, identify any potential obstacles, and gather intelligence that could be useful on the day of the vote. On election day, the teams move swiftly from booth to booth, aiming to complete a circuit of all polling stations in the shortest possible time while paying particular attention to locations identified as trouble spots. If an incident erupts, the mobile team is instructed to rush to the scene, secure the area, and relay detailed information to the nearest police station so that additional resources can be mobilised if necessary.

As the voting process draws to a close, the mobile units are required to position themselves in the final village along each route, at which point they escort the polling officials and the polled EVMs back along the same or alternate routes to a secure collection point. Throughout these operations, the teams are equipped with very high frequency (VHF) and ultra‑high frequency (UHF) radios, which provide reliable two‑way communication with other mobile parties, striking forces, special striking forces, and checkpoint posts. The communication system is essential for cross‑checking any information received from the public; however, it is also crucial that the mobile teams avoid converging en masse in a single area, which could leave other polling stations under‑protected and vulnerable to “booth capturing,” a term that refers to the illegal seizure or manipulation of a polling station. The final responsibility of the mobile units is to ensure that the electronic voting machines, after the poll, are safely escorted back to the counting centre without tampering or loss.

The Election Control Room functions as the central nerve centre for all activities related to the polling process. Its primary function is to monitor the overall progress of the election in real time, using live feeds and reports from the field to assess whether voting is proceeding smoothly across the district. In addition, the control room continuously watches for any acts of violence, intimidation, or attempts at booth capturing, and, when such incidents are detected, it coordinates the dispatch of reinforcement teams after obtaining the necessary authorization from the senior officer in charge of the operation. Situation reports, commonly referred to as SITREPs, are generated by the control room and disseminated to all relevant stakeholders, including senior police officials, election administrators, and, where appropriate, political party representatives.

When a political party or an individual candidate lodges a complaint on election day, the control room staff are responsible for recording the grievance, verifying its authenticity, and ensuring that it is forwarded to the appropriate investigative authority. Maintaining a constant liaison with the media is also a key task; the control room receives information from journalists about emerging trouble spots and, in turn, provides the media with accurate updates, thereby helping to manage public perception and prevent misinformation. The control room also manages the inventory of reserve personnel and equipment, ensuring that a ready stock of additional forces, vehicles, and communications gear is available for rapid deployment. After the polls close, the control room tracks the receipt of the electronic voting machines from each polling station, confirming that each unit arrives safely at the counting venue.

Once voting has ended, a series of post‑poll arrangements must be implemented to safeguard the integrity of the count. The venue selected for counting the votes must be evaluated carefully from a security perspective; it should be a location that can be easily secured, is not susceptible to sabotage, and provides a controlled environment for the handling of the electronic voting machines. Prior to the commencement of counting, a thorough anti‑sabotage inspection is performed to ensure that no devices or persons capable of interfering with the machines are present. Electrical safety checks are also carried out to rule out the risk of fire or short circuits, which could disrupt the counting process or damage critical equipment.

Security personnel are deployed in multiple concentric layers: an outer cordon to keep unapproved vehicles and pedestrians away, an intermediate cordon that acts as a buffer zone, and an inner cordon that restricts access to only those individuals who have been positively identified and are authorized to be present. An isolation cordon is established so that entry is limited exclusively to accredited officials and pass‑holders; every person who attempts to enter the counting hall undergoes a verification process that includes checking identification documents and conducting a frisking, or pat‑down search, to prevent the introduction of contraband. Separate lanes are constructed for the escorted movement of the polled electronic voting machines, ensuring that they can be brought into the counting area under tight supervision without mixing with other traffic. The counting venue is further fortified with robust barricades and meshing that prevent unauthorized entry, and additional barriers are installed to create distinct lanes for different groups of officials, thereby reducing the risk of crowding and potential clashes.

Strategically positioned searchlights illuminate the perimeter at night, while watchtowers provide elevated observation points for security personnel to monitor activity around the counting centre. The deployment plan is designed so that only those individuals who have been positively identified as pass‑holders are allowed to cross each cordon, and every entrant is subjected to a thorough frisk to detect any hidden items that could be used to disrupt the count. In order to maintain the stamina and morale of the security forces stationed at the venue, provisions for food and tea are arranged to be supplied at appropriate intervals throughout the counting process.

After the results have been declared, a victory procession is often organized to celebrate the winning candidate or party. Such processions have the potential to become flashpoints for conflict, especially when supporters of rival factions are present in the same area. Consequently, the authorities may impose restrictions or regulate the procession to prevent violent clashes. Coordination with the winning candidate’s team can facilitate smoother arrangements, but the police must still put in place strong security measures—such as deploying additional personnel, setting up barriers, and monitoring crowd movements—to ensure that supporters of different political groups do not come into violent contact.

Finally, the investigation of any election‑related offences must be concluded expeditiously. Police investigators are required to complete all inquiries into violations such as intimidation, illegal transportation of voters, or tampering with voting equipment as soon as the electoral process has formally ended. Once the investigation is finished, the police are obligated to file a detailed police report in the appropriate jurisdictional court without undue delay, thereby allowing the judicial system to take any necessary further action against those who have broken the law. This swift completion of investigations helps to reinforce public confidence in the electoral system and deters future attempts to subvert the democratic process.

During the period that runs from the announcement of the election schedule to the actual casting of votes, commonly referred to as the poll cycle, a number of disturbances that do not rise to the level of cognizable offences are nonetheless observed in many states. Such incidents include the creation of disorder at public meetings, the disturbance of order at polling stations on the day of the vote, and the conduct of political campaigning inside places of worship or other religious gatherings. Although these actions are technically covered by existing statutes and can be dealt with promptly under the appropriate sections of the relevant law, the individuals who commit them are frequently released on bail almost immediately. The rapid granting of bail, coupled with the recurrence of the same violations, points to a pattern of political patronage in which local political actors intervene to protect their supporters. To counteract this tendency, it is proposed that a systematic matrix be prepared for the purpose of daily deployment reporting. This matrix would record every measure taken to dominate an area, for example the conduct of patrols or other forms of area domination, and would specify the names of the police stations and the precise localities that have been covered. In addition, a dated video recording of each location that is known to be sensitive should be produced and made available each day to the District Magistrate, who is the senior administrative officer responsible for maintaining law and order in the district. The inclusion of visual evidence serves two important objectives: it demonstrates that confidence‑building efforts are being carried out in a transparent manner, and it reduces the opportunity for officers who are aligned with particular political interests to misreport the level of activity that has actually taken place.

In parallel with the daily reporting of area domination, a more thorough review of individuals who have been designated as “history‑sheeters” is recommended. The term history‑sheeter refers to a person who has been recorded in police records as having a history of anti‑social or criminal behaviour, often over a prolonged period. Rather than relying on the most recent list compiled by the district police, which may have been pruned to omit certain names for political reasons, the authorities should trace the records back for at least five to six years. By compiling an area‑wise list that includes all persons who have appeared in the records during that longer timeframe, it becomes possible to identify individuals who might have been deliberately excluded from the current list in order to protect politically influential persons. This comprehensive approach ensures that the security apparatus does not overlook potential sources of disruption simply because they have been temporarily removed from a shortened register.

A further measure aimed at preventing the misuse of confiscated weaponry concerns the handling of arms and ammunition that have been seized by law‑enforcement agencies. The practice of “recycling” – that is, re‑issuing or otherwise re‑using seized weapons for unlawful purposes – cannot be dismissed out of hand. Consequently, when a notification of the poll schedule is issued, every cache of illegal weapons and ammunition that has been recorded as seized in a particular Assembly Constituency or police station jurisdiction must be formally marked by the Election Commission of India as sealed. This immediate sealing creates a legal barrier that prevents the items from being moved or tampered with. Any new operations that result in the seizure of additional illegal weapons should be documented separately on a daily basis and must be certified by the Superintendent of Police of the concerned district. The certification by the superintendent, together with the earlier sealing order from the Election Commission, creates a double layer of accountability that makes it far more difficult for any of the previously confiscated material to be reintroduced into illegal circulation. If a seized weapon is later required as evidence in a criminal trial, the certification process still provides a clear chain of custody, thereby simplifying the court’s task of taking cognizance of the evidence while ensuring that both the district magistrate and the superintendent remain answerable for the handling of the material.

To reinforce these safeguards, a system of continuous monitoring should be instituted. This includes the creation of an electronic law‑and‑order portal that aggregates real‑time data on security operations, as well as the preparation of a daily law‑and‑order report that captures the status of deployments, incidents, and remedial actions. Such mechanisms allow senior officials to track the progress of the security plan and to intervene promptly when deviations are identified.

The execution of an election security plan is vulnerable to failure if certain risks are not addressed. One major risk arises when the deployment plan is drafted in an ad‑hoc manner, without a careful assessment of the specific conditions on the ground. There is a temptation to simply replicate the blueprint that was used for the previous general election, but this approach must be avoided because each electoral contest presents its own set of challenges. Another source of vulnerability is the improper prioritisation of the various types of security forces that are available. If the allocation of personnel does not reflect the distinct needs of each district, each assembly constituency, and each police station, the plan will be ineffective. The state and district authorities must therefore evaluate the entire “basket” of forces – ranging from state police to central paramilitary units – and assign them according to the precise requirements of each locality.

Commitment on the part of both the state‑level planners and the district‑level implementers is essential to guarantee that the plan is carried out in full and in accordance with the prescribed norms. The individuals who will be responsible for leading the execution must be thoroughly familiar with every aspect of the plan, a familiarity that should be cultivated by the architects of the plan through detailed briefings and rehearsals. Moreover, the plan must retain the flexibility to be adjusted at short notice should an unexpected situation arise; an unwillingness to modify the deployment in response to new information would render the plan useless. Selecting the right personnel for the right tasks is perhaps the most critical element of the execution phase; the competence and suitability of each officer, supervisor, and support staff must be matched to the specific duties they are expected to perform.

It is also important that all participants understand the serious consequences of deliberately ignoring the plan. The Election Commission of India exercises disciplinary authority over all election officials, including those who provide security, and this authority must be communicated clearly to every officer involved. A lack of foresight in the planning of resources – such as equipment, transport, and manpower – can lead to shortages at critical moments, so both state and district administrations should adopt a focused approach that builds sufficient reserves to cover any eventuality. Training constitutes a decisive factor in the success of any operation; every individual who is deployed must receive thorough preparation, and no one should be sent into the field without having completed the required training modules. Finally, the deployment plan must anticipate potential problems by incorporating an acute understanding of local realities, cultural sensitivities, and the historical patterns of disturbance in each area. By embedding this depth of knowledge into the planning process, the authorities can better navigate the complex landscape of election security, especially in regions that are affected by left‑wing extremism, insurgency, or militant activity, which present challenges that are markedly different from those encountered in the rest of the country.

Maoist organisations in India are fundamentally hostile to any form of democratic engagement, including the conduct of elections. Their ideology rejects the legitimacy of representative institutions and seeks to undermine the constitutional process through the use of armed force. To this end, they frequently employ a range of violent and coercive tactics that are designed to sabotage electoral initiatives. These tactics may include outright armed attacks on polling personnel, the use of terror‑inducing acts such as bombings or ambushes, intimidation of voters and candidates, extortion of money or resources from local communities, and the propagation of false or misleading information through the media and even through bodies that are meant to protect human rights. By flooding the public sphere with misinformation and by directly threatening the safety of election officials, Maoists aim to erode confidence in the criminal‑justice system and to subvert the administration of law and order. Because the nature of the threat varies dramatically from one locality to another, it is not possible to create a single set of universal standards that will address every situation. Instead, the planning and execution of free and fair elections in areas affected by left‑wing extremism must be tailored to the particular security conditions of each district, each constituency, and often each polling station.

The preparatory phase for an election in a region where left‑wing extremism is present should begin at least six months before the scheduled polling date. The first step in this long lead‑time is the identification of security forces that possess considerable experience in dealing with low‑intensity conflict, insurgency, and militancy, both from within the state and from other states that have faced similar challenges. These forces may include regular police units, paramilitary battalions, and specialized formations that have been trained for counter‑insurgency operations. Once the appropriate units have been identified, administrative procedures must be completed so that each company or battalion is assigned a unique identification number. This practice of allocating a distinct code to every unit allows for clear communication and coordination throughout the election cycle, ensuring that every deployment can be tracked under a common nomenclature. In addition, each unit that will be assigned election‑related duties in an area affected by insurgency should undergo a period of familiarisation and induction training. The length of this training will be determined by the severity and frequency of violent incidents that have been recorded in the specific locality; for example, a region that has experienced frequent ambushes and IED (improvised explosive device) attacks will require a longer, more intensive training programme than a comparatively tranquil area.

A guiding principle in the allocation of security personnel is the concept of placing the right force in the right place. Units that have already operated in low‑intensity conflict zones are far more capable of anticipating and responding to the tactics employed by Maoist groups, and therefore they should be deployed to the most vulnerable polling stations. To facilitate this placement, election officials should colour‑code each constituency according to its security vulnerability, using a scale that ranges from low to high risk. This visual system makes it easier to match the most capable units with the most dangerous locations. At the same time, a detailed resource‑mapping exercise must be carried out. This involves cataloguing the availability of battalions, dog squads, special equipment such as bomb‑disposal kits, and the number of police officers that can be drawn from both within the state and from neighboring states. By examining different models of security provision—whether a “area security” approach in which forces patrol a larger zone, or a “dedicated security” approach in which a team is assigned to guard a specific polling booth—planners can determine the optimal mix of deployments well in advance. It is crucial to recognise that there is a finite pool of additional forces that can be mobilised for election duties; therefore, planners must anticipate shortfalls and develop contingency scenarios that consider both the relocation of polling stations and the retention of existing sites.

A thorough vulnerability analysis must be undertaken from the perspective of insurgent intensity. This analysis should examine historical data on Maoist activity, the presence of IEDs, the terrain, and the accessibility of each polling location. Based on the findings, election officials may need to relocate some polling booths to safer venues, stagger the timing of voting in different areas to reduce crowding, and mobilise forces from multiple states to achieve the required level of security. The deployment criteria—often expressed as yardsticks—should be calibrated to reflect the specific threat level of each booth, and a sensitivity analysis should be performed to understand how small changes in the security environment could affect the overall risk profile. Training for state police must also be intensified, with a particular emphasis on the detection, recovery, and safe handling of improvised explosive devices, as well as on the operation of modern communication equipment that is essential for coordination during the election. The overarching objective of all these measures is to ensure that the election proceeds without any loss of life or limb, a goal that must be embedded in every decision made by the election machinery.

In regions where the presence of IEDs is especially high, predictable vehicular movements on roads that are known to be mined should be avoided wherever possible. When such movements cannot be eliminated, security personnel must follow prescribed standard operating procedures that dictate safe driving speeds, route clearance protocols, and the use of escort vehicles equipped with bomb‑defusal teams. Logistic considerations are equally important. The election authority must arrange for additional campsites, temporary holding areas, and adequate accommodation for the influx of security personnel, ensuring that basic amenities such as water, electricity, and sanitation are provided. Regular de‑mining operations should be scheduled for all major arteries that will be used by the incoming forces, and these routes must be cleared of explosives and other hazards well before the polling day. In addition, the main trunk routes that connect districts and states should be systematically sanitized through de‑mining and routine patrolling operations, a process that is often referred to as “road opening procedures” (ROP).

When it becomes necessary to relocate a polling booth, the proposal for moving the station must be prepared in a specific format and forwarded to the chief electoral officer of the state by the district election officer within a clearly defined time window before the election. Because candidates are often confirmed only a few days before the poll, obtaining their consent for relocation can be a last‑minute activity. To avoid delays, the consent of the district representatives of the major political parties should be secured in advance, and these endorsements should be given substantial weight in the decision‑making process. This ensures that the logistical and field‑planning aspects of the election are not held up until the final hours. Once the candidates are officially nominated, their individual preferences can still be considered, and minor adjustments to the relocation proposal can be made if they do not compromise security.

Following the completion of induction training, the troops assigned to election duties should be positioned at forward locations at least seven days before the polling date. This early deployment serves several purposes. It allows security forces to establish a visible presence, conduct flag‑marches that reassure the local population, and perform reconnaissance of the polling booths to identify any security gaps. The pre‑poll presence also acts as a confidence‑building measure, signalling to both voters and potential insurgents that the state is in control of the situation. The timing of this deployment is critical because it enables a smooth and orderly movement of security personnel onto the actual polling sites on the day of the vote. The overall scale and quality of the security deployment will depend on the number of troops available, the level of their training, and the extent of their prior exposure to low‑intensity conflict environments. If the forces on hand are insufficient to meet the ideal requirement for each polling booth, planners must avoid spreading thinly across all locations, which would leave each booth vulnerable. Instead, a strategic concentration of resources should be applied to the most critical booths—those that are high‑value targets for Maoist groups—while less sensitive booths can be protected through a broader area‑security strategy that relies on regular patrolling and domination of the surrounding terrain.

The decision on where to place each unit must be guided by an assessment of the vulnerability of the area, the specific security equipment that each unit possesses, and the unit’s previous experience in insurgency‑affected zones. By aligning the most capable forces with the most dangerous locations, and by employing a mix of dedicated booth security and area‑wide patrols, the election authority can create a layered defence that reduces the likelihood of successful attacks. All of these measures, from the earliest stages of preparation through the final deployment of troops, are designed to preserve the integrity of the electoral process while protecting the lives of citizens, poll workers, and security personnel in regions where Maoist insurgency poses a persistent threat.

Induction/ De-induction:

a) Induction and de-induction may involve a huge (100s of kms) road opening and demining operation exercise which may require mobilization of large numbers of BD/IED squads and DSMDs in advance. b) For safe induction, and from handling capacity and logistics point of view; landing stations should be identified in advance in consultation with Railway authorities. c) Induction of forces from outside the state should take place at least 21 days prior to the polling dates. d) Effective ROP should be laid to ensure safe and secure induction of these forces into these LWE/Insurgency/Militancy areas. e) Adequate BD/IED squads should be deployed for this purpose. f) Refresher training on counter IED measures for all BD/IED Squads needs to be conducted well before their deployment. g) Similarly, proper planning should be done for inter-phase movement of forces. Sufficient time gap of minimum 10 days should be provisioned for de induction of forces into the next phase of election. More care to be exercised for safe and smooth de-induction of the troops. h) Effective Area domination/ROP should be ensured enroute and in / around the polling booths. i) State authorities have to extend all possible support for final deinduction of the troops. 10.3 Campaign Security:

A. Campaign security is of prime importance in Maoist affected areas. However, ensuring such security in Maoist areas involve complex time taking processes that require considerable effort, resources, planning and tactics. These security operations are not only physically exhausting but also involve large force mobilizations & life-threatening risks. Therefore, it is advised that a SOP be put in place and the candidate be advised accordingly. The operational part of SOP should be intimated to all the political parties to facilitate a transparent security environment. B. It is to be noted with concern that, despite repeated instructions and letters to candidates, their seriousness towards following SOP is not as desired. In future, for more effective compliance, nodal officers of the rank of inspectors/ sub-inspectors should be designated for each candidate. Nodal officer will remain in touch with the candidate, take his location from time to time, get his next day’s program and will intimate the control room, relevant police station and SDPOs. C. Similarly, VIP visits and bandobast arrangements for public rallies should not be left to the political parties, owing to election expenditure booking considerations and citing code of conduct. Security cannot be compromised. D. Support from PWD, Health and Forest department is required for making requisite security arrangements. DM/SDMs should be instructed to play proactive role to instruct these departments to cooperate with police to ensure fool proof security arrangement. 10.4 Infra/Resources:
