Conduct of elections in LWE/Insurgency/Militancy areas requires mammoth logistics arrangements. State governments should make adequate budgetary provisions to handle such arrangements. The deployment locations should be finalized by the DEO much in advance. Minimum basic facilities like toilets, water supply, accommodation, electricity, communication, etc. should also be ensured. For example, adequate budgets for defense material (Sand bag, Balli, tin sheet, concertina coil, Barbed wire coil, Halogen lamp, electric wire, flexible wire, generator set etc.) should be provisioned. Modular plastic toilets should be made available in large quantities. 10.5 Training:

A. Training syllabus for induction should be designed for 3-7 days depending upon the exposure of the forces to similar field threats. B. Training syllabus should be carefully designed to include only aspects that will be of immediate use for election purposes only. C. Pre-Induction training of all the arriving forces should be conducted for the specified period to familiarize them with routes to polling booths, tactics, terrain, incident history, modus operandi of Maoists and necessary do’s and don’ts for operating in the area. Focus should be on counter IED training. D. Each district can prepare a training programme based on its local conditions. Resource persons and best trainers should be shortlisted much in advance and deployed for this purpose. E. Training should include initial briefing at holding areas followed by field training at the area of deployment. Training program should essentially focus upon the precautions to be taken, SOPs to be followed, to know the area where they are going to be deployed. Training sessions should be more of briefing sessions. Weapon training or GPS training in that short time and with very limited infrastructure for force under transit is not practical. By the time companies arrive in the district, campaigning already comes to full swing and force requirement gets multi folded for ROP, helipad area domination and bandobast duties on daily basis

F. Training should be ideally imparted at coy clusters to rationalize on scarce training resources. G. Dos & Don’ts and area specific information brochure should be prepared in advance and made available to each force on its arrival. Trainers to be deputed from training institutes. H.

The responsibility for ensuring that all participants in the electoral process receive thorough and continual instruction does not end with the initial training sessions. State authorities must also guarantee that briefings are conducted on a regular basis and that they are comprehensive enough to address any new developments that arise in the lead‑up to the election. These briefings serve as a mechanism for updating personnel on changes in procedures, emerging security concerns, logistical adjustments, and any intelligence that may affect the conduct of the vote. By maintaining a schedule of systematic briefings, the administration can reinforce the principles taught during training, correct misunderstandings before they become operational problems, and keep the chain of command fully informed about the evolving situation on the ground.

In a similar vein, the process of familiarizing incoming security forces with the specific terrain, infrastructure, and local conditions of the area where they will be deployed is best carried out by troops already stationed in that locality. The presence of experienced local units provides an invaluable source of practical knowledge that cannot be conveyed through classroom instruction alone. When a new contingent arrives, it must be escorted through the neighborhoods, shown the locations of polling stations, and briefed on the layout of the roads, bridges, and any natural obstacles that could affect movement. This hands‑on familiarization ensures that the incoming force can rapidly achieve situational awareness, thereby allowing it to dominate the immediate vicinity of each polling booth once the deployment plan—organized booth by booth—is finalized. The ability to dominate an area means that the force can effectively control access, deter potential disturbances, and respond swiftly to any incidents that might arise on the day of the poll. Moreover, when troops feel confident in their knowledge of the environment, their morale is bolstered, which in turn contributes to a more disciplined and effective performance on election day.

The planning and execution of helicopter operations constitute another critical component of election logistics, particularly in regions where ground transportation is hindered by difficult terrain, long distances, or security considerations. The demand for helicopters of specific makes and types must be assessed well in advance, taking into account the actual requirements that arise from the field and the availability of safe landing zones, commonly referred to as secured helipads. A secured helipad is a cleared, level area that has been prepared to receive aircraft safely, often equipped with lighting, markings, and protective barriers to prevent unauthorized access. The request for these aircraft should be transmitted to the office of the Chief Electoral Officer (CEO) at least one month before the polling day. This advance notice permits the procurement authority to allocate the appropriate assets and to coordinate with the aviation service providers.

If trial landings are deemed necessary—a situation that typically occurs when a helipad has not been used for aviation activities in recent months—these test operations must be scheduled no later than fifteen days before the election. Conducting trial landings allows engineers and ground crews to identify any deficiencies in the landing surface, such as uneven ground, inadequate drainage, or insufficient lighting, and to make the required modifications in a timely fashion. Similarly, the requisition of helicopter sorties—individual flight missions—for the purpose of moving Electronic Voting Machines (EVMs) and personnel into and out of the polling locations must be submitted to the designated nodal officer in the police department at least ten days prior to the polling day. By providing this lead time, the authorities enable a coordinated plan that allocates flight slots, ensures that fuel and maintenance support are in place, and minimizes the risk of delays caused by conflicting schedules or unforeseen technical issues. All District Election Officers (DEOs) and Senior Police (SP) officials are required to follow the Standard Operating Procedure (SOP) that has already been disseminated to their offices; adherence to the SOP guarantees that every step of the helicopter operation is performed in a uniform manner, reducing the chance of miscommunication or procedural errors.

Effective coordination across state boundaries and between different government agencies is essential for securing the integrity of the electoral process, especially in areas where the movement of people and goods can cross jurisdictional lines. To that end, high‑level border meetings—convened by officers such as the Senior Superintendent of Police (SP), the Deputy Inspector General (DIG), or the Inspector General (IG) of Police—should be scheduled at least three months before the election. These meetings bring together counterparts from neighboring states to discuss joint security measures, share intelligence, and agree upon procedures that will be uniformly applied along the shared borders. The state intelligence apparatus must be fully activated to collect, analyze, and disseminate relevant information, which may include reports of extremist activity, potential disruptions, or logistical bottlenecks. The intelligence gathered should be shared promptly with all stakeholders, including sister agencies such as the central paramilitary forces, to facilitate a coordinated response.

Specific focus areas for inter‑state cooperation must be identified, documented, and communicated to the relevant parties. Examples of such focus areas include the formation of Quick Reaction Teams (QRTs) that can be deployed rapidly to address emerging threats, the establishment of a Centralized Aviation Support (CAS) system for evacuating personnel or equipment (often referred to as EVAC), the sealing of borders to prevent unauthorized movement, the maintenance of dominance over border territories to deter infiltration, and the provision of logistical support such as fuel, food, and medical supplies. In addition to the meetings held with neighboring states, the state government should also convene internal coordination meetings at the state level well ahead of the election, ensuring that all departments understand their responsibilities and the timeline for implementation. Inter‑agency coordination structures—often formalized as joint task forces or coordination committees—must be created to provide a clear chain of command and to streamline decision‑making processes, thereby ensuring the smooth delivery of election services.

The performance of officers who are posted to areas afflicted by left‑wing extremism, insurgency, or militancy is heavily dependent on their familiarity with the local environment. Domain knowledge encompasses an understanding of the geography, cultural dynamics, socioeconomic conditions, and the operational patterns of various extremist outfits that may be active in the region. It also includes awareness of the friendly forces—whether they are local police units, state paramilitary formations, or central armed police forces (CAPFs)—that operate alongside the officer. To allow sufficient time for officers to acquire this specialized knowledge, transfers and postings to such sensitive zones should be executed at least six months before the imposition of a Model Code of Conduct (MCC), which is the set of guidelines that comes into force once an election is announced and that restricts certain activities to ensure a level playing field. The age profile of the officers should also be taken into account, as younger officers may possess greater physical endurance for demanding field duties, while more senior officers might bring a depth of experience that is valuable in complex operational contexts. The same principles should be applied to the posting of personnel from the Central Armed Police Forces, which include agencies such as the Border Security Force (BSF), the Central Reserve Police Force (CRPF), and others that are frequently called upon to provide security during elections.

The welfare of the forces tasked with safeguarding the election process is a matter of paramount importance. Comprehensive provisions must be made to guarantee that every individual—whether a member of the security establishment or a civilian staff member attached to the polling operation—has access to cashless medical treatment, rapid evacuation in case of serious injury (known as casualty evacuation or casevac), and air‑ambulance support for conditions that require urgent care. These medical arrangements are especially critical in the context of the ongoing COVID‑19 pandemic, where infection control measures, testing facilities, and specialized medicines may be required. The medical support framework should be operational from the moment the election‑bound forces arrive in a district until the final de‑induction, when the troops are formally withdrawn after the conclusion of the voting process.

To enhance the overall capacity of medical services in remote or conflict‑prone areas, the state should arrange for the deployment of additional doctors from other parts of the state, thereby supplementing the existing healthcare workforce. Essential medicines—such as antibiotics, analgesics, antimalarial drugs, and any other pharmaceuticals required for routine care or emergency treatment—must be stored in a centralized holding area in quantities sufficient to meet the anticipated demand. These supplies should be administered under the supervision of the medical officer assigned to the local unit, ensuring that dosage and treatment protocols are followed correctly. Prior to the election, agreements with hospitals capable of providing cashless treatment—particularly for advanced, super‑specialty care—must be secured. These agreements prevent confusion at the moment when a patient needs to be transferred, because the destination facility and the terms of payment have already been pre‑approved.

Logistical arrangements for the distribution of food packets also need to be planned well in advance, taking into account the number of personnel, the duration of their deployment, and the dietary preferences or restrictions that may be present. At the district level, every department of the state government—ranging from the revenue office to the public works department—should receive clear instructions to facilitate the forces in whatever way is required for the smooth conduct of elections. This could involve providing temporary accommodation, ensuring the availability of water and electricity, or clearing roadways for the movement of vehicles.

A recurring issue that has been highlighted by field reports is the failure to disburse honorarium payments to deployed forces in accordance with the guidelines issued by the election commission. To address this shortfall, it is advisable that the nominal rolls—lists that contain the names, ranks, and identification numbers of all personnel assigned to a polling party—be collected before the forces are dispatched. Having an accurate roll in hand enables the finance department to calculate the exact amount owed to each individual and to ensure that the payments are processed in a timely manner, thereby avoiding delays that could affect morale.

The roles of various other state departments in the electoral process must also be clearly defined at the highest level of government, and this clarification should be communicated well before the election so that each department can convey the relevant responsibilities to its field units. For the education department, the expectation is that schools and other educational institutions will provide infrastructure—such as classrooms, auditoriums, or hostels—that can be used to house additional forces arriving in the area. These facilities also serve as points of contact where the incoming forces can receive logistical support and coordination assistance.

The magistracy, which includes senior administrative officials such as the District Magistrate (DM) and the Sub‑Divisional Magistrates (SDMs), bears the responsibility of overseeing security arrangements for VIP visits and public rallies. The code of conduct that governs electoral behavior prohibits political parties from making security arrangements for such events, placing the onus on the administrative machinery to ensure that law and order are maintained without partisan bias. In fulfilling this duty, the magistrate must coordinate with the Public Works Department (PWD), the health department, and the forest department to secure venues, arrange crowd control measures, and provide necessary utilities.

The health department’s role is to proactively monitor the well‑being of security personnel, especially because many deployment locations lack permanent medical facilities. District hospitals, Community Health Centres (CHCs), and Primary Health Centres (PHCs) should conduct regular visits to security camps, provide basic medical care, and stock sufficient quantities of medicines and malaria prophylaxis kits. If the epidemiological situation demands it, special arrangements for COVID‑19 testing, isolation, and treatment must be put in place.

Finally, the department of food and civil supplies must guarantee that an adequate stock of essential food items, cooking fuel such as liquefied petroleum gas (LPG), and other daily necessities are available throughout the election period. By maintaining a steady supply chain, the department helps prevent shortages that could otherwise lead to unrest or distract security personnel from their primary duties.

In sum, the successful conduct of an election in a complex environment relies on a network of interlocking responsibilities that span training, briefings, local familiarization, air logistics, inter‑state collaboration, officer postings, and comprehensive welfare measures for the forces on the ground. Each element must be planned well in advance, documented clearly, and executed with precision, thereby ensuring that the democratic process proceeds smoothly, safely, and with the confidence of all stakeholders.

The coordination of the movement of armed forces for election duties must be undertaken with meticulous planning and close liaison with the Ministry of Railways, which is designated as the primary agency responsible for providing rail transport. Before any unit commences its journey, it is mandatory for the commanding officers to notify both the office of the Force Coordinator and the designated Nodal Officer. The Force Coordinator serves as the point of contact within the armed forces for all rail‑related matters, while the Nodal Officer acts as the liaison from the railway side, ensuring that the forces’ requirements are recorded and acted upon in a timely manner. In parallel, the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA) is required to develop a comprehensive movement plan for all forces well in advance of the election period. This plan takes into account the number of troops, their deployment locations, the timing of arrival and departure, and any special logistical needs. Based on the movement plan, the railway authorities can schedule special trains, allocate separate coaches for the troops, and arrange for any required ancillary services such as catering.

The catering arrangements are facilitated through the Indian Railway Catering and Tourism Corporation (IRCTC), a subsidiary of Indian Railways that manages on‑board meals and catering services for passengers and special trains. By projecting the anticipated demand for meals, the forces enable IRCTC to prepare sufficient quantities of food, thus avoiding shortages or waste. Train reservations themselves must be made in accordance with the final destination of each unit, the location where the troops will be deployed, and the station at which they will alight. This ensures that the soldiers are not forced to travel beyond their point of deployment, saving both time and resources.

When configuring the composition of passenger coaches for each company—referred to as a “Coy”—the guidelines stipulate that at least two sleeper‑class bogies must be provided for every company. Sleeper class bogies are standard rail coaches equipped with berths that allow troops to rest during longer journeys. In addition, a separate bogie is required for the headquarters of the Territorial Army Command (TAC), which functions as the command centre for the deployed forces. To accommodate the need for climate‑controlled accommodation, a minimum of two air‑conditioned third‑tier coaches must be attached to each special train. These AC III tier coaches provide a higher level of comfort and are particularly important in regions with extreme weather conditions.

Coordination between the railway authorities and IRCTC becomes especially critical when a special train experiences delays, whether due to technical failures, track congestion, or unforeseen circumstances. In such cases, it is essential that the railway staff promptly communicate the revised arrival times to the catering providers so that meals can be served at appropriate times and locations, thereby preventing soldiers from having to go without food. Railway stations, however, have a finite capacity to handle special trains, and they cannot accommodate an unlimited number of such movements in a single day. To alleviate this constraint, some special trains may be rerouted to satellite railway stations—smaller stations located on the periphery of the main hub. The decision to divert a train must be made and communicated to all relevant parties before the journey begins, ensuring that the troops are aware of the exact alighting point and can make necessary arrangements for onward travel.

The role of the railways extends beyond merely providing transport; it includes the planning, monitoring, and execution of the entire movement schedule to guarantee smooth and timely arrival of troops at their designated locations. This responsibility becomes even more pronounced during the de‑induction phase, when forces are withdrawn from deployment and returned to their home bases. During de‑induction, the number of special trains required can increase sharply, and a failure to plan adequately could result in bottlenecks and inefficient use of resources. Consequently, the railway must allocate sufficient rolling stock and prioritize the movement of election‑related trains over other traffic, thereby protecting the valuable man‑hours contributed by the security forces. To expedite problem resolution, especially in the event of unforeseen disruptions, a senior railway officer may be appointed as the nodal officer for the duration of the election operation. This officer wields the authority to make rapid decisions, allocate additional resources, and coordinate with both the forces and other railway officials to keep the movement on schedule.

In regions affected by Maoist insurgency, communication assumes the character of a lifeline, and therefore it must be accorded the highest priority. The establishment of a joint command and control centre that can reach even the most remote polling booths is essential for real‑time situational awareness. Such a centre would serve as the hub for receiving incident reports, weather updates, and operational directives, and it would disseminate this information to the Election Commission of India (ECI), the Ministry of Home Affairs, and all other stakeholders involved in the election process. Areas where the regular mobile network, high‑frequency (HF) radio, or very‑high‑frequency (VHF) radio signals are weak or absent must be identified in advance. These “shadow areas” should be equipped with satellite communication devices that operate independently of terrestrial infrastructure, ensuring that forces can maintain contact with their headquarters and receive critical updates.

The forces must be instructed to carry a sufficient complement of HF and VHF radio equipment, which enables communication over long distances and in difficult terrain, respectively. HF radios are capable of transmitting signals over thousands of kilometres by reflecting off the ionosphere, while VHF radios are more suitable for line‑of‑sight communication and are commonly used for intra‑unit coordination. In addition, the Ministry of Home Affairs should provision satellite phones in adequate numbers; these devices rely on orbiting satellites to provide voice and data services, making them invaluable when terrestrial networks are unavailable. Each company of the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) should also be supplied with locally appropriate mobile phone SIM cards, especially if the standard SIM issued to the unit does not function reliably in the deployment area. To keep these mobile devices operational, the forces should be provided with recharge vouchers that cover the cost of topping up the prepaid balances. A comprehensive communication plan that maps out all polling parties and polling stations, specifies the primary and backup communication channels for each, and outlines the protocols for escalation and reporting must be drafted well before the commencement of election duties.

The logistics of transporting troops from the railway stations where they disembark to their final deployment locations falls under the responsibility of the respective state governments. These authorities must ensure that road‑worthy vehicles—such as buses, trucks, or personnel carriers—are available and conform to the standards laid down for safety, capacity, and durability. Each vehicle should be supplied with a full fuel tank at the start of its mission, thereby eliminating the need for immediate refuelling and reducing downtime. To address unforeseen breakdowns or increased demand, spare vehicles should be positioned en route, creating a buffer that can absorb contingencies. In addition, a pool of mechanically sound vehicles and multiple fuel distribution centres should be identified or established in strategic locations, ensuring that troops have continuous access to both transportation and fuel throughout their deployment.

In districts where a large number of companies are deployed, it becomes necessary to set up multiple refuelling stations to prevent fuel shortages. Each company should travel with a dedicated spare driver and an automobile mechanic, along with a stock of frequently used spare parts such as filters, belts, and brake components. This arrangement enables rapid repairs and minimizes the risk of vehicles being immobilised due to minor faults. For night operations and road security, the troops must be equipped with night vision devices, including night goggles, night‑vision devices (NVDs), and monoculars with rechargeable cells, as well as a sufficient number of motorcycles that can navigate rugged terrain under low‑light conditions. These tools enhance the ability of the forces to conduct patrols, respond to incidents, and maintain a visible security presence after dark. Moreover, the timely provision of suitable accommodation—whether temporary camps, lodges, or hotels—along with other logistical supplies such as food, water, and medical kits, is essential for preserving the health, morale, and operational effectiveness of the personnel.

The safeguarding of Electronic Voting Machines (EVMs) during the election is a critical security task. One company of the Central Armed Police Forces should be dedicated exclusively to the guarding of polled EVMs, ensuring that the machines remain secure from tampering or unauthorized access after the voting process has concluded. In addition to the physical presence of armed guards, the deployment of closed‑circuit television (CCTV) cameras at storage sites and transit points provides an extra layer of surveillance, enabling real‑time monitoring and the creation of an audit trail that can be reviewed in case of any incident.

To facilitate the rapid exchange of information among the various levels of command—ranging from field units to higher headquarters—a password‑protected online document‑sharing system should be instituted. This digital platform would allow authorized personnel to upload, retrieve, and disseminate operational orders, intelligence reports, situation updates, and logistical data in a secure environment, thereby reducing the latency that can arise from traditional paper‑based methods or unsecured communication channels.

The Ministry of Home Affairs occupies a central coordinating role in the overall election security operation. It must issue detailed instructions to all participating forces regarding the carriage of weapons, ammunition, communication equipment, medical supplies, and other essential items. These instructions should also address the composition of the troops, taking into account age profiles and the specific operational requirements of the deployment area. For example, in regions affected by Maoist activity such as the Bastar district of Chhattisgarh, troops must be equipped with weapons that are suitable for jungle and hilly terrain. The Central Industrial Security Force (CISF), Railway Protection Force (RPF), and State Armed Police (SAP) have, in past elections, been equipped with pistols and carbines that are ill‑suited for the high‑trajectory engagements typical of that environment. Therefore, the Ministry must ensure that the forces receive the appropriate armaments—such as mortars, rifle‑mounted grenade launchers, and under‑barrel grenade launchers (UBGLs)—which are more effective in dense forest and mountainous conditions.

In addition, the Ministry of Home Affairs should issue advance notice to each force regarding the need for specialized units such as bomb‑defusing squads (BD Squads), and must specify the required weapons, ammunition, protective gear, wireless radios, and satellite phones. The procurement and distribution of these items must be synchronized with the deployment schedule to avoid delays. By tailoring the equipment to the local terrain and threat profile, the Ministry ensures that the forces are prepared to respond effectively to any insurgent activity that might arise during the election period.

The integration of modern technology into the election security framework can greatly enhance operational efficiency and situational awareness. Global Positioning System (GPS) devices should be employed to track the movement of polling teams and security personnel, providing real‑time location data that can be used for route optimisation, asset management, and rapid response to emergencies. Amateur radio operators, commonly referred to as HAM radios, can serve as an auxiliary communication network, especially in areas where conventional channels are congested or compromised. Vehicle‑mounted mobile towers can extend the range of cellular and data networks, enabling on‑the‑move connectivity for units operating in remote locales. Finally, the use of satellite phones—sometimes termed Direct Satellite Phone Terminals (DSPT)—offers a reliable means of voice and data communication that is independent of terrestrial infrastructure, ensuring that critical command and control links remain operational even in the most isolated polling stations. By leveraging these technologies, election authorities and security forces can maintain a cohesive, responsive, and resilient operational posture throughout the electoral process.

The use of unmanned aerial vehicles, commonly known as drones, is governed by a set of detailed procedural safeguards that are intended to guarantee openness and accountability throughout the electoral process. In order to achieve full transparency, every booth that has been moved from its original location as well as those situated in remote or hard‑to‑reach areas must be recorded in real time through a continuous webcast or videographic feed. This requirement applies especially when the local communications network is unable to provide reliable connectivity; in such circumstances a dedicated transmission system must be employed so that the visual record is uninterrupted and can be accessed by authorized observers and the public alike. In addition to the digital monitoring, the deployment of static magistrates—officials who remain at a fixed point within the polling environment to oversee the conduct of the vote—forms a core component of the supervisory framework. Complementing the static magistrates are micro observers, who are personnel assigned to watch over specific, often minute, aspects of the polling operation, such as the handling of ballot boxes or the movement of election workers, thereby providing an extra layer of scrutiny. The overall effectiveness of the oversight mechanism is further enhanced by the use of sector magistrates, who are responsible for supervising larger geographic zones that may comprise several polling stations; their role is to coordinate the activities of the static magistrates and micro observers within their sector and to ensure that any irregularities are promptly reported and addressed. Security of the electronic voting machines, which are the electronic devices used to record votes, must also be a matter of public knowledge; therefore, the measures taken to protect the storage of these machines, including physical locks, tamper‑evident seals, and controlled access protocols, are to be publicized in advance so that voters and watchdog groups can verify that the machines have not been compromised. Finally, clear and visible signage must be placed at each polling station in the languages spoken by the local population, ensuring that all voters can understand the instructions, the location of the booths, and the identity of the officials, thereby reducing confusion and the potential for manipulation.

Additional instructions complement the transparency measures by outlining how security and logistical arrangements should be prepared. The planning of deployment locations for additional security camps, which are temporary installations designed to house police and other forces tasked with protecting the election, must be undertaken well in advance by the District Electoral Officer or the Senior Police officer, known respectively by the abbreviations DEO and SP. Although these plans must be formulated early, they are not to be disclosed to the public in order to preserve the element of surprise and to prevent potential threats from exploiting the information. At each polling station, a contingent of security personnel is to be positioned with temporary defensive structures—such as barricades, sandbags, or reinforced barriers—intended to deter and withstand bold attacks, including the possibility of long‑range sniper fire. The extent of frisking, which refers to the systematic searching of individuals entering the polling place, is to be calibrated according to the assessed threat level for that particular venue, ensuring that security is neither excessive nor insufficient. The coordination between the election officials responsible for the polling process and the security forces must be meticulously planned, with detailed timetables, points of contact, and contingency procedures documented in advance. If a change in the time at which voting is scheduled is required, this adjustment must also be decided well ahead of the election day, allowing all stakeholders to adapt their operations accordingly. Specific guidance must be issued regarding the night‑time halt of parties responsible for transporting electronic voting machines; these parties must submit early dispatch reports and night‑stay reports in a timely fashion, and a designated nodal officer—an individual appointed to serve as the primary liaison—must remain on site at the security camp to oversee the safe storage of the machines overnight. The timing of the dispatch of polling personnel, whether they travel on foot, by helicopter, or by vehicle, must be predetermined and communicated to the Chief Electoral Officer, the senior official responsible for the overall conduct of the election, to ensure synchronized movement and to avoid any confusion that could disrupt the voting process.

During the conduct of elections, and in particular on the day when voters go to the polls, members of the state police forces as well as personnel from the Central Armed Police Forces and other security agencies are frequently engaged in duties that prevent them from physically casting a ballot at a regular polling station. In order to safeguard the fundamental right of these individuals to participate in the democratic process, the law provides for a special postal ballot system that enables them to vote by mail. This provision is also extended to the armed forces of the Union, which include the Army, the Navy, and the Air Force. Although the armed forces are not always directly involved in the electoral process, they are occasionally called upon for specific logistical tasks such as airlifting election officials, transporting election materials across bodies of water, or providing security in sensitive or high‑risk regions during the election period. Because the armed services are often on continuous deployment, the postal ballot system ensures that they are not disenfranchised by their operational commitments.

The security forces are divided into two broad categories for the purpose of registering for postal voting. The first category comprises members of the Union’s armed forces, the Central Armed Police Forces, and the State Armed Police who are stationed outside their home state. Individuals in this group may register as Service Voters, a classification that allows them to receive a postal ballot, or they may choose to vote by proxy, a method known as classified service voting, whereby a trusted person casts the ballot on their behalf. In certain circumstances, these service members may also be registered as General Voters in designated “peace stations,” which are electoral constituencies that are considered secure and free from conflict. The second category includes members of the state police and the State Armed Police who are posted within the boundaries of their own state. These personnel can only be registered as General Electors. From the moment an election is officially announced, these individuals are designated as “Electors on Election Duty.” This term refers to any person or staff member who has been assigned official responsibilities connected with the election on polling day and who, as a result, cannot attend a normal polling station to vote. The definition encompasses all police personnel who are actively serving on election duty, except those who have been granted leave from their duties.

General electors among the state police have two distinct pathways for casting their votes. The first pathway permits them to request a postal ballot in accordance with the provisions of the Representation of the People Act, provided that they have been assigned election duties in a constituency that differs from the one in which they are registered as ordinary voters. In such a case, the postal ballot is sent to the address where the officer is stationed, and the vote is returned by a specified deadline. The second pathway applies to those police personnel who are serving on election duty within the same constituency where they are already registered as voters. These individuals are issued an Election Duty Certificate by the Returning Officer, the official responsible for overseeing the election in that constituency, which authorizes them to vote at any polling station that has been allocated to them for the day. A concise summary of the actions required for each of the two categories—Service Electors and police personnel registered as General Electors—outlines how they should register and the procedures they must follow to ensure their votes are counted.

A Service Voter is defined as any individual who falls into one of several specific groups. First, any person who is a member of the Union’s armed forces, which includes the Army, the Navy, and the Air Force, qualifies. Second, any individual who serves in a force that operates under the provisions of the Army Act of 1950, whether those provisions have been applied without alteration or have been modified to suit particular circumstances, also qualifies. Third, members of an Armed Police Force of a state who are posted outside the geographical boundaries of that state are included in this definition. Fourth, any person employed by the Government of India in a position that requires posting abroad, such as diplomatic or defence staff stationed outside the country, is likewise considered a Service Voter. Finally, the spouse of a Service Voter, provided that the spouse ordinarily resides with the service member, may be entered on the electoral roll as a Service Voter in the same segment of the roll where the primary service member is listed. The enrollment of a spouse does not require a separate declaration; it is effected automatically on the basis of the declaration submitted by the service member in the prescribed form. It is important to note that while a spouse may be enrolled in this manner, an adult son or daughter of the service member does not qualify for enrollment as a Service Voter under the same rules. As an alternative to being registered as a Service Voter, an individual who qualifies for this category may opt to be enrolled as a General Elector at the place of posting, provided that the posting is in a peace station—a constituency that has been officially designated as secure. In such cases, the status of the posting must be communicated to the Election Commission of India, the central authority that administers elections, so that the appropriate registration can be effected. This dual option allows service members to choose the voting method that best aligns with their circumstances and personal preferences while ensuring that the integrity of the electoral roll is maintained.

No individual may be entered on more than one electoral roll at any time. This principle is intended to prevent a single person from exercising more than one vote in the same election, a situation that would undermine the fairness of the electoral process. For members of the armed forces, the police and other uniformed services, this rule is reinforced by the requirement that each service personnel must sign a declaration when registering as a service voter. In that declaration the person affirms that he or she is not already listed as a general elector on the ordinary electoral roll that is maintained for the civilian population. The declaration therefore acts as a safeguard against duplicate enrollment.

Service personnel have three distinct avenues through which they may cast a vote in an election. The first method is the use of a postal ballot paper. When a service member is registered as a service voter, he or she may request a ballot paper to be mailed to the place where the service member is stationed. The ballot paper is completed and returned by post, and the vote is counted in the same manner as any other postal vote. This option is available to all service voters except those who have been authorized to vote by proxy. The second method is proxy voting. In this case the service elector appoints another person, known as a proxy, to vote on his or her behalf. The proxy is chosen by the service elector themselves and must be a person who is eligible to vote in the elector’s constituency. Those service voters who make use of a proxy are referred to as Classified Service Voters, a term that distinguishes them from ordinary service voters who vote by postal ballot. The third method is to become a general elector and to vote in person at the nearest polling station that has been allocated for that purpose. This option is restricted to members of the state police and to armed forces personnel who are posted at what are termed peace stations—locations where the duties of the service do not involve active combat or internal security operations. In such cases the service member may register on the ordinary electoral roll for the civilian constituency in which the peace station is situated and may therefore appear on the standard list of electors for that area.

A natural question that arises is whether a service elector can be entered simultaneously on both the service voters roll and the general electoral roll. The answer is unequivocally negative. The law expressly forbids dual enrollment, and a service elector who attempts to be listed on both rolls commits an offence punishable under Section 131 of the Representation of the People’s Act of 1950. That provision makes it a criminal act to knowingly provide false information or to otherwise contravene the rules governing electoral registration.

The process by which a member of the uniformed services becomes a service voter involves several steps that are designed to verify the applicant’s identity, residence and eligibility. The first step is to determine the name of the electoral constituency and the specific part of that constituency in which the applicant’s permanent residence is located. A constituency is a geographically defined area that elects a representative to the legislative body, and each constituency is further subdivided into parts for administrative convenience. Once the appropriate constituency has been identified, the applicant must complete the relevant registration form. For members of the armed forces of the Union and for their spouses, the required document is Form 2. For personnel of the Central Armed Police Force or of the state armed police who are serving outside their home state, the appropriate document is Form 2A. The distinction between the two forms reflects the different administrative structures that oversee the Union forces and the state forces.

After the form has been completed, the name of the service voter is entered on the last part of the electoral roll for the chosen constituency. This placement reflects the fact that service voters are listed separately from the general civilian electorate, a practice that dates back to the colonial era when the British administration introduced separate rolls for military personnel stationed abroad. In addition to the ordinary postal ballot, a service personnel may also become a Classified Service Voter, which means that he or she may appoint a proxy to cast a vote on his or her behalf. The appointment of a proxy is a matter of personal choice and must be made in accordance with the rules set out by the Election Commission of India.

To locate the appropriate constituency, the applicant can use any of the official online resources that the Election Commission maintains. The main website, www.eci.nic.in, as well as the portal for the National Voters’ Service Portal (nvsp.in) and the dedicated voter helpline application, all provide tools for searching by state and district. By selecting the relevant state and then the district within that state, the user is presented with a drop‑down list of all the assembly constituencies that fall within that district. The applicant can then identify the precise constituency and part that corresponds to his or her permanent residence.

Form 2A, which is used by members of the state armed police and the Central Armed Police Force serving away from their home state, must be submitted to the officer who is in charge of the records office of the relevant force, or to the commandant of the state armed force. The designated officer examines the particulars entered on the form, verifies their accuracy, affixes his or her signature, and then uploads the form to the service portal that is used for personnel administration. Following this electronic submission, the officer forwards the form to the District Election Officer (DEO) of the state in which the service member’s permanent residence is situated. The addresses and contact details of all DEOs are published on the Election Commission’s website as well as on the website of the Chief Electoral Officer of each state. It is the responsibility of the service voter to ensure that the information supplied on Form 2A is correct, complete and free of errors, because any mistake may delay the registration or result in the rejection of the application. Unlike general electors, service voters do not receive an Electors Photo Identity Card, commonly known as a voter ID card. The absence of a physical card is compensated by the inclusion of the voter’s name on the service roll, which can be verified by election officials at the time of voting.

In certain circumstances a service member who is stationed at a peace station may prefer to register as a general elector rather than as a service voter. A peace station is defined as a posting where the duties of the service personnel do not involve active engagement in hostilities, internal security operations or other combat‑related activities. When a service member is posted at such a location and wishes to cast his or her vote at the polling station that serves the civilian population of the area where he or she currently resides, the individual may apply for enrollment as a general elector. The applicant must complete Form 6, which is the standard application for inclusion in the ordinary electoral roll, and must attach the requisite supporting documents. These documents typically include proof of date of birth, such as a birth certificate or passport, and proof of residence, such as a utility bill, rental agreement or a government‑issued residence certificate.

Form 6 may be completed online by accessing the official election website at www.eci.gov.in, the National Voters’ Service Portal, or the dedicated voter portal at voters.eci.gov.in, as well as through the voter helpline app that is available for smartphones. Once the application has been submitted, the Election Commission processes the request and, upon successful verification, issues an Electors Photo Identity Card, commonly known as an EPIC, which is mailed to the applicant by speed post. The EPIC serves as a photographic proof of identity and residence and is required for casting a vote in person at a polling station. The completed Form 6 must be submitted to the Electoral Registration Officer (ERO) who has jurisdiction over the constituency in question. The ERO is responsible for maintaining the accuracy of the electoral roll, and only those individuals whose names appear on the roll at the time of the election are entitled to vote.

It is important to emphasize that a service member who chooses to register as a general elector must not simultaneously be enrolled as a service voter. Dual enrollment would again contravene the statutory prohibition and could lead to criminal liability. Once the application for general registration has been approved and the EPIC has been issued, the service member can attend the polling station in person on election day. The voter should first confirm that his or her name appears on the electoral roll for the relevant constituency, a step that can be carried out by consulting the online roll or by visiting the local election office. Only those whose names are listed are permitted to enter the voting compartment and to mark their ballot.

Members of the Union Armed Forces and of the Armed Police Forces who are posted at peace stations enjoy the same right to vote at the polling station that serves the area where they are currently residing. The process for exercising that right is identical to that for any civilian voter. The individual must submit Form 6 to the Electoral Registration Officer, provide the required documentary evidence, and await the issuance of the EPIC. After the name has been entered on the ordinary electoral roll, the Electoral Registration Officer will have completed all procedural steps required under the law, and the service member may cast his or her vote in the same manner as any other resident voter. This provision reflects the democratic principle that every citizen, regardless of occupational status, should have an equal opportunity to participate in the selection of the nation’s representatives, provided that the procedural safeguards designed to ensure the integrity of the electoral process are observed.

In the electoral system that governs members of the armed forces and the central armed police forces, a person who is entitled to cast a vote because of his or her service status is known as a Service Elector. When a Service Elector is unable to appear in person at the polling station that has been assigned to the constituency of his or her domicile, the law provides an alternative method of voting through the appointment of a proxy. A Service Elector who has chosen to exercise this option and who has formally designated another individual to cast a ballot on his or her behalf is thereafter described as a Classified Service Elector. The proxy, who may be any ordinary resident of the same constituency, does not need to be a voter who is already registered on the electoral roll, but the person must not be disqualified from registration as a voter under any of the statutory disqualifications, such as being of unsound mind, being an undischarged insolvent, or being otherwise barred by law.

The appointment of a proxy is a procedural act that must be documented on a specific form, designated as Form 13F. The Service Elector who wishes to appoint a proxy must complete this form in the presence of the Commanding Officer of his or her unit, who acts as a witness to the authenticity of the request. After the Commanding Officer has signed the form, the Service Elector must then arrange for the proxy to sign the same document before a notary public or a First Class Magistrate, both of whom are authorized officials capable of administering an oath and certifying that the signatures are genuine. Once the signatures of the Service Elector, the Commanding Officer, and the proxy have been obtained and the form has been duly notarized, the completed Form 13F is to be forwarded to the Returning Officer who is responsible for the constituency in which the Service Elector’s native place lies. The Returning Officer is the electoral official charged with receiving, verifying, and processing all applications and documents related to the conduct of the election in a particular area.

The law further stipulates that the proxy must be a resident of the constituency in question. This requirement ensures that the proxy is familiar with the local polling station and can physically appear there on election day. Although the proxy does not have to be a registered voter, the proxy must not fall within any category of persons who are disqualified from registration, which includes individuals who have been convicted of certain offenses, who are of unsound mind, or who have been adjudicated as insolvent. The application for the appointment of a proxy, embodied in Form 13F, must be received by the Returning Officer before the deadline for filing nomination papers for candidates. This deadline typically coincides with the close of the period during which individuals may submit their intention to stand for election, and it ensures that the electoral authorities have sufficient time to process proxy appointments before the final list of electors is compiled.

In situations where the Service Elector is physically present at his or her native place on the day the proxy appointment is being made, both the Service Elector and the proxy may sign Form 13F together before a notary public or a First Class Magistrate. This joint signing eliminates the need for the Service Elector to travel to the unit’s headquarters to obtain the Commanding Officer’s signature, thereby simplifying the procedure when the Service Elector is already at the location where the proxy will later vote. Once the form has been signed and notarized, it is again transmitted to the Returning Officer for inclusion in the electoral register.

When election day arrives, the proxy is permitted to cast the ballot by using the Electronic Voting Machine that is installed at the polling station in the Service Elector’s native constituency. The proxy must remain physically present at the polling station, just as any other ordinary voter assigned to that polling station does, and must follow the same steps for recording the vote: presenting the appropriate identification, receiving the electronic ballot unit, selecting the preferred candidate on the touch screen, and confirming the vote. The electronic voting process records the vote in the same manner as any other vote cast at that polling station, and the proxy’s vote is counted together with the votes of all other electors assigned to that station.

The appointment of a proxy does not have a fixed expiration date; rather, it continues in force until it is expressly revoked by the Service Elector, until the Service Elector ceases to be a Service Elector because of retirement or death, or until the proxy himself or herself passes away. In effect, the proxy’s authority remains valid for as long as the Service Elector retains his or her status as a Classified Service Voter. If the Service Elector wishes to terminate the proxy’s authority before the election, the Service Elector must notify the Returning Officer in writing. The notification must be made by completing Form 13G, which records the revocation of the proxy appointment and, if the Service Elector wishes to appoint a new proxy, provides the details of the substitute. The Returning Officer then updates the electoral register to reflect the change, ensuring that the revoked proxy can no longer cast a vote on the Service Elector’s behalf.

The process by which service personnel become enrolled on the service electors roll begins each year with a formal communique that the Election Commission of India issues to the Ministry of Defence, the Ministry of Home Affairs, and the Directorate General of Border Roads. This communique announces the commencement of the summary revision of the service electors list, a routine exercise intended to keep the list current and accurate. Eligible members of the armed forces and the central armed police forces who wish to be included in the service electors roll must fill out either Form 2 or Form 2A, which are the standard application forms prescribed under the amended Registration of the Electors Rules of 1960. These forms collect personal details, service particulars, and address information. The applicant is required to submit the completed form in duplicate, together with a declaration affirming the truthfulness of the information provided, to the designated Record Office or the authority that has been identified as the nodal point for that particular force. The nodal office is the administrative entity responsible for collecting and forwarding the enrollment applications of its personnel.

Once the applications reach the Record Office or the nodal officer of the Central Armed Police Forces or the State Armed Police, the officer in charge conducts a careful examination of each statement submitted by a service elector. The verification process involves confirming that all required particulars have been supplied and that they are correct. Particular attention is given to ensuring that the full residential address is included, specifying the district, town, or village, so that the elector can be matched to the appropriate constituency. After confirming the accuracy of the information, the Record Officer signs a verification certificate that is attached to the application form, thereby attesting that the data have been checked. The Record Office then forwards the verified forms, together with the accompanying declaration, to the District Election Officer of the relevant district. In Union Territories and in the National Capital Territory of Delhi, the forms are sent instead to the Chief Electoral Officer of the respective Union Territory. The forwarding packet must include a covering list that itemizes the particulars of each statement being transmitted, allowing the receiving officer to track the documents easily. Importantly, the Record Offices are expressly prohibited from sending the forms directly to the Election Commission of India; the transmission must follow the prescribed chain of command to the district or territorial electoral authorities.

At the next stage, the forms arrive at the service voter portal, which is an online system accessible at www.servicevoter.eci.nic.in. The portal receives the electronic copies of the applications from the record offices or commandants. The District Election Officer then forwards the digital files to the Electoral Registration Officer (ERO) who is responsible for the electoral roll in that district. The ERO processes the applications using the online system, entering the data into the electoral database and preparing a draft of the final portion of the electoral roll that pertains to service electors. After the draft has been reviewed and approved, the ERO publishes the final version of the last part of the electoral roll. The completed list is communicated back to the Record Offices and nodal authorities and is also posted on the website of the Chief Electoral Officer in an image‑PDF format, thereby making the information publicly accessible and allowing for any further corrections before the roll is frozen.

The status of a Service Elector ceases under two primary circumstances. First, when the individual retires from the armed forces or from the armed police forces, the legal designation of Service Elector ends not only for the retiree but also for the spouse of the retiree, because the eligibility of the spouse to vote as a service elector is derived from the service status of the employed partner. Second, if the Service Elector dies, the status similarly terminates for both the deceased and the surviving spouse, as the legal basis for the spouse’s inclusion in the service electors roll is extinguished upon the death of the service member.

Finally, the question of whether votes cast by Service Electors through a postal ballot paper are counted in the final tally is answered unequivocally in the affirmative. All postal ballot papers that have been duly completed, authenticated, and returned to the Returning Officer are included in the counting process. The counting of votes commences with the opening and tabulation of these postal ballot papers before the counting of votes cast at polling stations, ensuring that the ballots submitted by service electors who were unable to appear in person are fully accounted for in the election result.

The Returning Officer, who is the official entrusted with the administration of elections in a particular constituency, is responsible for the physical counting of each Postal Ballot Paper that has been submitted by service electors. The counting may be carried out personally by the Returning Officer, or it may be delegated to Assistant Returning Officers who have been appointed specifically for this purpose. All of these officials conduct the count at a table that has been set up for the counting process, ensuring that the procedure is carried out in a transparent and orderly manner. When a service elector casts a vote by means of a Postal Ballot Paper, the same set of rules that apply to ordinary ballot papers also governs the way in which those votes are tabulated. First, every Postal Ballot Paper that arrives before the scheduled start time for the counting is taken into consideration; any ballot that reaches the counting centre after that deadline is excluded from the tally. The Returning Officer begins by opening the outer envelope that contains the declaration form known as Form‑13/A, which the service elector must have completed. He or she then checks the declaration to confirm that it bears the elector’s signature and that it has been duly attested by a competent authority, such as a commanding officer or another officer authorized to verify the authenticity of the declaration. If the declaration is missing, improperly signed, or not attested, the corresponding Postal Ballot Paper is rejected and not counted. Similarly, any Postal Ballot Paper that arrives without the prescribed declaration or that fails to meet the marking requirements stipulated in the instructions is set aside and does not form part of the final vote count. In this way, the counting procedure safeguards the integrity of the election by allowing only those postal votes that are complete, properly signed, and received on time to influence the result.

State police personnel who wish to become General Electors—meaning they will be listed on the ordinary electoral roll rather than the special service‑elector roll—must follow a specific registration process that is now largely digital. The applicant may submit an online application through the Election Commission of India’s website, by using the Voter Helpline mobile application, or by visiting the National Voter Service Portal at the designated web address. Alternatively, the applicant may obtain a physical Form‑6, which is the standard form for inclusion of a name in the electoral roll, and submit it in person. Information about where to collect the physical form can be found on the Election Commission’s official website, or the applicant can call the toll‑free helpline number 1950 during working hours to receive guidance. It is essential that the applicant correctly fills in the name of the Assembly Constituency in which they wish to be enrolled; to verify the correct constituency, the applicant can use the “Know your BLO and Polling Station” feature on the Election Commission’s portal, selecting the relevant state and district, after which a drop‑down list of constituencies will appear. Once the form has been completed and submitted to the appropriate Electoral Registration Officer, the applicant will be issued an Elector Photo Identity Card, commonly known as an EPIC, which serves as official proof of registration. The Form‑6 must be submitted only to the concerned Electoral Registration Officer and not to any other authority. After registration, the individual may vote in person at the designated polling station, provided that their name appears on the electoral roll; only those whose names are listed are entitled to cast a ballot. It is also important to note that a person who chooses to be enrolled as a General Elector should not simultaneously be enrolled as a service elector, because dual enrollment is not permitted under election law.

To support the application for registration as a General Elector, a set of documentary evidence must be attached to the Form‑6. The documents serve two main purposes: they establish the applicant’s date of birth and verify the applicant’s residence. For proof of date of birth, any one of several acceptable documents may be submitted. These include a birth certificate issued by a competent local authority such as a municipal body or the Registrar of Births and Deaths, a government‑issued Aadhaar card, a Permanent Account Number (PAN) card, a driving licence that displays the applicant’s birth date, a certificate of completion of class ten or twelve issued by a recognized educational board if it contains the date of birth, or any other document that reliably demonstrates the applicant’s birth date. For proof of residence, the applicant may provide a utility bill—such as a water, electricity, or gas connection bill—showing the applicant’s name and address for at least the preceding twelve months. Other acceptable documents include an Aadhaar card that displays the current address, a recent passbook from a nationalized bank, scheduled bank, or post office, an Indian passport, land‑ownership records maintained by the revenue department (including the Kisan Bahi), a registered rent lease deed if the applicant is a tenant, a registered sale deed if the applicant owns the property, or any other document that convincingly establishes the place of residence. By attaching one document from each of these two categories, the applicant satisfies the evidentiary requirements for inclusion in the general electoral roll.

When a service elector wishes to cast a vote by Postal Ballot, the process is initiated by the Returning Officer of the constituency in which the elector’s name is entered. The Returning Officer uses an electronic system known as the Electronically Transmitted Postal Ballot System, abbreviated as ETPBS, to send the complete postal voting package to the elector. This package includes several components: a list of contents page that outlines what is enclosed, the actual Postal Ballot Paper on which the elector will mark the choice of candidate, Form 13A, which is the declaration that the elector must sign, an inner envelope labeled Form 13 B (Cover A) that houses the ballot paper, an outer envelope labeled Form 13 C (Cover B) that contains the inner envelope, and Form 13 D, a set of detailed instructions intended to guide the elector through the voting process. Upon receipt of the package, the service elector must read Form 13 D carefully, as it explains the steps to be followed and the restrictions that apply. The elector then records the vote by placing a clear, unmistakable mark—typically a cross or tick—directly opposite the name of the chosen candidate on the ballot paper. The instructions stress that any ambiguous or faint marking may render the ballot invalid, because the mark must unambiguously convey the elector’s preference. The elector must not add any signature, word, or additional symbol on the ballot paper beyond the required mark, as such extraneous markings would also invalidate the vote.

After the vote has been recorded, the elector places the ballot paper inside the smaller inner envelope marked ‘B’, seals that envelope, and ensures that it is securely closed, either by applying the provided seal or by using an alternative method that prevents tampering. The next step is to sign the declaration form, Form 13A, in the presence of an officer who has been appointed by the commanding officer of the unit, ship, or establishment to which the service elector belongs. This officer must attest to the authenticity of the elector’s signature, confirming that the declaration has been duly signed. The attested declaration is then returned to the elector, who must keep it separate from the ballot paper; the elector is specifically instructed not to show the ballot paper to the attesting officer nor to disclose how the vote was cast. Once the sealed inner envelope and the attested declaration are ready, the elector places them inside the outer envelope, seals the outer envelope, and returns the entire package by the method prescribed by the Returning Officer, usually through a designated postal service. This systematic approach, which combines electronic transmission of the voting materials with strict verification and sealing procedures, is designed to preserve the secrecy of the ballot while ensuring that the vote of every service elector is counted in a manner that upholds the principles of fairness and transparency that are fundamental to the electoral process.

The procedure that governs the handling of a postal ballot paper is set out in a series of precise steps that must be observed in order to preserve the integrity of the vote and to ensure that the ballot can be correctly identified, counted, and recorded by the returning officer. The first of these steps concerns the serial number that appears on the cover of the ballot envelope designated as form 13B. Every postal ballot paper carries a unique serial number that is printed on its outermost cover, and this number serves as an essential reference point that links the ballot paper to the individual voter who is entitled to cast that particular vote. It is therefore mandatory that the serial number shown on the cover of form 13B be entered accurately in the field that has been set aside for that purpose on the declaration form, which is known as form 13A. The declaration form is the document in which the voter confirms, under oath, that the ballot paper contained therein reflects his or her true choice. By entering the serial number in the assigned place, the voter creates a direct correspondence between the declaration and the ballot paper, which allows election officials to verify that the ballot being submitted is the one that was issued to that voter and that it has not been altered or substituted. Any mistake in transcribing the serial number could cause the ballot to be rejected during the counting process, because the election staff would be unable to match the declaration to the correct ballot paper.

After the serial number has been recorded, the voter must proceed to the physical assembly of the ballot package. The ballot package consists of several nested envelopes, each of which is identified by a distinct label. The innermost envelope, which bears the letter ‘B’ on its cover, contains the actual ballot paper together with the completed declaration form. This smaller envelope must be placed inside a larger outer envelope that is marked with the letter ‘C’. The outer envelope is sometimes referred to as the “outer cover” because it is the final protective layer that will travel through the postal system. The purpose of this two‑layer system is to provide additional security: the inner envelope protects the ballot paper from accidental damage or tampering, while the outer envelope ensures that the entire package can be handled as a single unit and can be identified quickly by the returning officer when it arrives at the election office. The voter is required to affix a full, handwritten signature in the space that has been allocated on the cover of the inner ‘B’ envelope. This signature acts as a personal authentication mark that confirms the identity of the person who prepared the package and that the contents have not been altered after the signature was placed. The signature must be legible and must match the signature that the election authorities have on file for that voter, because it will be examined during the verification stage before the ballot is entered into the count.

Once the inner envelope has been sealed inside the outer envelope, the voter must close the outer ‘C’ cover securely. The closing of the outer envelope should be performed in such a way that it cannot be opened without leaving obvious signs of tampering, for example by using a self‑adhesive flap or by sealing it with glue. After the envelope is properly sealed, the voter must dispatch the package to the returning officer. The returning officer is the official who is responsible for receiving, safeguarding, and ultimately counting the votes that are cast in a particular electoral district. The postal ballot must reach the returning officer’s office before the commencement of the counting of votes. In practice this means that the ballot must be delivered in advance of the time when the election officials begin to open and tally the ballots, because any ballot that arrives after the counting has started may be considered late and could be excluded from the final tally. To guarantee timely delivery, the voter is instructed to use a reliable postal service, such as speed post, which offers expedited handling and tracking capabilities. Speed post is a service provided by the national postal corporation that promises delivery within a specified number of days and provides proof of dispatch, thereby giving the voter evidence that the ballot was sent in accordance with the prescribed deadline.

In addition to the procedural requirements concerning the handling of the ballot package, the voter must also keep in mind a fundamental principle of democratic voting: each eligible voter is entitled to cast only a single vote in any given election. This principle is enshrined in the electoral law to prevent the distortion of the election outcome through multiple voting. Consequently, the voter must not attempt to vote for more than one candidate on the same ballot, nor should the voter try to submit multiple ballot papers for the same election. The instruction that “you have only one vote” serves as a reminder that any attempt to vote more than once, whether by submitting additional postal ballots, by voting in person at a polling station, or by using a proxy, is a violation of electoral regulations and may result in the disqualification of the voter’s ballot or even legal penalties.

Another important detail concerns the postage that is required to send the ballot to the returning officer. Under the specific rules governing postal voting, the voter is not required to affix a traditional postage stamp to the outer envelope. Instead, the responsibility for covering the cost of delivery rests with the election authority, which arranges for the ballot to be sent by speed post at no charge to the voter. The voter’s obligation, therefore, is limited to ensuring that the outer envelope reaches the returning officer before the deadline. The use of speed post guarantees that the ballot will be transported quickly and that a record of its movement will be available, which helps both the voter and the election officials confirm that the ballot was dispatched and received within the legally prescribed timeframe.

The broader legal framework that governs the use of postal ballots and the procedures for their execution is set out in the section titled “Legal Provisions regarding Postal Ballots and their Execution Procedure.” This framework establishes a series of activities that must be undertaken in order to carry out electoral registration for members of the armed forces and other groups who are eligible to vote by mail. The first activity in this sequence is the identification of the voters who are entitled to receive a postal ballot. This identification process distinguishes between two main categories of voters. The first category comprises service voters, which includes members of the armed forces as well as their spouses, provided that they have not opted for an alternative voting method known as proxy voting. Proxy voting allows a designated individual to cast a vote on behalf of the voter, and anyone who has chosen that option is therefore excluded from the postal ballot list. The second category consists of electors who are on election duty. These are persons and staff members who have been assigned official responsibilities related to the conduct of the election on the day of the poll, and who, because of those duties, are unable to attend a regular polling station to vote in person. The election duty category includes a wide variety of personnel, such as police officers who are on active duty (except those who are on leave), higher grade officers, district election officers, returning officers, assistant returning officers, and their supporting staff. It also includes control room personnel, videographers and staff members of election enforcement teams, zone officers, sub‑zone officers, block level officers, municipal officers, drivers, cleaners, and other individuals whose official duties place them at polling locations or election centers during the voting period. All of these individuals are considered to be unable to cast a vote at a normal polling station because they are required to be present elsewhere to ensure that the election proceeds smoothly and securely.

Having identified the eligible voters, the election authorities must then ensure that the design of the postal ballot papers is uniform across all categories of voters. This means that the layout, wording, and visual elements of the ballot paper must be the same whether the ballot is being issued to a service voter, a spouse of a service member, or an elector on election duty. A common design helps to avoid confusion, prevents the possibility of a voter receiving a ballot that is not appropriate for his or her category, and simplifies the counting process because election staff can work with a single standardized format rather than having to manage multiple versions of the ballot.

The next step in the procedural chain is the management of postal ballots for persons who are on election duty. Because these individuals are often engaged in demanding and time‑sensitive tasks on the day of the poll, the election administration must develop a system that allows them to receive, complete, and return their ballots without interfering with their official responsibilities. This may involve arranging for dedicated collection points, providing pre‑addressed envelopes, or coordinating with the units to which the personnel are attached so that the ballot can be dispatched promptly after the duty is completed. The goal is to guarantee that the right to vote is not denied to any eligible individual simply because of the nature of their employment on election day.
