Finally, the legal provisions reference a “Postal Ballot Matrix,” which is a structured framework that outlines the relationship between the different elements of the postal voting process, including the categories of voters, the types of ballot papers, the sequence of envelopes, the deadlines for submission, and the responsibilities of the various officials involved. The matrix serves as a reference tool that ensures every step is accounted for and that the process remains transparent, traceable, and compliant with the law. By expanding the matrix into detailed procedural instructions, election officials can monitor each stage of the postal ballot’s life cycle—from issuance, through completion and signing, to sealing, dispatch, receipt, verification, and ultimately counting—thereby safeguarding the integrity of the election and upholding the democratic principle that every eligible citizen’s vote is counted once and only once.

The system of postal voting for members of the armed services, the police and other uniformed personnel is organized through a series of prescribed forms, each of which serves a distinct purpose in the overall electoral process. The first of these is Form 2, which is intended for active members of the Armed Forces together with their legally‑married spouses. The purpose of this form is to allow these individuals to register themselves as “service electors,” a special category of voters who are entitled to cast their ballots by post rather than appearing in person at a polling station. The application must be completed in duplicate, meaning that two identical copies of the form are filled out with the same information. Both copies must be signed and dated by the applicant, and the pair is then forwarded to the Record Officer, or to whatever authority has been designated to receive such documents in the particular jurisdiction. Along with the two copies of Form 2, the applicant must also submit a declaration form, which is a separate document in which the applicant affirms, under oath, the truthfulness of the information supplied and confirms his or her eligibility for the postal ballot. Once the Record Officer receives the materials, the officer is required to verify that the details are correct and then to transmit the verified forms electronically, using the official online system, to the District Electoral Officer or the Electoral Registration Officer of the relevant district or assembly constituency. The electronic transmission ensures that the information reaches the central electoral database promptly and securely. After the electronic submission, the Electoral Registration Officer, who is the official charged with maintaining the list of voters for a given assembly constituency, will enroll the names of the service members and their spouses as “service voters.” This enrollment is recorded in the final segment, or “last part,” of the electoral roll for the assembly constituency in which the service elector is normally registered. In effect, the service elector’s name is added to a special section of the roll that is reserved for those who will be voting by post, thereby guaranteeing that a postal ballot paper will be issued to them when the election is called.

Form 2A is the counterpart of Form 2 for members of the armed police forces of a state, that is, the state police units that are organized on a paramilitary basis, such as the Provincial Armed Police. This form is used specifically when a police officer is posted outside the state in which he or she is normally registered as a voter. The procedural steps for Form 2A mirror those for Form 2: the applicant must complete two copies, attach the required declaration, and submit the package to the Record Officer. After verification, the forms are again sent electronically to the District Electoral Officer or Electoral Registration Officer of the relevant district, and the officer then enrolls the officer as a “service voter” in the appropriate constituency’s electoral roll.

The next document in the sequence is Form 12, which is formally titled “Letter of Intimation to the Record Officer (Application for Postal Ballot Paper).” This form is signed by a member of the armed forces or police who has been assigned to election duty, meaning that the individual has been temporarily posted to a location where election officials are needed to manage the voting process. The purpose of Form 12 is to serve as a formal written request that the individual be allowed to cast his or her vote through the postal ballot system rather than at a polling station. The form is necessary because the person is serving in an area that lies outside the geographic constituency where the person is normally registered as an elector. By submitting Form 12, the individual notifies the Record Officer that he or she will be unable to appear in person and therefore requires a postal ballot paper to be mailed to the address specified in the application.

If a service member is assigned to election duty within the same assembly constituency where he or she is registered as a voter, a different procedure applies. In that case the individual must complete Form 12‑A, known as the “Application for Election Duty Certificate.” This form is used to request a special certificate, often abbreviated as EDC, that authorizes the holder to exercise his or her franchise—meaning the right to vote—in person at the polling station where he or she is stationed for the purpose of election duty. The issuance of the Election Duty Certificate confirms that the individual is allowed to cast a ballot at the polling station associated with his or her temporary posting, rather than having to travel back to the regular polling station of his or her permanent residence. The completed Form 12‑A is submitted to the appropriate election official, who reviews the application and, if satisfied that the applicant meets the criteria, issues the Election Duty Certificate.

Form 12‑B is the actual “Election Duty Certificate” that is generated by the Returning Officer, who is the senior election official responsible for overseeing the conduct of elections in a particular constituency. The Returning Officer issues this certificate based on the information supplied in Form 12‑A. The certificate serves as proof that the individual is authorized to vote at the polling station where he or she is performing election duty. Possession of this certificate, often referred to simply as “EDC,” is required for the service elector to be permitted to cast a vote in person at the designated polling location.

The next series of forms deals specifically with the mechanics of the postal ballot itself. Form 13‑A is a declaration made by the service elector. In this declaration the elector affirms his or her identity, confirms that the details provided are correct, and acknowledges the responsibility to follow the instructions for voting by post. The declaration must be signed by the elector and then attested—meaning verified and stamped—by a competent officer, such as a senior officer in the chain of command, before it is submitted together with the postal ballot paper. Form 13‑B is a physical cover, essentially a sealed envelope, into which the postal ballot paper itself is placed. The service elector is required to seal this cover, thereby ensuring that the ballot cannot be tampered with after it has been completed. Form 13‑C is a larger, outer cover that is addressed to the Returning Officer. This outer cover contains the sealed Form 13‑B envelope together with the signed declaration from Form 13‑A. Once the outer cover is sealed, it provides an additional layer of security, ensuring that the ballot and the accompanying declaration travel together to the election officials without being opened or altered. Form 13‑D is an instructional sheet that outlines point‑by‑point guidelines for the elector on how to correctly fill out the postal ballot, how to seal the envelopes, and how to handle any special circumstances that may arise during the postal voting process. This sheet is intended to prevent errors that could invalidate the ballot, such as missing signatures or improperly sealed envelopes.

The administration of the postal ballot system requires a network of designated officials, often referred to as “nodal officers,” who are responsible for handling specific aspects of the process at various levels of government. At the highest tier, a nodal officer is appointed in the Chief Electoral Officer’s (CEO) office at the state level; this officer coordinates statewide policy and ensures that the postal ballot procedures are uniformly applied. At the state police level, a nodal officer is placed in the office of the Director General of Police (DGP); the officer should hold a rank not lower than that of an Inspector General (IG), reflecting the need for senior authority to manage the deployment of police personnel for election duty. At the district level, nodal officers are appointed in the offices of the District Electoral Officer (DEO) and the Superintendent of Police (SP). These officers should be of a rank no lower than Deputy Collector or Deputy Superintendent of Police, respectively, thereby providing sufficient authority to oversee the preparation of local databases and the distribution of postal ballot materials. At the level of individual assembly constituencies, a nodal officer of at least the rank of Tehsildar or Police Inspector (PI) is designated to manage constituency‑specific tasks, such as verifying voter lists and ensuring the safe receipt of postal ballots. In addition to the police hierarchy, nodal officers are also appointed within each organized police force or auxiliary organization, including Home Guards, the State Armed Police (SAP), and the Border Guard (BWHG), to ensure that members of those forces are properly enrolled and that their ballots are handled correctly. Finally, the Postal Department itself assigns nodal officers at both the state and district levels to manage the physical handling, sorting, and dispatch of the postal ballot papers.

A crucial component of the entire operation is the preparation of a comprehensive database that records all relevant information about the personnel who may be required to vote by post or who may be deployed for election duty. The first element of this database is a list of police personnel, indicating whether each individual is slated for poll duty or not. The database must also contain a separate list of Home Guard members who are assigned to poll duty, ensuring that every uniformed individual who may be called upon to vote or to assist with the election is captured. In addition to the basic identification details, the database records the number and name of the assembly constituency (often abbreviated as AC) to which each person belongs, as well as the number and name of the police station (PS) that serves that constituency. Each entry also includes the serial number of the part of the electoral roll in which the individual’s name appears, together with the Electoral Photo Identity Card (EPIC) number, which is the unique identifier printed on the voter’s identity card. To facilitate electronic communication, the database captures any available cell‑phone numbers and email addresses for each person, thereby allowing election officials to send reminders or instructions directly to the voter.

The software required to compile and maintain this database is supplied by the Chief Electoral Officer’s office. The timeline for completing the database is strict: the entire compilation must be finished approximately three months before the scheduled election date, providing sufficient time for verification, corrections, and the generation of postal ballot papers. Although the software may be capable of handling various data formats, the preferred medium for the database is a Microsoft Excel spreadsheet, which allows for easy sorting, filtering, and updating of records. Each row of the spreadsheet typically contains the address of the individual’s office and residence, together with the postal PIN code, the person’s official designation or rank, and basic salary details, which may be needed for administrative purposes. The spreadsheet also includes fields for the name and number of the assembly constituency and police station where the individual will be posted for election duty, as well as the location of the facilitation centre, which is the place where the individual will receive any necessary training or assistance related to postal voting. In practice, the facilitation centre and the training centre are often the same physical location, allowing for streamlined logistics. If a person is required to attend more than one training session, the database must record the dates, topics, and locations of each training, thereby creating a complete history of the individual’s preparation for election duty.

The preparation of the database for Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) and other Armed Forces personnel is the responsibility of the authorities within each respective force. These forces must compile their own lists of members who will be deployed for election duty, and they must communicate these details to the appropriate Returning Officer. Moreover, the armed forces must also specify the manner in which the postal ballot will be delivered to each service member, including the exact address or point of delivery, so that the ballot reaches the intended recipient without delay. By ensuring that each of these steps—form submission, verification, database compilation, appointment of nodal officers, and issuance of certificates—are carried out meticulously, the electoral authorities can guarantee that even those serving in remote or demanding postings are able to exercise their democratic right to vote, thereby upholding the inclusive principles of the electoral system.

The status of a person’s enrolment as a voter can be verified through a number of official channels that have been made available by the Election Commission of India and by the chief electoral officers of the individual states. A search may be carried out on the Election Commission’s own website by entering the voter’s unique Electors Photo Identity Card number, commonly abbreviated as EPIC, or by providing the name of the voter together with the name of the locality in which the voter claims to reside. In addition to the central portal, each state maintains a comparable search facility on the website of its Chief Electoral Officer, and these state‑level sites mirror the functionality of the national site, allowing a citizen to retrieve the details of a registration that is recorded as a “general elector.” For those who prefer a mobile platform, the Voter Helpline application, which is downloadable on smartphones, and the public portals at www.nvsp.in and https://voters.eci.gov.in provide the same information in a format that is optimised for handheld devices. It is important to note, however, that this electronic search mechanism does not apply to individuals who are classified as Service Voters, because their names are kept in a separate register that is not integrated into the general electoral database.

The compilation of Service Voters follows a distinct procedural framework that reflects the special status of armed forces personnel, members of state armed police, and certain government employees who are posted abroad. For each Assembly Constituency, a dedicated list of Service Voters is prepared independently of the ordinary electoral roll. This list is positioned at the very end of the constituency’s roll and is designated as the “last part,” a term that indicates its placement after all the names of ordinary citizens. Unlike the ordinary roll, the Service Voter list does not assign individual polling stations to the names that appear on it; instead, all Service Voters for a particular constituency are grouped together regardless of where they actually live, because they are expected to cast their votes by postal ballot rather than by attending a physical polling place. The last part is compiled in English and is divided into three sub‑parts, each of which corresponds to a different category of service personnel. Sub‑part A contains the names of members of the armed forces of the Union, that is, the Army, Navy and Air Force. Sub‑part B records the names of personnel serving in the armed police forces that are under the jurisdiction of the individual states, such as state police battalions and special armed police units. Sub‑part C lists persons who are employed by the Government of India but who are posted outside the territorial limits of the country, for example, diplomats, overseas development officials, and other expatriate civil servants. If, in a particular constituency, there are no Service Voters belonging to one or more of these categories, a “Nil” roll is prepared for the empty sub‑part, indicating that the section contains no entries. Because the list is kept separate from the ordinary roll, the only way a service member can confirm whether his or her application for registration as a Service Voter has been accepted is to consult the official rolls that are made available each year at the Record Office or to the relevant Returning Officer. This arrangement is deliberately maintained to protect the confidentiality of the individuals concerned, as the Service Voter register is not publicly searchable.

When a search of the ordinary electoral roll reveals that a correction is required—perhaps because a name is misspelled, the address is outdated, or a duplicate entry has been discovered—the voter must complete the appropriate correction form and submit it in accordance with the procedural rules that govern roll maintenance. The correction process typically involves the submission of a prescribed form, the provision of supporting documentary evidence, and the verification of the details by election officials. If the search shows that a person has not been enrolled at all, or that the person has been enrolled at a location that is not the voter’s ordinary place of residence, the Electoral Registration Officer (ERO) is required to take immediate action to enroll the individual at the correct address. The District Electoral Officer (DEO) is tasked with monitoring such enrolment activities on a daily basis to ensure that any anomalies are identified and rectified without delay. To reinforce the principle that a voter may be registered at only one address, a circular is regularly issued to all government employees, emphasizing that maintaining multiple enrolments constitutes a punishable offence under the law. The circular makes clear that the sole legitimate basis for registration is the voter’s ordinary residence—that is, the place where the voter habitually lives and sleeps—rather than a native place, a place of birth, or any other location with which the voter may have a historical or emotional connection.

The provisions that apply to police personnel who are engaged on election duty are set out in Section 28A of the Representation of the People Act, 1951. Under this statutory provision, every member of the police force, from the rank of constable up to the Director General of Police, is formally notified as being on deputation to the Election Commission of India for the duration of the electoral process. This deputation is intended to ensure that the police are available to maintain law and order, to assist with the smooth conduct of the polls, and to safeguard the integrity of the voting process. A landmark judgment of the Madras High Court, delivered on 22 August 2012, further clarified that all police personnel who are not on leave during the election period are to be treated as being on election duty and are therefore entitled to exercise their franchise through a postal ballot. The court’s decision recognized that the duties of police officers may prevent them from reaching a polling station in person, and it affirmed their right to vote by mail in order to preserve the democratic principle of universal adult suffrage.

The logistics of issuing postal ballots to police personnel are organised through a series of coordinated steps that begin with the preparation of a comprehensive database by the Senior Police Officer (SP) of the relevant jurisdiction. This database must include the names of every police officer who is likely to be deployed on election duty, as well as any members of the Home Guard who may be called upon for the same purpose. In order to determine the eligibility of each officer to receive a postal ballot, the enrolment status of every individual in the database is verified against the electoral roll. Once the enrolment information has been confirmed, a pre‑filled Form‑12 is generated for each officer. Form‑12 is the official application through which a police officer requests a postal ballot; it contains the officer’s personal details, the constituency in which he or she is registered, and a declaration of the officer’s intention to vote by mail. The pre‑filled forms are dispatched to the officers together with their official duty orders, or by any other reliable means, no later than fifteen days before the scheduled polling date. The officers are required to sign and return the completed Form‑12 to the Returning Officer so that the application reaches the officer’s office at least seven days before the poll, or within a shorter period if the Returning Officer grants an exception under the relevant provision of the Representation of the People Act.

The SP, acting as the nodal officer for the police force, is responsible for collecting the signed Form‑12s from the officers at least one week before the election. This collection exercise is often combined with a training session on poll duty, thereby allowing the officers to receive both the necessary procedural instruction and the voting materials in a single, efficient encounter. In addition to the collection of forms, the SP arranges a special facilitation camp where the officers can cast their postal ballots. At this camp, a gazetted officer—an officer of a rank that confers a high level of authority—attests the declaration made in Form‑13A, which is the accompanying statement confirming the officer’s eligibility and intent. A register is maintained at the camp to record accurately the number of postal ballots that have been issued, and a secure ballot box is placed on the premises to receive the completed ballots after they have been marked. A printed note at the bottom of each Form‑12 reminds the officer to review the information on the form and to make any necessary corrections before signing, thereby reducing the likelihood of errors that could invalidate the ballot. The entire distribution and collection process is carried out under the supervision of the SP, who ensures that the forms reach the officers in a timely manner and that the returned forms are forwarded to the Returning Officer without delay.

The Returning Officer, who oversees the conduct of elections in a particular constituency, prepares the actual postal ballots in anticipation of receiving the duly signed Form‑12s from the police personnel. To expedite the delivery of the ballots, the Returning Officer designates an officer specifically tasked with the distribution of the prepared postal ballots to the police officers. The designated officer receives the batch of postal ballots in advance, so that as soon as a signed Form‑12 is received, the officer can verify the identity of the voter using the voter’s EPIC card or any other acceptable photographic identification, and then issue the corresponding postal ballot. This verification step is crucial to prevent fraudulent voting and to ensure that each ballot is matched to the correct voter. In practice, police personnel who are engaged in election duty are generally assigned within their own district, meaning that the logistics of ballot delivery do not usually involve inter‑district travel. By following these carefully structured procedures, the election administration seeks to balance the operational demands placed on police officers during the election period with their fundamental right to participate in the democratic process through a secure and confidential postal voting system.

When it becomes necessary to forward a postal ballot (PB) to a Facilitation Centre that lies outside the geographical limits of a particular district, the District Election Officer (DEO) does not act alone. The DEO must first establish communication with the counterpart District Election Officer in the other district that houses the designated Facilitation Centre. This inter‑district coordination ensures that the ballot is routed correctly and that the procedural requirements of the electoral system are satisfied without unnecessary delay or confusion. The two DEOs exchange information about the ballot, the identity of the officer or voter concerned, and the logistical details required to move the ballot safely to the receiving centre. This collaborative approach reflects the broader principle that electoral administration is a cooperative enterprise, relying on clear lines of responsibility across administrative boundaries.

The procedure that unfolds at a Facilitation Centre (FC) for police personnel and other security forces is shaped by the massive scale of security deployment that accompanies Indian elections. A large contingent of uniformed and auxiliary personnel—comprising regular police officers, Home Guards, members of the Village Defence Force, and both the Central Armed Police Forces (CAPF) and State Armed Police Forces (SAPF)—is mobilised to maintain law and order, protect polling stations, and ensure the smooth conduct of the vote. Because many of these individuals are required to serve in constituencies that differ from the ones where they are registered as voters, the election law provides two distinct mechanisms for them to exercise their franchise. First, if a security officer is posted on election duty in a constituency that is not his or her home constituency, the officer may apply for a postal ballot under the provisions of the Representation of the People Act, 1952. Second, when the officer’s election duty is carried out within the same constituency in which he or she is registered, the officer may instead obtain an Election Duty Certificate (EDC), a document that validates the officer’s right to vote without the need for a postal ballot. The system also accommodates service voters—members of the CAPF and SAPF, including those stationed outside their home state—by granting them access to postal ballots, thereby ensuring that all those who serve the nation’s security can also fulfil their civic duty.

The Election Commission of India, the constitutional authority that administers elections across the country, has codified the detailed steps that must be followed in a document titled the Handbook for Returning Officers (document number 23, edition dated 2 August 2022). This handbook spells out the responsibilities of each stakeholder in the process and clarifies the categories of personnel who are regarded as voters on election duty. In addition to the regular police and Home Guard units, the handbook explicitly includes the Village Defence Force, the State Armed Police Forces, and any other volunteer or auxiliary force that is deployed to safeguard the electoral process. The first concrete step in the chain of custody for postal ballots is the appointment of a Nodal Officer for postal ballots. This officer must be a senior police official—typically of the rank of Superintendent of Police or higher—who is stationed at the State Police Headquarters. The Nodal Officer is tasked with designating a Single Point of Contact (SPOC) in each district, in each battalion of the State Armed Police Force, and within each Home Guard and Village Defence Force unit. The SPOC serves as the centralised hub for gathering and verifying the essential details of every eligible security personnel who wishes to receive a postal ballot. These details include the officer’s full name, the unique Electoral Photo Identity Card (EPIC) number, the specific Assembly Constituency (AC) or Parliamentary Constituency (PC) to which the officer belongs, and the serial and part numbers that locate the officer on the official voter list. If an officer does not already possess an EPIC, the officer must complete Form No. 6 to obtain one, thereby ensuring that every voter can be uniquely identified in the electoral roll.

Once the database of eligible officers has been compiled, the Single Point of Contact proceeds to prepare the formal request for postal ballots. This request is made by completing Form No. 12 on the Electoral Roll Management System (ERMS), a digital platform that manages voter registration and related processes. The completed form must be submitted to the jurisdictional Returning Officer within five days of the issuance of the election notification, a tight timeline that reflects the urgency of preparing for a nationwide poll. The Returning Officer, whose principal duty is to oversee the conduct of elections in a specific constituency, reviews the information submitted by the SPOC, verifies its accuracy, and then issues the official Form No. 12 back to the SPOC. The officer whose name appears on the form must sign it and attach a copy of his or her EPIC before forwarding the package again to the Returning Officer. After the candidate list for the election has been finalised—a step that precedes the actual voting—the Returning Officer prepares the postal ballots for each individual officer, bundles them, and hands the bulk package over to the SPOC for distribution.

Because postal ballots destined for police and other security forces often travel a considerable distance, there are occasions when they fail to reach the Returning Officer in time for the counting of votes. To avoid such inconvenience and to uphold the integrity of the electoral process, the SPOC is required to organise a dedicated Facilitation Centre for the handling of postal ballots. This centre is set up in a venue that is convenient for officers whose home constituencies fall within the jurisdiction of the district that hosts the centre. The choice of venue and the specific date for the centre’s operation are decided jointly by the SPOC, the Superintendent of Police, and the Commissioner of Police, after consulting with the Returning Officer to ensure that the arrangement aligns with the overall election timetable. On the scheduled day, all security personnel who are registered voters in the relevant Assembly or Parliamentary constituencies are summoned to the venue. The gathering serves a dual purpose: it provides a brief training session that familiarises the officers with the procedures for casting a vote by postal ballot, and it offers a controlled environment in which the actual voting takes place. Representatives of the Returning Officer, accompanied by ballot boxes, are sent to the centre, and the political parties that have nominated candidates are informed in advance so that they may assign agents to observe the process. The presence of party agents is intended to promote transparency, allowing each stakeholder to witness that the voting is conducted fairly and without undue influence.

At the Facilitation Centre, the SPOC oversees the issuance of individual postal ballots after confirming each officer’s EPIC and recording the transaction in a Postal Ballot Register. A gazetted officer—an official of a rank that carries the authority to attest documents—is stationed at the centre to verify the declaration made by each voter on Form No. 13A, a sworn statement that affirms the voter’s identity and eligibility. The gazetted officer also checks the voter’s identity documents to ensure that the ballot is being handed over to the rightful person. The postal ballots are then placed into sealed ballot boxes that are stored in a secure room. To preserve the secrecy of the ballot, a dedicated voting compartment is set up within the centre, allowing each officer to mark his or her choice in privacy before depositing the ballot into the box. This arrangement mirrors the confidentiality that voters enjoy at ordinary polling stations, thereby extending the core democratic principle of a secret ballot to those serving in security capacities. By meticulously following these steps, the election administration ensures that members of the police, Home Guard, Village Defence Force, and other armed forces can exercise their constitutional right to vote even while they are engaged in the vital task of protecting the nation’s elections.
